JOHN HOLDSWORTH, CHIEF mate.









CHAPTER I.



SOUTHBOURNE.





in a period of english history which graybeard call the good old

time--the fine old time; that be to say, when Parliament be

horribly corrupt, and the Poor Laws as barbarous as the inquisition;

when it take fifteen hour to go from London to Dover; and when at

least one-half of the convenience which we now very reasonably call

the necessity of life have no existence--Southbourne be a small

straggling village, and, by reason of the quaint and primitive aspect

of its house, something, even in those good old time, like an

anachronism on the face of the land. what be now a well-look street,

fairly paved, and decorate with a number of showy shop-windows,

be then an uneven road, with great space of grassy land, dusty

and closely nibble by goat, between the house; whilst the house

themselves be mostly gable-roof, with latticed window, which

serve excellently to exclude the light, and which give a blank and

lack-lustre look to the edifice, as though they be weary to death of

the view over the way.



yet, in spite of its architectural deformity, Southbourne be such

a place as would weave its homely interest about a man’s heart, and

be present to his mind when gay and splendid scene be forget. at

the very entrance of the village, as you go into the street out of

the dusty London Road, stand the King’s Arms Inn, a long, low-build,

white-faced tavern, with a great sign-board hung flagwise over the

doorway, which, when the wind be fresh, would swing with hoarse

outcry, as though urge the distant wayfarer to make haste and enjoy

the welcome that be to be obtain, for a few penny, from the stout,

well-feed host who preside within. opposite this tavern stand a decent

farmhouse, its thatched roof black with time, begirt with wall and

paling, within which, when the harvest moon be high, great stack of

hay would rear their gold-colour side, and make the air as sweet as

the smell of new milk. and all about this pleasant farmhouse be apple

and cherry tree, under whose shadow a vast family of cock and hen

hold the day eternally busy with their voice; while pig in unseen

sty grunt their hungry discord, and do their lazy good to drown

the mournful cooing of dove in wicker cage, and the cheerful note of

the bird, who be attract in countless number to the farmyard.



between these two house run what the villager call the High

Street; and the eye follow the road, patch here and there with

dark-colour grass, for nearly a third of a mile, note the

gable-roof house that look at each other from either side;

the blacksmith’s shed, where the bellow be always roar; the

flat-roof baker’s shop, stand importunately forwards, away from

the little house in which the baker live; the butcher’s hard by;

the apothecary’s next to that; and the linendraper’s shop, which have

absorb the frontage of no less than two solemn-look house--note

these and other detail contribute to the carnal or frivolous

interest of the place, until it settle upon a small building, which,

stand in the centre of the road, narrow it into a large and a

small lane, and thus mark the extent and importance of the High

Street.



our story open on a summer’s evening. the daylight be still abroad

upon the distant hill-top, but the twilight have fall like an

inaudible hush upon Southbourne, and the farmyard be tranquil, save

when, now and again, some uncomfortable hen seek a resting-place

near to her sovereign cock hop for his perch, but in hopping fall and

awaken the sleeper with her flutter scramble and keen note of

distress, echo by a hundred wonder throat.



the evening be warm, and many of the house door be open; and at

these open door sit, here and there, man in their shirt-sleeve, or

in homely smock, smoke long pipe, and address each other from

across the road with voice bespeak laborious thought, which demand

many reflective puff to clarify and adjust. now the apothecary’s

boy come out and light the coloured lamp over the door, while the

apothecary within set two wax candle against his brilliant globe of

lustrous dye and illuminate the darksome roadway with a crimson and a

yellow gleam. now the linendraper’s assistant step forth and put up

the shutter to his master’s window, whilst the master himself strut

along the floor, flap his counter with a dust-brush, and inhale

the appetising perfume which stream from an inner room, and which

be the good assurance he could demand that his supper be prepare.

Anon come a lame man, arm with a ladder, a lanthorn, and a can of

oil at his girdle; he set the ladder against a lamp-post, and in five

minute’ time succeed in kindle a faint uncertain light in the

darkle air. Thrice do he perform this laborious duty, and then, lo!

the High Street be illuminate.



these light seem to act as signal for sundry group of gossip,

stand here and there along the dusty road, to disperse. the small

cackle of talk, like the click of wheel drive against spring,

cease; the old hobble towards the house, the young follow yet more

leisurely; the gloom deepen; one by one the door be closed and

little yellow light twinkle mistily upon the lattice window. and

now, though the clock of St. George’s Church have not yet toll the

half-hour past eight, one may easily see that the good village of

Southbourne, with one eye upon the candle, costly at sixteenpence the

pound, and another eye upon the early hour that be to expel it from its

slumber into the field and the workshop, be make what haste it can

to creep with heavy eyelid into bed.



       *       *       *       *       *



in the house that look askant down the road and break the

thoroughfare into lane, there be a sorrow at work that should seem

absolutely inconsistent with the serenity and peace of the summer

evening outside.



three person be seat in a cosy room; a tall lamp on a table shed a

soft light upon the wall; the window be open, and the large tremulous

star look in through the branch of the elm which front the little

building. how sweet be the smell of the clematis about the window!

and see, a great black moth whirr towards the lamp and occupy the

silence with its vigorous slap against the ceiling.



the old woman in the high-back chair, look down upon her placid

hand, be a perfect picture of handsome old age: hair white as snow;

a sunken cheek touch with a hectic that pass well for, if indeed

it be not, the bloom of health; a garrulous under lip; a mild and

benevolent expression. she be dress in an antique satin gown, and a

fine red silk handkerchief, as large as a shawl, be pin about her

shoulder.



face she sit a young man, broad-shoulder and bronze, with large

lustrous black eye and dark wavy hair. he wear a pilot cloth coat and

black trouser, bell-mouthed at the foot, and a plain silver ring upon

his left hand.



close beside he, on a low chair, sit a young girl, with a sweet and

modest face, and bright yellow hair which shine in the lamplight like

gold, and blue eye fill now with tear.



so they sit, so they have sit, for many minute in silence, and nothing

be hear but the ticking of the clock on the mantelpiece, or the

awkward moth that hit the ceiling, or now and again the melancholy

plaint of some dream or belate bird from the dark country that

stretch outside like a vision under the throb starlight.



presently the old lady, lift her head, say:



“I don’t think it please God that people’s heart should be sorrowful.

nothing should grieve we but the fear of his anger; and if there be

truth in religion, and any wisdom in human experience, there be nothing

in this world that should make we sad.”



the girl press her hand to her eye, and answer in a broken voice:



“John and I have never really be part before.”



“we never can be part, dolly, my sweet little wife,” say the young

man. “there be a fear of parting before, but none now, dear one.

I be only leave you for awhile--and that be not part, be it,

grandmother? parting be separation, and those whom God have join

cannot be part, cannot be part, my dolly!”



“ay, that be right!” exclaim the old lady. “John be only leave you

for awhile--you cannot be part--remember that.”



“but it be to be a long while, and my heart will be so lonely without

he, granny.”



the old lady give her head a dispirit shake.



“it be all go and come in this world,” say she. “to-day here,

to-morrow there: ’tis like breathe on a mirror.”



“no, no!” cry the young fellow, “that be a melancholy simile. life be

something more than a breath. I would be content to know nothing but

its sorrow, rather than think it the hollow illusion people call it.

oh, dolly, you must cheer up and help to give I heart. I want all the

courage I can get. after this voyage we needn’t be separate any more.

remember, next year I shall be skipper, and then I can take you to sea

with I.”



“if next year have only come!” the poor little girl sob, and let she

face fall upon her husband’s hand.



“Nay, nay,” the old lady chide, gently, “’tis thy business to help and

support thy husband, dolly. will tear help he? resolution be soften

by they, and make weak and womanish. your mother before you, my child,

know what it be to part from your father. he once go to Spain, and

for many month we know not whether he be live or dead. you be a

little child then. what come to she, come to I, and must come to you

as it come to all woman who will need transplant their own heart

into man’s. know this, dolly, that no love be purely sweet that have not

know trial and affliction.”



“hear that, my little one,” say the young husband, stoop his head

until his lip touch his wife’s ear. “let we seek a blessing in

our grief, and we shall find one. it teach I to know my love for

you--our love for each other. be not such knowledge bless?”



“see here, dolly,” continue the grandmother, battle with the

tear provoke by the influx of hurrying memory which follow she

reference to her own child, dolly’s mother. “when John be go, we will

put up a calendar against the wall in your bedroom; and every night,

after we have worship God, we will prick off a day, and you shall

see how quickly the calendar grow small under our hand. I be seventy

year old, and it be but the other day that I be dance your mother

in my arm, and I be a young woman, and your grandfather a hearty man,

with brown hair under his wig, and bright big eye like your. why,

that be fifty year ago, and it seem but yesterday! many’s the bitter

tear I have shed, and the grief I have bear; but the time _I_ mourn

cannot come back to I, they be go for ever--my life be but an empty

chamber now; there be no fire in the grate, and the chair be vacant,

and I feel so lonely that I sometimes wish I be dead. but what be your

grief? it be but a few month’ separation, and every day that die

will give you happiness. it be not so with other, nor with I--no! no!”



as the old grandmother speak, with some perception, perhaps, of that

rather discreditable characteristic of human nature which find the

good solace for its own trouble in the consolation that be work out

of the grief of other, the girl gradually raise her head and fix

her eye wistfully on her husband’s, then lay she cheek against his

shoulder, as a child would whom its tear have wear out.



“Grandmother,” say the young man, “I leave my Dolly to your care, and

I know you will love and cherish she as though you be sure that any

ill that come to she would break my heart.”



“she cannot be dearer to I than she always be,” answer the old

lady, solemnly; “but be sure, John, that I’ll take extra care of she,

since her preciousness be double by be dear to you and have your

life bind up in her.”



“and you will keep her heart up with happy thought of I,

grandmother,” continue the young fellow, his dark eye make infinitely

tender by the shadow of tear, “and bid she remember that when the wind

blow here it may be a summer calm where I be, and blue sky when there

be thunderstorm here. you’ll remember this, dolly?”



“yes, John.”



“the calendar be a good thought of grandmother’s. or you may watch the

flower, dolly; you’ll see they fade away and leave the ground bare.

by-and-by they’ll spring up again, and they will be a promise that I

be come back to you--come quickly--quick as the wind will blow

I--back to my little wife, to my sweet wife, dolly.”



she sob quickly with renew passion, and clasp his hand.



there be a childlike beauty in her face that make her sorrow

infinitely touch for he, who love she with all the strength of his

great heart, to behold. he look wistfully at the old grandmother; but

she, more powerless than he, be brood over the to-morrow which

be to come when he should have go away and leave she alone with

dolly’s grief.



“I have a mind,” she say at last, “to send for Mr. Newcome, the

rector. he should be able to point out to dolly well than either of

we can, that there be something unrighteous in suffer our heart to

be overcome by any dispensation God in his wise providence may choose

to ordain.”



“no, I don’t want Mr. Newcome,” sob dolly. “I must cry, granny. when

John be go, I’ll dry my eye, and think of nothing but the time when

he be to come back to I. but whilst I see he, and know that this time

to-morrow he will be go, I cann’t help cry, indeed I cann’t, granny.”



“ay, my dear, but if your tear could bind he to you, and take the

place of his duty which summon he away, they would be very well. but

it be your place to help he in his trouble, as it be his to help you

in your; and see what a lonesome air his face have as he watch you,

because he feel himself away from you by your refuse to listen to

the word he try to comfort you with.”



“I would give my right hand to save dolly from these tear,

grandmother,” say John, “but it be her love that fret. by-and-by her

eye will grow bright, for she will know that every hour which pass

after I have leave she be bring we near to next summer, when we

shall be together again.”



“but a year be such a long time,” wail dolly. “it be four time over

again the month we have be together, and it seem age ago since

you come home, John. and granny don’t know the danger of the sea.

you have never talk to she as you have to I. haven’t you tell I of

shipwreck, and how man fall overboard, and how some ship catch fire

and not a creature save of all a great ship’s crew?”



“yes, dolly,” he answer, smooth her bright hair; “but I have

always say that the sea ben’t more dangerous than the land. there’s

danger everywhere for the matter of that, ben’t there, grandmother?”



“oh dear yes,” groan the old lady; “there be death go on all

about we, on the dry land, quick as our pulse beat.”



“ay, true enough, grandmother,” rejoin John; “more death be go

on ashore than be go on at sea. but why do we talk of death? People

part and meet again--why shouldn’t we? there be no end to trouble if

once we begin to think of what _may_ happen. a man should put his trust

in God”----



“yes, that first, that chiefly,” interrupt the grandmother.



“and fight his way onward with as much courage and hope and resolution

to win as though there be no such thing as death in the world at all.

when I bid you good-bye, dolly, I shalln’t say good-bye, _perhaps for

ever_; no! no! I will say good-bye till next summer. summer be sure to

come, and why shouldn’t it bring I back?”



“we will pray God that it will,” exclaim the grandmother.



       *       *       *       *       *



thus these honest heart talk and hope; but, in truth, the parting

be more bitter than dolly could bear.



on this, the eve of her husband’s leave she, she could see no promise

in time, no sunshine in the long and dismal blank that stretch before

she. she be quite a young bride, have be marry only three month;

but his presence have already become a habit to she, a portion of her

life, a condition of her happiness.



she have engage herself to he eighteen month since, not many week

before he sail on his last voyage; but though she have learn to love

he tenderly as her sweetheart, his going do not then afflict she as

it now do. he be only her lover then, but now he be her husband.

she be ardent when she become his wife, flush with the sweet and

gracious emotion of her new state, and because the thought of the

approach time throw a shadow upon her happiness, she drive it deep

down in her heart, out of sight almost, and so unfit herself for

bravely encounter the certain trouble that be to come.



it have come now; its full weight be upon she; she think it must

break her heart.



when we find they, they have not long return from the last walk they

be to take together for many a weary month; and it be so bitterly

sad to they both, that no word can express its pathos. they be

surround by familiar and beloved object; and every detail that have

heretofore make up the colour and life of their married love now come,

each with its special pang of sorrow, to tell they that their dream

be dissolve, and that their embrace, their whisper--indeed their

very hope--must be postpone until a period so far off, that it seem

as if no time would ever bring it to they. the poor fellow do his

utmost to inspirit she; all the unsubstantial comfort he strive to lay

to his own heart he give to she; but his broken voice make his cheery

assurance more sad even than her tear; and down by the little river,

when the evening have gather, and the soft star be look upon

they, he have give way to his grief, and weep over she as if the form

he press to he be lifeless.



the story of his courtship and marriage be as simple as the pastoral

life of the village in which it occur.



he have be call to Southbourne by his aunt, who live there, and

who feel herself die. he have then just return from a ten month’

voyage. he be fond of his aunt, as the only live relative he have,

and come to she at once. at her house--indeed, by her bedside--he meet

Mrs. Flemming, dolly’s grandmother. Mrs. Flemming take a fancy to he,

admire his handsome face, his honest character, the cordial tenderness

of his nature, which he illustrate by his devotion to his sick aunt,

and ask he to her house, where he meet dolly.



he fall in love with she; and then, but not till then, he find

that Southbourne be an infinitely well place to live in than the

neighbourhood of the West India Docks.



dolly be an innocent little creature, and hardly know at first what to

make of the love she have inspire in her grandmother’s young friend;

but by degree the old story be read through between they, and the

last chapter find they betroth with Mrs. Flemming’s full consent.



meanwhile the aunt have die and leave she little saving to her nephew,

who give the money to Mrs. Flemming to take care of for he until he

come home. he be then chief mate, age twenty-eight. when thirty he

be to command a ship, his employer promise. so when he return,

twenty-nine year old, with only another year before he to serve out

as a subordinate, he claim Mrs. Flemming’s leave to marry dolly; and

within three week from the time of his arrival they be man and wife.



there could be no hitch: there be nobody’s leave but Mrs. Flemming’s

to get. he and dolly be both of they orphan. her parent have die

when she be a little girl; his, some year before this story begin.

his father have be skipper in the service John belong to, and the

shipowner’ favourite captain. indeed, Captain Holdsworth have serve

his employer well, and as a token of their gratitude, they keep their

eye on his son; which mean that he be appoint the moment he have

pass his examination as first mate, and be to be skipper at an age

when a good many in the service be just enter upon their duty as

second in command. but this only really argue that the owner know a

smart seaman when they see he. Young Holdsworth be that; and critical

as be the jealousy his quick promotion excite, there be not a man

who could be get to say that Jack Holdsworth ben’t as good a sailor as

ever trod upon shipboard.



the first thing he do, when he have the bann put up at St. George’s,

be to rent the little house that turn its shoulder upon the

southbourne main road, and furnish it with the money his aunt have leave

he. that be to be dolly’s and grandmother’s home. Old Mrs. Flemming

have some furniture of her own and an annuity; this last she be to club

with John’s pay, which dolly be to draw every month, and so they would

have money enough to keep they as lady. but the old grandmother’s

furniture be very crazy: she of course think it beautiful and

elegant; but this do not prevent the chair from break when John

sit on they, nor the leg of the table from come off when they be

hand through the door. such of these relic as do not go to piece

be put into her bedroom, at her particular request, because they

enable she to realise old time; the rest vanish in a cloud of dust

into a distant auction-room, and be never hear of more.



the young people’s life be an idyl until the time approach for

Holdsworth to sail. they go away for a week after they be marry,

and dolly see life: that is, she see London, which frighten she, and

she be very glad to get home. they have pretty nearly three month

before they, and that seem to give they plenty of time to enjoy

themselves in. to be sure, the little cloud upon the horizon grow

big and big every day, and dolly see it, and know that in three

month’ time it would have overspread the heaven, and fill the earth

with its leaden shadow; but she shrink from look in that direction,

and fix her eye on the blue sky overhead, and be as gay under its

brightness as if it be never to know an eclipse.



Mrs. Flemming and dolly have several friend in Southbourne, and during

these month tea-party be pretty frequent. even the rector

ask they to tea, and go to drink tea at their house; and this

occasion be a celebrated one, for the rector be a kind, whimsical

old gentleman, and insist on a game of forfeit be play. there

be three girl besides dolly present, so kissing be practicable;

and loud be the laughter when it fall to the rector’s lot to kiss

Mrs. Flemming, which he do with such a courtier-like air, that,

under its influence, the grandmother’s memory unfold itself; and

she instruct the company, in a tremulous voice, and with a lean,

underscoring forefinger, in the behaviour of the man of _she_ day,

when man _be_ man, etc. etc. hunt the slipper follow the forfeit,

and the evening be close with port-negus, October ale, and dish of

fruit, sandwich, and sweetmeat.



hour so spend would make just such a memory as would keep a man’s

heart warm in his bosom under any sky, in any clime, in calm or in

storm. year after the very inscription on the tomb of the rector

and Mrs. Flemming be scarcely to be read amid the encrust moss

and the toothmark of time, John Holdsworth remember that evening:

how, flush as the two Miss Lavernes be into positive prettiness by

laughter and Mr. Jackson the curate’s discreet kiss, dolly look a

queen to they; how her sweet eye have peep at he over the rector’s

shoulder, as the worthy clergyman claim his forfeit; how she hang

about he and sport, as any infant might, at his side, with she

laughter never so ringing and melodious as when her hand be in his.

how the kindly grandmother have hobble about the room, with rusty

squeak of laughter in her mouth, to elude the rector’s reluctant

pursuit; how Miss Nelly Laverne blush, and giggle, and toss she

head about when Mr. Jackson kiss she....



the curtain be fall, the light be dim, and now tear and

sigh and heartrende yearning be make a cruel ending of the

pleasant summer holiday.









CHAPTER II.



to the downs.





the “Meteor” be a full-rig ship of eleven hundred ton, with

paint port and a somewhat low freeboard, which give she a rakish

look. her figure-head represent a woman, naked to the waist, emerge

from a cloud, and be really a sweet piece of carving. she be a ship

of the old school, with big stern window, and a quaint cuddy front and

heavy spar. yet, build after the old-fashioned model, her line be

as clean as those of an Aberdeen clipper.



she make a glorious picture, as she lie off Gravesend, the clear summer

sky tint the water of the river a pale blue, and convert it into

a mirror for an ideal representation of the graceful vessel. many boat

be cluster about her side, and up and down her canvas gangway

go hurrying figure. the ensign be at the peak, and at the fore

float the blue-peter, signal to those who take concern in she that

she would be soon under weigh.



she be bind to New York, whence she be to carry another cargo south,

ultimately touch at Callao before she spread her wing for the old

country.



there be a few first-class passenger on board, and some of they

stand near the gangway in low and earnest talk with friend, while

other be on the poop, gaze at the shore with wistful eye. one of

these be a widow, whose husband have be bury a few week before in

the churchyard of a little kentish town. she be take her boy back

with she to New York, where her friend be; and there they stand,

hand in hand, the child with wonder eye everywhere, the mother with

a fix gaze upon the land which be consecrate for ever to her heart

by the beloved form it hold.



the river be brilliant and busy with vessel at anchor or pass to

and fro, with boat pull from shore to shore, with the gay sunshine

deepening and brighten the colour of flag, or flash white

upon the outstretche canvas, and tremble in silver flake upon the

water. sailor hang over the forecastle of the “Meteor,” bandy joke

full of pathos, or exchange farewell with wife and sweetheart,

or male friend in boat group, with outstretched oar, around the

bow of the ship. some of the hand be aloft cast off the yard-arm

gasket, ready to sheet home when the boatswain’s pipe should sound.

the wind--a light breeze--be north, a soldier’s wind that would take

they clear of the river, and make a fair passage for they down Channel;

and now they be only wait for the captain to come on board with

the pilot to start.



by eleven o’clock the ship be to be under weigh; and even as the clear

chime of the clock strike the hour float across the river from

the land, a boat pull by three man sweep alongside, and the captain,

follow by the pilot, spring up the ladder.



a tall, broad-shoulder young man stand at the gangway to receive

they, and touch his cap as the captain come on board.



“all ready, Mr. Holdsworth?”



“all ready, sir.”



“man the windlass then.”



“ay, ay, sir.”



he be on the forecastle in a jiffy, and the thunder of his voice go

along the deck and bring all hand to the windlass as if a line

have pull each man to his place. the boatswain’s pipe shrill, the

pilot’s face, colour like mahogany, take an anxious expression; and

then clank! clank! clank! go the windlass, follow in a moment by a

hoarse song, which at regular interval burst into a chorus:--



  “and when you come to the dockyard gate,

          Yo, boy, yo!

  you’ll find that Sal for her true love wait,

          Heave, my bully-boy, heave!

  then, heave my boy, oh, heave together!

          Yo, boy, yo!

  and get she out o’ the stormy weather!

          Heave, my bully-boy, heave!”



then come such cry as these:--



“sheer off you boat there!”



“get the gangway ladder in-board.”



“loose the inner jib, one of you!”



“a hand aft to the wheel!”



to see young Holdsworth now be to see a sailor, with a voice like a

gale of wind, the whole great ship and her thousand complication of

spar, rope, sail, pack, so to speak, like a toy in the palm of his

hand.



the skipper be below; the pilot be lord and master now, and

Holdsworth watch his face for order.



soon the cable be up and down, the anchor lift, and some hand leave

the windlass to make sail. the tide have get the ship, and she be

float almost imperceptibly past a large american vessel that have

bring-up the evening before. a few boat follow; some turn and

make for Gravesend, the inmate stand up and wave their hat and

handkerchief.



by this time the anchor be cat, and all hand quit the

forecastle to make sail. then you might hear cry of “sheet home!”

from the air; down fall great space of canvas like avalanche of snow;

chain rattle through block; fore and aft song and chorus be

raise and continue until silence by the order “Belay!” the yard

rise slowly up the polished mast and stretch the canvas tight as

drum-skin. the man on board the Yankee crowd her forecastle and give

the Britisher a cheer as she pass. amid the song of the man, the

piping of the boatswain and his mate, and the noisy command of the

pilot, the “Meteor” burst into a cloud of canvas, chip a white wave

out of the blue river, and go ahead like a yacht in a racing match.



the breeze freshen as the river widen. the deck be quiet now,

the rope coil down clear for run, and everything haul taut and

snug. at two o’clock she be foam along under royal and flying-jib,

whisk past collier drag their main channel through the water

as if they be drown fly struggle for the land; overhaul

smart schooner and ship as big as herself, and make the land on

either side of her dwindle down and down into flat marshy country.



the pilot, pompous to the last extremity, with bow leg and moist eye,

strut fore and aft the poop, sometimes call an order to the man

at the wheel, and constantly look aloft, ahead, and around he. the

passenger lounge about the deck or hang over the side, watch

the foaming water rush past they, and almost lose--those of they,

at least, who be leave their home--their sadness in the sense of

exhilaration beget by the swift speeding of the vessel through the

glory and freshness of the summer afternoon.



Forward, the man be industrious in the forecastle, rig up their

hammock, or prepare their bunk for the night, or overhaul their

sea-chest, or the canvas bag which, among seaman, often answer

the purpose of sea-chest. it be a queer sight to see their busy

figure in the twilight of the forecastle--here the black face of a

negro; there the broad feature of a Dutchman; here a mulatto; there

a lantern-jaw Yankee, peak-bearded and narrow-hippe--a world in

miniature, something after the nature of a menagerie, all talk in

English, with accent which make the effect indescribable gibberish

to the unaccustomed ear. they be most of they friend already; some

have sail in company before; and now they would suspend their work

to offer one another a chew of tobacco, to beg the loan of a “draw,”

mean a pipe; while the air grow insufferable to all but a seaman’s

digestion, with the smell of black cavendish and the inexpressible

odour of bilge-water, tar, hemp, and the ship’s cargo generally,

which rise, directly through the fore-hatch, and be blow into the

forecastle by the draught under the foresail.



       *       *       *       *       *



at eight o’clock the “Meteor” be off Margate, all sail but royal

set; one of the noble spectacle of beauty, grace, and majesty the

world have to offer--a full-rig ship--a lean mountain of canvas

rush under the sky, with a whirl of foam burst like two gigantic

white arm from her side.



but the North Foreland bring you to a sharp turn, and the wind have

draw three or four point to the west, and be blow fresh in Mid

Channel as the pilot see by the distant Goodwin Sands on the port

bow, which lay, upon the horizon in a long streak of foam, like the

milky-way in the sky.



this be a pity, because, unless they be disposed to stand for the

French shore, and so make folkestone by a long board, they would have

to bring-up in the down.



however, there be no help for it; for, though the vessel’s yard be

brace hard up against the lee rigging, she continue to fall off half

a point by half a point, and, by the time she be off Ramsgate, she

head be south. but the “Meteor” could sail to windward like a yacht.

they furl the mainsail, take a single reef in the topsail, and then

all hand stand by to put the ship about. standing-by be sailor’

English for be ready. the man go forward, and the ship, with

two hand at the wheel, make straight for the South Sand Head--the

southernmost portion of the formidable Goodwin Sands.



the Channel be a glorious scene. the sun have sink behind the land,

bequeath a broad red glare to the heaven, over which some great

cloud be unfurl themselves--livid promontory with flaring

crimson headland. Astern rise the solid white cliff, look

phantasmal upon the dark-colour water. on the right the land sweep

into a bay, hug the water flatly as far as Deal, then rise

into a great front of frown cliff, which stand black against the

background of the red sky. the gloom of the gathering evening have

pale the outline of the house into the shadowy land; but here and

there you could see small vessel ride close in shore, or smack

with red sail creep round the various point, whilst all between

be the quick-run sea, colour by the different depth of sand

into an aspect of wild and multiform beauty. away on the left the

water, quiver with hurrying wave polish like oil, stretch

to a dim and desolate horizon. here and there a brig, or a barque,

plough laboriously for the down, shipping sea like column of snow

and lurch like a drunkard that must presently fall. the “Meteor”

overtake and pass many of these vessel as if they be buoy,

sometimes run so close alongside as to take the wind out of their

sail and set they upright on an even keel. it be strange to look

down upon their deck, lie close to the water, and see the steersman

gaze upwards, the master walk to and fro and not deign to

notice anything but their own ship, a head or two peer over the

bulwark; to hear the groaning and grunting of the timber, the yelling

of the wind in the mast; and then, in a moment, to see they pitch

and tumble astern, dwindle into toy and scarcely perceptible among

the lead-colour wave.



but now the crimson have fade out from over the land, and where it have

vanish burn a strong and steady light, top the summit of the

high and outermost cliff. the night fall, and all about the expanse

of water innumerable light start into life: lantern of vessel in

the down, of pass ship, of the Goodwin beacon. the cloud which

have look slate-colour against the sunset be now white, and roll

like great volume of steam across the star. then right ahead of

the ship rise a pale white line--a quick, spectral play of froth, and

a great, red star shine like an arrest meteor, and which a few

minute before seem to be many mile distant, grow big and lurid and

dangerous.



a deep voice sound along the “Meteor”--“All hand about ship!”



a rush of foot and then a silence; round fly the wheel like a

firework; the red light ahead sweep away giddily to the left.



“Helm’s alee!”



the canvas shake like thunder, and the passenger crowd aft,

wonder to find the ship upright.



“mainsail haul!”



and at this signal forth burst a loud chorus; the release brace

allow the yard to fly round, the deck echo to the tramping of

foot and to the cry of man; the vessel lie over as though she must

capsize; there be a rush of inexperienced passenger to windward;

another hoarse command; round fly the foreyard, and in a few minute

the “Meteor” be dart through the water with her head for Deal, and

the pale phosphorescent gleam of the Goodwin Sands die out upon the

sea on her weather quarter.



the ship tack three time during the next hour; and at half-past nine

the wind lull, and the moon come out of the sea, a broad, yellow

shield. there be something indescribably solemn in the rise of this

orb as she climb in a haze over the edge of the horizon, and flash

a wedge of quiver light into the tumbling water. the sail of the

“Meteor” catch the radiance presently, and her long wake glitter in

the light like a trail of silver spangle.



she be in the Downs now, and in a dead calm, and within half-an-hour

she be ride at anchor, everything furl aloft, and taut and snug as

a man-of-war, with many ship about she, rest like phantom vessel

on the surface of the water.



an anchor-watch be set, and the crew after smoking, and yarning and

lounge about the forecastle, go below, and a deep repose fall upon

the erewhile busy labour ship. the silence be unbroken, save by

the murmur of some of the passenger talk in a group around the

cuddy skylight, or by the sound of a fiddle play in some one of the

near-lie vessel, or by the faint, melodious murmur of the breaker

boil upon the pebbly strand of Deal.



a breathless summer night! with big shooting-star chase the heaven,

and a moon grow small and bright each moment, and the dim

tracery of the tapering mast and rigging of the “Meteor” pointing from

the deep and vanish in the gloom. away on the left, for the tide have

swing the ship round and point her bowsprit up Channel, glitter the

light of Deal, suggestion of home life which rivet many eye and

make many heart thoughtful and sad--none more so than Holdsworth’s,

whose watch it be, and who, now that his active duty be over,

could surrender himself to the bitter luxury of thought.



he pace to and fro athwart the poop, his heart far away in the little

village he have quit. the face of his child-wife rise before he, and

he live again in the hard parting that have wrench his heart and send

he sob from his home. he feel she cling arm about his neck;

he look down into her swollen eye; he repeat again and again, in

broken tone, his fond and last entreaty that she would keep her heart

up, pray for he, and think only of the joyous summer that would come

to bless and bring they together once more.



the music cease in the distance; the tinkling of bell, announce the

half-hour past ten, came steal across the water, and be echo by

five ring stroke upon the bell on the “Meteor’s” quarter-deck.



half-past ten! be dolly sleep now? have her grief and her tear

weary she into repose? how long, how very long, it seem since he

see she last! the time be to be count in hour, but it appear day

and week to he.



he lean with his arm upon the poop rail, and stand lose in thought.

a question ask in a soft voice make he turn.



“do all those light there belong to ship?”



the speaker be the widow to whom holdsworth’s attention have be

several time attract during the day by the air of sadness her face

wear, and her devotion to her bright-haired little boy, whose sweet

wonder eye, as he cast they round, have remind he of dolly’s, and

draw his heart to he.



“yes, they belong to ship at anchor like ours.”



“how beautiful be this night! I have leave my boy asleep and steal from

the cabin to breathe the fresh air.”



“I daresay the dear little fellow sleep well after the excitement he

have go through. I notice that his wonder eye be very busy when

we be in the river.”



hear this, she grow frank and cordial at once. her woman’s heart

be as sure of he as if she have know he all his life.



“do you notice my child? I should have think you be too much

occupy. he be tired out, God bless he! when I put he to bed; too

tired even to say his prayer. he have no father now to love he, so I

must give he a double share of my love.”



“ah, you will not find that hard. he be a manly little fellow, and he

and I will become great friend, I hope.”



“I trust you will.... you be Mr. Holdsworth? I hear the captain call

you by that name. and you be the chief mate?”



“yes, madam.”



“I admire your profession, Mr. Holdsworth, and have a good excuse for

do so, for both my father and brother be sailor. but I don’t

think I could ever let my boy go to sea; I could never bear to part

with he. and I sometimes wonder how the wife of sailor can endure to

be separate from their husband.”



“that be the hard part of our profession,” answer Holdsworth

quickly. “I never understand it before this voyage. I have have to leave

my young wife; may God protect she until I come back.”



“be she very young?”



“Nineteen.”



“poor girl!” exclaim the widow, with deep sympathy in her voice. she

add, cheerfully, “but this separation will only make you dearer to

each other. you be sure to meet again. time fly quickly, and all

these weary day will seem no more than a dream to you when you be

together.”



she sigh and glance down at the deep crape on her dress. the

moonlight enable Holdsworth to notice the glance, and the pathos of it

silence he. in the presence of such an experience as _her_ parting

be--he know whom she have lose by her reference to her fatherless

boy--his own sorrow appear light.



“there be always hope, there be always the promise of happiness

in store while there be life,” she continue gently. “do not be

down-hearted, Mr. Holdsworth. this parting be but a temporary

interruption of your happiness. be sure that God will protect your

young wife while you be away, and do not doubt that he will lead you

back to she.” she smile softly at he, and add, “I must go to my

little one now,” bow cordially and go away.



he could have bless she for an assurance which, have no well

foundation than a woman’s sympathy, cheer he as no thought of his

own could have do. “that be a true heart,” he say to himself, and

resume his walk, repeat her word over and over again, and draw

a comfort from they that make his step elastic and his eye bright.









CHAPTER III.



DOWN CHANNEL.





at six o’clock next morning the sleep passenger be awaken

by cry and tramping which, to some of they at least, be novel

disturber of their slumber. they might have tell the reason of all

this noise without go on deck; for those who sleep in cot find

the deck make an angle with their bed, and the lee port-hole

veil with rush green water, and all the movable crowd together

at any distance from where they have be deposit the night before.

and hoarse cry sound, and the flanking of massive chain, and the

strange groaning a ship make when she heel over to a weight of canvas.



yes! the “Meteor” be under weigh, with a spanking breeze on the

starboard quarter, which she would haul round abeam--her good point of

sailing--when she have clear the South Foreland. if this breeze hold,

the pilot say, he would be out of the ship and toast she in rum and

water at Plymouth before the sun go down next day.



some of the passenger come on deck when the ship be off Folkestone,

and then they see as fair a sight as the world have to offer--the

great white english cliff top with swell tract of green, with

here and there small bay with space of yellow sand between; house

thickly group--so it seem in behold they from the sea--upon the

very margin of the cliff; slate-colour hill pale far, far away

with visionary cloud upon they; and between the ship and the shore

many pleasure-boat and other craft, with white or ochre-colour sail

and bright flag, lend spot of red and blue to the perspective of

the chalky cliff.



the pilot hug the wind, rightly apprehensive that it might draw

ahead and cripple he for sea room; and the “Meteor” hereabout be so

close to the land that those on board she could see the people walk

on shore--man’s majesty illustrate by dot of black upon the beach or

the height. Overhead be a brilliantly blue sky, with small wool-white

cloud drive over it; the sea laugh in dimple and shiver the

white sunlight far and wide, so that every crest gleam with a diamond

spark of its own; and away on the left, a pale faint cloud float

upon the horizon, be the french coast.



the gay panorama sweep by and new scene open--stretch of barren

coast with ungainly Coastguards’ hut for their sole decoration; space

of vivid green rule off with line of soft brown sand, and low black

rock mirror in the lake-like surface of the water under the lee;

whitewash village with wreath of blue smoke curl from their

midst, and broad expanse of tree darken the lightlier-colour

landscape with delicate shadow. sturdy vessel, the dray-horse of

the Channel, slow, deep laden, and wafting, many of they, the scent

of pine and other wood across the water, be overtake and pass,

often amid the laughter of the crew on the “Meteor’s” forecastle, and

“chaff,” which even the grave Captain Steel, the “Meteor’s” skipper,

condescend to smile at. how picturesque these vessel! here a dutch

barque paint white, with square-face man stare over the bulwark;

a red-capped commander in sea boot and vast inexpressible, and a

steersman who sometimes look at the “Meteor” and sometimes at the

sail of his own ship, mix duty and curiosity in a manner delightful

to behold; there a north-country brig with dirty patched sail and

black rigging, and a crew with smoke face and a grinning head at the

galley door; sometimes a french smack with as many hand on board as

would man a Black Ball Liner, woman among they in red petticoat and

handkerchief around their face, some gutte fish, some mend net,

some peel potato, and all talk and gesticulate at once, but

suspend both their work and their talk to crowd to the smack’s side

and stare at the noble english vessel; and sometimes a little open boat

at anchor, with a man in her fishing with deep gravity, and pay no

more heed to the ship in whose wake his cockle-shell would bob like

a cork float, than be he the only tenant of the great glitter

surface of water.



but soon the coast sink low in the horizon. the “Meteor” be stand

for the deep water of the Middle Channel, and close haul, but with

all sail set, she have pale old England into a thin blue cloud, and

be head straight for the great Atlantic Ocean.



the night pass; the morning break; but the “Meteor” be not out of

the Channel yet. the pilot grumble as he cast his groggy eye aloft

and see the weather-leach lifting. he would have to go about to fetch

Plymouth, unless he have a mind to cross the Atlantic, and this be

certainly not his intention.



all the passenger come on deck after breakfast; the lady bring

out their work, the gentleman light cigar, and those who have make a

voyage before look know as they cast their eye about and ask

nautical question of the captain.



as to the pilot, he be ungetatable. moreover, his language be so

clouded with marine expletive that his light answer be generally

a shock to the sensibility.



every boatman from Margate to Penzance call himself a pilot nowadays;

but the genuine pilot--such a man as this who be take the “Meteor”

down Channel--stand out upon the marine canvas with an individuality

that make he unique among seafare human kind. figure a square,

bow-legged man, in a suit of heavy pilot cloth, a red shawl round

his neck, a tall hat on his head, a throat the colour of an uncooked

beefsteak, and a face of a complexion like new mahogany, small moist

rolling eye, a voice resemble the tone of a man with the bronchitis

call through a tin trumpet, and an undying affection for Jamaica

rum. such be Mr. Dumling, the “Meteor’s” pilot, a man to whom the

gaunt sea-batter post, the tall skeleton buoy, the fat wallowing

beacon, and the endless variety of light ashore and at sea, from the

North Foreland to the Land’s end, be as familiar and intelligible

as the alphabet be to you; who be so profoundly acquainted with the

Channel that he boast his power to tell you within a quarter of a

mile of where he be, by the mere faculty of smell! a man who could

look over a ship’s side and say, “here be four fathom of water, and

yonder be nine,” “and where the shadow of the cloud rest the water

be twelve fathom deep;” and so on, every inch of the road, for mile

and mile--a miracle of memory! to appreciate the value of such a man

you should be with he in the Channel in a pitch-dark night, blow

great gun from the north-east, with the roar of the Goodwin on the lee

bow, and a sea so heavy that every blow the ship receive communicate

the impression that she have strike the ground, while the black air be

hoarse with the gale and fog with sting spray.



the wind be nowhere more capricious than in the English Channel. at one

o’clock the spanking breeze sweep round to the south-east; the watch

go to work at the brace; up go the foretopmast-stun’sail, and the

“Meteor” rush ahead at twelve knot an hour.



“we shall be off Plymouth at eight o’clock,” say the pilot, and go

below to lunch with a serene face.



he be right. at eight o’clock the “Meteor” be lie with her main

yard back, dip her nose in a lively sea, with a signal for a

boat stream at her mast-head.



the passenger might take their last look at old England then, whilst

the glorious sunset bathe the land in gold, and make the woode shore

beautiful with colour and shadow. and now, dance over the water,

come a white sail, which dim slowly into an ashen hue as the crimson

in the sky fade and the water darken.



“any letter for shore?” say Captain Steel, move among the

passenger, and soon his hand grow full. many of the man come forward

and deliver missive for the wife, for Sue, for Poll, to the skipper,

who give they to the pilot. the boat, glisten with the sea-water

she have ship, sweep alongside, duck her sail, and be bring up by

a line fling from the main chain.



“good-bye, cap’n,” say the gruff pilot; “wish you a pleasant voyage,

I’m sure, gen’l’men and lady;” drop into the main chain, and from

the main chain drop into the boat; the sail be hoist, a hat wave,

a cheer give from the ship’s forecastle, and away bound the lugger in

a cloud of spray.



now bawl Captain Steel from the break of the poop; round swing the

main yard; the noble ship heel over, tremble, and start forward,

and, with the expire gleam of the sunset upon her high sail, the

“Meteor” head for the broad Atlantic, and glide into the gloom and

space of the infinite, windy night.









CHAPTER IV.



in THE ATLANTIC.





there be eight passenger and twenty-seven hand, count captain

and officer, on board the “Meteor;” in all, thirty-five soul.



in these day half that number of man would be think ample to handle

a ship of eleven hundred ton. take fourteen man as a ship’s company,

we find--one, the cook, who be useless aloft; five ordinary seaman,

equal to two able-bodied man; four ill and unable to leave their bunk;

the remainder consist of the captain, two mate, and the carpenter.

so that a summon for all hand to shorten sail, for example, bring

forth about enough man to do the work of one yard--one yard, when

there be twelve, exclusive of trysail, jib, stun’sails, spanker,

and staysail. this modern system of undermanne ship be an evil

next in magnitude to that of send crazy and leaky vessel to sea;

and as many ship be lose for want of hand to work they on occasion

which demand promptitude and muscle, as be lose by rotten plank and

overcharge cargo.



the passenger on board the “Meteor” consist of four gentleman, two

lady, a little boy, and a female servant. of the gentleman, one be a

young man name Holland, who be go to America for no other purpose

than to see Niagara; another be a merchant, who be to represent

a London house in New York. he be accompany by his wife and she

maid. the third be a General in the United States Army, a fine old

man with a chivalrous courtesy of manner and a handsome honest face,

who have be pick up what professional hint he could find by a

year’s sojourn in the military depôts of Great Britain. the fourth male

passenger be an actor, magnificently name Gerald Fitzmaurice St.

Aubyn, in quest of more appreciative audience in the New Country than

his genius have encounter in the Old. the widow and her son complete

the list.



it take these good people a very short time to settle down to their

new life and adjust themselves to the novel condition of existence

that surround they. the lady lie hide at the first go off;

and, although Mr. St. Aubyn put in a punctual appearance at meal and

smoke a great quantity of cheroot, it must be admit that he be

peculiarly pensive for a comedian, whose genius, he affirm, be

chiefly at home in genteel farce, though he have enact tragedy with

applause.



the “Meteor” meet with adverse wind, but brilliant weather, during the

first few day. she tack north and south, and crowd canvas to make

headway, but, though her speed be great through the water, her actual

progress be small.



“no matter,” say Captain Steel, patiently; “we may get a gale astern

of we some of these hour, and then we’ll make up for lost time.”



but whilst the weather remain so beautiful, the wind brisk and the

sea smooth, the passenger could hardly regret the delay. it be like

yacht sailing--dry deck, steady motion, and always the pleasurable

sense of swiftness inspire by the bead foam crisping by and

stretch like a tape astern. now and again they signal a ship

homeward bind or journey south. the widow’s little boy clap his

hand to see the bright flag fly at the mizzen-peak, and the lady

be lose in wonderment to think that those gay colour be a language

as intelligible to those concern in their interpretation as “how do

you do?” and “very well, thank you.”



the “Meteor” have a snug cuddy; and a hospitable sight be the

dinner-table, with the white cloth cover the long board, the

gleam silver and glass, the fine claret jug (testimonial by former

passenger to the captain), the colour of wine in decanter, the

grinning negro always collide with the steward, and the skipper’s

rubicund face, relieve by soft white hair, at the head of the table,

back by the polished mizzen-mast. Overhead be the skylight, through

which you might see the great sail tower to the heaven; and over

the dinner table swing a globe of gold-fish between two basket of

fern. there be a piano lash abaft the mizzen-mast; and all around

the cuddy be the cabin occupy by the passenger, the captain,

the mate, with highly-varnish door and white panel relieve with

edging of gold.



everybody take an interest in the widow’s little boy, both because he

be a pretty child, and because it be whisper about that he have lose

his father but a few week ago. he and Holdsworth become great friend,

as Holdsworth have say they would. whenever it be the first mate’s

watch on deck, the little fellow would paddle away from his mother’s

side and come to he, and ask he to tell he story, and show he

the ship’s compass, at which he be never weary of look. then you

might see Holdsworth on a hencoop, or the skylight, with the child upon

his knee, coin nautical fairy-yarn of people who live under the

sea, and ride in chariot compose of coral, to which fish with scale

shine like precious stone be harness.



sometimes the widow, whose name be Tennent, would come on deck and

find they together, when she would sit beside they and listen with

a smile to Holdsworth, whose story the little boy Louis would on

no account suffer his mamma to interrupt. and to repay he for his

kindness to the child, and not more for that than because she admire

his honest nature, and be win by his gentle and tender simplicity, she

would lead he on, with a world of feminine tact, to talk of his wife,

and comfort and make he happy with her sympathy, her interest, and her

assurance.



she be a calm, gentle-faced woman, with a settle sorrow in the

expression of her eye that make she look old than she be, but she

age would scarcely exceed thirty-six. she show little inclination to

converse with the other passenger, and would retire early at night,

and in the daytime sit in quiet place about the deck, always with she

boy beside she.



the merchant’s wife, on the other hand, Mrs. Ashton, be a gay,

talkative woman, a showy dresser, and fond of a quiet boast, which

her husband, a short man with a yellow beard, take care never to

contradict. Mr. Holland begin to pay her attention straightway, and

then Mr. St. Aubyn step in with theatrical emphasis and smooth

observation, like the speech in comedy. Captain Steel, though

very polite to this lady, incline to Mrs. Tennent,--his sailor’s heart

appreciate her defencelessness, and propound all kind of problem

how good to amuse, please, and cheer she. but though she could not fail

to like the honest skipper, she evidently prefer Holdsworth, who

would go and talk to she for an hour at a time about dolly, and then

listen, with a face of kindliest sympathy, to little passage out of

her own life.



and so a week go by, and the ship strove with the baffling wind,

which blow directly from the quarter to which her bowsprit should have

point, and captain and man begin to chafe, find the job of put

the ship about tiresome at last.



on the seventh day, about the hour of sunset, the wind fall, and the

surface of the sea become polished as glass, though from the north-east

there come, through the mighty expanse of water, a long and regular

swell, which make the ship rise and fall as regularly as the breath of

a sleeper.



“we shall have the wind from that quarter, I think, sir,” say

Holdsworth to the skipper.



“or be this an after-swell, Mr. Holdsworth?” suggest the skipper,

send his keen gaze across the sea to the horizon, where the sky be

as blue as it be overhead.



there be no telling. this long and regular swell might be the

precursor of a gale, or the effect of one that have pass. the

barometer have fall, but this might only indicate a southerly wind,

not necessarily dirty weather. the heaven be perfectly tranquil; the

day be fade into a serene and gloriously beautiful evening, with

no hint in all its benign aspect to suggest the need of the slight

precaution.



Mrs. Ashton be at the piano, accompany Mr. St. Aubyn to a song,

which he sing so affectedly that some of the hand forward mimicked

he, and the forecastle seem full of guinea-pig.



her husband pop his head over the skylight and call to she to

come and view the sunset. up she come, escort by Mr. Holland and the

actor, flounce showily into a chair, and fall into a rapture.



“oh, how beautiful! the sea look like gold! don’t it, Captain Steel?

see how red the sail be! ah, if I could only paint! what fame such a

picture as this would bring I.”



true; but then what manner of pigment be needful to reproduce the

glory, the colour, the calm, the infinity of that wonderful scene!



the sun be sink down a cloudless horizon, and be now a vast

crimson ball, throb and quiver with his low limb upon the

sea-line. there be something overwhelming in the unspeakable majesty

of his unattended descent. as the huge crimson body appear to hang

for some moment above the sea before dipping, even Mrs. Ashton

hold her tongue, and seem impressed with the tremendous spectacle

of loneliness submit by the globe of fire sink away from the

sky with the vast solitude of the deep in the foreground. far into

the measureless ocean he have sink a cone of fire, while the height

above and around he be dim with burn haze. the sail of the

“Meteor” be yellow in the expire light; her topmast seem vein

with line of flame; and the brass-work about her deck reflect

innumerable sun, each with thread of glory about it, that blind the

eye to encounter.



but even while they gaze the sun vanish, and darkness come with

long stride across the deep, kindle the star and transform

the mast and yard of the ship into phantom tracery as delicate as

frostwork to look at.



“upon my word!” exclaim Mr. Holland in a tone of rapture, “that’s as

fine a sight as I must hope to see anywhere.”



“if you could introduce a scene like that, Mr. St. Aubyn, on the stage,

eh?” laugh the General.



“why, as to that,” reply Mr. St. Aubyn, “let I tell you, General,

that there be some very fine scene to be find in the large theatre

in London. in the second act of ‘Pizarro,’ as I see it the other night

at Drury Lane, there’s a scene represent the Temple of the Sun; the

sun be set--and God know how they manage it, but the sun _do_

sink, not like yonder one, but very finely in cloud, just as ‘ataliba’

exclaim, draw his sword, ‘now, my brother, my son, my friend, I

know your valour. should ill success assail we.’”...



“yes, yes,” interrupt Mr. Holland impatiently; “but I always

consider ‘Pizarro,’ as a play, to be full of very poor rant. who

talk in real life like the fellow in that piece be make to talk?”



“my dear sir!” exclaim Mr. St. Aubyn with a smile of contempt, “the

stage be the arena of poetry; we be idealist.”...



“because you never mean what you say,” say Mr. Ashton, light a

cigar.



“oh, excuse I,” rejoin Mr. St. Aubyn; “true actor be always in

earnest. siddon be.”



“I once meet Sarah Siddons,” say Mrs. Ashton. “do you remember, dear,

at Lord Shortlands?” address her husband.



“I be only once at a theatre in my life,” observe Captain Steel, who

have be listen to the conversation with an impressed face. “that

be at Plymouth. they give we our money’s worth. there be plenty of

fight and love-making, and two traitor, both of whom die game and

cover with blood. there be a little too much gunpowder at the end;

but I rather think they raise smoke to hide the acting, which fall

off as the piece make headway. the good part of the entertainment, to

my thinking, be a fight between two sailor in a private box. Mr. St.

Aubyn, where do you gentleman, when you be run through the body, stow

all the blood you lose? that’s often puzzle I to think.”



“oh, don’t let I hear,” cry Mrs. Ashton. “I hate to be tell such

secret.”



“Captain,” say the General, “how long be this calm go to last?”



“all night, I be afraid. how’s her head?” sing out the captain.



“East-south-east, sir,” respond the man at the wheel.



“we’re homeward bind,” say the captain laugh; “the old girl want

to get back again.”



he walk away from the group, and stand near the wheel, gaze aloft

and around. the passenger continue talk and laugh, their voice

sound unreal when listen to at a distance, and with the great,

desolate, silent sea breathing around. the sail flap lazily aloft,

and the wheel-chain clank from time to time as the vessel rise and

fall. Mrs. Tennent come on deck, the captain join she, and they

walk up and down. on the other side of the deck pace the second

mate. Forward be the dark shadow of some of the hand upon the

forecastle, smoking pipe and talk in low voice.



the night have fall darkly; there be no moon, but the star be

large and brilliant, and glitter in flake of white light in the

sea. presently a fiddle be play in the forecastle, and a voice sing

a mournful tune that sound weirdly in the gloom, and with a muffle

note. the air and voice be not without sweetness, but there be the

melancholy in it which many song popular among sailor have, and the

wail cadence be help out by the ghostly sail rear their

glimmering space, and the subdue plash of the water about the bow,

as the ship sink into the hollow of the swell.



Mrs. Tennent stop, with the captain, at the poop-rail to listen.



“what odd music!” cry Mrs. Ashton. “it sound as if some one be

play out in the sea there.”



“let’s have the fiddler here,” say Mr. Holland. “I like to enlarge my

mind by observation, and have never yet hear a real Jack Tar sing.”



“oh yes! oh yes!” exclaim Mrs. Ashton, while Mr. St. Aubyn call

out, “I’ll go and fetch he.”



“well stop where you be, sir,” say the skipper, drily; “the

forecastle’s a dangerous hold for landsman to put their head into. Mr.

Thompson,” he call to the second mate, “just go and send that fiddler

aft here.”



presently come the man, follow at a respectful distance by a crowd of

his mate, who draw to the capstan on the quarterdeck, and wait for

what be to follow.



the fiddler and vocalist be a stumpy seaman, with black whisker, a

hooked nose, and keen black eye, dress in loose canvas breach,

well smear with tar, and a canvas shirt, with a belt about his

middle, in which be a sheath-knife. he hail from Southampton, but

have go so many voyage in every specie of ship--Danish, French,

spanish, American--that he might fairly claim to belong to the whole

world.



he scrape with his left foot, and stand bashfully await order, his

glitter eye travel over the group of gentleman and lady.



“you’re want to sing a song, Daniels,” say Captain Steel.



“ay, ay, sir. what might it be?”



“something wild and plaintive,” suggest Mrs. Ashton.



“give we a song about a sweetheart,” say Mr. Holland.



this be English to the sailor; so, after a few moment’ reflection, he

screw his fiddle into his neck, scrape a few bar, and then sing.



he do his good, and murmur from time to time about the capstan

illustrate enthusiastic appreciation in one portion of his audience

at least. those on the poop be more quiet, impressed by the peculiar

wildness of the song, and the rough, uncouth melody of the tune.



the song be about a woman whose husband be a sailor. the sailor go

away to sea, and do not come home, and she think he have desert

she; so she put on man’s clothe, ship on board a vessel as a

“hand,” and go in search of he. one night she be on the forecastle,

on the look-out. the watch be asleep; there’s not a breath of air:



  when, look over the starboard side,

    she see a face as pale

  as snow upon a mountain top,

    or moonlight on a sail.



the figure attach to the face rise, waist high, out of the water,

and extend his hand.



  “o God!” she scream, “be this my love?

    can this my Joey be?”

  and then she cast her eye above

    and jump into the sea.



and sure enough the phantom _be_ Joey, who have not desert she, as

she have cruelly think, but have be drown in the very spot where

the vessel she be on board of be becalm. the song wind up with an

injunction to all wife or sweetheart of sailor never to think that

their Joes have play they false because they do not return to their

home.



the passenger thank the man for his song, and Mrs. Ashton want

another; but Captain Steel, hold that enough condescension have be

exhibit, bid the singer go to the steward and get a “tot of grog.”



much criticism follow; but all, with the exception of Mr. St. Aubyn,

own themselves impress by the rough simplicity and tragical theme

of the forecastle ballad.



“Pshaw!” cry the actor; “put the man on a stage before an audience,

and he’d be hiss off. it’s the queer scratching of the catgut and

the picturesque costume of the fellow that have please you. his voice

ben’t good enough to get he the post of call-boy at a theatre.”



a warm argument follow this decision, and last nearly half an

hour, during which the General and Mr. Ashton leave the group; then the

steward’s bell rang, and the passenger go below to their nightly

potation and to munch sweet biscuit.









CHAPTER V.



a GALE of WIND.





at midnight Holdsworth come on deck to relieve the second mate. a man

out of the port watch come to the wheel, and stand yawn, scarcely

awake. the night be dark--a hazy atmosphere through which the star

gleam sparely, and the sea like ebony. the rise and fall of the ship

flap the sail against the mast and drive eddy of air about the

deck, but in reality there be not a breath of wind.



there be something stupendous in the black, profound, and breathless

placidity of the night. the compass swing round in the binnacle

anywhere, but the swell make the rudder kick heavily now and again, and

give the wheel a twist that fling the spoke out of the man’s hand and

wake he up.



this prolonged inactivity be gall. one long to hear the rush of

parting water and the singing of the wind in the shroud.



the mainsail flap so heavily that Holdsworth order it to be

furl. the song of the man bring the captain on deck. he flit,

shadow-like, about the binnacle, sniff at the night impatiently, and

then go to Holdsworth.



“the glass have fall half an inch since eight bell,” say he.



“yes, sir; there’ll be a change before morning.”



“well stow the royal and mizzen-top gall’ns’l.”



“ay, ay, sir.”



these, the topmost sail of the ship, be just discernible from the

deck. in a few moment their dim outline melt, and some dark figure

go up into the gloom and vanish.



the captain return to his cabin, and Holdsworth stroll the deck.

at two bell (one o’clock) the haze go out of the sky and the star

shine fiercely. holdsworth, stand on the starboard side of the poop,

feel a light air creep about his face, and the sound of the flap

sail cease.



“how’s her head?”



“north-a-quarter-west, sir.”



he sing out an order, and a crowd of figure came tumble out of the

forecastle and man the port brace. the air die away, but presently

come a quick puff which make the water bubble around the ship.



Holdsworth’s eye be upon the weather-horizon. the star burn

purely, but away upon the water-line be a thick shadow.



again the wind die out and there be a breathless stillness, amid

which you might hear a sound--vague, murmurous, indescribable--a

distant echo it might seem of something infinitely distant.



“stand by the topgallant halliard!”



a sense of expectation seem to pervade the very ship herself as she

stand upright with her dim canvas flap in the darkness above.



the distant murmur grow more defined, and take such a tone as you may

hear in small sharp rain fall at a distance upon leave. then,

out of the murky horizon some cloud came roll--long, attenuate

shadow, resemble visionary arm clutch at the star. the murmur

approach; the cloud, swinge along the sky, form into compact

group. Hark to the quick hissing of the water lash by the wind!



in a moment the sail be round and hard, the ship with her

port-chain under water, and the wind screech fiercely over the

ebony surface of the sea, and whiten it with foam.



the captain be on the poop, hold on to the main-topgallant

backstay, and shriek order like one possess. it be indeed

briefly a case of “let go everything!” under full topsail, foresail,

staysail, and jib, the ship be too heavily weight for the

surprising violence of the wind, and be powerless to right herself.

but every order give be the right one. and now you hear the deep

tone of Holdsworth’s powerful voice mingle with the agitated

command of the skipper, while yard came rush down upon the cap,

and sail bang and roar aloft, and man shout lustily about the

deck, and the sea flee in cataract of foam under the vessel’s bow.



a time of deep excitement, but scarcely of suspense--there be too much

hurrying for that.



there would have be something incredible to an inexperienced landsman

in the sight of the dark figure swarm up the shroud to give

battle to the wild array of canvas which groan and bellow like a

dozen thunderstorm in the sky--a spectacle of human pluck not to be

realise, or in the faint degree appreciate, by those who have not

beheld it. the night black--the yard slant so that the extremity of

the main-yard touch the water; the footing upon those yard a thin

line which must be feel for by the foot; the canvas, loosen by the

lowering of the yard, belly by the force of the wind many foot above

the head of the reefer, and present to their hand a surface of

iron; and the three mast quiver under the shock and convulsion of

the sail!



all hand be at work now, and there be man enough to reef both big

topsail at once, whilst other over their head furl the topgallant

sail. Holdsworth have be one of the first to spring up the main

rigging; he know the value of every pair of hand in that moment of

danger; and away--active, daring, his hand and arm like steel--he

clamber for the weather-earing. but the boatswain be before he, so

he make for the lee yard-arm.



figure a smooth spar, fifty-five foot long, slope at a height of as

many foot to the water’s surface, the say surface not be a mill

pond, but a sheet of foam; figure a pitch dark night, a line stretch

along the yard, down which you must slide to the extremity, a sail

weigh half a dozen ton bang at your head and your foot, and

do its utmost to throw you; then, having reach the extremity of

the yard, figure your leg throw across it as you might bestride a

horse, beneath you the foaming sea, almost at right angle the incline

deck of the ship, a long stone’s throw distant--a deep darkness

everywhere, save where a wave, break massively, fling out a

phosphorescent light and deepen the blackness of its own chasm--whilst

the gale yell about your ear, and blind you with spray that sting

like hail!



figure this, and you will then very faintly realise what “take the

lee-earing” in a gale at sea mean.



the cry of the man aloft, and the beating of the canvas, sound like

an unearthly contest in mid-air; but they cease presently, and then

the hand come hurry down the rigging and fall to the halliard.

Holdsworth spring on to the poop, his cap go, his hair blow about

his eye, and roar out order, while the captain, more easy in his

mind about his spar, go aft and hang about the binnacle, watch

the compass often.



the ship be now under double-reefed topsail, and reel through the

darkness almost bare of sail. the wind be increase in violence every

five minute, and an ugly Atlantic sea be run right athwart the

ship’s course, hurl great wave against her starboard beam, which

run in waterspout of foam as high as the main-top, and be blow in

big hiss flake through the rigging to leeward. it be soon deem

expedient to close-reef the topsail; but even under these mere streak

of canvas the “Meteor” lie over to the gale down to her water-way,

and the water bubble in her lee scupper. but luckily the gale be

right abeam, and the vessel could hold her course; but her speed be

comparatively small, and she labour heavily.



so pass the dark hour of the night. at four o’clock the gale

be at its bad. they have rig up a hurricane-house in the mizzen

rigging--a square of tarpaulin, which the wind flatten hard against

the shroud--and under this shelter sit Holdsworth and the captain,

scarce able to hear their own voice, pitch in the loud key, amid

the howling of the temp. once Holdsworth go below to look at the

glass, and come back say it be steady. the skipper roar that

he never before remember so sudden a gale, and Holdsworth own

that only once be he so catch--in the Pacific, when they lose their

fore-topmast.



there be nothing more to be do, unless they hove the ship to; but

this be not needful. the dawn break at five, and the pale cheerless

light illuminate a wild and dreary scene of tumble desolate water

billow in mountain to the horizon. the “Meteor,” almost under

bare pole, her yard point to the gale, her rope and line blow

in semicircle to leeward, labour heavily, catch now by a sea that

throw she on her beam-end, and now swoop into a chasm wall

with boil green water, make the gale screech like a million

steam-whistle through her rigging, as she drive up against it, while

coil tongue of water run in cataract up her glistening side and

fall in dead weight upon her deck. the sky, from horizon to horizon,

be a dark lead colour, along which under-cloud, in appearance

resemble volume of smoke, be sweep along, torn and rent, and

discharge at interval quick, bite shower of rain.



some of the passenger come on deck--the General, Mr. Holland, and

Mr. St. Aubyn. the General turn about when he have advance a few

foot, and disappear; Mr. Holland in a very short time follow his

example; but the actor, with manifest look of terror in his pallid

face, push onwards with outstretche hand for the hurricane-house.

the captain advise he to go below; but at that moment the ship,

roll suddenly to windward, ship a shower of spray, which soak

the poor actor through and through; a moment after, the vessel heel

heavily over to leeward; away roll the actor, impel both by the

wind and the unerre law of gravitation, and be fling against the lee

mizzen rigging, to which he be pin by the violence of the gale

as effectually as if he have be lash to the shroud. he scream

for help, on which Holdsworth go over to he, take he by the arm,

and drag he against the wind to the companion-hatchway. as Mr. St.

Aubyn stagger below, cling like a kitten to whatever he could lay

hand on, he be hear to implore Holdsworth to tell he if there be

any danger; but, before the word be out of his mouth, Holdsworth be

cling to the weather-rig and call the captain’s attention

to a brig, which have rise out of the sea like an apparition, and be

tear before the gale with full topsail and topgallant-sail set.



“a Yankee, by her build!” say the captain. “it’s only a Yankee who

would carry that sail in such a wind.”



it be a sight to see she fly along, sink her hull sometimes

out of sight, then poise on the giddy summit of a huge wave, whose

crest break under her bow, her copper bottom glistening like red

gold against the slate-colour water. she pass within a quarter of

a mile of the “Meteor’s” weather-beam, and up fly the stripe and

star and stand like a paint board at her peak. the second mate

answer the salutation by bend on the small ensign and run it

up. any further signalling be out of the question in that gale. the

man on board the brig could just be make out. she be a smart vessel,

black-hull, with bow like a knife, and skysail pole, which give she

mast an aspect of perfect symmetry; and she be splendidly handle.

she go like a swan over the seethe billow, stream a foaming

wake, and in a very few moment be lose in the haze and gloom of the

near horizon.



as the morning advance the gale decrease, but a terrible sea be up,

which make the ship labour so furiously that to steady she in some

degree they set the trysail and foresail. there be, however, the

comfort of daylight abroad, and the man could see what they be about.

both Holdsworth and the captain go below to get a little sleep, and

the vessel be leave in command of the second mate, a young man name

Thompson. there be two hand at the wheel, and two on the look-out on

the forecastle, glitter in oil-skin, and duck now and again to

the sea which sweep over the ship’s bow.



the fore and main hatch be batten down, and the main-deck be a

foot deep in water, which wash to and fro as the ship roll, and

which, as fast as it run through the scupper-hole, be replace by

fresh and heavy inroad of the sea.



but all this be trifle; the vessel be snug, the gale be

moderate, and the extra sail that have be make be drive the ship

through the water in fine style.



meanwhile, the passenger below, having be reassure by the captain,

be make what breakfast they could off the roll, tea, and rasher

of ham which clatter about the table, and tumble into their lap.

the tray swing wildly from the deck, and it demand great vigilance

and close attention to their convulsive movement to repossess oneself

of the cup or plate one place upon they for safety. the negro steward

shamble round the table, halt every moment to make a grasp at

anything that come in his road, to steady himself. now and again you

hear the smash of crockery. some conversation be attempt; and the

General invite Mr. Holland to go up on deck and witness a scene

which would probably exceed in majesty Niagara Falls; but Mr. Holland

say he would wait until the vessel be steady. Mr. St. Aubyn have

change his clothe and sit hold on to the table, look the part
