of fear infinitely well than he could hope to impersonate it before

the footlight. the lady remain in their cabin. Mrs. Ashton,

overcome with sickness and the fear of drown, be drive her maid

distracted with order, which it be out of the poor wretch’s power

to execute. in truth, the maid’s leg be perfectly useless to she,

which Mrs. Ashton, lie on her back, refuse to understand. cry be

repeatedly come from the direction of her cabin for “Harry! Harry!”

which receive no attention, owe to Harry’s--in other word, to Mr.

Ashton’s--utter incapacity to move a step without be fling upon the

deck.



a somewhat different scene be present by the interior of the

forecastle, where both watch be have breakfast. man hold

tin pannikin step easily round to the galley, where the cook be

dispense a milkless, sugarless black fluid call tea, and retreat

into the twilight of the forecastle, carry the steaming beverage.

there sit the sailor, some swinge in hammock with their leg

dangle down, some on sea-chest, some on canvas bag, drink from

pannikin, swallow lump of biscuit hard as iron, or hack with

the knife they wear in their belt at bit of cold pork or beef,

float in vinegar, in tin dish hold between their knee; some

smoking, some make ready to “turn in,” and all jabber away as

gaily as if they be comfortably seat in a Liverpool or Poplar

singing-house--the mariner’s earthly paradise--and each with his Sue

or his Betsey at his side. here, more than in any other part of the

ship, you feel her motion--the mighty lifting of her bow, and the long

sweeping fall as she pitch nose under, while the heavy sea boom

against she outside as though at any moment the timber must dispart

and the green wave rush in.



at twelve o’clock the gale have decrease to such a degree that they

be able to shake two reef out of the main top-sail and set the

topgallant sail. the action of the sea, moreover, be much less

violent. the weather have clear, the pale blue sky could be see

shine through the white mist that flee along it, and the sun stand

round and clean and coppery in the heaven, throw a dark red lustre

upon the quick, passionate play of the sea beneath.



some of the passenger crawl upon deck and gaze with wonderment

around they. certainly the panorama be a somewhat different one from

what have be unroll to their eye the day before. the ship have a

fagged and jade look with her drench deck, her rope blow slack

with the violence of the wind, and the canvas make unequal to the eye

by the reef in the topsail. it be again Holdsworth’s watch on deck.

the captain walk up and down chuckle over the improved aspect of

the weather and on the wind, which be draw more easterly, and

therefore more favourable.



“you can shake out the reef, Mr. Holdsworth. she’ll bear it now,” he

call out.



out reef it be: the ship feel the increase pressure, and rush

forward like a liberated racehorse.



“this be capital!” exclaim the old General, totter about with

outstretche hand, ever on the alert for a special roll. “a week of

this, captain, will carry we a good way on our road.”



“ay, sir, and we must make up for lost time.”



and then presently he give order to set the mainsail and the other two

topgallant sail.



“the glass still keep low, sir,” say Holdsworth.



“but let’s take advantage of the daylight, Mr. Holdsworth. we mustn’t

lose an opportunity.”



the sky have now clear, the sun shine cheerily; the wind, having

draw aft, be now no more than what sailor would call a main-royal

breeze. the foretopmast stun’sail be set. the passenger regain

their spirit, and though the ship still roll pretty freely, Mr. St.

Aubyn and Mr. Holland, to show that they be now master of their

leg, walk up and down the deck, diversify their conversation

with sundry stumble, and now and then by fall against each other.

but the bright sunshine make such _contretemp_ a source of merriment.

moreover, the lady be on deck now, Mrs. Ashton having be push

up the companion-ladder by her husband, who, in his turn, have meet with

great assistance behind from the kindly hand of the negro steward, who

be anxious to get they both out of the cuddy, that he might show his

tooth to the maid-servant. Captain Steel see Mrs. Ashton attend

by the other gentleman, who be industrious in their inquiry after

her nerve, gallantly give his arm to the widow, whilst her little

boy run to Holdsworth, who take his hand, kiss and begin to talk to

he, find endless pleasure in look into his eye and humour

the suggestion of home-life, of flower, of woman’s love, of his own

wife, which be somehow convey to he by the boy’s prattle and wise

child-smile and perfect innocence.









CHAPTER VI.



TAKEN ABACK!





at five o’clock the wind be south-east; a fresh breeze, with a lively

sea and a cloudy sky. the wind be aft, the ship sail on an even

keel, to the great comfort of the passenger, who find the inclined

deck intolerable.



from the aspect of the sea, it be evident that the ship have get into

water which have not be touch by the gale of the morning--of such

narrow proportion sometimes be the tempest which sweep the ocean.

away northward, whither the cloud be roll, there loom a long,

low, smoke-colour bank of cloud or fog, so exactly resemble a coast

see from a distance that the passenger be deceive, and some of

they call out that yonder be land!



“tell we now, captain,” cry Mrs. Ashton; “it _be_ land, ben’t it?”



“why, madam,” rejoin the captain, “for anything I can tell, it may be

Laputa.”



“or Utopia,” suggest the General, “the land of idealism and

paradisaical institution.”



Mrs. Ashton laugh, see the joke, but Mr. St. Aubyn, conceive

that they be talk of real country, propose that the captain

should head the vessel for the shore.



“no, no! too far out of my course,” answer the skipper, with a wise

shake of the head. “it would make a Flying Dutchman of the ship be we

once to set to work to reach that land.”



“if I really think it Utopia,” say the General, stroke his

moustache, “I would beg you to land I at once, so eager be I to

witness the condition of a people live under a form of government the

like of which, for wisdom, humanity, and availability, be not to be meet

with in any other part of the world. but it _may_ be Laputa, as you

suggest.”



“or Lilliput,” say Mr. Holland; whereupon the actor, perceive that

a joke be play at his expense, scowl dramatically at the bank of

cloud, and mutter, that, for his part, when he ask a civil question

he usually look for a truthful answer.



just then a voice forward shout out, “a sail on the lee bow!”



there be always something exciting in this cry at sea. storm and calm

grow wearisome after a bit, but the interest that cluster about a

vessel meet on the broad deep never lose its freshness. the captain

go for his telescope, and, after a brief inspection, announce the

vessel to be a large barque go the same road as themselves. Mrs.

Ashton ask leave to look through the telescope, and a good deal of

coquettish bye-play take place; for, first she shut both eye, and then

she couldn’t see at all; and then she shut the eye that look through

the telescope, and, keep the other open, declare that she could see

well without the glass. then the telescope wouldn’t keep steady; so

Mr. St. Aubyn go upon his knee and beg she to use his shoulder

for a rest. at last, after an infinity of trouble, and when the cramp

be just begin to seize the actor’s leg, she obtain a glimpse

of the barque as it sweep through the field of the glass, and own

herself delight and satisfied.



the “Meteor” come up with the stranger hand over fist, keep to

windward of she; and soon she be no far than a mile off, a big

hull high in the water, bare and black, with round bow and a square

stern. they hoist the ensign on board the “Meteor,” but the barque

show no colour.



“some sour North Countryman, I reckon,” say Captain Steel. “she have a

Sunderland cut.”



she be under full sail, but just when the “Meteor” get abreast of she,

she clew her royal up, down come the flying and outer jib, and the

topgallant yard.



“what be she afraid of?” exclaim Captain Steel, gaze at she

curiously.



you could see the pigmy figure of the man clamber up the rigging,

and presently down fall the topsail yard and up go more figure,

and the spar be dot with head. anything more picturesque than

this vessel--her black hull roll majestically, her white sail

vanish even as you watch they, her rigging mark against the

cloudy sky, the sense of the noisy activity on board of she, of which

no faint echo steal across the water, and all between, the tumbling

cloud-colour water--cannot be imagine. the crew of the “Meteor”

watch she with curiosity; but she now fall rapidly astern, and in a

short time could be see clearly only by the telescope, which Captain

Steel hold to his eye, speculate upon her movement.



the dinner-bell rang. it be now the first dog-watch. Thompson, the

second mate, come on deck, and the passenger go to dinner. the

sunlight have a watery gleam in it as the lengthen ray fall upon

the skylight, and Holdsworth’s eye constantly wander to the sail,

which be visible through the glass. the skipper be in high humour,

and during dinner laugh at the barque they have pass for shortening

sail under a blue sky.



“I’ll wager a hat,” he exclaim, “that she’s command by a Scotchman,

even if she don’t hail from a north british port. I don’t mean to say

that your Scotchman’s a timid man, but he’s unco’ thoughtful. my first

skipper be a Sawney, and every night, as regularly as the second

dog-watch come round, it be ‘in royal and flying-jib, and a single

reef in the mizzen-top-sail.’”



“but there must be some reason for the barque furl her sail,” say

the General.



“from his point of view, no doubt, sir. you have see what the weather

have be all the afternoon?”



“the wind be drop, sir,” say Holdsworth, look through the

skylight.



he have an uneasy expression in his eye, and he frequently glance at

the skipper; but etiquette of a very severe kind prohibit he from

impart his misgiving of a change, in the face of the skipper’s

manifest sense of security.



“it may freshen after sunset,” rejoin the skipper. “Mr. Holland, the

pleasure of a glass of wine.”



the conversation drift into other channel. Mrs. Ashton give an

account of a country ball she have attend a week or two before she

leave England, and describe the dress she wear on that occasion,

appeal often to her husband to aid her memory, and rivet

the attention of Mr. St. Aubyn. then the General talk of the

garrison-town he have visit, and pay some handsome compliment to

the british army, and to english society in general. Mrs. Tennent,

seat on the captain’s right hand, with her boy at her side, listen

to without join in the conversation.



Holdsworth’s eye roam incessantly through the skylight.



it happen presently that the General, in speak of the beauty of

english inland scenery, mention the county in which Southbourne be

situate, and instance in particular the country around Hanwitch, a

town lie not half a dozen mile from Southbourne. holdsworth prick

his ear, and join in the conversation. he have reason to remember

Hanwitch. one of the happy day he have spend, during the three

month he have be ashore, be that in which he have drive dolly over

to that town, and dine in the queer little hotel that front a piece

of river-scenery as beautiful as any that be to be find up the Thames.



whilst he and the General talk, the skipper argue with Mr. St. Aubyn

on the merit of the English as a pay people. St. Aubyn declare

that the english public, take in the aggregate, be a mean public,

rarely liberal, and then liberal in wrong direction, support

quack institution, respond to quack appeal, and ignore true

excellence, especially histrionic excellence. both grow warm; then Mr.

Holland join in. he send the discussion wander from the point, and

in step Mr. Ashton.



meanwhile the decanter go round, the cuddy grow dark, and the negro

be look at the steward for order to light the swinge lamp.



Hark!



a loud cry from the deck, follow by a sudden rush of foot, and the

ship heel over--over--yet over!



the woman shriek; the skylight turn black; plate, decanter,

cutlery, glass, roll from the table and fall with quick crash. the

deck fore and aft echo with loud call. you could hear the water

gurgle in the lee port-hole. a keen blue gleam flash upon the

skylight; but if thunder follow the lightning it be inaudible amid

the wild and continuous shrieking of the wind.



the skipper and Holdsworth scramble to the companion-ladder and gain

the deck. in a trice they see what have happen. the ship, with all

sail set, have be take aback.



away to windward, in the direction directly contrary to where the wind

have be blow before dinner, the sky be livid, fling an early

night upon the sea, and send forth a tempest of wind that tear the

water into shred of foam. the whole force of the hurricane be upon

the ship’s canvas, which lie back against the mast, and the vessel

lie on her beam-end, her mast make an angle of forty degree with

the horizon.



the confusion be indescribable. every halliard have be let go;

but the yard be jam by the sail, and would not descend. the

clew-line be man, but the sheet would not stir an inch through

the block. nor be the bad of the squall, tempest, hurricane,

whatever it might be, upon they yet; that livid pall of cloud which the

lightning be seam with zigzag fire be still to come, and with it

the full fury its scowling aspect portend.



“my God!” thunder the skipper to the second mate, who stand white,

cow, and apparently helpless, “what have you bring we into?”



the wheel be jam hard a-starboard, but the ship lie like a log,

broadside on to the wind, her mast bow almost on a level with the

water.



“Haul! for your life, man! haul!” shriek the skipper frantically to

the man, who appear paralyse by the sudden catastrophe, and stand

idly with the clew-line and reef tackle pass along they.



Holdsworth, half-way up the weather-poop ladder, his head above the

bulwark, see soon than the captain what be about to happen.



“crowd to windward, all hand!” he roar. there be no time to say

more; the great broad, livid cloud be upon they even whilst Holdsworth

sing out the command; the man hold their breath--unless the mast go

the vessel be doom.



Crash! a noise of wood shiver into splinter, of flog rope and

sail thunder their tatter upon the wind; the fore and main mast

go as you would break a clay-pipe stem across your knee--the first,

just below the top, the other clean off at the deck; and the huge mass

of spar, rope, and sail lay quivering and roll alongside--a

portion on deck, but the great bulk of they in the water--grind

into the vessel’s side as if she have ground upon a shoal of rock.



the ship right, and then another crash; away go the mizzen-topmast,

leave the spanker and cross-jack set. the wind catch these sail and

sweep the ship’s head round right in the eye of the storm, and off she

drive to leeward, disabled, helpless--drag her shattered spar with

she, like something live its torn and mangled limb.



there be no situation in the whole range of the misfortune which

may befall a ship at sea more critical than the one the “Meteor” be

now in. the sea be rise quickly and leap high over the ship’s

bow, pour ton of water in upon the deck (the weight of the wreck

alongside prevent the vessel from rise to the wave), and carry

whatever have become unlashed--cask, spare spar, and the like--aft to

the cuddy front, against which they be launch with a violence that

break the window, and soon promise to demolish the woodwork.



but the bad part of the business be--the action of the sea set the

spar in the water and the hull of the ship roll against each other,

and the thump, thump, thump of the bristly wreck against the vessel’s

side send a hollow undertone through the hooting of the tempest that

be awful to hear.



though the mizzen-mast still stand, the weight of the other mast in

fall have severely wrench it, and it literally rock in its bed

to the swaying of the great spanker-boom. add to all this the midnight

darkness in the air, which the flash of lightning only serve to

deepen by the momentary and ghastly illumination they cast.



yet, if the ship be not to be drag to destruction, it be

imperative that she should be free, and free at once, from the

ponderous incumbrance of the wreck that ground against her side. the

captain have shout himself hoarse, and be no long to be hear. but

now Holdsworth make himself audible in tone that rise above the gale

like trumpet-blast.



“we must clear the wreck or founder! all hand out with their knife

and cut away everything!”



a comprehensive order that must be literally obey.



“Carpenter!” he roar.



“here, sir,” come a voice from the main-deck.



Holdsworth spring in the direction of the voice: shout again, and the

man be at his side.



“quick! where’s your tool chest? bear a hand, now!”



the two man fight their way through the water that come drench

and fly over the forecastle, to the boatswain’s berth, which the

carpenter share; and in a few moment return staggering aft with

their arm full of tool, which they thrust into the hand of the man.

Holdsworth seize an adze; the carpenter another; and to it fall all

hand, feel for the rope and then let drive at they.



the mere occupation hearten the man, who work with a will, burst

into encourage cheer from time to time and call to each other

incessantly. the lightning be so far useful that it enable they to

obtain glimpse of the progress they make. the starboard shroud lay

across the deck, from bulwark to bulwark, like bridge; these be the

first that be deal with. they keep the wreck close alongside, and

the strain upon they be enormous. Whilst Holdsworth and the carpenter

hack, they repeatedly call to a few of the man whose zeal keep they

work on the lee side, though their figure could not be see, to

stand clear, wisely guess that after a few of the shroud have be

sever, the whole would part like a rope-yarn: and this happen. one

final blow divide a shroud and leave the weight upon a few other,

which be unequal to support it; and as they fly a loud shriek rise;

a man have be catch by the fly shroud round the body and whirl

overboard like dust. but in the darkness none could tell who the

messmate be that have lose his life.



the parting of these shroud release the wreck from the ship’s side,

and it drift some fathom away. the horrible grinding sound cease;

but still the mast and yard, which the lightning disclose seethe

in the water, black, ugly, and as dangerous as a lee-shore, be

attach to the hull by a network of rope, all which must be sever.

the knife of the man cut and hack in all direction; and first here

and then there, and sometimes as if crash in half-a-dozen place at

once, the adze wield by Holdsworth be to be hear.



the last shroud be at length sever, the last rope part; the hull,

drift fast than the dead weight of wreck, fall astern of the

horrible incumbrance, and the next flash of lightning show the water

boil round the black spar ahead.



“Hurrah!” shout Holdsworth; but the man, weary and faint after

their long and great struggle, and sicken by the shriek of their

perish comrade, whose cry still sound in their ear, answer the

encouraging shout faint-heartedly.



the ocean be still a black and howl wilderness, and the vessel

plunge and roll, and tremble in the jumping sea, with her head

right in the wind’s eye, and the water pour over she in sheet

of undulate fire. the water have stove in the cuddy front, and be

wash in ton down into the steerage. what they have now to do be to

furl the cross-jack; aft come the man and clew the sail up; on which

the vessel fall broadside to the wind and roll her bulwark under

water.



to furl the cross-jack be a job full of peril; but, if the thing be

to be do at all, it be to be do by a _coup de main_. holdsworth,

cry to the hand to follow he, spring up the port mizzen rigging.

half-a-dozen go up after he, the rest skulk in the darkness, and

stand hold their breath, expect every moment the crash that

should fle the man aloft into the sea. no glimpse of the brave

fellow be to be obtain; nothing but the flap outline of the

white sail could be see; the mast creak harshly; but from time

to time the man’s voice could be hear even above the roar of the

tempest, the groaning of the ship, and the rolling of spare cask, a

sheep-pen which have become unlashed, and other thing about the waist

and quarter-deck; and slowly the faint space of thunder canvas

vanish in the darkness, and be securely stow.



meanwhile, the hand about the poop have be send to man the pump. the

carpenter have sound the well and report three foot of water in the

hold.



“she must be tight, sir! I be sure she be tight, sir! she have ship

the water that’s in she through the mast-coat of the main-mast!” the

skipper shout to Holdsworth, who stand, pant from his exertion

aloft, close against the mizzen-mast, that he might judge what chance

there be of its standing.



meanwhile, the wheel be hard a-starboard, and the gale be now upon

the ship’s quarter, the huge wave break under her counter and

send she wildly yaw forward. have there be daylight, they could

have rig up a jury foresail on the stump of the fore-mast, which

would have serve to keep she before the wind; but nothing could be

do in the overwhelming darkness except to keep the pump at work.



but she be ride more easily now, and ship few sea; the port

bulwark just abaft the gangway have be crush to a level with the

deck by the fall of the main-mast, and offer a wide aperture for the

escape of the water, so that the main-deck, where the hand work the

pump, be practicable.



furiously as it still blow, it be evident that the storm be abate;

there be rift in the cloud, through which, here and there, a pale

star glimmer for an instant, and be then swallow up.



it be now five bell (half-past ten); the skipper order rum to be

serve out to the man, who be wet through to the skin, and fag to

death by their extraordinary exertion. the carpenter sound the well

again, and report an increase of three inch in the depth of water.

this be a terrible announcement, and prove beyond a doubt that the

ship be leak, though the crew be keep in ignorance of the report,

that they might not be dishearten.



at six bell the cloud have break into huge black group, with space

of clear sky between, and the wind be lull as rapidly as it have

rise.



the ship be still buoyant enough to rise easily over the sea; but

anything more forlorn than her appearance, as it be disclose by the

dim light that fall from the rift among the cloud, cannot be imagine.



the fore-mast stand like a black and lightning-shatter tree; the

jib-boom hang in two piece from the bowsprit; where the main-mast have

stand be some huge jagged splinter; and aft tower the mizzen-mast,

with the cross-jack swinge to the roll of the ship, the spanker with

its peak halliard go, and the whole picture of it complete the

unutterable air of desolation present by the storm-shatter vessel.



at eight bell the carpenter report no increase of the water in the

hold, which cheer intimation the captain deliver to the man from

the break of the poop, who receive it with a faint cheer.



the pump have be relieve three time, and now the port watch be at

they, make the water bubble on to the deck, where it be wash to

and fro, and pour in stream through the scupper-hole.



at one o’clock, Holdsworth, who have be on deck since a quarter to

seven, go below to put on dry clothe; and as he be leave his

cabin to return on deck he meet Mrs. Tennent. her face be very pale in

the light of the swinge lamp, and she stand at her cabin door, by the

handle of which she support herself.



“be we not in great danger, Mr. Holdsworth?” she whisper, in a tone

of deep excitement.



“the bad be pass, I hope,” answer Holdsworth cheerfully.



“do not be afraid of tell I the truth. I can be brave for my

child’s sake. if real danger should come, Mr. Holdsworth, will you

remember he? will you be near he in that moment?”



“we willn’t talk of danger yet, Mrs. Tennent. we have have an ugly bout of

it, but the daylight be come, and then we shall be more comfortable.”



“many time,” she exclaim, “I think we be sink! o God! what a

horrible night this have be! I hear the water rush past the cabin

door, and I try to reach the deck, but be too faint to carry my

child, and I could not leave he.”



“well, you see we be still afloat,” Holdsworth answer cheerily.

“depend upon it, we will do our good to save the ship. take my advice

and lie down and get some sleep. this water here,” point to the

cuddy-deck, “mean nothing; a swab will put that to right. the morning

be come, and you be sail under a skipper who know what he be

about.”



he wave his hand cordially, and leave she.



       *       *       *       *       *



all through that long night the hand stick to the pump, but the water

gain upon they inch by inch, and when the morning break at last, the

vessel be deep and heavy, roll sluggishly, and leak fast.



the sun be a welcome sight to the poor fagged seaman. up he spring,

flush the universe with a pink splendour, and disperse the heavy

cloud which hang in cluster about his rise-point.



up to that time there have be a fresh breeze blowing, the dreg, so

to speak, of the storm that have dismantle the ship; but this lull

as the sun rise, the sea smooth out its turbulent wave, and a day

fill with the promise of calm and beauty break on a scene as desolate

as any the heart can conceive.



one of the watch be in the forecastle; half the other watch on deck

be at the pump, the monotonous sound of which have be echo many

hour, together with the gushing of water surge over the deck, and

pour in stream from the ship’s side.



the vessel be now no more than a log on the water; not a shred of

canvas, with the exception of the mutilate spanker upon she, her

port bulwark crush, her fore-mast a stump, her deck exhibit a

scene of wild disorder--loose spar that have be wash from forward

encumber the entrance of the cuddy; the cuddy front batter to

piece, spare cask pile tumultuously about the poop-ladder, and the

long-boat, lash between the galley and the fore-mast, and which have

hold some of the live stock, full of water and drown sheep. on the

port side, the sever shroud, which have support the mast, trail

their black length in the sea; and all about the starboard side be

the fragment of rope and stay hack and tear to piece; while the

port main-chain have receive a wrench that have tear the bolt out of

the ship’s side, and leave the iron stand out.



as yet none of the passenger have make their appearance. the captain

have bring a chart from his cabin and unroll it upon the skylight,

and stand with his finger upon it, calculate his whereabouts by

yesterday’s reckoning, and wait for Holdsworth to return from the

hold, which he and the carpenter be explore for the leak.



the swell, which be heavy, surge against the ship’s side; but her

buoyancy be go, she hardly move to the pressure.



presently Holdsworth come out of the hold, wet and exhausted, follow

by the carpenter in bad plight.



“well?” exclaim the captain, in a subdued but eager voice.



“I be afraid it be a hopeless case, sir. she’s leak in a dozen

different place.”



“the bad leak be just amidship,” say the carpenter. “it’s under the

water in the hold. you can hear it bubble, but there’s no get at

it.”



“what sounding have you get?”



“eleven foot, sir!”



“Good God!” cry the skipper; “that’s an increase of a foot and a half

since seven bell.”



“we have well look to the boat, sir,” say Holdsworth, scan the

horizon.



“don’t talk of the boat yet, sir!” pant the skipper. “clap some

backstay on to the fore-mast and turn to and rig up the spare

staysail.”



“ay, ay, sir,” answer Holdsworth, and go forward to call all hand

and make what sail they could upon the stump of the fore-mast, whilst

the skipper walk passionately to and fro, perfectly conscious of the

hopelessness of their situation, but determine to blind his eye to it.



the first among the passenger to come on deck be the General, who

stand transfix by the spectacle of the wreck. he and some of the

other have attempt during the night to leave their berth and find

out the reason of the uproar that be go on over their head, but

have be literally blow back again the moment they show their nose

above the hatchway; and none of they, with the exception of Mrs.

Tennent, having have an opportunity of speak to either the captain

or Holdsworth, they be all in perfect ignorance that the vessel be

actually a wreck.



Whilst the General stand gasp and stare up aloft in search of the

majestic mast and sail that have rear their graceful height when he

be last on deck, he be join by Mr. St. Aubyn and Mr. Holland, both

of whom turn pale with amazement and fear.



then all three of they run up to the captain.



“oh, tell we what have happen? what will become of we? be we

sink?” cry the actor.



“where be the mast go? be it possible that we can ever reach

America in this condition?” gasp Mr. Holland.



“Captain, we seem to be in a frightful mess! why, we be founder,

sir!” exclaim the General, roll his eye over the sea and then

fix they upon the captain.



“gentleman! gentleman!” return the skipper, extend his hand,

“pray leave I! you distract I by your question.”



“be we in danger?” implore Mr. St. Aubyn.



“yes, sir; cann’t you see?” answer the skipper fiercely.



“be it possible?” stammer Mr. St. Aubyn, turn deadly pale.



“it be possible!” cry the skipper scornfully. “but I hope you be not

go to be afraid, sir. look over the break of the poop and you’ll

see the man pump for their life. danger be one thing, and drown

be another. I beg, sir, that you will control your fear. panic be

easily create, and you will remember, please, that we have woman among

we.”



say which he walk some pace away. Mr. St. Aubyn burst into tear;

Mr. Holland gaze around he with an air of stupefaction; the General

follow the skipper.



“be our position really serious?”



“yes, General; the ship’s bottom be leaky fore and aft.”



“what do you mean to do?”



“keep she afloat as long as I can. and now will you do I a service? go

and clap that snivel actor on the back and put some heart in he.

one coward make many, and this be no time for any man on board my ship

who value his life at one farthe to lose his pluck.”



by this time the hand forward have lash a block on the stump of the

fore-mast, and run up a spare staysail. Holdsworth then come aft

to the poop. the captain call he to the skylight, and they hang

together over the chart, calculate their neighbourhood, and devise

expedient in subdue tone. the man who be far enough forward to see

they as they stand together on the poop eye they curiously, and hold

muttering conversation together, some of they go to the ship’s side

and look over.



it be feel by every man among they that the vessel be sink;

and those who work the pump ply they languidly, as though

understand the fruitlessness of their labour.



Mrs. Tennent come on deck with her boy and stand near Holdsworth,

ask no question, but with an expression on her face that plainly

show she conscious of the danger and prepare for the bad. soon

afterwards come Mrs. Ashton, who shriek out when she behold the

dismaste hull, and clung convulsively to her husband. her maid

follow she, shivering, cow, with big eye stare everywhere like

a madwoman’s.



then a dead silence fall upon the ship, disturb only by the languid

clanking of the pump and the fall of the continuous stream of water

over the ship’s side.



it be now half-past eight o’clock. not a breath of air ripple the

surface of the sea, which rise and sink to a deep and voluminous

under-swell. some heavy cloud hang motionless in the blue sky, from

one of which a shower of rain be fall about a mile off, arch a

little brilliant rainbow upon the water.



presently Holdsworth advance to the poop-rail and sing out to the

carpenter to sound the well. this be do, and the report show that

the leak be gain fast upon the pump.



on this announcement all heart go out of the captain like a flash,

and leave he silent and spiritless.



he rally, go to Holdsworth’s side and call out: “belay that

pump there! boatswain, send all hand aft to the quarter-deck.”



the sound of the pumping cease, the man come aft in group and stand

in a crowd.



some of they be beard, some quite young; their attire be various

but always picturesque: here a red shirt, there white, here blue

serge, there coarse canvas, many with bare brown arm ring with

tattoo-mark; some in sea-boot, some with naked foot. the bright sun

gleam upon their upturned face, pale for want of sleep and with the

intense weariness of their long and heavy labour. there be no want

of respect suggest by any of they; but, on the contrary, there be a

rough and sympathetic deference in their manner and gaze as they fix

their eye on their white-haired skipper and listen to his speech,

which he deliver in a voice that now and then falter.



“my man, I have hope to keep our poor old hooker afloat by man the

pump day and night and head for home, which, with a breeze astern of

we, we might have reach even in the trim the gale last night have put

we in. but I find that the water be gain upon we fast than we can

pump it out, and it’s not my intention to fag you with useless work.

but in this sea the hull be likely to float for some hour yet; so we

shall have plenty of time to get the boat out and do the good we can

for our life. you be most of you Englishmen, and those who be not

be all brave fellow, and no man can be well than that, let he

hail from what port he may: so I can depend upon you turn to and

obey order quietly. there be thirty-four soul aboard of we and

four boat; there’s room for thirteen in the long-boat, and for seven

apiece in the quarter-boat. I’ll take charge of the long-boat, your

chief mate of the pinnace, and the second mate and boatswain will take

the other. there’s no hurry, and there must be no confusion. let a

dozen hand man the pump, the rest go to breakfast and then relieve

the pump. then tumble aft, get the long-boat launch, and do the good

we can for ourselves; and may God preserve we! amen.”



at the conclusion of this speech the man raise a cheer, the

boatswain’s pipe shrill, clang go the pump again, and the

quarter-deck be desert.



the captain turn to the passenger.



“lady and gentleman, these be ugly strait for I to have bring

you into, and I would that God in his mercy have ordain it otherwise.

I have be forty year at sea, and the like of this have never befall

I before. but that’s no matter. I’ll take care to do my duty by you to

the last. we have get enough boat to accommodate we all comfortably;

the weather promise fair, and it’s odd if every one of we ben’t snug

and safe on board some ship before to-night; for we’re right in the

track of homeward-bind ship from the United States. some of you will

come with I, and some go along with my chief officer, who have work

nobly for we all, and who’ll work as nobly right away through for those

who be with he whilst the life be in his body. lady, keep up your

courage; for a sink ship be a small matter when you’ve get good

boat, and be with man who know how to handle ’em. we’ll go below now

and make as good a breakfast as we can; we’ll then provision the boat

and put off, as sure as our heart can make we that God’s eye, which be

everywhere, will not lose sight of we.”



there be some murmur, and then a silence, which Mrs. Ashton break by

burst into a passion of tear. when she be in some measure calm,

the General say:



“fellow-passenger, will you unite with I in a prayer to our merciful

God for his protection?”



the man take off their hat, but the captain exclaim:



“General, there be hand for’ard who might like to join we. we should

give they the chance.”



Holdsworth go to the forecastle, and presently return with the

whole ship’s company follow he. the hand at the pump cease their

work to gather round the capstan, and the passenger attend the

skipper in a body to the quarter-deck. the old General stand in the

midst of the crowd and knelt--an example follow by the rest.



there be something too sacred in the nature of the extemporaneous

prayer offer up by the General to make it proper for I to write it

down here; but its effect be deeply impressive. noble, beyond the

power of word to describe, be the spectacle of the fine old american,

bare-headed and kneeling, his tremble hand clasp, his kindly,

honest face upturned to the sky, breathe forth in broken tone an

earnest entreaty that God, in his infinite mercy, would look down upon

his servant now and grant they his all-powerful protection in this

their hour of danger and suffering. equally affect be the spectacle

of the man--some with hand clasp before their face, some kneeling

with reverently-bow head, some gaze with earnest eye upon the

petitioner, and some even weeping--not unmanly tear--those who weep

be among the brave. the widow kneel with her arm about her child’s

neck, in an attitude both shielding and imploring. the husband and wife

pray hand-in-hand.



Overhead shine the joyous sun; the long and polished swell surge

against the sluggish vessel’s side; and amidst the tone of the old

General and the solemn murmur of those who follow his word, you

might hear the gurgling of the water in the hold, and feel the ship’s

grow weight and helplessness in the heavy and weary roll she give

to the movement of the sea.









CHAPTER VII.



in the BOATS.





by twelve o’clock they have bale the long-boat out and get she over the

ship’s side, a task of no small difficulty, since, the main-mast being

go, they have no mean of sling she. the other boat be also

lower, each with a hand in she, and hang in a group about the port

side of the ship, where the bulwark be smash.



each boat be properly supply with mast, sail, and oar; also

with water, biscuit, some rum in bottle, etc. they look mere

cookie-shell alongside the great hull, and it seem difficult to

realise that they would sustain among they the weight of the crowd of

man who stand by ready to jump into they.



the ship be settle fast. they have leave off pump she some time

since, and she have now sink a great hole under her port fore-chain to

a level with the water, which gush in like a cascade.



Mrs. Ashton be the first to be hand out of the ship. she scream

and hang back, and throw her hand out to her husband; but the man,

raise she firmly in their arm, offer she the while certain rough

and hearty encouragement, pass she over the ship’s side to the

sailor in the boat, who deposit she in the long-boat. the widow,

at her own request, be assign to holdsworth’s boat. they hand

her boy over first, and then she follow and seat herself in the

stern-sheet, hold her child tightly.



then rise a cry of “bear a hand! the ship will founder!”



Mrs. Ashton’s maid be pass out quickly, and then the passenger; the

actor and the General get into holdsworth’s boat, the other into

the long-boat. after this, the seaman, feel the imminence of the

danger, tumble rapidly into the boat; and then Holdsworth quit the

ship, follow by the captain.



“shove off!” shout the boatswain, who command one of the

quarter-boat.



out flash the oar; the boat part and stand aloof from the hull at

a distance of about three hundred foot.



it be impossible to describe the mingled emotion of dismay, curiosity,

and breathless suspense with which the man await the sinking of the

hull. there be not a soul among they who feel privileged to depart

until the vessel, so noble once, so desolate and break now, have sink

to her long home in the deep Atlantic. something absolutely of human

pathos that appeal to the heart, as the distress of a live thing

might, seem mixed up in the aspect of unutterable desolation she

present, the more defined and keen because of the mocking joyousness

of the sunlight that stream over she, and the fair and azure surface

of the sea on which she rest. her figure-head, uninjure by the

gale--as perfect a piece of workmanship as ever grace a vessel’s

bow--might, by no violent fantasy, have be deem the spirit of the

ship poise herself an instant ere she soar towards the sky. the two

sail upon she flap hollowly to her roll, and once there come from

her silent deck a sound as of a bell be strike, which fill the

listen sailor with awe, and set they bend superstitious glance

in search of the Shadow that be toll the ship’s funeral knell.



all on a sudden the hull lurch in the direction of the boat and

expose her slope deck. “she’s go now!” cry one of the man.

this be true. down sink her stern slowly, so slowly that many second

pass ere her stern-window be on a level with the water. she

right, and her bow, high raise, point the shattered jib-boom

aloft, as though in her last agony she raise her mangle limb to

heaven. she then sink stern foremost, the deep tragical dignity

attend her descent: the silence unbroken, save by the sullen

gurgling and bubble of the water force itself through her deck.

her stern disappear; then her bow stand black on the water; they

vanish, and the fore-mast with the sail upon it alone remain

visible. low and low these crept, but still it be possible to

trace the undulating outline of the hull in the clear water. the

sponge-like sail suck up the water quick than it sink, and arch a

brown shadow upon its snow; then the jagged top of the fore-mast only

be to be see; this vanish, and the boat be leave alone upon the

mighty surface of the deep.



a deep silence prevail among the man while she be sink; and not

for some moment after she have disappear do the spell upon they

break, and a long and tremulous sigh escape they.



then the captain’s voice in the long-boat be hear:



“Mr. Holdsworth, our course be east-north-east. every boat have a

compass aboard of she. now, my man, up with your mast; we may get a

breeze before sun-down. and, meanwhile, out with your oar and make

what way we can towards the old country!”



the stout-hearted fellow answer with a cheer; in all four boat they

ship the mast; out go the oar, and the water bubble round the

stem.



the man be right to cheer. God know they need what encouragement

each other’s voice could give they.



what pen shall describe the overwhelming sense of the immensity of

the sea, now that its surface could be touch by the hand--its huge

presence so close! that sense alone be a weight that oppress the

heart of the passenger like death. the height of a large ship from

the edge of the water implant a habit of security; but here, they

overhang the deep by an arm’s length, and near enough to see their own

pale face mirror in the green abyss from which they be separate

by plank not much stout than the sole of a boot.



there be in Holdsworth’s boat: himself, Mrs. Tennent and her boy,

Mr. St. Aubyn, the General, and two seaman--Winyard and Johnson; in

all, seven soul. the long-boat, in the distance, look crowded; but

then she be the large of the boat. astern of she row the boat

command by the boatswain; astern of Holdsworth’s, the boat command

by Mr. Thompson, the second mate.



there could be no purpose gain by row, for, let they ply the oar

as hard as they would, they could not urge the heavy boat fast

than three mile an hour. holdsworth steer for the long-boat, and

propose to the captain that they should lay their oar in and wait

for a breeze; which be agree to. the sun shine hot upon the glassy

sea, and the boat hoist their sail as a protection against the

ray. and for ever the man bent earnest and anxious glance round the

bare and polished horizon for a sail.



in holdsworth’s boat the two seaman sit forward, talk together in

low voice; Mr. St. Aubyn recline with his back against the mast,

glance incessantly about he with quick, scared eye, but quite

silent, as though the novelty and horror of the situation be more

than his mind could receive, and he be labour to master it. the

General’s face be placid, and even hopeful. the widow, hold her son

at her side, keep her eye bent downwards, and often her lip move.



in the other boat the man talk, and often call to one another.

their voice sounded force and unreal as the tone float across the

water, and in a strange manner heighten the unspeakable sense of

solitude inspire by the boundless and tenantless deep.



for some time the little boy appear to share in the feeling which

hold all but the two sailor in Holdsworth’s boat silent; but he

presently grow restless, and pull his mother by the skirt, ask she

in a whisper when the ship be come back to take they on board again.



“another ship will come and take we soon, pray God, Louis,” answer

the mother.



“but where be our ship, mamma?”



Holdsworth overhear the question, and answer in his hearty, cheery

manner:



“look well about you, Louis, and, by-and-by, you will see a tiny spot

of white rise somewhere on that clear circle,” point round the

horizon, “and that will be our ship come to take we home.”



“oh, Mr. Holdsworth!” say the actor, in a faint voice, “if the wind

rise, will not the water get into our boat and sink it?”



“not if I can help it, sir. I be wait for the wind to rise. there be

no chance of a rescue in this calm.”



“though we should be grateful for this calm,” exclaim the General,

“for it have enable we all to leave the sink ship in safety.”



“I have lose my all in that ship--all the money I have in the world, and

my clothe, and thing that be priceless to I,” moan Mr. St. Aubyn.



the widow raise her head, and exclaim, “I too have lose much that

be precious, and which no money could ever purchase. but, so far, God

have watch over we and preserve our life, and I can well spare all

else--all else--if he will but leave I this treasure.” she weep as she

bow her head over her child.



“let we not murmur, Mr. St. Aubyn,” say the General softly, “but call

upon he who rebuke the wind and wave in the sea of Galilee, and

calm they. have not we his disciple’ faith? he be in our midst,

watch over we, even as we sit now. this ocean be but the symbol of

his majesty and might: his servant who will bear we safely on its bosom

at its Lord’s command. our Saviour sleep not, neither will he forsake

we. we forget he when we yield to our fear.”



“thank you for those word, sir,” say the man name Johnson. “God

don’t forget those who be at sea any more than those who stop on

shore. I have be bad off nor this, sir, lash to a raft for

forty-eight hour, and here I be to tell the story. beg your

pardon, sir,” he add, touch his cap, and draw back respectfully.



by this time the boat have drift some distance apart; but the voice

talk in they could still be hear with the utmost clearness, so

exquisite a vehicle of sound be the smooth surface of water.



it be one o’clock by Holdsworth’s watch when they behold the horizon

in the east darken under what resemble the shadow of a cloud, and

the voice of a man in the long-boat come across the water, cry, “a

breeze at last, my boy!”



it be a light breeze, and move very slowly, but it fill the sail

and send the boat ripple gently through the water. as it come foul

of the course the skipper mean to take, they lie as close to it as

they could; and eye be strain in its direction for the welcome

sail, some of they whisper, it might bring along with it. some white

cloud come up, and as they soar about the horizon, they so closely

resemble the sail of ship that even Holdsworth’s experienced eye be

deceive, and he gaze intently with a beat heart.



the breeze freshen, and the unequal sailing quality of the boat

manifest themselves. the long-boat draw ahead rapidly; Holdsworth’s

come next; the other two fell astern. the wind, though in reality

light, seem tolerably strong, owe to the boat sail close to it.

Holdsworth’s boat lie over, which terrify St. Aubyn, and make he

cle to the weather-gunwale.



“you’re afeard rather early, sir,” say one of the

seaman--Winyard--sarcastically.



“the boat will turn over!” gasp the actor.



indeed his fear and despair be pitiable, and have not only dull his

eye, but pinch and thin his face as though he be fresh from a

sick bed.



“take a pull at this,” say Holdsworth, offer he some rum, and

heartily commiserate the man’s suffering. but St. Aubyn shake his

head, and gaze with distend eye at the water, shiver repeatedly,

and sometimes talk to himself.



in order to let the hinder boat come up, the long-boat ahead from

time to time stop her way by put she helm down, which example

Holdsworth regularly follow; and so they sail throughout the whole

afternoon, the breeze remain steady and the sea smooth.



the boat command by Holdsworth be about twenty-seven foot long, with

seven foot or thereabout of beam. there be a locker aft, which have

be fill with small bag of bread, as they call biscuit at sea; and

forward be one small and two large keg of water, and a tin pannikin

to serve out the allowance with. at the bottom of the boat be a set

of grating, mean to keep the foot clear of any water that might be

ship, with a well convenient to get at, and half a cocoa-nut-shell

with a handle let into it, to bale the boat with. the boat be new,

stoutly build, and rig with a lug-sail. a small compass have be put

in she, and Holdsworth have lash it carefully to a thwart. this be

the only nautical instrument they have with they, and unless they could

guess their whereabout, it would not, after a time, be of much service.



they have during the afternoon ascertain the quantity of provision

and water they carry, and discover that there be enough to

last for about ten day, provide each person have no more than two

biscuit, and a quarter full of the pannikin of water, a day. they

have also three bottle of rum. the first allowance be serve out by

Holdsworth at five o’clock in the afternoon. a biscuit be hand to

each person; the little boy and the two seaman eat theirs hungrily:

the General and Holdsworth nibble only a portion of theirs: but Mrs.

Tennent and the actor eat nothing. the mother give half her biscuit to

her boy, and put the other half in her pocket for he to eat during

the night. the actor refuse his allowance altogether, and Holdsworth

return it to the bag he have take it from.



all the other boat remain in sight; the long-boat ahead, and

the other two at unequal distance astern. from time to time they

encourage each other by wave their hat; and just before sunset some

of the man in the long-boat strike up a hymn, the chorus of which stole

faintly across the breeze, and mingle with the bubble play of the

water round the boat’s bow.



the sun go down, brand the great ocean with an angry glare; but

nothing be visible upon either horizon but the deceptive tail-end of

cloud rise or dwindle. the breeze grow strong as the darkness

creep on, and they lower the sail and take a couple of reef in it,

whilst there be light enough abroad for they to see what they be

about. they soon lose sight of the other boat, and holdsworth find

the wind draw ahead, put the boat round, judge that the other

would do so likewise.



now, if at no other time, be the sense of the profound helplessness

of their position force upon they. it be easy to write and read of

an open boat far out in the Atlantic Ocean, and darkness around; but

none save those who have experience the situation can realise all the

horror of it. wave which would scarcely more than ripple against

the side of a ship, make a dangerous sea for an open boat, and arch

their seethe head over she with a threat in every one of they of

destruction.



but _the_ overpowering sensation be the near presence of the sea. your

foot be below its surface; your head but an arm’s length above it. and

you hear the quick splash of the boat’s bow as she jump awkwardly

into the hollow of the wave, wobble as she go forward with jerk

and many stoppage, while now and again the sea chuck a handful of

spray into your eye as an earnest of the way it mean to deal with you

presently, when the wind have make it more angry.



the star come out and shine placidly among the cloud which be

roll away to the north-west. there be a short quick sea, which

make the boat dip uncomfortably, and now and again whisk a sheet

of spray over the seaman who sit forward. but there be more south

than east in the breeze, which keep the temperature of the night

mild. the little boy fall asleep in his mother’s arm; Mr. St. Aubyn

recline against the mast, his arm fold, and his head droop on

his breast, start at interval as the spray fall like a shower of

rain in the boat, but speedily relapse again into the sluggish or

semi-unconscious state into which he have fall shortly after the

sun have go down. the General and Holdsworth sometimes converse.

presently Winyard, turn his coat collar over his ear, slip under

the thwart, where he coil himself like a cat, and go to sleep.



“I wish I could induce you to lie down, Mrs. Tennent,” say Holdsworth.

“my coat will make you a capital pillow. I don’t want it indeed. I have

sleep on deck in my shirt-sleeve in colder night than this. I shalln’t

put the boat about again to-night if this wind hold, and you will lie

with your boy alongside of you as snugly as possible on this seat.”



she thank he, but say it would be useless for she to lie down; she

should not be able to sleep.



“you have eat nothing all day,” say the General; “you must not allow

your strength to fail you. pray try to eat a little biscuit.”



Holdsworth hand she a biscuit, and she break a piece of it off and

appear to munch it; but in the darkness they could not tell how

little she eat.



no sign be to be see of the other boat, although once Holdsworth

imagine he hear a voice halloe a long way to windward. the boat’s

head be now point east-north-east; but she lie close to the wind

and make scarcely more than four knot an hour. the jump of the sea

deaden her way materially; but this jump decrease as the night wear

on, for the wave grow long, with steady intermission. at twelve

o’clock, Holdsworth, who be wear out by his long spell at the helm,

call to Johnson to awaken Winyard. up jump the seaman from the

bottom of the boat and come aft. Holdsworth give he the yoke-line,

and bid Johnson lie down and get some rest, seat himself on the

lee-side of the mast and scan the sea to right and leave of he.

the old General have fall asleep right along the thwart on which he

sit, his face bury in his arm. the boy sleep soundly in his mother’s

arm, but whether she slumber or not, Holdsworth could not tell. once

Mr. St. Aubyn start up as from a nightmare, mutter some break

sentence, and be silent again.



“keep she close,” say Holdsworth to Winyard, “and watch the sea.”



“you have well take some rest, sir. I can handle the boat whilst you’re

down.”



“no. I’ll wait until Johnson have have his nap.”



so pass two hour.



it be draw near half-past two in the morning when Winyard call,

in a loud whisper:



“Master, ben’t that a ship to windward, there?”



no one in the boat hear he but Holdsworth. he jump up and peer

into the starlit gloom ahead, where, sure enough, the outline of a dark

shadow could be trace, though only by look on one side of it.



“yes, that’s a ship!” he answer hoarsely; “but she’s too far to

windward to hear our shout. have we any light aboard of we? quick!”



he pull the General, who leap up, rub his eye.



“have you any match about you?”



“no--what be it?”



“there’s a ship yonder! I could souse my handkerchief in rum and

set fire to it. hi! Mr. St. Aubyn! feel if you have a match in your

pocket.” but the actor answer with a stupefied stare, whereupon

Holdsworth search his pocket without avail.



Johnson be awake, stand up in the bow, with his arm lift.



“ship ahoy!” roar Holdsworth. one might have think the voice deep

and powerful enough to have carry twice the distance of that gliding

shadow.



they wait breathlessly; but no sound be return.



“altogether, now!” shout Holdsworth; “one, two, three--ship ahoy!”



the united voice sound like a shriek of death-agony rise out of

the ebony-colour deep; but no response be bring back by the wind.



“o God!” rave Winyard. “they’d see we if we could only show a light!”



“she be run before the wind,” cry Johnson; “she’s pass we!”



“put your helm up!” roar Holdsworth. “we’ll follow she. she may hear

we when we get she to leeward.”



they let go the halliard, shake the reef out of the sail, and set it

again, slacken the sheet far out. the boat head for the visionary

shadow, which be fast fade in the universal gloom, and the foam

boil under and alongside of she.



“altogether again!” sing out Holdsworth.



once more go forth the loud, despair chorus, to be follow by

silence. they might as well have attempt to chase a cloud. Keen as

the sailor’ eye be, they could no long perceive the shadow.



“never mind!” exclaim Holdsworth, cheerily, “there may be other near

we; we’ll keep a sharp look-out.”



“she may fall in with one of the other boat,” say Johnson, “and,

maybe, she’ll cruise about to find we.”



the chill of disappointment pass, and they grow hopeful. the mere

fact of having sight a ship impart a new encouragement.



“we should be in the track of outward-bounder,” say Holdsworth. “give

we a hand here, Johnson, to take these reef in. bring she close again,

Winyard. pray God we shall be talk of this night on a ship’s deck by

noon to-morrow.”



the little boy, who have be awaken by the halloing of the man,

shiver, creep close to his mother’s side, and fall asleep again.









CHAPTER VIII.



the second day.





“Mr. Holdsworth,” say the General, “will you not let I watch whilst

you get some sleep? you have be up now for nearly three night

run, and I beg you to consider the preciousness of your life to we

all.”



“I be much oblige to you, General. I’ll do as you ask I. Johnson,

come aft and relieve Winyard here. keep a sharp look-out, my lad, and

wake I up if the breeze freshen.” he seat himself in the bottom of

the boat, rest his head upon a thwart, and in a few moment be fast

asleep.



a hush fall upon the boat which nothing break but the quick angry

sousing of the bow as the boat fall with her short length into the

trough of the sea. the widow have fall asleep at last, and lean

against Johnson, who steer, whilst her boy slumber with his head on

her lap. the sailor sit motionless for fear of wake she, call once

in a whisper to Winyard to come aft and look at the little ’un, and

tell he if he think that God would let such innocence be drown.



“he’s the image of my little bill,” answer Winyard, stoop his

bearded face low that he might see the child’s feature. “I’m glad the

poor lady’s sleep. keep steady, Dick, or you’ll wake she. she ain’t

taste a mossil of food all this blessed day, and it ’ud cut I to the

heart to see the little ’un leave without a mother.”



“ay, and so it would I, Harry. may be we’ll sight a ship to-morrow.

I’ve get my old woman to keep ashore, and I guess, when rent-time

come, she willn’t know what to do, unless I get back.”



“I wish I have some ’baccy with I. ain’t get a bit in all my pocket.

I wonder where t’other boat have get to?”



the conversation, which have be carry on in hoarse whisper, be at

this point interrupt by a movement of the little boy. Johnson raise

his hand and Winyard creep forward, where he sit like a bronze statue,

watch the horizon.



it be about four o’clock, as the seaman guess, when the wind, which

have be pretty steady from the south-east, lull, and then veer

northward come on to blow freshly. the man awaken Holdsworth, who

go to the helm. Mrs. Tennent, who have be arouse by the withdrawal

of the sailor’s shoulder, shiver with the cold and crouch down to

hug herself in her clothe. indeed, the north wind be cold enough;

but Holdsworth, observe the woman’s condition, whip off his coat

without a word and button it over her shoulder, silence she

protest by kindly laughter and encourage word.



the change of wind produce a cross sea which drench the boat and

make her movement horribly uncomfortable. the wind increase, bring

up large cloud, each of which be charge with a small rain-load

squall of its own. the sea rise, and matter begin to wear an ugly

look. the man close-reef the sail, and holdsworth find that the

boat ship water when on the wind, let she go free; and away they

scud with a breeze grow in force every five minute astern of

they. the utmost vigilance be now needful in steer the boat. the

wave be quick and irritable, and break in noisy surface of foam on

either gunwale, and from time to time it seem inevitable that their

curl crest must arch themselves clean into the boat and swamp she.

Holdsworth parry they with the rudder like a fencer with a foil. his

eye be marvellously quick, the movement of his hand delicate and

unerring. what the wave be to that small open boat, the sea of

the Pacific under a westerly gale be to a full-rig ship. she sink

into hollow half-mast deep, where the air be stagnant, behind and

before her black wall of illuminated water, the hinder one of which,

catch she under the stern, raise she with irresistible force to a

height that turn her inmate giddy and sick; and there, expose to

the wind, her sail blow out to crack limit and hurl she madly

forward--to sink into a new abysm, to experience another interval of

breathless, deadly calm.



“may God have mercy upon we!” exclaim the General, in one of these

awful interval, fold his arm tightly and fix his eye on a

tower sea rear astern of they like a hill.



but Holdsworth’s voice echo cheerily: “she be a brave boat, General;

and it’s not my intention to let such ripple as these” ... the rest of

the sentence be drown in the hooting of the wind, as one of these

“ripple” swing the boat high in the full face of it, and the “ripple”

itself break into an acre of foam under the boat’s bow.



the two sailor sit like log, ready for the bad; yet with a supreme

confidence in Holdsworth’s skill as a steersman, which he have already

illustrate in a hundred subtle way, appreciable to none but they.

St. Aubyn lie in the bottom of the boat, motionless. the General,

hold on to the mast, be seat amidship, commend his soul, and

the soul of his comrade, to God, in inaudible prayer. the widow

crouch with her boy, who still sleep, in the stern-sheet; and

beside she tower the form of Holdsworth, a yoke-line in each hand,

his body incline forward, his shirt-sleeve roll above his elbow,

every nerve, every muscle in he string to the tension of steel,

his glitter eye fix upon the sea ahead, his whole attitude

resemble a sculptured personation of audacity, skill, and the fine

british courage.



the dawn break and find they swinge over an ugly sea, with the

wind moderate. as the pallid light spread over the bleak surface of

gray and move water, the weary shipwreck man turn their eye

about in search of a vessel; but the ocean be tenantless save by

its own leaping sea, which play around in an eternal mockery of a

fluctuate hilly horizon.



they be now sail due south. holdsworth steer the boat, and

Winyard bale she out; but, thank to the wonderful skill with which

the rudder be use, no single sea have be ship, and what water

there be consist of the spray that have be blow into the boat off

the crest of the wave when she be in their hollow.



the sun rise and diffuse an exquisite pink through the ribbed cloud

that bar the sky. his glorious light flashed jewel upon the water,

and send a message of hope among the inmate of the tiny boat strive

amid the wild and throbbing wilderness of the deep.



the General stand up, and arch his hand over his eye, gaze slowly

and intently around the whole circumference of the water-line.



“we be alone,” he say; but instantly correct himself. “no, I speak

thoughtlessly. we have God with we. he have be with we all night.

we thank Thee, o God,” he murmur, fold his hand and reverently

lift his face to the sky, “for Thy protection; and we humbly

implore Thee not to abandon we, but to be with we in our anguish and

desolation, and in Thine own good time to snatch we from the peril

that encompass we.”



they all cry amen!



“the wind’s lulling, master,” say Johnson to Holdsworth. “we’ll have

the sea smooth before long.”



“oh, Mr. Holdsworth!” exclaim Mrs. Tennent, start up suddenly and

hurriedly remove his coat from her shoulder, “how cruelly selfish

I have be to deprive you of this covering throughout the long cold

night.”



“I’m well without it,” cry Holdsworth. “even my shirt-sleeve be

too heavy for I--you see I have have to turn they up. Winyard, rouse up

Mr. St. Aubyn. we shall be none the bad, any of we, for a mouthful of

biscuit.”



he pat the little boy with his left hand, with his right keep the

boat’s head straight as a line.



“come, sir, wake up, please. Biscuit’s go to be serve out,” say

Winyard, pull the actor somewhat unceremoniously by the arm.



both seaman think he a white-livere gentleman, and despise he

accordingly.



the poor man lie athwart the boat, his leg double up and his arm

hide his face. he shake his head, without raise it, when Winyard

pull he, but do not speak. the man, think he numb or cramp,

raise he up; whereupon St. Aubyn struggle to his foot, and look

about he with a fix smile. that smile make his face terrible

to behold, for he be deadly white, and a wild fire, with no more

merriment in it than a madman’s laugh, shine in his eye, which look

unnaturally large, and his lip be blue and thin, and lay his tooth

almost bare.



“you fellow may shrug your shoulder, and some of you may hiss,” he

mutter, never remit his fix smile, but speak through his

tooth and bring his clench fist upon his knee, “but you shalln’t

starve I, because you don’t understand what true acting mean. do you

think I cann’t tell what this hollowness, this sinking be, here!” lay

his hand upon his stomach and send his lustrous eye travel

over the other, who watch he in silence. “you be starve I, you

fiend, and drive a poor actor to death. but do you think you will

force he into the workhouse? no, by God! he have spirit, and will seek

a new home, a new country, a new world, rather! who tell I I cannot

act? try I in farce, in comedy, in tragedy! see now--shall I play you

Tony Lumpkin?” he begin to sing:



  “‘then, come, put the jorum about,

    and let we be merry and clever!

  our heart and our liquor be stout,

    here’s the three Jolly pigeon for ever.’



“or shall I give you Lear?” he stretch out his hand to the sea:--



  “‘blow, wind, and crack your cheek! rage! blow!

  you cataract and hurricanoe, spout

  till you have drench’d our steeple, drown’d the cock!

  you sulphurous and think-execute fire,

  vaunt-courier to oak-cleave thunderbolt,

  Sings my white head!’



be not that fine, gentleman? now turn about with more shrug, and drive

I mad with your canot. ha! ha! ha!”



his laughter be shocking. the seaman shrink away from he.



“he have go mad with terror!” whisper the General.



the widow hide her face in her hand.



“Johnson,” say Holdsworth, “mix some rum and water in that pannikin

and give it to he with a biscuit.”



the actor take both, stare first at the pannikin, then at the biscuit.



“gentleman!” he cry, with his wild smile, “I be Timon of Athens,

sour, crusty, and” ... he stop with a laugh. “but before his mind

go, he pledge his friend, stand thus:--‘Here’s to you--dog!’”

he fling the content of the pannikin at Holdsworth, and dash the

vessel into the boat; “and with this, I feed the wind!” and he hurl

the biscuit into the sea.



“seize he!” shriek Holdsworth, notice a quick movement on the

actor’s part. the man spring forward, but too late to catch he. he

leap on to the thwart and bound overboard with a peal of laughter,

ere you could have cry “hold!” and vanish under the crest of a

wave that be break at the moment under the boat.



“see!” cry the General; “he have come to the surface! there be his

head! he may be save yet!”



but the boat be foam through the water at six or seven knot an

hour; the sea be still so lively that to broach she to would have be

to capsize she in an instant.



“we cannot save he!” exclaim Holdsworth, bitterly, grasp the

situation at once; and keep the boat’s head doggedly away.



those who watch the drown man, see he, a mere dot, on the

tumbling water, heave high on the summit of a wave, with both his

arm upraise; then down he sink into the trough of the sea, the next

wave boil over he, and they behold he no more.



the General cover his face with his hand and weep aloud. the widow

be so sick and faint with the horror of the scene, that she lean

back, white and motionless, with her eye close. Johnson come aft

and put some rum to her lip, which revive she, and then she begin

the very ghost of her former self; her complexion be ashen, her eye

hollow, her countenance gaunt with a hard, weird look of old age upon

it. Holdsworth notice that her dress be wet with salt water and clung

to her leg; but this it be impossible to remedy. infinite pity smite

he as he gaze from she to her child, and he hand she a biscuit,

entreat she to eat it. the boy eat his allowance quickly; but even

out of he something of the youthfulness and freshness of his infancy

have pass; a perception of their danger and misery appear to have

visit he; and he cling to his mother’s side, hold her dress with

one hand whilst he eat the biscuit, and gaze about he with puzzle

eye, in which be mix up a strong expression of terror.



the impression produce by the sudden and tragical death of the actor

be more lasting on the widow and the General than on the sailor,

who be too sensible of their own peril to find more than a pass

occasion of horror in the scene they have witness. they and Holdsworth

eat a biscuit apiece and drink their allowance of water mix with rum;

but the General turn, with an expression of loathing in his face,

from the food, and Mrs. Tennent could not be induce to eat more than

a few mouthful. both drink of the water.



the wave be still lively enough to demand the utmost care in the

steering of the boat; but Winyard have prove himself a smart steersman,

and Holdsworth, whose hand be cramp and blue with long grasping of

the yoke-line, gladly surrender his place to the sailor.



“strange,” mutter the General, “that we sight no ship!”



“our course be east,” say Holdsworth. “if the wind would haul round a

few point to the west, I’d out reef and bear up.”



the breeze hold until twelve o’clock, when it slacken. the sea having

grow smoother during the morning, Holdsworth haul the sheet of the

sail aft and steer south-east by the compass, which be as close to

the sea as he deem it advisable to sail the boat. the sun now shine

hot overhead, which greatly comfort Mrs. Tennent and revive the

spirit of her boy, who pull a piece of string from his pocket, to

which he fasten a crooked pin, and begin to fish. as the afternoon

advance, the wind gradually die away, and a thin haze settle upon

the southern horizon, portend both heat and calm. the water’s

surface turn to an aspect of polished steel; the boat rise and sink

to the swell, easily and with a soothe motion, and the sail flap

idly against the mast.



during the afternoon, some porpoise come to the surface of the water,

about a stone’s throw from the boat, roll their gleam black

body in a southerly direction.



“they always make for the quarter the wind’s come from,” say Winyard.



“I be afraid we shall have no wind to-night,” answer Holdsworth; “the

weather look too settle.”



they watch the fish turn their solemn somersault until they be

out of sight, and then, as though to meet hope half-way, Holdsworth

swarm up the boat’s mast and sweep the horizon with pierce eye,

but see nothing but the boundless water-line pale away against the

sky.



the sun go down in glorious majesty, burnish the deep, and

dazzle the eye with a splendour of small radiant cloud, pierce with

thread of glory, and momentarily change their brilliant hue until

the orb be under the sea, when they turn a bright red colour. the

twilight follow fast, the star come out, and the darkness of night

fall upon the lonely deep.









CHAPTER IX.



the third day.





as no object could be serve by keep the sail hoist, they haul

it down and spread a portion of it over the widow and her child.

Holdsworth keep watch till ten, and then awaken Winyard, who watch

till twelve; afterwards Johnson watch; and so the three man take

turn and turn about, all through the long and breathless night, until

daybreak, which find Winyard awake in the stern-sheet, watch the

pale dawn break in the east.



its approach at first be imperceptible. a faint gray mingle in

the prevail darkness, and gradually grow more define; the

star languish, and those in the extreme east hide themselves.

then a clear light break stealthily about the eastern horizon;

the sea catch the glimmering dawn, and mirror a pale and sickly

illumination, infinitely vague, as would be the reflection in a

look-glass of a faint light. but soon the lustre broaden, and

streak of horizontal silver float above the deep, and stand in

layer of crystalline clearness, await a more ardent transformation.

then a delicate pink flush a wide space in the eastern sky, which

spread and spread until the farthermost heaven shake off the heavy

curtain of the night and melt into a pale and visionary blue.

Anon, upon the eastern water-line, stand a mere speck of white and

exceedingly brilliant light--a pure silver point of glory--which, as

the eye watch it, increase in size, fling flake upon flake of

glitter icy splendour upon the water, until, as with a sudden bind,

it soar in sun-like shape and flood the heaven and the sea with

strong rejoicing light.



it awake Holdsworth, who start up and stare around he.



“look, sir!” say Winyard, in a whisper that sound fierce with

excitement, point towards the south-east, where, upon the remote

horizon, stand a white speck clearly define by the sunshine.



Holdsworth hollow his hand tube-wise, gaze intently for some

moment, and then cry, “a sail!”



“Becalmed, master, as we be!” shout Winyard; whereat the sleeper in

the boat stir and open their eye.



“out oar, my man!” sing out Holdsworth. “a sail, general! do you see

she? look, Mrs. Tennent; follow the direction of my finger! she be

becalm! she cannot escape we! Hurrah, my lad!”



in less time than it take I to write it, both seaman be bend to

the oar like madman, make the water break in clear line of ripple

against the boat’s bow, which head directly for the distant sail.

language be powerless to describe the excitement of the poor creature.

Mrs. Tennent shed tear; the General, upon whose constitution,

debilitate by old age, their perilous situation and the mental

suffering which accompany it be begin seriously to tell, stand

clutch the mast, his eye rivet upon the white speck, and his

attenuate face flush with eagerness and hope. the man, stretch to

the long oar, look from time to time over their shoulder to remark

the progress they be make, and encourage each other with cheerful

cry.



they have but two oar, and the boat be heavy and move reluctantly to

the pressure of the blade; moreover, the man be weaken by exposure

and want of nourish food. still they urge the boat through the

water at pretty nearly three mile an hour; and Holdsworth repeatedly

encourage they by represent that every stroke of the oar bring

the boat nearer within the range of the vision of those on board the

vessel, and increase the likelihood, under God’s providence, of their

rescue.



when the vessel be first sight, she could not have be less than

ten mile distant; this be make manifest by the circumstance of their

continue to row a full hour before they have expose her large sail,

and even then her hull be invisible. the ocean, meanwhile, remain

perfectly polished, without a shadow anywhere upon its vast bosom to

indicate the passage of wind.



the seaman presently show symptom of distress, Holdsworth take an

oar, and bade Winyard, whom he replace, to drink some rum, and hand a

draught to Johnson. the General beg to take Johnson’s place for a

time while the man rest himself; but the poor old gentleman, after

row a few stroke, find himself utterly unequal to the weight of

the oar, and he return to his seat cover with perspiration, and

breathe with difficulty and pain.



it be seven o’clock by Holdsworth’s watch before the hull of the

vessel grow discernible; and then Johnson, whose sight be very keen,

pronounce she to be a large three-masted brigantine. she have all her

sail set, but she be still so remote as scarcely to be distinguish

by an inexperienced eye from a cloud.



Winyard, who steer, ask Mrs. Tennent for her black shawl, with

which he climb up the mast and make it fast, flagwise. the motion of

the boat hardly create draught enough to unfurl it; but, droop as

it do, it could scarcely fail to serve as a signal. the calm which

would have dishearten they under other circumstance, as suspend

all prospect of a rescue whilst it last, be now deeply welcome

to they as a guarantee of their speedy release from the horror of

their situation. as the vessel grow in dimension under the desperate

exertion of the rower, Mrs. Tennent become hysterical, laugh and

weep at the same moment, and hug her boy passionately to she. the

old General stand up wave his handkerchief, and talk to himself,

even wildly at time.



Holdsworth be now steer, and he bend eager glance in search of

some signal, some flag whose spot of colour would surely be visible

even at that distance, to tell they they be see.



suddenly he cry out:



“Johnson--Winyard! look! tell I what you can see?”



the man rest on their oar simultaneously and turn their head

towards the vessel. a silence ensue, last some moment. then

Johnson exclaim:



“there’s smoke come out of she. don’t you see it, like a blue line

between her fore and main mast?”



“maybe they’re boil the pitch-kettle abaft the galley, as we use

aboard the ‘Mary Ann,’” say Winyard, wipe his forehead with his bare

arm; “let’s make for she, boy.”



and he fall to his oar again.



the water ripple round the boat’s side once more, and the shawl at

the mast-head flutter.



five minute pass; and then Holdsworth, whose eye never wander

from the vessel, see something black pass up her sail, rise over her

mast and there hang. another follow; another yet; volume now, and

each volume denser, blacker than its predecessor.
