“she’s on fire!” he shout; at which the man tilt up their oar and

stand up.



quicker and quicker the black volume, like ball grow in size as

they mount, be vomit up, and resemble an endless series of

balloon rise from the deck; they meet when they reach a short

height above the mast, mingle and form into a livid line which

gradually stretch north and south, but very slowly. the spectator

in the boat be paralyse; but their emotion be too various and

conflicting to permit the deep, deadly one of disappointment and

despair to make themselves feel as yet.



Holdsworth break a long silence by exclaim, “can you see they

put off?”



“no--I see nothing. I reckon she’s abandon hour ago,” answer

Winyard.



the General sink upon his knee with a groan, clutch the gunwale and

stare at the burn vessel over his knuckle. there be no sign of

a boat anywhere--no sign of living creature be on board the doom

craft. the smoke, which appear to have be pen up in the hold,

have now escape on a sudden; and thick and thick yet it mount,

mark an ugly stain upon the pure morning sky, and hang sombre and

menacing over the smoulder vessel like a thunder-cloud. soon a short

tongue of flame protrude; then come another and a long one, which

seem to whiz with a yellow radiance up the rigging and bury itself

in the smoke. then the fire burst out in all direction; in the time

it would take you to count ten, the vessel be a mass of flame, keen,

brilliant, coiling, with stream of thin blue smoke sail out of

each yellow ray, mingle with particle of burn matter that wink

among the heavy cloud like firefly in a dark evening. she be four

or five mile off; a mere toy on the surface of the sea; and yet those

who watch she from the boat could distinctly hear the crackling of

the fire, and the seething of her flame spar as they fall into the

water. Anon the fire flicker, and up drive new volume of smoke,

which pale and thin as the rekindled flame burst forth again and

dart their spear-shape fang into the smoke-hide sky.



for above an hour this terrible and magnificent spectacle last,

during which not a word escape the lip of the inmate of the boat.

their mind seem incapable of understand the extinction of the

hope that have sustain they since sunrise, by a catastrophe so

unexpected, by a horror which unite the extreme of sublimity with

the extreme of misfortune, and which appear scarcely more than a

vision--so unforeseen, so incredible, so illusive, so ghastly, so

terrific be it.



by this time the whole of the upper mast be go, add fuel to the

interior furnace of the hull, and the three low mast be burn

stump. suddenly the blaze mass appear to rise in the air; the

fire go out as if by magic, and an opaque cloud, burnish a livid

blue by the sunshine, float on the water. not for many moment after

the flame have vanish come a concussion that rent the air, loud and

violent as a thunder-clap among mountain. the cloud lift, and where

the vessel have be the sea be a smooth outline, reflect only the

dark shadow of the slowly-mount smoke, and dot here and there

with black remnant of the wreck.



“Mr. Holdsworth,” say the General in a faint voice, sink backwards

against a thwart, “I be die.”



his hand be press to his heart; he be breathe quickly and

convulsively, and his face be bloodless. his exclamation break the

spell that hold the other gaze in the direction of the smoke. they

turn quickly, and Holdsworth jump over to the old man and support

his head on his knee.



“no, no, General; don’t say that. this be a bitter disappointment; but

we believe in God’s goodness. he cannot mean that we should perish.

Johnson, pour some rum into the pannikin. Mrs. Tennent, dip your

handkerchief into the sea and kindly pass it here.”



they put the spirit to the old man’s lip, and he drink a little, but

gasp for breath when he have swallow it and clench his hand. they

spread the wet handkerchief over his forehead and loosen his cravat.



“I----I know not what this giddiness may mean,” the General stammer,

while the lustre fade out of his eye. “if it be death ... I be ready

to meet it. God be merciful and good. his Son be my redeemer ... he

will take I to himself ... how faint! how faint! but I have eat

nothing”...



he cease with a sudden gasp.



“you will feel well presently,” say Holdsworth, while Mrs. Tennent

take the old man’s hand and fan his face. “the shock of the burning

ship have be too great for you. but you will live to recall this time.

you have as manly a heart as ever God bless his creature with. don’t

let it fail you now.”



“I have ... I have strive to do my duty,” murmur the old man, so

faintly that his word be scarcely audible. “I have serve my country

... she be a great empire ... a great empire ... and my heart be with

old England, too;” force a smile, “we should know each other well,

sir, and our prejudice would leave we, for .... for.... see! yonder be

Charleston!” he suddenly exclaim, his eye kindling, and draw his

hand from Mrs. Tennent’s, to point with it into the infinite horizon.

“do you see that house on the left, there, with the green face it?

I be bear there, sir. observe the barberry-bush with the red fruit

on they--just there I fight my cousin, when we be boy ... he’s a

senator now, and they tell I a good speaker. oh, how the time go!”

he sigh wearily. “but there’s my wife ... she be hold the little

one by the hand, and nod to I to attend she.... a moment, Sarah,

a moment! gentleman, farewell. I beg your kind word in my favour among

your countryman, whom I honour. I be a plain american gentleman--a

general, gentleman ... but tell they that my sword be never draw from

its scabbard for any cause but a good one, and ... ah, farewell! you

see, gentleman, my wife await I, and the little one beckon.”



he make a gesture as though he would bow; his venerable and honour

head sink upon his bosom; then he start, look about he with a

glazing eye, and smile sweetly, whisper, “Sarah, I be come,” lay

back and speak no more.



when, after long watch he, they know that he be dead, they cover

he over with the sail, mean to commit he to the sea when the widow

should be asleep.



Holdsworth be so greatly overcome that for many minute he could not

raise his head nor speak. the widow, with her eye fix on the water,

sit motionless, a fix image of despair. her boy creep about the

bottom of the boat at her foot, with somewhat weakly movement, though

his body have not yet suffer enough to kill the infancy in his mind.

the sailor, make selfish by the bitter disappointment of the morning,

talk of their chance of rescue, and discuss the subject of the

burn ship. Johnson probably solve the mystery of the deserted

vessel when he suggest that during the night the hand have find the

cargo on fire--and he judge by the blaze she make, and the smoke, and

the long time she be smoulder, that she be freight with cotton

or coal--and have batten down the hatch; but not have the mean of

get the fire under, they strike and take to the boat, oblige the

skipper to go along with they, and leave the vessel to her fate. just

such another case happen to a messmate of his in the Bay of Biscay.

the crew leave their ship smoulder under batten hatch. but _she_

be board by a Frenchman, who smother the fire and tow she into

Bordeaux.



“where be the brigantine’s crew, now?” Winyard wonder.



“I wish we could fall in with they, if only for company’s sake,”

reply Johnson.



but of that there be very little chance.



all the afternoon the calm last, with light mist hang in wreath

upon the horizon. but about the hour of sunset the smoke that have rise

from the burn ship, and which have not drift more than a couple of

mile to the southward throughout the day, came sail slowly towards

the boat and pass high overhead, thin its bulk as it travel

in an easterly direction. a light breeze herald it; they hoist the

sail, put the boat’s head round, and stand east-south-east.



the night fall, but the light breeze hold steady. when they think

Mrs. Tennent be asleep, they raise the body of the General in their

arm from the bottom of the boat. the night be lustrous with yellow

star, which diffuse sufficient light to enable they to see the old

man’s face. the eye be closed, and, though the under-jaw be fall,

there yet linger an expression both of firmness and sweetness about

the mouth. the draught under the sail move his white hair.



“Mates,” say Holdsworth in a whisper, “we pray that God have take

this noble gentleman’s soul to himself, and that, though his body be

disperse in the sea, it will rise again at the Day of Judgment, in

the shape we now behold it, to become a partaker of life everlaste,

through Jesus Christ our Lord.”



the two sailor answer amen!



all three of they then tenderly hand the body, over the boat’s side,

and let it gently slip into the water. the white hair glimmer for

a brief moment on the dark surface, and then the body sink or be

swallow up in the gloom; and the boat ripple onwards, cut the

star flake in the sea with her stem, and leave they glitter in

silver fragment in her wake.









CHAPTER X.



the fourth day.





a fourth day break, and find the boat almost becalm again. the

intense tedium of their captivity cannot be express by word. the

eternal iteration of the water-line become a torment and a pang, and

force they to look into the boat or upon one another for relief of the

strained and weary eye. their limb be cramp for want of space to

stretch themselves. holdsworth’s cheek be sink, and the hollow of

his eye dark; and a black beard and moustache, sprout upon his chin

and lip, give he a gaunt and grizly look. the man sit with rounded

back and hopeless eye, fix downward, and sinewy hand clasp upon

their knee.



but the effect of the suffering, bodily and mental, they be

endure, be most visible in the widow, whose face be scarcely

recognisable for the waste, aged, pallid, and heart-break aspect that

it present.



when the little boy awake he begin to cry and complain of pain in his

limb and back. his mother seem too weak to support or even soothe

he with speech. Holdsworth take he upon his knee and talk to he

cheerfully, that he might inspirit the other as well as himself.



“Louis, you be a little man; you must not cry, because it grieve your

poor mamma, who cannot bear to see your tear. your back ache because

your bed have be a hard one; but you willn’t have that uncomfortable

bed long. don’t you remember what the poor old General say: that God,

whose eye be everywhere, see we, and will pity we, and send a ship

to our rescue if we will but have patience, and not murmur against

Him. many vessel have be wreck as well as the ‘Meteor,’ and their

crew take to the boat, and rescue by pass ship, after they have

suffer more anguish and misery than we can dream of. the fortune that

befell they may befall we. we must put our whole trust in God, and

watch the horizon narrowly. this be but our fourth day, and the very

breeze that be now blow may be gradually bear we towards a ship.

so no more tear, my man. here be a biscuit for you. give this one to

your mamma. here, Johnson--Winyard.”



he hand the man a biscuit apiece, and bade Johnson serve out the

water.



there be three keg in the boat, as state elsewhere. they have

calculate that by allow each person half a pannikin of water a

day, their stock would last they ten day. but now there be two

mouth less, and they might hope to make the water serve they for as

long as thirteen day. it would seem, however, that, in spite of the

injunction of Captain Steel, the boat have be provision hurriedly.

of biscuit, Holdsworth have an abundance; but nothing but negligence

or haste could account for the absence of other provision, such as

rice, flour, beef and pork, dry pea, and such fare; unless, indeed,

it be consider that none of these thing would be eatable unless

cook. though Holdsworth’s boat might not have fare the bad, it be

manifest that the quantity of water that have be put into she be out

of proportion with the biscuit that fill the locker. they have use

the water in one of the large keg first, and Johnson, in measure

out the allowance, find that scarcely enough remain to fill the

pannikin by a quarter. Holdsworth tell he to pull the bung out of

one of the other keg, and when the little boy, who be first serve,

have empty the pannikin, the next draught be hand to the widow.

she raise it to her lip eagerly, her mouth be feverish; but have

scarcely sip it, when she put it down, exclaim that the water be

salt.



“impossible!” cry Holdsworth quickly, and taste the water.



the widow be right. the water be not indeed salt, but so brackish as

to be quite unfit to drink.



he spit it out at once, his instinct caution he that he would

increase his thirst by swallow it, and look blankly at the man.



“what! _be_ it salt?” exclaim Winyard furiously.



“try the other keg,” say Holdsworth, throw the content of the

pannikin away.



Johnson draw some of the water and taste it, but also spit it out, as

Holdsworth have do.



“be that salt too?” shriek Holdsworth.



“try it!” answer Johnson grimly, come aft with the pannikin.



that, too, like the other, be brackish and unfit to be drunk.



“Great God!” exclaim Holdsworth, clasp his hand convulsively;

“how could this have happen?”



“it be the steward as fill these keg,” say Winyard. “I see he

myself pump out o’ the starboard water cask, which the sea be

wash over when the mast go, and drain the salt water in.” he

add fiercely, “I’ll lay he take care to fill the keg of the boat he

belong to with the right kind!”



“hand that pannikin here,” say Holdsworth; and mix some rum with the

water and taste it, but the dose be indescribably nauseous.



this discovery be a frightful blow; so overwhelming that it take their

mind some minute to realise it in its full extent.



they be now absolutely without a drop of fresh water in the boat;

which fact be make the more terrible by the consideration that, up

to the moment of discovery, they have believe themselves stock with

sufficient water to last they for another week at the very least.



they be appal and subdue to image of stone by this last and

bad addition to the series of heavy misfortune that have befall

they.



then Winyard, who be already torment with thirst, for they have

permit themselves to drink no water during the night, begin to

blaspheme, roll his eye wildly and call curse on the head of

the steward for his murderous negligence. he terrify the boy into a

passion of tear, which increase his fury, and he stand up and menace

the child with his outstretched fist.



“sit down!” exclaim Holdsworth, in a voice that fall like a blow upon

the ear. “you be go mad some day too soon, you lubber! do you hear

I? sit down!”



the man scowl at he, and then throw himself backwards into the bow

of the boat.



“will your shriek and oath give we water?” Holdsworth continue

bitterly. “you be not more thirsty than I, nor this poor lady, from

whom you have not hear one syllable of complaint since she be hand

into the boat!”



he turn to she with a look of deep compassion.



“try to sustain your courage under this awful trial,” he exclaim.

“our position be not yet hopeless. there be no sea more largely

navigate than the Atlantic, by vessel bind to all part of the

world, and I say it be almost inevitable that we should fall in with a

ship soon.”



she force a wan smile for answer, but do not speak; merely put she

hand on her child’s shoulder and draw he to she.



as the morning advance the heat of the sun increase, and the ray

seem to absorb the light breeze out of the atmosphere; the sea turn

glassy, and by noon the boat be becalm. meanwhile, Winyard remained

doggedly bury in the bow of the boat, suck his dry lip, with

despair legibly write upon his countenance. Johnson appear to find

relief by plunge his arm in the water and moisten his head and

face. the very boat take a white, baked, thirsty aspect; and the heat

make the paint upon her exhale in a faint and sickly smell.



when the afternoon be wane, Winyard get up and creep stealthily to

the after-part of the boat. Holdsworth keep his eye steadily upon he.

his intention, however, be no more than to take up the pannikin, which

he snatch at hastily, as though fear that his purpose would be

frustrated. he then hasten forward and fill the vessel from one of

the keg.



“don’t drink it!” exclaim Holdsworth; “it will increase your thirst.”



but the man, point to his throat, swallow the briny draught

hastily, then put the pannikin down with a sigh of relief and with a

face clear of something of its peculiar expression of pain.



Johnson seize the pannikin, mean to follow Winyard’s example.

Holdsworth entreat he to desist. “the salt will madden you!” he

exclaim. he have scarcely say this, when Winyard begin to roll his

body about, uttering short, sharp cry.



immediately afterwards he vomit, his face turn slate colour, and

they think he would expire. Holdsworth drain some rum into his

mouth, and pour sea-water from the pannikin in long stream over

his head. this somewhat revive he; but he lie groan and cursing,

and clutch at the side of the boat with his finger-nail for many

minute.



his suffering frighten Johnson, who call out:



“Master, if the water in the keg be poison, we should let it run away.”



“it be bad than poison,” reply Holdsworth. “pull out the bung--the

sea-water around we be as wholesome to drink as that stuff.”



Johnson then turn the keg over and let they drain themselves empty.



after this a silence fall upon the boat which last a full hour, when

the boy say:



“Mamma, I be thirsty. give I something to drink.”



it be shocking to hear the child’s complaint, and feel the

_impossibility_ of satisfy he. the mother start up with a wild

gesture, and cry in a fierce whisper, that be thicken in its

passage through her swollen throat:



“o my God! let we both die! end our misery now.”



Holdsworth watch she mutely.



her appeal die away, and she sink back, exhaust by the sudden

outbreak.



the sun go down and some cloud come up behind the horizon to receive

the glow disc. these spread themselves slowly over the heaven,

albeit the sea remain breathlessly calm; and think that the wind

be come up that way, the poor sufferer turn their eye wildly and

eagerly towards the west, hope with a desolate hope for the vessel

that be to rescue they, but which no day bring.



when the night fall, Winyard begin to sing in a strange husky voice;

but his tone soon die out, and then come the small weak cry of,

“Mamma, I be thirsty! give I some water!” from the little boy,

wound the ear with an edge of agony in the stillness and the gloom.



presently a soft sigh of wind come from the west, which back the

sail. holdsworth put the boat’s head round until the sail fill, and

then haul the sheet aft, mean to lay close to the wind, that they

might soon encounter the ship that the wind be to bring. the air

sink into a calm again; but another puff follow which make the water

gurgle, and it be plain that a breeze be come, by the cloud which

be drift eastward. the wind freshen and then become steady, and

the boat, bend to the weight of the full sail, stir the water

into fire, which flash and vanish in her wake.



it matter little which way Holdsworth steer the boat; but let he

head she as he would, there be always the haunt sense upon he that

he be speed away from the ship that would rescue they; that by

pointing yonder, or yonder, or yonder, a vessel would be encounter.

the breeze and the movement of the boat revive Winyard, who loll

over on the lee side, find relief in let his hand trail through

the water. the boy have cease his complaint, and lie sleep along

the thwart, with his head on his mother’s knee. Johnson also sleep.



the thirst that have torment Holdsworth during the afternoon have now

in some measure abate. there be four or five bottle of rum still

leave in the stern locker, and, hope to hit upon some mean to deal

with the suffering with which they be threaten by the absence of

water, he soak a piece of biscuit in the spirit and taste it. but he

at once perceive that no relief be to be obtain by this expedient,

but that, on the contrary, the spirit would irritate the throat and

increase the dryness. he throw the piece of biscuit away, and begin to

think over all the story he have ever hear of man who have suffer

from thirst in boat at sea, that he might recollect any one way they

adopt for diminish their torment. he have be shipmate with a

man, in one of his early voyage, who, together with three other

man, have be miraculously rescue by a vessel, after they have be

at sea in an open boat exactly twenty-one day, during which they have

drift above seven hundred mile from the spot at which their ship have

go down. holdsworth could only remember two of the expedient they

resort to when madden with hunger and thirst: one be, tear off

piece of their shirt and chew they; the other, cut wound in

their arm and suck the blood. this last be a remedy from which he

recoil with horror; nor be his suffering so great just then as to

tempt he to try the other.



“Master!” call out Winyard, in a husky voice, “what longitude do you

reckon we’re in?”



“we be in twenty-eight west when the ship go down, and I doubt if

we be many mile distant from the same place.”



“ain’t there no chance of our sight a ship, master?”



“yes, every chance.”



“I reckon the skipper have run the long-boat into the regular track by

this time,” grumble the man; “it’s curse hard upon we that we should

be leave to die here like dog.”



to this Holdsworth make no answer, and Winyard, after mutter awhile

to himself, begin to splash the water in his face by scoop it out

with his hand. then Johnson, in his sleep, call out for something to

drink, on which Winyard, with an oath, answer, “ay, you may call out!

if call ’ud bring it, I’d make noise enough, I’ll lay!”



the cloud overhead, though widely sunder one from another, be

heavy, and Holdsworth constantly direct his weary eye at they,

pray for a shower of rain. at midnight, or thereabout, Johnson be

awaken, and come aft to relieve Holdsworth at the helm. the two man

whisper together about Winyard, say that he be not to be trust

with the management of the boat whilst the breeze hold; and it be

agree that Holdsworth should replace Johnson at the expiration of

two hour, by the watch, which Johnson take and put in his pocket. but

before lie down, Holdsworth dip the sail and put the boat around.

her head on the port tack be north-west and by north.



“keep a sharp look-out to windward, Johnson, and call I at once if you

sight anything,” say Holdsworth; then pack himself against the mast

and fall into a doze.



when he be asleep, Winyard come out of the bow, and step to the

stern-sheet and begin to talk to Johnson. after awhile, he say he

should like to see what quantity of biscuit they still have, and lift

the seat over the locker. Johnson, who suspect nothing, have his eye

fix on the weather horizon; and Winyard, snatch at a bottle of

rum, thrust it cunningly into his bosom, and hurry forward.



all this time the boy be sleep; but it be impossible to tell

whether his mother slumber or not. she never once stir. she sit on

the weather side, close against Johnson. her child’s head be upon she

knee, and her hand be clasp upon his shoulder. she keep her face

bow, her chin upon her breast.



at two o’clock by the watch, Johnson call Holdsworth, who instantly

sit upright, and before rise, bent his head under the foot of the

sail to take a look to leeward. he have scarcely do this when he

utter a cry, and then fall dumb, point like a madman. Johnson

lean sideways, and see the outline of a large ship, about a mile

distant, run with the wind free on her starboard quarter.



“put your helm up! head for she!” gasp Holdsworth, spring aft;

and then as the boat sweep round, he jump on to a thwart, and

hollow his hand, shout, but his shout be feeble and hoarse; the

constrict throat dull and choke his voice. Johnson also shout,

but his voice be even weak than Holdsworth’s.



“they will not leave we! they will not leave we!” shriek Mrs.

Tennent, rise suddenly and extend her hand towards the ship,

which the movement of the boat’s rudder have bring on the starboard

beam.



as she cry, Winyard stand up in the boat’s bow, reel wildly,

and mad with the drink he have abstract. his gesture and fury be

horrible to witness. his husky screech sound as the voice of one

suffer indescribable torment. he brandish his arm towards the

ship, which be draw ahead rapidly, and in his drunken excitement

leap upon the gunwale of the boat, where he stand balance himself,

and toss his clench fist above his head. just then the boat

dip and sink into the hollow of a swell; the drunken madman make a

grab at the leach of the sail to steady himself--miss it--and go

head backwards overboard.



Holdsworth bound aft to catch he as he float past; but he remain

under water until the boat be some yard ahead; and then they could

hear his bubble cry and the splashing of his arm.



Holdsworth’s first instinct be to bring the boat round; but Johnson

divine his intention, and twirl the yoke-line furiously around his

hand, cry:



“no! no! we cann’t save he! he’ll have sink before we can reach he!

let’s follow the ship--she may see we!” and he bawl “ahoy! ahoy!” but

his hoarse voice faint in his throat.



Holdsworth grasp one of the yoke-line, and there be a short

struggle. the boat’s head yaw wildly. but by this time nothing be to

be hear astern but the wash of the water as the boat suck it into

eddy.



Holdsworth let go the yoke-line, spring forward and dip the sail

clear of the mast; cry that there be four life to be save, and

it would be as bad as murder to stop the boat now.



the ship be distinctly visible on the port bow, every sail on her

stand in a clean black outline against the sky. she show no

light, and far than that she be a full-rig ship, it be

impossible to tell what she resemble. they watch she with wild

despair, utterly powerless to attract her attention, and dependent upon

the faint possibility of their glimmering sail be distinguishable

on the black surface of the water. if the wind would only lull now,

if such a calm as that which have hold they motionless the day before

would fall, their rescue be inevitable. but the light breeze remain

steady, and the ship ahead slip forward nimbly, and become soon a

square shadow against the wink star over the horizon.



how horrible to be abandon for lack of mean to make their presence

know! any kind of light would have serve they.



the widow moan and beat her breast as the vessel fade into the

darkness; Johnson fling himself doggedly down, and sit rest his

elbow on his knee, gnaw his finger-nail; whilst Holdsworth stand

upright forward, gaze with wild, passionate, intense despair, in the

direction of the ship long after she have vanish.



there could be little doubt that, have Johnson keep a proper look-out,

he would have see the ship in time to put his helm up, and run within

easy hail of she. Holdsworth know this, but would not increase the

misery of their situation by useless reproach.



the child, who have be awaken by their cry, now that silence have

fall begin to ask eagerly and importunately for water, and even

reproach his mother for not attend to he.



“I be hot--hot!” he petition. “Mamma, give I water.”



once during his appeal she start up and glare about she, as if

there _must_ be some mean of relieve his suffering; and then

cry, “I shall go mad!” fall back with a low, heart-break sob,

and speak no more, though the child persist in his entreaty for

a long while. finally he burst into tear, and after pluck at his

throat for a time, sink into an uneasy slumber, in which he utter low

moaning cry repeatedly.



a stupor now fall upon Holdsworth--a species of drowsy indifference

to his fate and to the fate of his companion. he have fall wearily

upon a thwart and sit with his back against the mast, and vision begin

to float before he, and his whole physical being seem lap into

a dreamy insensibility, that subdue, whilst it last, that subtle

agonising craving for water which, since he be awaken from his

sleep, have torment he with a pang more exquisite than any other form

of human suffering. he fight with the dangerous listlessness for some

time, terrify at without understand its import; but in spite of

he, his mind wander, and he presently think that dolly be at his

side; whereupon he address she, and seem to receive her answer,

and ask her question in a low, strange voice, often smile as

though the light of her eye be upon his face and his arm around she.



his language be audible and intelligible; but Johnson, with one of the

yoke-line over his knee, his head support in his hand, pay no

more heed to he than to the flapping of the sail as the boat sometimes

broach to; which insensibility be as shocking as the other’s

delirious chattering.









CHAPTER XI.



the FIFTH DAY.





in this way the boat drift on until the dawn break, when the wind

fall.



Johnson lift his head and look about he; and first for the ship

that have pass they in the night--but she be nowhere visible; then

at Holdsworth, whose delirium have yield to sleep, and who slumber

with his foot on the thwart, his back arch like a bow, and his head

between his knee; then at the widow, who droop against the boat’s

side, her arm over the gunwale, and her hand in the water.



the boy be wide awake, recline in the bottom of the boat, with his

head against his mother’s dress. his eye look large and glassy,

his lip white, and his skin dusky-hue. when he meet Johnson’s gaze,

he smile as though he would coax he to give he what he want, and

try to speak, but his lip rather form than deliver the word

“water.” the man stare at he with the insensibility of despair in

his eye: and the boy, think that he have not hear he, and that he

would get what he desire, if he could but articulate his wish, try

to stand up, mean to draw close to the man’s ear; but his leg sink

under he, and so he remain at the bottom of the boat smile wanly

and point to his throat, as though such dumb-show must need soften

Johnson’s heart, and obtain some water for he.



Holdsworth awake with a start and try to speak; but the roof of his

mouth be dry, and his tongue feel rusty like a cat’s; moreover, his

throat burn, and the sound he utter scathed and lacerate he.



the boy, see he awake, turn to he as a friend who would

relieve he: and moan his distress. the spectacle of his agony, and

his own suffering, madden Holdsworth. all along he have dread

the temptation of the rum, the fiery quality of which, whilst it

momentarily allay, would, he be sure, aggravate tenfold the craving

for water. but suffer master he now. he seize the pannikin and

pour out some of the liquor, put it to the boy’s dry lip. he drink

greedily, but the ardent spirit check his breath, and he struggle

wildly, beat the air with his little hand.



but meanwhile Holdsworth have also drunk, and hand the remainder of

the draught to Johnson, his throat soften, and his tongue capable now

of articulation. Johnson draw a deep breath, and exclaim:



“thank God for that, master. I should have take it before have I

think it good for I.”



Holdsworth give the boy a biscuit, which he grab at, and thrust

large piece into his mouth, as though seek to extinguish the fire

that the rum have kindle.



when the pain of the burn spirit have pass, he say, “give mamma

some. when you be asleep, Mr. Holdsworth, I hear she call for

water.”



Holdsworth, think that she sleep, would not arouse she; but notice

that her arm hang awkwardly over the boat’s side, and leave the

half-close finger trail in the water, he raise it gently to place

her hand on her lap. in do this, he observe a lifelessness in her

arm such as sleep could not induce. he peer into her face, and cry

out quickly:



“o my God!”



then bade Johnson move, that he might get beside she, and reverently

lift her head.



there be no need to glance twice at her face to know what have

happen, although the heart-break expression in it would almost

suggest that she sleep, and be dream a painful dream. her eye be

half-closed, her under-jaw have drop, yet she look even in her

death a sweet, long-suffer woman.



“give she something to drink,” plead the little boy, passionately,

imagine from her silence, and the expression on her face, that she

be suffer as he have and could not speak.



“she don’t want it--she’s dead!” answer Johnson.



Holdsworth half turn, but check the exclamation that rise to his

lip, feel that the bitter truth must be make known to the child

soon or later.



the boy do not understand the answer; he crawl upon his mother’s

knee, with the pannikin in his hand, which he hold out whilst he say:

“wake up, mamma! open your eye! Mr. Holdsworth will give you something

to drink.”



Holdsworth remove the child, and seat he near the mast, and bade

he stop there. he then return, and lift the poor woman from her

seat, place she gently in the bottom of the boat, throw her dress

over her face to hide the anguish in it, and blot out the mockery of

the daylight.



the boy begin to cry, and ask Mr. Holdsworth to wake his mamma up.



neither of the man could answer he.



shortly after this, the wind veer round to the north, and come on to

blow in quick, fretful puff. the sky grow cloudy, and indication be

not want of the approach of a gale. Holdsworth take the helm, whilst

Johnson lower the sail and close-reef it, and the quick jump of the

sea, couple with the small space of sail show, make it impossible

for they to head for the east, without drive bodily to leeward, they

slacken out the sheet and let the boat run, keep the wind about

two point on her port quarter.



a squall of rain come up and wet they. they turn their mouth in

the direction whence it come, and gape to receive the delicious drop;

but it blow against their face and slantwise along the sea, and be

soon over. it leave a little pool on one of the thwart, and Holdsworth

tell the boy to put his lip to it. he do so, and lap the moisture

like a dog; whilst Holdsworth and Johnson remove the handkerchief

from their neck which the rain have damp, and suck they.



the wind increase, the sea become heavy, and the heaven overcast

with a vast extent of lead-colour cloud that stretch from horizon

to horizon. at noon, when the boat be on the summit of a wave,

Johnson catch sight of a vessel on their lee quarter. the boat

plunge downwards, and the vessel be lose to view; but, on mount

again, they behold the vessel, under double-reefed top-sail, stand

westward right across their stern. she be not above a mile and a half

away, but she might as well have be a thousand, for the boat could no

more have make for she in that sea than she could have sail in the

wind’s eye. there be a faint chance that the people on board of she

might catch sight of the shawl that stream like a black flag from

the mast-head; and, each time the boat sink into a hollow, the poor

man wait with wild and dreadful eagerness for she to rise, that they

might observe whether the vessel have see and be follow they. but

she do not alter her course, and in ten minute’ time vanish in the

haze.



neither of the man speak: Johnson, by the expression on his face,

appear to have resign himself to despair, and all Holdsworth’s

thought be concentrate in keep the boat clear of the sea which

boil around she. he be very weak; so much so that there be moment

when, a sea catch the boat under the stern, he have scarcely the

power to keep the yoke square and prevent the rudder from be jam

athwart-ship upon its pintle by the pressure of the water; which, have

it happen, would have sweep the boat broadside on and fill she.



add to this, the torment of thirst be again upon he. he keep the

end of his handkerchief in his mouth, literally chew it to pulp, and

constantly direct thirsty glance at the cloud, and pray for

another shower of rain.



his own suffering make he perceive that the rum would be a curse

to they whilst it last, induce they to drink it, and presently

madden they with fresh access of thirst. the boy be suffer

again, and be crawl upon his hand and knee over the thwart in

search of some rain moisture; and presently Holdsworth see he put

his tongue against the mast and lick it. Johnson hung, with an air of

despairful recklessness, over the boat’s side, dash the water in

his face, and let the foam fly up his arm and soak his breast.



the boat presently make a plunge downwards--a long, wild, sweeping

fall; the roaring of the wave sound overhead; the sail flap, and

there be a pause of breathless calm that last some moment.



Holdsworth look behind he and shriek out: “seize the boy, and

throw yourself down!”



the man extend his hand, and, grapple the child, roll backwards,

under a thwart.



it come--a huge, green, unbroken sea, arch its emerald top on a

level with the yard of the sail, and follow the boat with a spring

like a tiger’s. Holdsworth stretch himself out, his foot hard against

the aftermost thwart, his back square, his elbow out, his hand

grasp the yoke-line with a death grip.



up go the boat--stem up--yet up! as though she must be fling clean

over on end; then come the rush and roar of water--it fall with a

weight of lead on Holdsworth’s back, and beat, with a ponderous single

blow, the breath out of he, but could not root he from his seat; it

break into a vast surface of foam, divided, and sweep forward, hiss,

splutter, bubble, rage; meet at an angle at the boat’s bow and

half fill she! down she swoop into another hollow, and half the

water run out over her bow, the remainder, as she rise, come rush

aft and fill the stern-sheet; and up and down, up and down, it

wash.



but the boat still live, and Holdsworth be her master.



“bear a hand aft here and bale she out!” he shout.



Johnson let go the half-drown child, and struggle over the thwart,

blow and shake his soaked hair like rope-yarn off his face, his

clothe stream with water; flop down, find the cocoa-nut shell,

and bale with fury.



the child crouch in the bow, too terrify to cry.



the boat flash along, skim the froth head of the wave; she

have outlive an exceptionally heavy sea, and seem to feel her triumph

as she fly.



but, oh! the ghastly burden that she bear! the dead and dripping woman,

off whose face the water have wash the covering, and leave it naked

to the daylight; the gaunt bearded spectre bale out the boat on his

knee, his wet clothe cling to his frame like a skin of silk, and

disclose the piteous attenuation of the body; the steersman with wild

and lustrous eye sink deep in livid socket, the yoke-line writhe

around his lean brown hand, his lip pale and crack, and his long

neglect hair hang like a wet mat over his forehead and down his

back; and the shiver little figure in the bow, his hand squeeze

together in an attitude of prayer, and his small face glimmer with

unearthly ghastliness upon the gray background of the boat’s interior.



some fly-fish leap out of the sea close to the boat, and bury

their silver arrow-like shape in a wave some distance ahead. then

the sun break through a rent in the broad sombre cloud, and make the

pelting ocean joyous with a snatch of cheerful light. but the strong

wind last all the afternoon, and when it lull just before sunset,

Holdsworth be so exhausted, that in rise to give his seat to

Johnson, he reel and sink in a heap close beside the corpse at the

bottom of the boat, and lie motionless and insensible. Johnson make

no effort to restore he. indeed, he think he be dead. his own

brain whirl; his tongue seem to fill his mouth; there creep over

he such a stupor as have visit holdsworth; he let the yoke-line go,

and fix his eye on the sea, prepare to meet the death which his

sensation lead he to believe be at hand.



the boat, toss like a cork on the troubled water, broach to; but

happily the wind be momentarily die away; her head come round to the

sea and she ride with as much safety as if Holdsworth be at the helm.



for a whole hour the interior of the boat present the same scene;

the man motionless as the dead body, the boy squat in the bow

with nothing seemingly alive about he but his eye, which wink

as he roll they seaward, where the sun shine on the water. then

Holdsworth begin to groan and stir; whereupon Johnson fix his dull

eye upon he, and watch he without any curiosity, without any

sympathy, without any interest--indeed, scarcely, I might say, with

human intelligence.



the boy, see Holdsworth move, come creep aft and remain on his

knee, first look at the man awake to consciousness, and then at

his mother, whose motionlessness and drown aspect, and face make

unfamiliar to he by its total want of expression, terrify he.



Holdsworth raise his head and look about he in bewilderment.



“where have I be? what have happen?” he cry.



he fix his eye on the dead woman, his glance revert to the boy,

and then consciousness fully awake. he rise wearily to his foot,

and sink with a heavy sigh near Johnson, at whom he look, scarcely

know whether the man sleep or be dead.



the boy beg for water.



“Water!” exclaim Holdsworth in a choking voice; “there be none.”



but there be biscuit, and he turn to the locker to give he one,

think that the food might relieve the child’s thirst. he stretch

out his arm to lift the seat of the locker and find the locker fill

with salt water. with a cry of despair he drag out a bag streaming

with wet, and thrust his hand into it find its content soak into

pulp. the other bag be in the same condition; and to make matter

bad, of the three bottle of rum that have be in the locker, one

only be leave; the other two be crack and empty.



it be easy to understand how this have happen. a sea break over

the boat’s stern could not have fill the locker; the water which

the boat have ship over her bow have come rush aft when the boat

mount the next wave, and fill the stern-sheet, raise the seat

that form the lid of the locker, and pour over the biscuit, at the

same time force the bottle against each other and break they.



“do you see what have happen?” exclaim Holdsworth, grasp

Johnson’s arm. the man look over his shoulder, shake his head, and

mutter, “we’re doom to die. there’s no hope, master.”



what be to be do? Holdsworth think that if the biscuit could be

dry in the sun they might be fit to eat, and endeavour to spread

some of they along the thwart; but the stuff squeeze up in his hand

like thick paste. he taste a little and find it no well than salt,

and he fling the bag down with a groan that seem to express the

extinction of his last faint hope.



but there be a bottle of rum left. he prise out the cork with the

blade of his knife, and give a spoonful to the boy dilute with two or

three drop of sea water. he then set the pannikin to Johnson’s lip,

who suck the hard metal rim as a baby might. finally moisten his

own throat with a small quantity of the liquor, he carefully cork the

bottle and stow it away.



no! to say that hope have entirely abandon he would not be true.

whilst the heart continue to pulsate hope will still be find to live,

however faintly, in its throb, though each moment be heavy with pain,

and nothing seem sure but anguish and death.



the wind have die away, but the boat rise and sink to the long and

heavy swell that billow the gleam surface of the sea to the

horizon. far away in the south be an expanse of gray cloud with

slant line radiate to the sea from it, and a bright square of

rainbow embed in its shadow. it be travel eastward, and

the rain would not touch the boat. elsewhere the sky be a bright

blue, with here and there cloud of glorious whiteness and majestic

bulk--mountain with shine defile and a splendour of sunshine in

their skirt--hang their swell form over the sea. the sun be

hot, but then, ever since they have be in the boat, they have be

steer more or less south, and, take the parallel in which the

ship have founder as a starting-point, every degree the boat make

southward would furnish an appreciable change of temperature.



the rum have work beneficially in Johnson, who now begin to stretch

his body and look about he.



“another calm, master,” he say, in a voice to which the dryness of his

throat impart a harsh unnatural tone. “I think I be dead and go

just now. God help we! I don’t think none of we three’ll live to talk

of this here time!”



“we must put that poor body overboard,” say Holdsworth. “it ben’t fit

that her child should see she like that. will you take he for’ard and

stand between he and I, so that he cann’t see what I’m do, and talk

to he a bit? I almost wish they have both die together. the sight of

his suffering make mine more than I can bear.”



he stifle a sob, and Johnson get up languidly and hold on to

the gunwale of the boat with one hand, take the boy by the arm and lead

he into the bow.



Holdsworth slacken off the halliard to lower the sail and screen

the after part of the boat from the boy’s sight. he then, with what

strength he have, and as quickly as he could, raise the dead body and

let it slip over the stern, mutter a simple prayer as he do so, that

God would let she meet her child in heaven, where they would never more

be part; and then turn his back upon the water and hide his face in

his hand.



at the end of five minute he steal a glance astern--the body have

disappear.



“Four,” he mutter, “and three more to go! o God, what work--what work

this have be!”



his thought go to dolly. if he die, what would become of she? not

for many day yet, even suppose the other boat should make their

way to land or be rescue by a pass ship, would the news of the

“Meteor’s” loss reach she; and he think of her pray night and

morning for he, strain her fond eye into the dim future, where

the come summer be, with all its flower and its sunshine; where

the happy day be that should bring he to she. if the news of the

shipwreck ever reach she, how would her gentle spirit support the

blow! but worse would it be if she remain ignorant of her loss;

because in that case she would live on in hope for month and month,

waken every morning with the idea that--to-day he may come! to-day

he may come! until hope sicken and despair should bring cruel

assurance of eternal separation--the more unendurable because she

should not know _why_ he do not come--whether he be live or

dead--whether he be true or false to she!



oh for the power of give peace to that manly unoffending heart!



we shed tear, and well we may, God know, over the privation of

shipwreck man, over the hunger and thirst and the mortal bodily

agony of poor soul doom to die a linger and shock death

in open boat, storm-toss, or bake in breathless calm under the

burn eye of the sun; but do we think of that deep misery of

theirs--that poignant mental torture compare to which the suffering

of the flesh be as naught--the thought of those they shall see no

more--of wife, and sister, and mother, and little child, many of

whom may perchance never hear the story of their fate, and can have

no tear for the famine and the thirst that waste the flesh off their

skin and submit they to great torture than the heart can bear to

think of?



Holdsworth have believe that the suffering of the boy would engross

all his thought in himself, and that though he might miss he would not

cry for his mother. but he be deceive; for, no soon have the little

fellow discover that she be go from her place in the bottom of the

boat, than he utter a sharp cry, and ask Holdsworth where his mamma

be.



Holdsworth take he upon his knee, but could not answer. the child

persist in his inquiry, look the while suspiciously and eagerly

about he, particularly over the stern, where he have remember see

the actor disappear.



“she be go to God,” Holdsworth say at last. “my little man, you will

meet she again.”



“to God!” cry the child. “that’s where papa be!”



he look up with startled eye at the sky, and then sob

passionately, “have she leave I alone? have she leave I alone?”



“no, she have leave you to I. be a good boy now, and don’t cry, and I

will take care of you, and love you dearly.”



his word smite he as the idle mockery; but apart from his mental

suffering, the mere effort of raise his voice pain he intensely.

he put the child down, force a smile which seem no well than a

grin of pain upon his emaciated face, and then stand up to sweep the

horizon, but soon sink down again with a sound as of a clanging of

bell in his ear, and his throat constrict and burn with a dry,

feverish heat, the pain of which be exquisite.



he be now sensible that his memory be go, for in try to think

of the child’s name he find that he could not recall it. but this,

somehow, give he no concern, for his whole physical being be in

perfect accord with such lapse of the intellectual faculty, and the

discovery breed not the light movement of surprise or apprehension in

his mind.



at noon, Johnson ask for more rum, and Holdsworth measure out a

small quantity for the three of they, dilute the draught, as he have

before do, with a few drop of salt water.



the boy never move from the seat where he have be place by

Holdsworth; he know not, in reality, where his mother have go, but

there be plainly a suspicion in he that she be in the sea, and he

keep his eye fix on the water, as though in expectation of she

rise at the side of the boat. he shed no more tear; indeed, physical

weakness have so far conquer he, that it have render he incapable

of tear. the sight of his white, young, piteous face, his head move

on his shoulder in convulsive jerk, and his helpless down-hang

arm, be enough to make one pray to God that death might remove he

speedily, if the term of horrible misery be not to be end at once.



the afternoon pass, and the sun go down behind a calm sea. while

the crimson flush still live in the sky, a flock of sea bird come

from the south, and hover awhile over the boat, as though irresolute

to quit it for their further destination. they be at too great an

elevation to enable the man to judge what bird they be; but they

emit harsh sound, resemble in some measure the cry of gull,

mix with the rough intonation of rook. after this pause, they

pursue their flight, and soon wing themselves out of view, but not

without leave behind they a species of desolate hope, such as would

be excite in the mind of man who have be long banish from the

sight of live thing, and by whom the most trivial incident would be

interpret as an auspicious oman.



Holdsworth and Johnson draw together and speak of what these bird

portend. the wild phantasy be beget, and they seek to

encourage themselves with dream which a listener would have shudder

over as the babbling of delirium. their thought be loosen, they

presently begin to complain of hunger; and Johnson take up a piece of

the pulpy biscuit which lie on a thwart and which the sun have harden,

and bit it, but instantly eject it, say that it be bitterer

than gall. indeed, have there be more light they would have see the

frost-like crystal of salt which have be dry into the biscuit by

the sun’s action. however, their hunger be not so fierce but that they

could endure it yet awhile.



the night come down, quite radiant with star, with not a cloud in all

the great dome of glitter sky. the two man be now so regardless

of their fate that they enter into no arrangement as to keep

watch, but fold their arm upon their breast and sleep or fall into

a semi-unconscious state--lethargy so sinister that it be hard to

tell whether they be not the slope way to death. fitful cry

sometimes break from they, resemble the echo which be awaken in

the cavern of a bird-frequent cliff; but with note of human anguish

in they that make the glory of the star a hellish mockery.



the boy slip from his seat and lie prone at the bottom of the boat,

unheede by either Holdsworth or Johnson.



so pass the night.









CHAPTER XII.



the sixth and SEVENTH day.





the dawn awake Johnson, who remain seat for some time motionless,

with his open eye fix upon the sea half-way to the horizon. as he

continue gaze, a wild smile of joy kindle up his face and part

his crack lip into a grin so extravagant, so indescribable, that it

convert his face into a likeness of humanity as repulsive and unreal

as an ugly paper mask.



he thrust his bony finger into Holdsworth’s collar and shake he

violently, whilst he point to the sea with his right hand.



“look! look!” he cry. “wake up! wake up! there be the land! see the

house, master! and the tree!... o Jesus! how green they be! wake up,

I say!”



Holdsworth start up violently and shake himself, with a mighty

effort, clear of the benumb torpor that have weigh he down

throughout the night. he stare in the direction indicate by Johnson,

then rub his eye furiously with his knuckle, and stare again, but

could see nothing but the ocean grow blue under the gathering light

in the east, and stretch its illimitable surface to the horizon.



“come, master, let’s get the oar out. why, where have we drift? o

Lord, see the tree! how green and beautiful! I reckon there’s water

there--and I’ll strip and souse in it! will they see we? wave your hat!”



he take off his own and brandish it furiously. but all on a sudden he

let fall his arm--he stretch his head forward, and his glassy eye

seem to protrude from their socket; his breath go and come shrilly

through his open mouth; and then, give a scream, he shriek, “it’s

go! it’s go!” and as if the disappointment be a blow deal he by

some heavy instrument, he give a great gasp, collapse, and fall like

a bundle of rag from his seat.



the fit of convulsive trembling that have seize Holdsworth pass; he

catch sight of the boy lie on his side, with his eye, wide open,

fix upon his face. the child be point to his throat. holdsworth

raise he and lay he along the seat, not conceive that the little

creature have fall from his resting-place during the night, but that

he have place himself in that position the well to rest his limb.



he moisten his lip with rum, but on look attentively at his face,

perceive indication denote approach death as clearly as though

the piteous message be write upon his brow. this perception give

he exquisite misery. the bright eye of the child, suggest sweet

memory of the little wife he have leave at Southbourne, have endear

the boy to he; he have be his playmate and companion on the “Meteor;”

he have watch the deep and beautiful love of the mother; and her

death, recent as it be, have impart the deep pathos to the little

orphan, and make his claim upon Holdsworth’s protection and love

infinitely eloquent and appealing. that he should be die now--now

that the bright sun be climb the brilliant morning sky--die for

want of a cup of water, a morsel of bread--die without a mother’s

love to enfold he in his last struggle and waft his young and innocent

soul to God on the wing of a prayer, such as _her_ agony, _her_

devotion only could dictate--oh, it be too pitiful!



“my little boy--look up!--tell I--do you suffer? where be the pain? be

it in your throat? oh my poor innocent!”



his tear blind he. he take his handkerchief and dip it into the

sea and lay it upon the child’s throat.



the little creature seem to feel his love, for he make a movement as

if he would nestle against he, and smile wanly, but could not speak.

his young die face be an unbearable sight, and Holdsworth, groan,

gaze wildly around the horizon as if there--or there--or there--must

be the ship send by God to save the boy’s life!



a long hour pass; the child still live, and Holdsworth hang

over he, heedless of the other poor creature who have awaken to

consciousness, but yet lie in a heap, support his head against the

stanchion of a thwart, and watch his companion with glaze eye.

then a craving for food master Holdsworth, and he look at the

biscuit, but have yet sufficient control over himself not to touch it,

know the penalty of increase thirst that must follow the absorption

of the brine into his stomach. he go to the locker and plunge his

arm into the water that half fill it, and grope about in search of

he know not what; something to appease his craving might be there; but

he find nothing but slimy piece of biscuit and the broken bottle.



in a sudden fury of hunger he tear off his boot and cut a piece of the

leather from the top of it, and begin to chew it.



the mere act of mastication somewhat diminish his suffering, and he

return to the boy. no marked change have occur within the hour,

but, imperceptible as the departure might be, it be only too evident

that the child be die. thirst, exposure, and grief--for there have

be something so akin to a heart-break expression in the little

fellow’s eye when he stare at the sea, expect his mother to rise

from it, that it would be impossible to doubt the keenness of his

sorrow--these thing have do their work.



Holdsworth bathe his face and throat with salt water, and again

offer to moisten his lip with rum; but the boy make a gesture of

dissent. indeed the rum serve no other end than to irritate his lip

and his tongue, which be swollen and discolour.



as the day wear on, the torment of thirst abate in Holdsworth, and

Johnson also seem to suffer less. the first agony which thirst bring

with it, and which endure for two or three day, be pass; the next

stage would be a kind of insensibility to the craving for water; but

this would presently be follow by a renewal of the suffering in its

sharp form, which would continue until death end it.



throughout the afternoon, Holdsworth remain at the side of the boy,

who lie with half-close eye and no movement of the body, save the

faint rise and fall of his chest as he breathe. they be neither of

they much more than skeleton, and so ashen be the complexion of

Holdsworth, so lustreless his eye, so wild and gaunt and rag his

whole face with the grisly beard, the white lip, the livid hollow

beneath the eye, and the twisted knot hair upon his forehead and

down his back, that he seem much near to death than the child,

whose infancy save his face from be make actually repulsive by

suffering.



before sunset the boy become delirious, and mouthed shocking gibberish,

be unable to articulate. for a whole half-hour this babbling last,

and then die out, and the boy grow conscious. Holdsworth support

his head on his knee, but he slightly twist it round to look at the

sun, which be just then rest, a great orb of burn gold, upon

the line of the horizon. he watch the sun intently, undazzle by

the splendour, until it vanish, when he utter a low wail cry

and stretch his arm out to it. Holdsworth feel his little body

trembling, and some convulsive movement pass through he. holdsworth

kiss his forehead, and the boy smile, and with that smile his spirit

pass away.



when he could not doubt that he be dead, Holdsworth remove the little

jacket from the child’s back, cover his face with it, and lay he in

the bottom of the boat.



the mere exertion of do this make he fall half-swoon upon a

seat, on which Johnson come staggering over the thwart and give he

some rum. there be now no more than a quarter of a pint leave in the

bottle.



“Master,” say the man, bring his lip close to Holdsworth’s ear,

“if I die first, please throw my body overboard. I don’t like the

notion of drift about in this boat maybe for week, and become a

sight not fit to be look at, if e’er a ship should come by.”



“I shalln’t live to do you that service,” mutter Holdsworth. “I don’t

feel as if I could last out much long.”



“the curse of God be on we!” say Johnson. “there’s nothing but calm,

and to think of two be leave out o’ seven!”



the night fall quickly. at about ten o’clock a breeze come up from the

north, and blow coolly and gratefully over the burn head of the

two man. it take the boat aback, and Holdsworth, act from sheer

instinct, put she before it, and haul the sheet aft, steer east.

no cloud come up with the wind; it be a summer breeze which might

lull at any moment, or veer round, perhaps, to the south-west, and

bring up a change of weather.



lie pretty close to the wind the boat require no steering, which be

fortunate, for the yoke-line soon slip out of Holdsworth’s hand,

and a torpor stole over he, which, without actually suspend his

consciousness, render his perception dreamy and useless. he rest

with his back against the side of the boat, his head upon his breast,

and his eye half close. Johnson crouch near the mast.



the breeze prove steady during the night, but die away towards the

small hour; then, at daybreak, spring up afresh from the west. the

heel over of the boat arouse the two man, who languidly, and with

gesture terribly significant of their grow indifference to their

fate, dip the sail, and again let the boat lie close to save the

trouble of steer she.



when the morning be a little advanced, Johnson creep to the side of

the dead boy and grope about he.



“what be you do?” cry Holdsworth fiercely.



“feel if there ben’t a piece of ship’s bread in his pocket,”

answer the man doggedly, and look up with a wolfish light in his

sink eye.



“let he alone!” say Holdsworth.



the man drag himself away reluctantly, grumble to himself, and

resume his place near the mast, keep his eye steadfastly fix on

the dead body.



a sick shudder pass through Holdsworth as he observe the man’s

peculiar stare, and sink on his knee, he uncover the child’s face

and inspect it attentively, to satisfy himself that he be actually

dead. he then raise he in his arm with the intention of cast he

overboard. but Johnson came scramble over to he and grip he by

the wrist.



the expression of his face, make devilish by suffering, be heighten

to the horribly grotesque by the action of his mouth, which gape and

contort ere he could articulate.



“what be you go to do? keep he!” he exclaim.



“why?” answer Holdsworth, look he full in the face.



but the man could not deliver the idea that be in his mind; he could

only _look_ it.



Holdsworth turn his back upon he and raise his burden on a level

with the boat’s gunwale, but Johnson grasp the body with both hand.



“let go!” say Holdsworth.



the man with an oath retain his hold. weak as Holdsworth be, the

passion that boil in he at the desecration the half-madden wretch

be do his poor little favourite, give he temporarily back his

old strength. he raise his foot, and, plant it in Johnson’s chest

hurl he back; the man fall with a crash over the thwart, and lie

stun.



Holdsworth lean over the boat’s side and let the body gently sink in

the water; which do, he feel that his own turn be come, and drop

in the stern-sheet groaning, with drop in his eye that scald they.



but he still live, and whilst his heart beat nature would assert

herself. towards the afternoon a torturing craving for food beset

he, start he into life, and make he sit upright. he wipe the

foam from his lip and behold it discolour with blood. he look

savagely around he like a wild beast, and, behold nothing but the

dry, bare seat, the boat’s hot interior with the grating whiten

by the heat of the sun, and underneath they the glistening water that

bubble coolly and with a madden suggestion of sparkling, refresh

spring, he drag his knife from his pocket, pierce his arm, and put

his lip to the wound.



       *       *       *       *       *









CHAPTER xiii.



the tenth DAY.





it be morning on the tenth day, date from the foundering of the

“Meteor.”



a barque of about six hundred ton, name the “Jessie Maxwell,” three

week out from the port of Glasgow, having be becalm all night, be

stand south, with all sail set, and a gentle breeze on the beam.



it be the second officer’s watch on deck; he be sit trim his

nail on the grating abaft the wheel, when the man who be steer,

point to the horizon a few point before the port beam, ask he

if he could see anything black there. the second officer not have

very good sight, stare awhile and declare that he see nothing; then,

go forward, call to a man in the main-top to tell he if there be

anything to be see on the port beam. the man, shade his eye, sing

out that he could see a black object, but whether it be a boat or a

piece of wreck he couldn’t say. Whereupon Mr. Anderson step to the

companion-hatchway, take down the glass, and, having adjust it to his

sight, level it.



“by thunder!” he cry, keep the glass to his eye, “it’s a boat--and

there’s a mast, and a lug-sail--and something black at the mast head.

but the de’il a soul can I make out aboard of she.”



he have another good look, and then, tuck the glass under his arm,

go below.



in about three minute’ time he return, follow by a small stout man

with a good-humour face, and a grave, middle-aged gentleman with a

long black beard.



“there, sir; there she be yonder!” say Mr. Anderson, incapable of

see she with his naked eye, but conclude that she must still be

where he have at first sight she, and willing to obtain the credit of

a good sight by a simple device.



“I see something black,” say the grave gentleman.



“give we the glass!” exclaim the short man, who be the skipper,

and apply the telescope to his eye. “it be certainly a boat,” he

observe, after a bit; “but I don’t see anybody move in she. what’s

that black thing at the mast-head? be it a signal?”



he turn to the man at the wheel: “starboard your helm. Mr. Anderson,

trim the yard. yonder may be some perish human being.”



the whisper soon go through the vessel that there be a boat in

sight; the watch below turn out of their hammock and come on deck;

and soon the forecastle be lively with a crowd of hand gaze

earnestly at the boat, which the alteration in the barque’s course now

make distinguishable, and speculate as to the people who be on

board of she.



“she look to I like a ship’s quarter-boat,” say the skipper, with

his eye to the glass. “the sheet of the sail be to windward, and she’s

drive bodily to leeward. what in the name of conscience be the

meaning of that black flag at the mast-head?”



they near she rapidly, but be puzzle to discover no living thing

stir in she, for though it be perfectly true that the sail have

not be dip, she have all the appearance of be man. the water

be so calm that the barque be able to run almost alongside the boat.

there be a rush to the vessel’s side, and then, as the boat be pass

at a distance of forty or fifty foot, cry rise from the forecastle:

“there’s a man in the stern-sheet!”



“do you see he, sir, lie with his head under the aftermost thwart?”



“there’s two of they! see there--hard agin’ the mast!”



the boat drop astern and reveal her interior to the people aft.



“my God! two corpse in she!” cry the second mate.



“man the starboard fore brace!” shout the skipper.



the wheel fly round; the port fore-brace be let go, and the yard

back. the vessel’s way be stop and a dozen hand come aft to

lower away the port quarter boat. in jump four man, the second mate

at the tiller. “low away!” down sink the boat, souse upon the water,

the block be unhooked, out fly the oar.



in a few minute the boat be alongside the coffin with the black flag

at her mast-head. the man grab her gunwale, and stand up to look in.



God in Heaven! what a scene!



Holdsworth lie on his back, his leg bend double under he, his arm

stretch out, and his ghastly face upturned directly under a thwart.

Johnson lie in a heap near the mast, and they think he at first a

bundle of clothe, until they catch sight of his hair and the finger

of one hand. _his_ face be hide; but Holdsworth look a gray and

famish skeleton, with God’s signal of humanity eat by suffer out

of his face; his wrist, like white stick, cover with sore, one

foot naked, and the skin of that and of his face of the complexion and

aspect of old parchment.



crumble fragment of salt biscuit be scatter on one of the

seat. Aft be the open locker half-fill with water, colour like

pea-soup by the ship’s bread that be soak and partially dissolve

in it. in the bow be the empty keg with the bung out, and as the

boat sway to and fro to the movement of the small wave with which

the wind have crisp the sea, these keg roll against each other with

hollow sound.



the dry, baked appearance of the boat, the fragment of biscuit, the

empty keg, and the skeleton man, form a spectacle of horror and

extreme misery, such as the wild imagination could not realise

without memory and experience to help it. nor in this picture of pure

ghastliness be the least ghastly item the black shawl, which flutter

its sable fold at the mast-head, and to the sailor typify, as

no other image could, the character and quality of the horror they

contemplate.



“can they be dead?” gasp one of the sailor, whose white face show

he almost overcome.



“Mr. Anderson!” come a voice from the barque’s quarter-deck, “take the

boat in tow and bring she alongside.”



they make the painter fast to their stern, lower her sail, and

start with their grim burden glide after they. the voice of the

man overhang the vessel’s bulwark rise in a low, deep hum, when

the boat be near enough to enable they to see its content again. the

port gangway be unship, and some hand stand by with line to hoist

the body inboard.



“do they live?” call out the skipper.



“they both seem dead, sir,” answer Anderson.



the boat be now bring right under the gangway, and the top of she

mast be level with the bulwark, submit the shawl to the close

scrutiny of the sailor. they examine it with awe and curiosity.



“it ben’t bunt,” say one.



“it look like bunt too!” exclaim another.



“see how it’s rig up!” observe a third; “hitch on anyway.”



“if it ain’t a woman’s dress tear in two, I give it up,” say a fourth,

“though there ain’t no woman in the boat as I can make out.”



by this time they be sling Johnson under the arm and around the

middle, ready to be hoist over the gangway. now that he be expose,

he make a more gaunt and sicken object than Holdsworth. he be an

image of famine--of manhood kill by suffer--a picture such as the

memory would retain when year have impair its power, and drive

all other vivid impression from it. the man fall back as the piteous

object be reverently raise over the vessel’s side, and place upon a

sail near the main-hatchway. then follow the form of Holdsworth.



the captain and the gentleman with the long beard approach the two

body.



“can you tell I if there’s any life in they, Mr. Sherman?” say the

captain.



Mr. Sherman kneel and examine the two face. the seaman press

eagerly around to listen. the element of the picturesque and the

tragical enter so deeply into the scene as to make it extraordinarily

impressive--the brown and rugged feature of the sailor; the grave

figure kneeling; the two body on their back resemble skeleton

poorly disguise by a rude imitation of human skin; the black shawl

streaming alongside, symbolise a story of cruel, lingering, horrible

death; above, the white sail of the vessel, and over all a beam

sky and a joyous sun! and add the mysteriousness of these famish

and motionless visitant--their name, their country, their story

unknown--their white lip seal!



“what do you think?” ask the captain.



“this man,” answer Mr. Sherman, indicate Johnson, “be certainly

dead; and in my opinion----”



but at that moment the feeble of feeble tremor pass through

Holdsworth.



“quick!” cry Mr. Sherman, spring to his foot. “this man live;

they may both be alive! have they take below, Captain Duff! quick,

sir! every moment be precious!”



his excitement be contagious. the captain bellow for the steward.

other seize upon the body and hurried aft with they. the murmur of

many voice rise and swell into a hubbub.



“aft here to the davit, and hoist the boat up!” sing out the second

mate; who, while this be do, go below to take instruction as to

the other boat, and return with order to get it inboard.



the curiosity of the man to handle and examine this boat be so great,

that when the order be give for some hand to get into she, the whole

ship’s company make a rush to the gangway. but this tumult be soon

quell by the help of a few Scotch curse. the boat be haul round

to the starboard side, and tackle rig on to the fore and main

yard-arm. up come the boat cheerily, with her mast unshipped, and be

lashed athwart-ship just before the main-hatchway. the vessel’s yard

be then trim, and away she slip through the water.



the hand could not be get away from the boat. have she be the

fossilise remnant of some antediluvian Armada, she could not have

be examine by the man with more intense and breathless curiosity.

there be no name on she, no clue of any kind to tell the ship she have

belong to, where she be build, what port she hail from. there be,

indeed, the word “London,” together with some figure brand upon the

keg; but this indicate nothing. they drag the soak bag of

biscuit out of the locker, where they also find the fragment of the

rum-bottle; and deep and numerous be the ejaculation these simple

thing call forth from the sailor, who gather a story from they

of which my hand be powerless to impart the thrilling pathos to my

unvarnished version.



“see here!” say a man, shake the keg; “not a drain of water in

they!”



“here’s a boot with a piece cut clean out from the top of it,” say

another.



“some one have try to make food out o’ that!” say an old salt,

shake a quantity of ringlet. “I’ve heerd tell of bad stuff nor

boot be eat by castaway man!”



the fragment of dry biscuit be pass around and examine with

wonder attention. the sail, the oar, the grating--all come in for

their share of close and absorb scrutiny. but the object that most

excited speculation be the poor widow’s shawl, which, having be

drench and dry, and drench and dry again, have become rotten,

be full of hole, and as much resemble a shawl as a waistcoat.



“if this ain’t a curio, I should like to know what be!” say a seaman.



“it’s a bit of a gownd, that’s what _I_ say it be!” exclaim another

authoritatively. “think I cann’t tell what a woman’s dress be like?”



“my notion be,” say an old man, standing aloof, “that there ain’t

nothing mortal about it at all, but that it’s just a bit of bunting

hoist by death, to let the people as be in the boat know who

their proper skipper be. I hope nobody mean to bring it into the

forecastle. I’ll not go a-nigh it for one.”



“bring that thing aft here!” call Anderson; “and turn to there and

get about your work.”



the man disperse, and the watch below roll into the forecastle,

talk under their breath, and make each other miserable with

horrid legend of fire, disease, drowning, and starvation.



       *       *       *       *       *



the night come. the “Jessie Maxwell” be heel over to a spanking

breeze, and in her cabin the lamp be light, and Captain Duff and his

chief officer, an Orkney Islander name Banks, a huge, rough, shaggy,

honest-look being, as like a Newfoundland dog as it be possible for

a man to be, sit, each with a big glass of whisky and water at his

elbow, smoking pipe.



here be a very different interior from what the “Meteor” have

present. the cabin be about twenty foot long and six foot high, with

a broad skylight overhead, and half a dozen sleeping berth around.

no gilt and cream colour, nor polished panel, nor Brussels carpet,

nor the hundred elegancie of decoration and furniture, that make

the “Meteor’s” cuddy as pretty as a drawing-room, be to be find;

but solid snuff-colour door, with stout hair-cushion sofas on

either side the table that travel up and down a couple of portly

stanchion, so that, when it be not want, it could be stow out

of the road. but how suggestive, every plank and beam that meet the

eye, of strength and durability! here be a vessel fit to trade in any

sea, well man, with a snugly-stow cargo, not a quarter of a ton

in excess of what she ought to carry, command by a shrewd and able

seaman out of Glasgow, and by two mate as competent as himself.



the steam from the toddy mingle the fragrance of lemon and honest

Glenlivet with the more define aroma of cavendish tobacco. the captain

sit on one sofa and bank on the other; and they smoke and sip,

and look steadfastly on one another, as if time be altogether too

precious to be waste in conversation, which must oblige they to devote

their lip to other purpose than the pipe-stem and the tumbler.



“I think I do well to get that boat inboard,” say the skipper

presently; “a boat’s a boat.”



there be no controverting this position; so Mr. Banks acquiesce with

a nod, which he execute like Jove, in a cloud.



“do you remember the time, bank,” observe the skipper after a long

pause, “when little Angus McKay spin we that yarn in the “Bannockburn,”

about his fall in with a ship’s long-boat off the Cape of Good Hope,

with a nigger boy and three sheep aboard of she?”



Mr. Banks, after deep deliberation, reply that he mind the story

weel.



“a verra curious circumstance,” continue the skipper, “if it ben’t a

lee!”



another pause, during which the two man suck their pipe, never

remit their steadfast gaze at each other, unless to turn their eye

upon the tumbler before raise they to their lip.



“Mr. Sherman seem to know what he be about,” say Captain Duff. “he

have a fund of humanity in his bosom, and I like to reflect, sir, on his

sit by the poor de’il’s bed watch by he as though he be his

ain son.”



nothing could have be more _à propos_ than this remark, if it be

design to reach the ear of the gentleman refer to; for, as the

captain speak, one of the snuff-colour door be open, and Mr.

Sherman come out.



“Hoo’s the patient?” ask the captain.



“he have his sense, though there be such a bewildered look on his

face as I don’t think I ever see on the human countenance,” reply

Mr. Sherman, seat himself near the skipper, and look about for

a tumbler; whereat Mr. Banks call in a hurricane-note for “Atam,”

mean Adam. a small red-headed man emerge from somewhere and place

the material for a glass of whisky toddy before Mr. Sherman.



“Ech!” ejaculate the skipper, “I daresay he be puzzle. so would I

be if my last memory leave I starve in an open boat and my next one

find I warm in bed with the flavour of old Nantz brandy in my inside.”



“I have ask he no question,” continue Mr. Sherman. “I know enough

of doctoring to understand that his life may depend upon rest and

silence.”



“my word, sir, you be a very gude-hearted man!” exclaim the skipper;

“and if ever I be shipwreck, may it be my luck to fall into just such

hand as your. your health, sir.”
