say which, he half-empty his tumbler, a performance that make his

merry eye glisten with delight.



“and the other man be te?” say Mr. Banks.



“quite dead, poor soul! do you ever see anything more heart-rending

than his body, captain? mere skin and bone--and, oh! sir, the

expression of his face!” exclaim Mr. Sherman, hold his hand over

his eye a moment.



“thirst be an awfu’ thing,” say the skipper, glance at his tumbler.



“and so be hunger,” observe Mr. Banks, who look as though a whole ox

might hardly serve he for a meal.



“I expect, when the other poor fellow be capable of speak, that we

shall hear a terrible tale,” say Mr. Sherman. “it be very providential

that my slight knowledge of medicine should qualify I to deal with

he. the great care be require in treat person nearly dead

of starvation. I have feed he so far in spoonful only. it be my

intention to remain with he through the night. I’ll borrow one of your

easy-chair, captain, which will serve I very well for a bed.”



“certainly. I be sleep close at hand, and if you want I, just give

my cot a shove, and I’ll be out of it before you can tell which side I

drop from.”



this settle, the captain mix himself another tumbler of spirit,

refill his pipe, and enter into speculation as to Holdsworth’s

nationality, the length of time he have be in the boat, the probable

longitude and latitude in which the ship have take fire or foundered,

with many other matter, all of which he relieve with long pause,

and a variety of thoughtful puff and attentive glance at the

light through the medium of his tumbler. presently four bell--ten

o’clock--strike, which make Mr. bank rise from his seat, wish his

companion “coot night,” and withdraw to his cabin to get a couple of

hour’ sleep before his watch come on. the other remained chat for

half an hour, and then the skipper go on deck to have a look around

before turn in for the night.









CHAPTER XIV.



HOLDSWORTH’S RECOVERY.





the cabin in which Holdsworth lie be a spare one, next the captain’s.

it be light by an oblong piece of frosted glass let into the deck

overhead, and by a port-hole which be a standing and comfortable

illustration of the immense thickness of the timber that separate the

inmate from the sea. there be a square of cocoa-nut mat on the

deck to tread on; up in a corner an immovable washstand, contain a

pewter basin; a row of peg against the door; and a mahogany bunk.



in this bunk lie Holdsworth, and at the hour of which I be now write,

Mr. Sherman sit beside he in an easy-chair, his leg up and his head

back, in a deep sleep. from the centre of a beam hang a small oil-lamp,

the frame carefully protect by wire network; and the light diffuse

by this lamp be clear enough to exhibit Holdsworth’s face distinctly.



he, too, be asleep, if sleep that can be call which, plunge the

sense into unconsciousness, yet leave pain and misery to play their

active part upon the darken stage of the mind. of his youth, of his

beauty, I might almost say of his very manhood--such as be willnot to be

suggest by the open, brave, and win expression of his face--not a

trace be leave. the spoliation of suffering have be so complete, that

the bare wreck of the noble temple it have ruin be all that remain.

now, even more completely than in his wake hour, might we master the

full extent of the cruel transformation that have be work, since

the candour of sleep be on the slumberer, and the self-consciousness

that mask the subtle facial truth, inactive. his hair, formerly dark

and luxuriant, be thin about the forehead, be tangle and coarse,

and mix with gray and white. the protrusion of the cheek-bone form

a conspicuous feature; under they the flesh fall into a hollow, and, as

much of it as the bristly moustache and whisker suffer to be see,

be pucker and dry up like the rind of an old winter apple. the

under lip be enlarge, and entirely alter the remembered aspect of

the mouth. the eyebrow droop where they have formerly arch, and

the hair of they near the temple have fall off. time might, perhaps,

efface some of these disfigurement deep grave by the stylet of

pain; but no man could have look upon that sleeping face without a

conviction of the permanency of much that he beheld.



he sleep; but though his slumber be deep, his movement be so

restless, convulsive, and feverish, that it seem every moment as if

he must start up.



once during the night the ship’s bell sound seven awaken he,

and he open his eye and raise his head, but soon let it fall

again. then it be during this short interval of wakefulness that

the bewildered look of which Mr. Sherman have speak might have be

perceive; and it linger for some minute on his face after he have

drop asleep once more.



several time during the night the kind-hearted man who watch by the

poor fellow’s side rise from his chair, and scrutinise he anxiously;

and once Mr. Banks pop his shaggy head in to ask how the sufferer

do, but find both patient and doctor asleep.



the morning creep over the sky, and turn the port-hole and the piece

of deck-glass white; and at six o’clock Mr. Sherman wake, and creep

quietly to his cabin to refresh himself with a plunge in cold water;

then go on deck, where he find the captain smoke a cigar, his foot

in galosh, and the hand wash down. a sparkling, genial morning,

with a warm breeze from the west, the barque in full sail, and the

green sea caress her bow, and leap back from their keen salute

in avalanche of foam.



“good morning, Mr. Sherman,” say the skipper. “this be the weather,

eh, sir? out of the Doldrums by Tuesday week, I hope. how’s your

patient?”



“sleep soundly. he have pass a good night. if he can only get over

the next few day, the tropical sun will set he to right.”



“be he awake now?”



“I think not. but we’ll go and see if you like.”



the captain throw away his cigar and follow Mr. Sherman below, not,

however, before cast a look above and around, and singe out to the

man at the wheel to “keep she at that.”



open the cabin door they creep to the bunk and stand look at the

sleep man, who, arouse perhaps by the magnetic influence of four

eye upon he, start and stare up at they from his pillow.



Captain Duff draw back a step, scare a little by the wild gaze

that Holdsworth fix upon he, and which be make in some measure

repellent by his gaunt and waste face, and by the pitiable expression

of bewilderment that pass slowly into it, and make it almost as

meaningless as an idiot’s.



“how do you feel, my poor fellow?” ask Mr. Sherman; “strong, I

hope?”



Holdsworth make no answer, but knit his brow with an air of profound

perplexity, gazed slowly round he, then attentively at Mr. Sherman,

then at the skipper, then at himself, finally press his hand to his

head.



“how do you know he be English? perhaps he don’t understand you,” say

Captain Duff.



“I hear he mutter in English before I join you last night,”

answer Mr. Sherman.



“pray tell I where I be?” say Holdsworth, in a faint voice.



“that’s English!” exclaim the captain, though he look as if he must

take a thought of it yet, before he should allow himself to feel sure.



“you be among friend,” reply Mr. Sherman softly, and in a voice

full of sympathy; “on board a vessel call the ‘Jessie Maxwell,’ bind

to Australia. we sight your boat yesterday morning.”



“my boat!” whisper Holdsworth, with an expression on his face of such

deep bewilderment that it be painful to behold it.



“do you not remember?”



“my boat! my boat!” repeat Holdsworth; but no light come into his

eye to show that he apprehend the other’s meaning.



“he have lose his memory,” say Mr. Sherman aside to the captain. and

then to Holdsworth: “do you feel as if you could eat anything?”



“yes, I be hungry,” answer Holdsworth.



“that be a good sign!” exclaim Mr. Sherman, cheerfully. “captain,

will you stop here a few minute, while I ask the steward to get the

soup heat?”



the skipper, be leave alone, station himself near the door, and

watch Holdsworth with mixed emotion. brave to foolhardiness in

a gale of wind, on a lee-shore, in confront a mutinous crew, in

deal with the severest of marine exigency, this little gentleman,

in some trivial matter, be as timorous as a mouse, and would have

make his escape overboard, rather than be grasp by Holdsworth,

who, if he be not the dissembled madman his ragged, withered face

suggest he to be, be still hedge about with enough of mysterious

and secret horror to make he awful in the practical little Scotchman’s

eye.



meanwhile, Holdsworth rest upon his pillow, cast eager and

restless glance about the cabin, and at the skipper, and battle with

an oblivion of the past as thick and as impenetrable as that mystery of

be which the infant emerge from at its birth.



“tell I, sir, who I be--where I have be take from!” he exclaim,

presently, look with implore eye at the skipper.



“indeed, my man, I cann’t tell you who you be,” reply the captain,

wish that Mr. Sherman would return, or that a squall would give he

an excuse to withdraw. “all that I know be, we find you in a boat, and

pick you up, and that the gentleman who have just go out, save your

life.”



“strange!” mutter Holdsworth; “I remember nothing.”



“oh, it will all come if you give it time. memory often leave people

after a bad illness, but return again with the strength. Ech!” he

cry, strike, as though for the first time, by the poor creature’s

lean and hollow face. “but you have kenn’d some awfu’ time, man, since

ye last stand upon honest shipboard. sailoring, if you be a sailor, be

a poor look-out when it come to want bread and water.”



the door open and Mr. Sherman come in, follow by the steward

bear a dish of soup and some mild brandy and water, with which Mr.

Sherman proceed to feed Holdsworth. when as much of the soup as be

think good have be administer, Mr. Sherman bid his patient turn

his back to the light and get some sleep.



“I will, sir; thank you for your kindness,” return Holdsworth, with

affect docility. “but, first, will you help I--will you help I to

recall something--anything--to give my mind rest? I can see nothing for

the darkness that be over I.”



“I have tell he that his memory will come back with his strength,”

say Captain Duff.



“yes, have a little patience!” exclaim Mr. Sherman. “we will get you

on deck in a day or two, and when you see the boat we take you from

your memory will return to you.”



“can you not tell I my name?” ask Holdsworth, with that striking

expression of painful anxiety you may see on the face of a blind man

desert by his guide and totally at fault.



“we will endeavour to find it out,” reply Mr. Sherman. “come,

captain, our friend must talk no more, or all our trouble to get he

well will be of no use.”



Holdsworth put out his hand with a smile of gratitude that soften and

almost sweeten his miserable and skeleton-like face; then turn in

his bunk and close his eye.



“a strange thing to happen to a man,” say the captain to Mr. Sherman,

as they go on deck. “I never could have believe that the memory of

a creature could go out of his brain like that!”



“we may guess the nature and magnitude of his suffering by this

effect,” answer Mr. Sherman. “God alone know how many day he may

have pass in that boat, and what scene of horror he have witness

and what torment he have endure. but we must help his memory as far as

he can. will you allow I to go forward and examine the boat?”



they walk to the main-deck, where the boat be stow. a little knot

of man gather around and watch their movement with interest. but,

in truth, the boat be as unsuggestive as a sheet of blank paper. there

be no name in she; nor by her build, sail, oar, shape, or anything

else, be it possible to tell her paternity. the broken bottle and

bag of bread that have be fish out of her locker be in her

bottom, but no clue be to be get from they; nothing but a story of

deep tragical misery. the captain send for the shawl that have be

unhitched from the mast-head, and he and Mr. Sherman hold it open

between they and inspect it. brown by the wet and the heat--in its

fray and tattered shape, its very texture modify by exposure--it

be positively no more than a black rag.



they return to the after-deck, and send the steward for the clothe

which have be remove from the two man. holdsworth’s pilot coat be

of good quality, and his linen also seem to suggest that he have

hold a very superior position to that of Johnson, whose dress be a

sailor’s, a brown woollen shirt, serge trouser, boot with high top,

and the invariable belt and knife. Holdsworth’s linen be mark with

H; nothing more. they find in his pocket a watch, a clasp-knife, some

money, and one or two other article, which Mr. Sherman make into a

parcel, hope that the sight of thing which would be familiar might

help the poor fellow’s memory.



“it be evident,” say the skipper, “that whatever we be to learn must

come from the man himself. his clothe tell we nothing.”



“they be a sailor’s, don’t you think?”



“why, they be such clothe as I or bank might wear; but that don’t

prove that the man be a sailor. he certainly haven’t a nautical cut.”



“his language be that of an educate man, and his linen be that of a

gentleman. pray God that the poor soul’s memory will return. without it

he will be scarcely well off with we than he be in the boat.”



“Eh?” cry the literal skipper, “not well off with good meat and

drink and a good bunk to lie in, than when he be perish of thirst,

with no well blanket than the sky to cover he?”



“I mean that he may have friend at home who, while his memory remain

torpid, must be as dead to he and he to they, as if he have remain in

his open boat.”



“yes, I see your idea, sir,” reply the skipper. “and now about the

other puir creature. we must bury he this morning. he be dead you say?”



“we will go and look at he.”



“why,” return Captain Duff, shrinking, “to tell you the plain truth,

I be not over fond of these girne body. by your leave, sir, I’ll

hae the puir creature sew up in canvas, and if you’ll tak’ the reading

of the burial office I shall feel oblige, Mr. Sherman, as I have but

a varra moderate capacity for the delivery o’ write word.”



at this juncture, Adam, the steward, ring the breakfast-bell, and the

captain and Mr. Sherman go below.



       *       *       *       *       *



there be scarcely any ceremony more impressive than a burial at sea;

perhaps because nowhere do man feel his littleness more than when

the mighty ocean surround he. the grave of the dead on shore in a

measure localise their inmate, and our association be fortify by

the power of refer to the depart as being who slumber in green

place, and be at all season visitable.



but a burial at sea be the launching of the dead into infinity. the

sense of his extinction be absolute. he be swallow up and annihilate

by the universe of water, which also seem to overwhelm his very memory.



at twelve o’clock the body of Johnson, sew up in canvas, with a weight

of lead attach to his foot, lie extend upon one of the grating

of the main-hatchway, one end rest on the bulwark of the ship,

the other upon the shoulder of two sailor. the crew stand round,

hold their cap in their hand; and near the body stand Mr. Sherman

read the Burial Service. the mournful and impressive spectacle be

greatly heighten by the tolling of the bell on the quarter-deck,

which mingle its clear chime with the word deliver by Mr. Sherman.

the vessel be sail on an even keel, her white sail swell and

soar one above another, and form a lovely picture against the

bright blue sky. the water leap and sparkle and froth against her

clean side, and those swallow of the deep, the stormy petrel, chase

her flash wake, and give by their presence a finish detail to the

whole of the sun-light scene.



how unutterable the mystery hedge the motionless figure in the

canvas shroud--his name unknown, a waif of dead humanity snatch for

a brief moment from the imperious deep, whose will it be to keep he!

the seaman send shrink glance at the bundle on the grating. that

he have suffer; that famine have make a skeleton of he; that thirst

have twist his lean face into an expression of agony which death be

powerless to smooth out, be all they know.



“_we therefore commit his body to the deep, to be turn into

corruption_----”



the captain motion with his hand; the grating be tilt, and its

burden go like a flash from the bulwark; the steersman turn his

face upon his shoulder, hear the hollow plunge; but those on the

main-deck stand without a move among they, listen to the final,

comfortable, glorious words:--



“_look for the resurrection of the body (when the sea shall give up

her dead), and the life of the world to come, through our Lord Jesus

Christ, who at his come shall change our vile body, that it may be

like his glorious body, accord to the mighty working whereby he be

able to subdue all thing to himself._”



the reader close the book; the grating be restore to its place; and

the man in two and three move slowly forward, talk in subdued

tone; and for the remainder of that day at least no sound of loose

laughter or reckless word be to be hear in the forecastle.









CHAPTER XV.



“no light, but rather darkness, VISIBLE.”





Holdsworth regain his strength slowly, and on the fourth day Mr.

Sherman, who attend he with the gentle and unobtrusive solicitude

of a perfectly benevolent mind, suggest that a visit on deck might

freshen he up and contribute to his recovery.



to this Holdsworth have be look forward with indescribable

eagerness, believe that the sight of the boat of which Mr. Sherman

have speak would recall his memory. his mind, indeed, present a

phenomenon. he remember nothing--literally _nothing_. his actual

life, as he be then live it, practically date from the moment of

the return of his consciousness. all that have go before be pitch

darkness. that the faculty of memory be not _dead_, be prove by his

capacity to remember his thought and feeling, the office and face

of those who wait on he, the food he have eat, the name of those

he converse with, during the time he have be in the cabin of the

barque. but behind this be impenetrable gloom, every glance at which

torture he, so inexplicable be his helplessness to penetrate it.



the mind of an infant have be liken to a sheet of blank paper;

we may extend the image in Holdsworth’s case by conceive that all

the character which experience have write upon his mind have be

effaced, and that, what new character there be upon it, be the

impression only which he have receive since he have be awaken from

the deadly stupor that have conquer he a few hour before his rescue.



Mr. Sherman be fully persuade that Holdsworth’s memory would return

with his strength; and have therefore foreborne from make any

experiment by question or allusion until the time should come when

the renewal of health would enable the sufferer to sustain the fatigue

of thought. he be impressed and touch by the poor fellow’s docility,

his sweetness of temper, and his gratitude, which move he to tear

as often as he attempt to express it. but no clue be to be obtain

from his conversation as to the profession he have follow. there be

not a shoppish expletive in his language. he name thing after the

establish prescription of Johnson’s Dictionary, and might as well

have be a clerk, a dentist, a builder, a Member of Parliament, or

even an attorney, as a sailor.



the “Jessie Maxwell” be now in the hot latitude. the fourth day be

lovely, with a north-east breeze on the port quarter, and a burning

sun, from which an awning protect the deck. an easy chair be place

near the skylight for Holdsworth, who gain the deck, lean on Mr.

Sherman’s arm. he halt on the last step of the companion-ladder, and

clung to his friend with a look of mingle surprise and fear in his

face.



have there be any one, among those who watch he with curiosity,

who have know he as the chief mate of the “Meteor,” he could scarcely

have contemplate this wreck of a man without deep emotion. Conceive,

if you can, a face with every characteristic that have once contribute

to give it manly beauty, wring out of it by suffering which have

leave ineffaceable mark on every inch of the whole surface of the

countenance. conceive a stoop and trembling figure, the shoulder

forward so as to hollow the chest, and the back bow like an old

man’s, the arm lengthen by the abnormal attitude and defeat every

faint suggestion of symmetry which the eye might still hope to find.

but this express nothing of the real transformation that have be

wrought; of that subtle modification of expression, of the spiritual

condition of the face, of change achieve by the most delicate

stroke, but which be as effectual as a recasting of the whole figure

and countenance could have be. he be dress partly in his own

clothe, partly in some of the clothe belong to the second mate,

who be a slight man, but whose garment hang loosely on Holdsworth. he

wear his own coat, which formerly have button tight across his chest,

and which his muscular arm have fill out, as the finger a glove; and

he could now have button it nearly twice around he. the ring that

he have wear on his left hand have slip from his skeleton finger long

ago, when he have be splash the sea-water over his face in vain

endeavour to quench the burn agony in his head and throat. he might

have wear dolly’s wedding-ring on his middle finger now, for his hand

be indeed scarcely more than bone.



Mr. Sherman eye he anxiously as he stand totter at the

companion-hatchway. it seem as if the long-desire revelation have

come to the suffer man, and that he could now remember.



“look about you,” he say, “and tell I if there be anything you see

that recall old impression.”



“I see nothing that do this,” reply Holdsworth in a low voice.

“where be the boat I be take from?”



“on the main-deck yonder.”



“I should like to see she,” say Holdsworth eagerly. “_one_ idea may

light up all.”



they walk slowly forward. here and there a seaman repair a sail,

or work in the low shroud, or do one of the endless job of

splicing, whipping, tarring, cleaning, which be so many condition

of the maritime life, look at Holdsworth earnestly, but never

intrusively; and when he be at the boat some of the hand come up to

he with a spokesman, a middle-aged sailor in ear-ring, who say:



“Beg pardon, sir, but all hand wish to say as they’re werry glad to

see you up and do; and if there’s e’er a thing any man among we can

do in help to make you comfortable while you’re with we, they’ll do

it and welcome; and no liberty be intend.”



“thank you, and God bless you!” answer Holdsworth, greatly move by

this speech, and with an expression on his face that could hardly fail

to let the honest seaman know that their goodwill be not the less

appreciated because it provoke no lengthened reply. the man retire,

say among themselves that, “though the gentleman warn’t a sailor, he

ought to be one; and though he be nothing but a skeleting, he have as

honest a face on he as ever they seed.”



“this be the boat,” say Mr. Sherman.



Holdsworth steady himself by hold on to the gunwale and look

into it. the bag of bread lie under the aftermost thwart; there be

the open locker which the sea have fill with water; there be the

empty keg, whose hollow rolling, as the boat have sway to and fro,

have form such suggestive note of torture, as one might think would

nevermore depart from the ear that have receive the echo. if there

be impression like red-hot brand to sear the mind with burn

transcript of the ugly agonising fact they counterfeit, one, if

any of they, would surely be the impression convey by the scene of

which the interior of the boat have be the theatre. here the widow

have die, with her arm hang over the side, yonder the General have

expire, point to the phantom of his native town, which die memory

have evoke from the air; from that spot the actor have leap; and on

that seat the boy have die, hold out his hand to the sink sun.

the little arena should have be vital with memory, so small be the

space in which infinite human misery have be pack. but to Holdsworth

it convey no idea. not the faint illumination enter his face

in survey it. to Mr. Sherman it be a thousandfold more significant

than to Holdsworth, who be the chief actor in the heart-break

tragedy that have be enact in it. yet he know that it _ought_ to

have an interest for he; and he stand clutch and stare at it,

with a frown forehead, wrestle wildly with his mind, in which the

corpse of memory lie deep and hide.



after a long interval he pass his hand across his eye, and turn to

Mr. Sherman.



“it will not come,” he say.



Mr. Sherman be both disappointed and astonish; disappoint by the

fruitless result of an inspection, the good effect of which he have

count upon, and astonish by this phenomenon of the utter extinction

of the most life-give faculty of the mind.



he draw he to the boat again, and say:



“see now; you be find there, lie under that seat, and beside the

mast lie another man, a dark-face man, dress in sailor’s clothe. do

you remember?”



“no.”



“look at those bag of biscuit. they be find soak in the locker.

those bag contain all the food you have on board. you must have

suffer horribly from the dread of starvation when you find the

biscuit spoilt by the salt water. recall your thought on make the

discovery. can you?”



“no,” reply Holdsworth, press his hand to his head.



“there be a black flag--a piece of stuff, a portion of a woman’s dress

it seem--fasten to the mast-head. do you remember?”



Holdsworth say “no.”



“have you a woman with you?”



“I cannot tell.”



“see if you can go far back. try to recollect where your ship

sail from. be it England?”



“England? yes--I know England--but I do not remember if I come from

England,” Holdsworth reply, with profound anxiety in his eye.



“come! you remember England! do you sail from Liverpool?”



“I know Liverpool!” he exclaim quickly.



“and London?”



“yes! yes!”



“and what be the name of your ship?”



Holdsworth think and think without avail.



Herein be the deception that mislead Mr. Sherman: Holdsworth could

perfectly remember familiar name, but they have to be pronounce in

his hearing before he could recall they. in like manner he could tell

the name and discourse of the thing he beheld, _because he see they_.

have Johnson live, he would have know and call he Johnson. have Mr.

Sherman speak of Dolly, of Southbourne, of the London Docks, of the

“Meteor,” of any of the incident connect with the “Meteor’s” loss,

Holdsworth would have remember exactly as much as he hear. but,

in the absence of suggestion, his memory be powerless--absolutely

helpless--to generate independent conclusion as to the impression his

mind have receive previous to his rescue.



the real miracle lie in _this_ contradiction--in the death of memory,

date _up to_ the moment of the swoon in the boat; in its resurrection

to health and vigour, date _from_ the moment of his recovery.



he return to the chair that have be place for he near the

skylight, and Mr. Sherman, still not despair of arouse this

dormant faculty, go below and return with the parcel of thing that

have be take from Holdsworth’s pocket. these be give he one by

one, but he handle they without recognition.



“but you know their name?” say Mr. Sherman.



“yes. this be a knife. this be a watch.”



“they be your; find in your pocket.”



his hand tremble, and he gaze at they with devouring eye; but no

other idea be convey to he by Mr. Sherman’s assurance than the

bare fact that they be his property. he could not remember having

purchase or own they.



“it’s only a question of time, my man,” say Captain Duff, who stand by

look on at these strange ineffectual experiment.



no mere effort of imagination can do justice to Holdsworth’s suffering.

the feeling that he _ought_ to remember, couple with his incapacity

and the sense of the past holding, perhaps, memory of vital

consequence to he to recall, create a mental torture more afflicting

than it be in the power of any man, who have not suffer in this way,

to conceive. loss of memory, even in trifle matter, always partake

of the nature of pain. the fruitless effort to recall a name, a date,

beget uneasiness, and be soon convert into a positive torment. but

figure your mind haunt with a sense of the significance of the past,

not one faint glimpse of which it be permit to you to obtain.

figure yourself grope in a dense gloom, say: “there be thing

here which I _feel_ be precious to I, which be of deep consequence

to my happiness and to the happiness of other, but I cannot recall

their name or their aspect!” and meanwhile the subtle of your

instinct be drive you mad with importunity to prosecute your

search and lie the store of memory open to the light! this be bad

than blindness; it be death in life. the year that you have live

be cut away from your existence, and with they all the precious

accumulation of experience--love, sorrow, and think itself. God

preserve we all from such an affliction!









CHAPTER XVI.



SAILORS’ sympathy.





the “Jessie Maxwell” be bind for Sydney, New South Wales, freight

with what be call a general cargo--piano, nail, scent, and such

matter. she carry only one passenger, Mr. Sherman, whose cabin be

give he as a favour by Captain Duff, who partly own the barque, and

who have a great friendship for the gentleman, whose house he visit

in Sydney. Mr. Sherman be a merchant, do business in wool, tallow,

and other australian export, and have be visit London and Glasgow

for agent and consignee, and also to benefit his health by a sea

voyage. he be one of the most humane man in the colony, very well to

do, but prosperous by his own effort. he have a commanding figure,

a large, mild, intellectual eye, and the kindly smile that ever

grace the human face. the strong benevolence of his character make his

manner singularly fascinating; and before Holdsworth have know he a

fortnight, he be bind to he by a feeling of affection, which, though

it might have owe something of its depth to gratitude, must have

exist in a complete form, without reference to the great kindness

that have be show he.



the day pass quickly. in the equatorial latitude the barque be

becalm for two day; and then a gale rise, and drive she into the

south-east trade-wind.



if Mr. Sherman and Captain Duff have ever feel disposed to believe that

Holdsworth might have be a sailor, they consider that probability

entirely disposed of by his behaviour on the first day of the gale.



he be on deck when the wind be freshen, walk to and fro with

Mr. Sherman, whose arm he could now do without, having recover as

much of his strength as it seem likely he would ever get back. the

wind come up in a sudden squall, and take the barque on the starboard

beam. her royal be set, but the yard fortunately be trim to

receive the breeze. the vessel heel over under the great weight

of canvas, and there be some hurry amongst the man as they let go

the royal and topgallant halliard, though there be nothing in the

confusion to occasion the least alarm, even in a passenger who have

be a month at sea. but the effect of the squall upon Holdsworth be

extraordinary. as the vessel lie over, he grasp Mr. Sherman’s arm

with look of terror in his face, and run to windward, fling fearful

glance at the sea on the lee side. Mr. Sherman offer to help he to

go below, but he decline to leave the deck, and clung to the weather

mizzen rigging, apparently speechless with alarm.



as it come on to blow heavily, the man reef the topsail; and

Holdsworth literally tremble as the yard rush down upon the cap,

and the canvas thunder as the helmsman luff to enable the hand to

pick up the sail more easily.



“my dear friend,” exclaim Mr. Sherman soothingly, “you must endeavour

to control yourself. there be no danger, indeed. this uproar will

cease presently. we encounter much bad weather than this in the

North Atlantic, shortly after leave Glasgow.”



“yes, I be ashamed of my weakness; my nerve be go,” answer the

poor fellow. and then, see the man tumble up aloft and lay out

upon the yard, he cover his face with his hand, say he dared not

look, lest he should see they fall.



the ship be make snug presently; but the sea rise, and now and again

a shower of spray came fly over the forecastle and the main-deck,

which so violently agitate Holdsworth that he let go the rigging and

make for the companion. he walk like a paralysed man: his hand

outstretche, and his head turn about on his shoulder. he gain

his cabin and lay himself down in his bunk, exquisitely alive to his

pusillanimity, and weep over his incapacity to control himself.



the skipper go up to Mr. Sherman.



“our friend be no sailor. I think you can tell _that_, Mr. Sherman?”



“No; that be prove. the instinct of his old life, have he be a

sailor, would have keep up his courage without respect of his memory.

but let we bear in mind that his nervousness be the result of the

terrible experience he have go through. if illness--if fever, for

instance--will rob we of our nerve, how much more the unspeakable

agony of hunger and thirst, and the deadly, hopeless captivity and

exposure in an open boat for day, and maybe for week! it would drive

I mad!”



“ay, that be verra true. understand I, I be not speak

disrespectfully of the puir soul. I would only bid you obsairve by his

fear that he canna hae be a tarry-breek. the auld speerit would live

in spite o’ his nerve, and would have rise to the cry of the man

and the bocke o’ the water. that’s my opeenion.”



thus we may learn how some opinion, deliver in sound earnest, be

manufacture.



not a tarry-breek!



there have never sail out of any port in Christendom a finer, a more

courageous sailor than Holdsworth. what would Captain Duff have think

of his “opeenion,” have he be tell that that same halting, cripple

figure, who have hasten to his cabin with movement full of fear,

have be, only a month before, an upright, handsome man, with an eye

full of light and spirit, with nerve and skill equal to occasion

which would have overwhelm the honest Scotch skipper and leave he

nowhere, with a heart as gentle as a maiden’s and manly as Nelson’s;

always foremost in the moment of danger, with the voice of a trumpet to

deliver unerre command; a leader in measure of which the peril make

the stoutest-hearted tremble and stand still; scale the dizzy height

of whirling mast and spar, to whose summit he might have beckon

in vain to those very seaman of the “Jessie Maxwell,” whose movement,

now in the weakness of his crushed and broken life, he dared not even

watch?



of all sight, that of the strong and lion-hearte man, smite down by

sickness, by misery, by misfortune to the feebleness of an infant, to

the timidity of a girl, be surely the most affecting. such a man I have

see--a sailor--enter the forecastle full of the courage that make

hero of man, and leave it, after two month of confinement, with

nerve and health so shatter, that he have not dare to approach the

bulwark of the ship for fear of fall overboard!



give the full measure of your pity, kind reader, to such as these.

there be no form of human suffering whose pathos be more unqualified.



so Mr. Sherman, agree with Captain Duff, be confident that,

whatever else Holdsworth might have be, he be not a sailor. this

be, at all event, a negative discovery, which lop off one of the

numerous conjecture with which the mystery of Holdsworth’s past be

consider. strange it be to talk to the poor fellow, to hear his

rational language, his discussion, his sensible remark, and to feel

that he be speak, so to say, on this side of a curtain, behind

which be hide all the true interest of his life. once or twice

he stagger Captain Duff by a nautical question, the very nature of

which imply an intimate acquaintance with the sea; but his unaffected

timidity when the vessel roll, or when the weather be squally,

always drive the skipper back upon his first conclusion, and make he

think that the knowledge of sail, rope, yard, etc., which Holdsworth

display, have be pick up by he as a passenger, or even out of

book.



however, his marine allusion be few and far between. his horror of

the sea be remarkable, and he repeatedly inquire how long it would

be before they reach Sydney. moreover, he be render taciturn by

his ceaseless struggle with memory; and would pass whole hour lose in

thought, during which, it be observe, no gleam ever enter his face

to indicate his recurrence to any action, phase, or condition of his

past.



often, when the main-deck be clear, he would steal to the boat and

stand contemplate she with his hand lock, and his brow corrugate

with anxious thought. it be strange to see he run his eye over

she, handle the yoke-line, peer into the locker, and literally

grope for an inspiration.



once, the boatswain of the vessel, a shrewd English seaman, who, as

well as every other soul on board the barque, know of Holdsworth’s

total loss of memory, see he alone stare at the boat, come out of

his berth, and address he:



“they say, sir, that you don’t remember this boat?”



“I be try to recollect,” answer Holdsworth, look at he

with the expression of painful eagerness that be now almost a

characteristic of his face.



“see here, sir, when that there boat be sight, there be only two

person find aboard of she. you be one, and the other be the poor

fellow we bury. now, what I’m always say to my mate be this: this

here’s a ship’s quarter-boat, and more hand go in she than two when

she put off. now, sir, try and think how many there be.”



“I remember nothing. I would to God I could!”



“but don’t you reck’let what your thought be when the bread get

soak with the salt water?”



Holdsworth shake his head.



“here,” continue the good-natured boatswain, “might be the bread,”

point to the locker. “here,” he go on, point to the

stern-sheet, “might you be sit, steer of she; when up come a

sea and wash over you or the chap that have the yoke. now, may be,

you notice this, but can only groan, have to keep her head well

before it--put you for the man as steer. but you can think, for

all that; and it must ha’ scare the blood out of you to guess that

the little food that remain be all spoilt. cann’t you remember?”



Holdsworth, who have follow every syllable with tremble anxiety,

shake his head again.



“many thing have happen; something tell I that,” he answer; “but

I can remember nothing.”



“would you like to step into the foks’le, sir? perhaps you might see

something there as will help you,” say the boatswain, who be move by

Holdsworth’s hopeless reply.



they descend through the fore-scuttle into the dim semicircular

abode, with huge beam across the upper deck, from which depend a

number of hammock, and bunk all around, with their edge chip and

hack by the man, who use they for cut tobacco upon; and on the

deck, sea-chest and bundle, and pannikin and tin dish scatter

everywhere. the gloom be scarcely irradiate by a couple of lamp

resemble teapot with wick in their spout; and the face of the

man glimmer over the side of the hammock or in the darkness of

the bunk. up in a corner be a group of man, consist of a portion

of the watch on deck, assemble around two sea-chest, on which be

seat a couple of ordinary seaman, fasten down by nail drive

through the seat of their breech into the lid of the chest. their

sleeve be tuck above the muscle of their arm, and they be

decide, by mean of their fist, an argument which have be commence

half an hour before in the main-top. be nail very nearly at arm’s

length from each other, their effort to deal each other blow throw

they into contortion irresistibly ridiculous; but the looker-on,

have probably no very lively sense of the absurd, stand around with

grave face, thoughtfully chew tobacco, and now and then offer

the combatant a friendly suggestion where good to hit each other. some

man lie in hammock directly over the head of the pugilist, but take

no further interest in the proceeding go on under their bed, than

now and again to pop a burn and hairy face over the edge of the tight

canvas, and in polite and genteel term recommend the youngster not to

make too much noise if they don’t want to be nail fore and aft upon

the lid of the chest like bat.



“now, sir,” say the boatswain, advance a few step into the

forecastle, but not even deign to notice, much less offering to

interfere, between the combatant, “see if there ain’t nothing here to

give you an idea.”



there should have be many thing; for the forecastle of a ship be

as familiar to Holdsworth as any part of she; and though, when he

have first go to sea, he have sleep in a cabin near his father’s, he

have spend the great part of his time forward among the man, take

instruction from they in all kind of seafare work, and never more

happy than when squat on a chest, ply a marlin spike, and

listen to the yarn of the sailor around he.



the boatswain watch he with look of interest, which fade into

disappointment.



“be there nothing?” he ask.



“nothing,” say Holdsworth gaze blankly around he.



“but you know those thing be call hammock?”



“yes, I can tell you the name of everything that I see, but that don’t

help I.”



“well I _be_ blow!” mutter the boatswain, under his breath;

whereupon Holdsworth, thank he for the trouble he have take,

withdraw, pain by the glance and whisper of the man, and render

nervous and dispirit by the smell, the fight in the corner, and the

strong movement of the ship, feel here more than anywhere else.









CHAPTER XVII.



a presentation.





not know how to address or speak to Holdsworth, the skipper and Mr.

Sherman and the other call he Mr. H., that letter be all they

know of his name.



he be treat by captain and officer with great kindness, share

their table, and be even furnish by they with clothe, of which, you

may conceive, he stand very much in need.



none of they could doubt that he have friend, that he hold a position,

that he might have money; and they wait day after day for the return

of his memory, which be to solve the mystery his silence wrought,

and set he square with the world again. indeed, his utter incapacity

to recall the small incident connect with his past, be almost

provoking, despite its pathos. Captain Duff want to know the name of

the vessel that have be wreck, the port she hail from, the port

she have be bind to, her cargo, who her captain be. how astounding

to this healthy little man that such plain and easy question should

provoke no reply. perhaps, have he be keep without food and water

for six or seven day, subject to a long series of appalling mental

torture, expose on the sea in an open boat that be scarcely visible

a mile off, with Death the skeleton for a helmsman, he might have

moderate his wonderment--nay, even admit that such experience be

not only highly calculate to deprive a man of his memory, but to drive

he rave mad for the remainder of his life.



but the barque be draw near her journey’s end, in long. 120°. the

pale outline of Van Diemen’s land must heave in sight shortly away on

the port bow.



they be now in the beginning of November, and have be seventy-two

day from Glasgow. one bright morning Holdsworth be seat on the

skylight, with his eye on a book that have be lend he, but with his

mind groping, as it more or less always be, in the darkness that hide

the past from his sight. there be a blind man’s look on his face when

he be thus think, that be more conclusive of the ghastly sincerity

of his intellectual bereavement than anything that could be say or

do. you see by the blank expression in his eye that his gaze be

turn inward, and by his general air that the search he be make

be a fruitless one.



he have be take out of the boat a ghost--a gray skeleton; he have

pick up a little since that time, but his present aspect be merely a

slight improvement on the forlorn image he have present when rescue.

the familiar picture of a broad-shoulder, hearty, vigorous, handsome

young man, smooth-cheek, clear-eye, be go; in its place be a

waste shadow, a drooping, hesitate figure, with a characterisation

of deformity in its movement, though there be no positive deformity;

thin, feeble hand whiten by sickness, and a pale face hollow in

the eye, and make ragged with a growth of black beard and moustache.[1]



[1]



  “famine, despair, cold, thirst, and heat have do

  their work on they by turn, and thinn’d they to

  such thing a mother have not know her son

  amidst the skeleton of that gaunt crew.”



      _Don Juan_, Canto ii. 102.



the change be altogether too remarkable to have be effect

by physical suffering only; the heart have work the deep

transformation--the soft, tender, womanly heart bring face to face

with suffering it be constrain to contemplate, to hearken to

murmur of agony it could not soothe nor silence. consider, I pray you

consider, that he have behold five shocking death, each one accompany

by circumstance of unspeakable horror or misery. stretch over a

long space of time, they might, by give his heart breathing-space

between, have inure it to the inevitable scene; but crowd upon he

one after the other in quick succession, they grind his sensibility to

dust, and though he have now no memory whereby to renew the suffering

of these ten day, its blight effect be not the less clearly

visible in he, its operation have not be the less complete.



whilst he thus sate, as lonely now in a ship full of man as ever he have

be in the boat with Johnson die under the thwart, Mr. Sherman come

on deck and take a seat at his side. holdsworth be so engross that

he do not perceive his companion, and Mr. Sherman, unwilling to break

in upon his thought, remain silent, watch he.



suddenly Holdsworth turn; the blank dead look go out of his eye,

and he smile.



“so memory still defy you?” say Mr. Sherman kindly, and with just as

much anxiety as would let his companion understand the sincerity of the

interest take in he.



“yes,” answer Holdsworth, the smile fade off his face. “once--once

only, just now, a fancy come into my mind--I cannot explain its nature,

or what it betoken, but it vanish the instant I attempt to

grapple with it.”



“do it leave no impression--no idea whatever?”



“none. I can compare it to nothing well than a dim light steal

across the wall of a dark room and disappear.”



Mr. Sherman be silent; and presently say:



“what do you propose to do when you reach Sydney?”



“I have often think of that. I must seek work and wait.”



“wait until your memory return?”



“yes.”



“the captain and I be talk about you just now, and I suggest

that, be you to return to England, which I be persuade be your

native country, you might come across a friend who would give you your

memory back at once; or fail such a friend, you might encounter some

scene which would achieve the same end.”



“I don’t think I could bear another long voyage just yet,” answer

Holdsworth, glance at the sea. “what should make the water so hateful

to I? sometimes I fancy I must have pass many year upon it, and

that it have serve I badly.”



“oh, your dislike be easily understand. but now with regard to your

prospect. will it be wise for you to remain in Australia? you must

have friend at home--suppose England to be your home.”



“but how shall I find they?”



“ay, that’s it. much might be do if I could only discover your name.

I must make a list of all the name begin with H. the only question

be, would you know your name if you be to see it?”



“I would try,” answer the poor fellow humbly.



“well, now, I’ll tell you what I have in my mind,” say Mr. Sherman,

lay his hand kindly on Holdsworth’s. “I look upon you as a man whom,

having bring to life--for I take the credit of your recovery to

myself--it be my proper privilege to support. but I shall not allow you

to be dependent on charity. I have an office in Sydney, and you shall

have a desk in that office, and so earn a salary that will maintain you

in comfort. by-and-by your memory will come back. you will then return

to England, and I shall heartily wish you God’s blessing, for you have

suffer--yes, you have suffer very much--more than I, more than any

of we, can conceive.”



he break off suddenly, his voice falter.



Holdsworth seize his hand.



“Mr. Sherman ... good, kind friend ... God will reward you ... I have

suffer ... I ... I feel it here,” press his hand to his head.

“Sir, dear sir, believe I grateful!”



“I do believe you grateful, and it please I to believe it, for it

be a pleasure to serve the grateful. well, we have settle that.

but understand, though you will remain with I as long as you like,

yet, at the first prompting of your memory, I shall exhort you to

return to England, for I cannot persuade myself that you be not

leave relative and friend who will mourn you as dead, and suffer

unnecessary sorrow on your account.”



“yes--yes! I think this sometimes!” cry Holdsworth passionately.

“_that_ be the haunt thought--but it may not be! oh, sir, it

_cannot_ be! be there dear one belong to I, _could_ my memory

forget they? could they be _very_ dear to I, and be forget?”



Mr. Sherman draw a deep breath, and say, “no, I believe that would be

impossible. if love unite you to any person at your home, that love

would be an instinct to prompt you with an influence that should have

no reference to memory. but the mind of man be a great mystery.”



there be a short silence, and then Mr. Sherman ask: “do you ever

dream?”



“no.”



“but you may dream, though your mind cannot retain the impression of

its dream. could you awaken from a dream of home, your darkness might

be make light.”



“I have think of that,” answer Holdsworth, with the air of a man

who, have exhausted speculation, can find no inspiration in any kind

of suggestion.



“and you have no inclination to return to England?”



“it cannot matter where I be whilst my memory remain dead. England!

you speak a familiar word, and I know it be a country, but I cannot

bend my mind back to it. it give I no idea. I stretch my thought

over the great waste of water we have traverse in this ship, and

find nothing more than sea and sky--sea and sky! I can find no

country lie beyond--nothing to give I thought of home. oh, sir!”

he cry, “you cannot understand this! how should you? it be horrible

for I to look back and see the whole of my life eclipse--to see a

wall of darkness rear close behind I through which I _cannot_ see!

what thing precious, infinitely precious to I, may be hide! it

would ease I, sir, greatly ease I, if God would but illuminate my

mind only for a moment, that I might know what be lose to I. and the

loneliness of it all!--the feeling of desolation that come over I in

my solitude! Mr. Sherman, consider how lonely I be! not a voice, not

an echo of anything that may be dear to I, come out of that darkness

behind I. who would believe that memory be life, and the loss of it

bad than death?”



he bow his head and cover his face with his thin hand, and some

tear trickle through his finger.



God help he! suffering have subdue that manly nature to the feebleness

and weakness of a child, and tear, though there have be a time when

no anguish of his own could have wring such thing from his eye, now

easily rise with the expression of his feeling.



here Captain Duff, come up to Mr. Sherman, interrupt the

conversation, and Holdsworth, ashamed of the weakness he have no power

to control, go with his slow step and shaky movement below.



       *       *       *       *       *



when they be within two day’ sail of Sydney, the boatswain and two

hand come aft to the skipper, and the boatswain, touch his cap,

speak as follow:



“Sir, the ship’s crew have ask I to turn-to and say this here for

they: that they werry well know that the gentleman who have lose his

memory haven’t any clothe, and, maybe, no money; and as shipwreck’s

a thing that may happen to any of we, and as the poor gentleman’s

suffer more nor he’s allow to remember, though, as my mate Bill

here say, it ain’t werry hard to guess what he’s go through, as

there be some of the man for’ard who have bin short of water in their

time, and spin ’arrowing yarn such as I never heerd the like on; why,

what I be a sayin’ be this: that the ship’s company, barrin’ one,

which be an Isle o’ Dorg’s man--but _he’ll_ come over--want’s to make

up a purse o’ money for the poor gentleman, and though some o’ they

ain’t get much to give, leastway to spare, they’ll all lend a hand,

and only wait to hear if you and the mates’ll start the list, which ’ud

be more ship-shape.”



the boatswain deliver this speech with great hesitation--not from

nervousness, but from a perception of the puzzling nature of word,

which have a trick of fall athwartship along the course of his

meaning, and bring he up with a round turn. having conclude, he

glance at his mate to see if they approve, on which they nod a

good deal of hair over their eye, and then wipe their mouth with

their wrist.



“right you be,” say the skipper, address they with his eye fix

on the main-topsail, and his hand out to motion the man at the wheel to

keep she steady. “you can put I down for five pound, and Mr. Banks

and Mr. Anderson for a sovereign apiece. if they don’t fork out, I’ll

pay for they. Steady, I say, steady! dom it, man, you’re a point off

your course!”



that evening, the weather be mild and balmy, and a glorious breeze

right astern of the barque, Holdsworth be seat aft when the skipper

come up to he and say:



“the ship’s company have be make you up a purse, sir, as a token

of their sympathy with the temporal loss you must have sustain by

the wreck of the vessel, which there canna be a doubt you be on board

of, and with the suffering you endure in the boat. the bo’sun wait to

know when it’ll be agreeable to you to receive the gift.”



“no--no--really--the poor fellow must keep their money--I cannot

accept it,” reply Holdsworth, greatly agitate and move.



“oh, you must tak’ it, sir, or they’ll think you _paughty_, as we say

in Scotland. the bo’sun be wait at the capstan yonder, and the man

be on tiptoe for’ard--look at the head loupe in the fore-scuttle!”



Holdsworth leave his chair and go slowly to the boatswain. when

the hand see he draw near the capstan, they wriggle out of the

forecastle, out of the galley, out from behind the long boat, and come

slip aft, advance and draw back fitfully, and some on tiptoe,

to catch the speech. a seaman somewhere aloft come hand over fist

down a backstay, finally land himself on the bulwark, where he

stood look on.



the skipper, and Mr. Sherman, and the second mate approach; and when

the boatswain be go to speak, the captain call:



“draw close, my lad. the gentleman cann’t talk to you out of earshot.”



the man, like shy schoolboy, elbow each other into a small

semicircle, and stand stare and grin over one another’s shoulder.



“all ready, sir?” ask the boatswain.



“fire away!” answer the skipper.



the boatswain take off his hat and place it on the capstan; then draw

from it a handkerchief of the size of a union-jack, with which he dry

his face and mouth; he next fish in his coat pocket and produce

a small canvas bag, very neatly sew. this he hold in his hand, and

turn to Holdsworth.



“we don’t know your name, sir, and we’re werry sorry that we don’t,

’cause there’s a great deal in a name when you give a thing, ’specially

to they as have to speechify, and it help ’em along like. (‘so it dew,

mate!’ from the crowd, and several head nod emphatically.) I’m

a seafarin’ man myself, and come from Greenwich, and have no larnin’

teach I when I be a boy, and so the present company will please

hexcuse bad grammar and the like of that, seein’ that a seafarin’ man

don’t want to know many word besides those as consarn a ship. we’re

all sailor here, if the skipper will let I call he a sailor----”



“Ma conscience! and what else be I?” cry the skipper.



“well, as I be a sayin’,” continue the boatswain, look discomfit

for a moment, “we’re all sailor here, barrin’ you, sir, and Mr.

Sherman, and it’s only man as go to sea as can know what an awful thing

a shipwreck be, and what a bad look-out thirst and hunger be, and the

feeling that overcome a man when he be in a open boat mile away from

the shore. we reckon that you’ve pass through a deal o’ sufferin’,

and be sailor, it’s only right and proper that we should all of we,

from the skipper down, let you know by a well sign than mere talk,

which don’t go far, though it may be werry comfortin’ sometimes, when

you can understand what’s say to you--I say that we want to let you

know how sorry we be for you, and how werry grateful we be that we

belong to the wessel that pick you up; and so, sir”--here he hand

the bag to Holdsworth--“all hand club together to make up this

trifle o’ money jist to buy you a few thing when you get ashore; and

I’m proud to say there ain’t a man among we, though I _do_ think one

be goin’ to back out, that haven’t give something----Beggin’ your

parde a moment; they scrap o’ paper in the purse be horder write

by I, and sign by the man as haven’t get ready money about ’em, for

the captain to pay you the valley in silver which they bear in figure;

and that money to be deduct from their wage. that’s all, I think,

sir,” he conclude, look at the skipper.



a voice call out, “three cheer for the gentleman!” and forth burst a

roar from the iron throat of the man that make the deck ring again.



Holdsworth be unmanned, and look downwards, struggle with his

emotion. but glance up and catch sight of the swarm of rough

kindly face around he, he break away, so to speak, from his

agitation, and answer as follow:



“if it have please God to leave I my memory, I believe I could have

do my gratitude more justice, though I couldn’t have feel more

grateful. for, sometimes, when I have watch you at work, it have

come over I--not as a conviction--no! I wish it have; but as a mere

fancy only--that I too have be a sailor; and if that be so, then I

can understand why your kindness do not overcome I with surprise,

because I ought to know that sailor’ heart be the large, the

truest, the most manly in the world, that there’s not a sorrow their

purse will not fly open to relieve, and that a man, let he be what

he will, be never so well recommend to they as when he be poor and

break down and friendless. I don’t know how properly to thank you

for your generous gift. (‘we don’t want no thank,’ say a voice; ‘if

there’s enough to rig you out and put some ’baccy in your pocket,

that’s all we want.’) miserable, indeed, I shall be if my memory play

I false in this--if it do not suffer I to carry the recollection of

your kindness to my death-bed. may God bless you and guard you all back

in safety to your home.”



he cease, unable to say more.



“Sir,” say Captain Duff, “we have do no more than our duty in all

this business from begin to end. in the name of all hand I return

your good wish by pray that God may speedily give you back your

memory, and make you happy for the rest of your day, as a proper

compensation for what you have go through.”



he shake he by the hand, and then Mr. Anderson step forward; then

come the boatswain; and then an able seaman; and then another able

seaman; until presently Holdsworth be engage in shake hand all

round, scarcely a man quit the quarter-deck until a grasp have be

exchange.



when all this hand-shake be over, Captain Duff order rum to be

serve out to the man, who then return to the forecastle with a sense

of festivity upon they, and pass the rest of the second dog-watch in

singing song and dancing.









CHAPTER XVIII.



SYDNEY.





at nine o’clock on the morning of the third day from the time occupy

in the last chapter, a hand station on the look-out in the fore-top

send a roar from the sky:



“land right ahead!”



in half an hour’s time it be to be see from the deck, a mere blue

vision stretch eel-shape, upon the horizon.



Australia! the great and wealthy continent of which there be man then

live whose father could recall the time when this vast tract of land

have no place in the world’s knowledge of the Pacific.



of all sensation, the first glimpse of the land towards which a

ship have be steer for week and week, with seldom even so much

as a pass sail to relieve the monotony of the ocean, be the most

thrilling. the old seaman will desert his bunk or hammock to make

for the forecastle and have a look at the dim cloud. the pale-face

steward, seldom see on deck, sneak from his berth in the steerage

redolent of lukewarm soup and resonant with the ceaseless clattering of

crockery, to peer over the bulwark at the far-off coast. if there be

passenger on board, you be sensible of an uneasy movement among they,

strangely suggestive of mingle excitement and reluctance, as though

they be at once eager and loath to quit their float home, the

familiar cabin in which so many hour have be pass, the white deck

which have become to they what the pavement in front of your house be

to you. the ship be endear to they, and the hold she have upon they be

feel now that they shall shortly leave she. how nobly she have struggle

with the wave and the wind! what grandeur she assume when think

of with respect to the immense universe of water she have traverse

in safety! but a few week ago, one might say, she be in English

water, and now she be breast the wave of the antipode, raise

her graceful canvas to the heaven with almost conscious elegance, as

though exult in the knowledge of the feat she have perform--a feat

of which no repetition can ever diminish the wonder, the courage, and

the triumph.



and now the land loom large and bold upon the horizon, a gray and

iron coast, inhospitable enough to scare away all rash adventurer, one

might think, in search of new home and bright fortune.



what be Captain Duff about! do he mean to run his ship bow on to

those granite-colour cliff stretch to right and leave, with their

swart base marginate with a line of crawl foam? screw your eye up

attentively, and you will see two break in the shore. the bowsprit of

the “Jessie Maxwell” head for the break on the right. slowly the coast

grow clear. that break on the left be but a deceptive hollow, with a

vast block of rock lie in the blue, shark-stud water, upon which,

many year later, a noble ship call the “Dunbar” shall be wreck,

and, of a great crowd of human being, but one man save.



and now behold the miracle of the seaman’s art!



for week and week, count from the English summer, the “Jessie

Maxwell” have be surround by the ocean, direct through light and

darkness, through bright sunshine and howl tempest charge with

sleet and spray, by no more than a little needle, but gift with a

steadfastness of intelligence more unerring than the lofty that

humanity be endow with. for week and week this little needle point

and the helmsman obey, and onwards the ship sail through hundred of

mile of water, until one morning those on board awake and look ahead,

and lo! there be the land, with the ship’s head point accurately

towards the little cleft in the coast through which the great Bay of

Sydney be to be enter.



this bay be a vision of beauty. no hint of its existence be give until

you have sail into it. the effect produce by the contrast between

the rugged, iron, sterile coast beheld from the sea, and the loveliness

of deep blue water and summer island richly woode, and green hill

slope to the water’s edge, and sandy creek, with the heavy bush to

the right, and the tropical splendour of vegetation that meet the eye

upon the outlying land--all which form the noble bay into which you

pass through the narrow and most repellent of headland--be not to

be describe. one might think that Nature have stoop to the human

device of a pantomimic surprise, and rear the bleak australian coast

in this latitude for no other reason than to give effect to the grand

transformation scene she exhibit behind it.



to the eye of Holdsworth, weary by the eternal glancing or leaping

of the sea, how sweet and refreshing be the green shore, the house

peep out here and there upon the outskirt of the bush, the tree

overhang the margin of the island like live thing never weary

of admire their own shape! here and there a boat row from shore

to shore. small coaster lie at anchor, their sail clew up but not

furl, and the man lounge drowsily aboard of they. Hark to the

humming of the locusts!--comparable to nothing so much as the murmur

of a sleepy congregation recite the response in church.



Anon the city of Sydney open; at its foot a great semicircular basin

of water, with the mast of many vessel stand out against the

farther house, and the green hill back all. how picturesque from a

distance the combine colour of the streaming flag, the white-front

house, the green of the hill, and the heavenly azure of the sky! all

the way on the left run the house into the country beyond, and close

at hand be shaggy abutment of woode land, with deep shady recess

through which the sunlight sparkle on the emerald ground, with many

boat upon the water to give variety and life to the picture. far, far

away, almost like an echo from the old world, the strain of a band

play a hearty english melody could be hear.



no thoughtful man can behold such a colony as this without find

something at once pathetic and inspire in the spectacle. a great

rugged continent, lie hide in the distant Pacific main, be

encounter by human enterprise; and in a few year we witness town

and city throng its seaboard, and all along the surf-beat

shore be hear the hum of industry. we mark the inalienable love

of home, of the mother-country, in a thousand token, and find the

measureless ocean bridge by sympathy and memory, and Old England

renew in such form as make we scarcely conscious of our distance

from it; though sometimes think itself, when the thousand league

of water that flow between be remember, seem almost powerless to

present our beloved home to we as something real, so vague, so dim, so

inaccessibly remote have it become since we leave its shore. sign of

remember thing be about we. we think of the home love which give

that name to that street; which rear yonder house in the likeness of

one in the far-off land, that enshrine the emigrant’s most precious

memory of childhood; which parcel out yonder garden in the fashion

of the little tract of land in the distant country, whose soil be

sacred to the mind as the favour retreat of a beloved parent. the

very nomenclature by which the colonist dignify some mean spot or

small building by the name of a noble city or a spacious edifice in

the old home, be full of pathos, since it can signify no more than a

deep-rooted affection (not to be weaken nor divorce by the harsh

recollection of the impracticable struggle for bread which drive whole

family across the sea) for England, and a tender impulse to give

permanent form to memory which survive through many generation, and

create loyalty and patriotism among a people who owe nothing to the

country and the sovereign whom they reverence, and would at any moment

serve. british face be around we; british accent sound in our ear;

and on all side we behold sign of that british courage, audacity,

and genius, which grow sublime under our gathering appreciation of the

difficulty which have be conquer and the triumph which have be

achieve.









CHAPTER XIX.



HOMEWARD BOUND.





in the year 1832, within a week or two of the date that would make the

time exactly five year since the “Meteor” lie off Gravesend, wait

to embark her captain and start for the port it be her doom never to

reach, a large ship be sail slowly up the river, her poop crowd

with passenger, and many head range along her bulwark.



far away aft, hard by the wheel, stand a man thickly bearded, dress

in dark clothe, his arm fold, and his eye bent steadfastly upon

the pass shore. he be alone; for the rest of the passenger, of

whom there be many, be group about the break of the poop talk

to one another excitedly, or point to the house ashore, or watch

the steerage passenger on the main-deck cord their box, cram

their clothe into bundle, and make preparation for land that

afternoon.



there be something in the expression of this man’s face which would

have attract and detain your attention; a mixture of profound

melancholy and struggle surprise, cloud with what might have pass

very well for a mood of deep abstraction. his feature be thin and

haggard, the nose pinch and white, his eye dark and gleaming, and

sink in hollow, shag by eyebrow of black hair mingle with white,

which meet in a perpendicular seam in his forehead. he present the

appearance of one suffer from some incurable constitutional malady

which have waste the flesh off his bone, arch his back, hollow

his chest, and bring into his face a permanent expression of mingle

pensiveness and sorrow.



a round-face, brisk, and busy-look little man happen just then

to pop his person out through the companion, stand look awhile at

the shore with eager twinkle eye, and then, direct his gaze aft,

catch sight of our lonely individual and approach he.



“ah, Mr. Hampden! there you be! still puzzle, puzzling, eh?” he

exclaim in a hearty manner. “come now, you have see Folkestone,

Margate, the Reculvers, eh, now? Confess that those place have help

you to remember all you want to know.”



the person address as Mr. Hampden, but whom we will continue to call

by his proper name of Holdsworth, turn his eye from the shore and

answer with an effort, as though he could not at once break away from

his thought.



“I know all those place well; and there’s not a house yonder, I may

say, that don’t assure I I be on familiar ground. but they tell I

nothing. my past be still a puzzle, doctor, of which these scene be

only fragment. there be many more thing to come before I can piece

it into a whole.”



“what _be_ a cure for a decayed memory? what _ought_ to revive old

impression?” exclaim the little doctor, hammer a snuff-box with

his knuckle. “you’ll never know, Mr. Hampden, how you have weigh

upon my mind. I feel, sir, that I have no business to let you quit this

ship uncured. and yet, what more than I have do _can_ I do? I have

exhaust my imagination in question.”



“yes, doctor, you have be very kind, and I thank you heartily for the

interest you have take in I.”



“ay, but interest be of no professional use,” return the doctor,

sniff up a huge pinch of snuff. “we look to result in our calling.

I must say I should like to have be able to tell Mr. Sherman when I

get back that I leave you remember everything. eh, now? but I don’t

believe there’s a medical man live who ever encounter such a case

as your. so much density of mental gloom, sir, seem psychologically

impossible. if you could only have give I one end of the thread, so

to speak, I might have draw the whole skein out smooth. look about you

now. here be genuine Thames scenery, which, if you be an Englishman,

ought to go home straight to your heart and recall a thousand matter.”



Holdsworth stare around he, puzzle and bite his lip.



“I have often feel, and I feel now,” he exclaim, “that if I could

see something which be prominently identify with my past my memory

would return. when we be off Margate, I grow breathless--breathless,

doctor, believe I, under the shock of an indefinable sensation. I make

sure that my memory be about to rush upon I--oh! it be impossible to

explain what be inexplicable to myself. but there have be moment,

since we first enter the river, when I have feel that a revelation

be close at hand--and I have tremble whilst await the flooding in

of memory, which will not come--which _will_ not come!”



“it _will_ come. the power that you possess to remember the name and

quality of thing which you see, have long ago persuade I that

your memory be not dead, but torpid. keep your body up, when you get

ashore, with nourish food. walk the street constantly and use your

eye, and, when a recollection rise to the surface, don’t rush upon

it voraciously, but leave it to its own will. consider, memory be

nothing but shadow; you cann’t dodge and drive they into corner ...”



here somebody call to he, on which the little man shake Holdsworth’s

hand, and dart towards the group of passenger.



the ship be rapidly near Gravesend, where she would disembark her

passenger. the Thames look noble, with many vessel of all shape

and size break its shine water, with the house and wharf

ashore, with here and there a short wooden pier run into the

stream, and the green summer country smile beyond.



it be a bright July morning, and the air have an exquisite transparency

that so clarify and sharpen the outline of object, that it be

like look at they through highly-polished glass. just such a day

should greet all homeward-sailing ship, and make their inmate merry

with a foretaste of the shore-life they be to enjoy after their long

strife with the distant treacherous ocean.



Anon Gravesend open, and then the pilot volley some quick order

along the ship. down rattle staysail, and jib, and yard with their

spacious breadth of canvas; and the stately vessel, denude of her

tower costume, swam lazily into position off the town. then rise a

cry, “stand clear of the chain-cable!” and the second mate, on the port

side of the topgallant forecastle, brandish his arm as a warning to

the people on the main-deck to crowd out of the road.



“let go the anchor!”



Clank! clank! go the carpenter’s hammer. and then, with a deafen

roar, down plunge the mighty weight of iron, and tear the huge cable

with shriek through the hawse-pipe. the ship swing slowly around and

become stationary, with many hand aloft furl the sail, and the

quarter-deck throb with the movement and struggle of excited

passenger.



and now a dozen boat, some large some small, came tear through the

water to the ship. how the waterman pull! their face all vein, and

their arm all knot, and their hat anywhere! the canoe of cannibal,

sneak from the secret creek and hidden point of an unexplored

island, advance not more swiftly, nor, maybe, with feller or more

rapacious design, upon the intruder in their water, than do our

gravesend wherry upon this ship fresh from Australia.



many of the waterman be soon upon the quarterdeck, demand

monstrous sum to row three-quarter of a mile. you see box and

bundle seize and disappear, and excited ’tween-deck passenger

elbow a lane to the gangway, fire with a resolution to disembark

or perish, while child scream, and woman implore, and man

gesticulate, and even menace one another. one by one the wherry

put off, load to the gunwale with people and baggage. these wherry

return and return again, until the ship be clear of the majority

of her passenger.



“good-bye, captain,” say Holdsworth.



a sunburnt man in a blue cloth coat with gilt-button take Holdsworth’s

hand, and grasp it cordially.



“good-bye, Mr. Hampden, good-bye to you, sir. any time these three

month, if you have a mind to let I see your face, you will be able to

find I out by call at the Jerusalem Coffee House. I shall be glad,

sir, as we all of we shall be, to hear that London have stir up your

recollection and restore your memory.”



then the chief mate and second officer and some midshipman press

forward and shake his hand, and Holdsworth, point out his luggage to

a waterman, descend the gangway ladder, and be row to Gravesend.



       *       *       *       *       *



and now, whilst our hero, having be put ashore and eat a hurried

dinner, climb on top of the coach that be to land he at Southwark,

let we beguile an uninteresting interval by cast a brief glance

backwards.



on the arrival of the “Jessie Maxwell” at Sydney, Holdsworth have

accompany Mr. Sherman to his house, and be then and there

establish as an inmate as long as he choose to remain. he be also

give a clerkship in Mr. Sherman’s office, worth £250 a year, which be

by no mean an out-of-the-way price for a man’s labour in Australia in

those day, though in Holdsworth’s case the salary be render nearly

worth as much again by his friend provide he with board and lodge

free. the truth be (1) Mr. Sherman want an honest man in his office;

(2) holdsworth’s suffering, friendlessness, and perfect amiability,

couple with his deprivation of memory, which affect all whom he

converse with as something bad even than blindness, have obtain a

permanent hold for he on his generous patron’s sympathy long before

the “Jessie Maxwell” have sight the australian shore.



Mr. Sherman be a widower and childless. a maiden sister of his live

with he, a woman whose character and face be as like his as an egg

be to an egg. not know Holdsworth’s name, they agree to call he

Mr. Hampden, which would serve as well as any other, and which have at

least the merit of begin with the letter of his real name.



as Mr. Hampden he be introduce to Mr. Sherman’s friend, who take

a very great interest in he. indeed, some of these people go to

the extent of give dinner-party in his honour, and for a time he

be a lion. all this attention, mean in perfect kindness, greatly

disturb he, for his loss of memory make he singularly sensitive,

and his nervous system have entirely give way under the extraordinary

suffering he have endure. Mr. Sherman would have keep his secret, but

Captain Duff and the officer and man of the “Jessie Maxwell” go and

talk of he all over the city, and then the tale of his discovery and

rescue be publish in a newspaper and make the property of the public.



but the public soon forget he. the colony be young, and the New

Hollander have too many mine to sink, and house to build, and acre

to clear, and convict to protest against, and home oppression of

every specie to deal with, to keep his mind long fix on one object.

Holdsworth settle into a regular clerkly routine, and every day

improve himself in Mr. Sherman’s opinion, by the peculiar sweetness of

his amiability, and by his gratitude express in every delicate form

that could vehicle the emotion of his full heart.



there be an able doctor at that time practise in Sydney, and Mr.

Sherman invite he to his house, and introduce he to Holdsworth,

believe that, by skilful handling, it be possible to restore the

poor fellow’s memory. but the doctor after a few week shake his head,

and pronounce the case hopeless, or at least beyond the reach of human

skill.



indeed, rarely have a more curious and baffle problem be submit,

than Holdsworth’s mind in those day.



here be a man capable of recollect with precision every incident

that have befall he since his rescue, exhibit shrewdness in
