conversation, and accuracy in matter of current fact. his intellect

be as healthy as that of the healthiest-head man who converse with

he, but up to the period of his rescue everything be in darkness. the

conjecture which be offer he--so close to the mark some of they,

that they brush the very skirt of real fact, and tell the truth by

implication--convey no idea. his eternal rejoinder be no more than

a shake of the head. have he be a sailor? do he remember the port

from which he sail? the rig or name of the vessel? his native town?

such question, and hundred of they, be ask, but though he grasp

familiar name with almost passionate eagerness, they establish no

faint clue as to his real past. and then inquiry become at last

no well than fruitless importunity, be drop, and Holdsworth

be consider incurable.



yet this could hardly have be think, have those who give in this

opinion be conscious of the under-current of secret, but not the

less powerful, yearning, absolutely objectless, scarce own definite

form, which yet the restless instinct of the man urge with greedy

emphasis. these movement be, indeed, purely spiritual--the action of

the soul grope in her cell and search for that window of the mind

which have be blacken, and through which no light could break. of

the mental torment this intellectual blindness occasion, no word

that I possess can describe the anguish. month after month go by and

still find he search heart-brokenly in the gloom for some image,

some substance, some sign, that should appease the piteous craving of

his instinct, _which know all, but could not speak_.



whatever feminine tact could suggest to give light to his mind, Mr.

Sherman’s sister do. she make out a long list of name begin with

H, trust that one among they might be his, and that the sight of it

would recall many thing or all.



but, long and patiently compile as the list be, many name there must

be which she would omit; and amongst they his own. then she make out a

list of the name of the ship; but here be an endless job, prosecute

for a long while with benevolent industry, and then abandon in

despair. she read the european paper carefully, hope to find some

account of the loss of the vessel in which, it be surmise, holdsworth

have be a passenger; but no such account ever reward her search.

Numerous be the other remedy she resort to, but none of they

produce any result.



a few month of such unavaile work would soon extinguish hope. both

she and her brother desist at last from their merciful endeavour in

the full and final conviction that nothing but the hand of God could

ever draw aside the black curtain that hang over Holdsworth’s past.



but not to dwell at needless length upon this part of the story:



More than four year have pass since Holdsworth have arrive in

Sydney. Mr. Sherman have long learn to think of he as settle in the

colony, have increase his salary, and congratulate himself not only

on the possession of a valuable and trustworthy assistant, but upon a

pleasant, amiable, and thoroughly gentlemanly companion. no expression

of a wish to leave have ever escape Holdsworth’s lip. he appear not

only content, but resign to the affliction that have practically

deprive he of all knowledge of his past existence.



he come down to breakfast one morning with a face betoken great

agitation. Mr. Sherman be in the breakfast-room, and instantly notice

Holdsworth’s air of bewilderment and distress.



“Mr. Sherman,” exclaim Holdsworth at once to he, “do you remember

tell I that it be possible for my memory to be revive by a dream?”



“yes--have it happen?”



“I cannot tell; but this much I know, that a voice sound last night

in my ear, and bid I return at once to England. it be a woman’s

voice--it have a clearly-remember tone--and I know it in my sleep; but

when I awake and try to recall it I could not.”



“but your dream?”



“that be all.”



“be your dream merely confine to the utterance of this voice?”



“I can remember nothing more than the voice.”



“and you cannot recall whose voice it resemble?”



“no.”



Mr. Sherman be silent, and Holdsworth watch he with anxiety, that

be almost pathetic, so eager be his hope that his friend would find

some light in this dim and curious night-fancy to help he with.



“I can see nothing serviceable in this,” say Mr. Sherman presently,

“but it be hopeful. wait a while. this voice may return, or you may

dream something more tangible. remember,” he add with a smile, “that

the morning light do not flood the world suddenly. the pale, faint

gray come first, and there be many gradation of brightness between

the first peep of dawn and the rise of the sun.”



but though Holdsworth wait, the voice do not return. nevertheless

it have sound in his ear to some purpose. day by day a longing grow

in he to return to England, which become in time deep and fervent

and irresistible. superstition be the root of this yearning; but the

poor fellow, urge by his instinct into an eternal searching amid

the darkness, would scarcely pause to consider the nature of his keen

desire, but submit to it as an ordinance from God commission to impel

he into the divine light of memory.



Mr. Sherman watch his increase restlessness in silence, wait for

he to declare his intention. he announce it one day.



“Mr. Sherman, I feel guilty of deep ingratitude in harbour a wish

to leave you. but my longing to return to England have become so strong

that I can no long resist it. God know if I be not take a foolish

step in voluntarily quit so good and belove a friend. but what be

these instinct which govern I? ought I to blind myself to they? be

they not give I for some end which can only be accomplish by my

obey they? I do not know what I be leave you to seek; but I _feel_

that whatever my past may hold which be precious to I be to be find

in England.”



“if you have this confidence in your impulse,” answer Mr. Sherman,

“you be right in obey it. I be at least sure that your memory

stand but a poor chance of recovery in a strange land, surround

by object which have no possible reference to your past, and can,

therefore, have no value as an appeal. I shall be sorry, very sorry, to

lose you, Mr. Hampden, but I think I can trace the hand of Providence

in this longing of your, and in all humbleness and sincerity I ask

God to bless your endeavour and restore you the illumination of your

memory.”



this resolution, now take, be final. Mr. Sherman’s encouragement

give new strength to Holdsworth’s wish, and the restlessness that

beset he become almost unbearable to he. in ten day from that date

a ship name the “Wellington” sail for London; she be to carry many

passenger, but one saloon berth be still vacant, and that Mr. Sherman

himself procure and pay for, for Holdsworth. nor do his kindness

stop with this. a few day before the ship leave Sydney he ask

Holdsworth if he have save any money.



“yes, four hundred pound.”



“come! that will help you along for a time. Dr. Marlow, a friend of

mine, be attend an old lady to England. I have explain your case

to he, and beg he to give you his close attention. he be clever,

and the long term of intimacy you will enjoy before you reach London

may be productive of some good to you. and now do I the favour to put

this in your pocket,” he continue, hand Holdsworth a little parcel.

“no need to examine its content now. it be a small gift from my sister

and myself. you will find my address inside it, which will remind you

to write to we; for be sure that nobody can take more interest in you

than we do. above all, remember that if ever you should want a friend,

you will find two very steadfast one in Sydney, who will rejoice to

welcome you back.”



the parcel contain bank-note to the value of three hundred pound.









CHAPTER XX.



an INSPIRATION.





in the fine old time--the good old time--a short journey take a long

time; and it be evening when the Gravesend coach put Holdsworth down

at the door of an old inn in Southwark name the “Green Dragon.”



he be now in London, but in an unfamiliar part of it, and he stand for

some minute gaze up and down the wide long street, with its hurrying

crowd, and throng vehicle, and endless shop, without get one

idea more from it than ever he have get out of Pitt Street or George

Street, or any other street in Sydney.



it matter little to he where he should sleep that night. he have as

yet form no plan as to how he should act with respect to begin

the inquiry which be to give he back his life’s history. so he

enter the bar of the “Green Dragon” and ask for a bedroom, with

which he be at once accommodate.



on descend the stair he be encounter by a very polite waiter,

who beg to receive his order for refreshment. the house be a

very old-establish one, and the waiter, with a smile of concern, as

though the necessity be a melancholy one that oblige he to suggest

such obvious truth at that time of day, venture to observe that the

gentleman might travel the whole breadth and length of the United

Kingdom without meet with well wine and choicer cooking than be

to be find at that inn. on the strength of this and a small appetite

Holdsworth order supper, and be conduct to the coffee-room, where

he seat himself, the only occupant of the dark bare apartment, at

a table furnish with a mustard-pot large enough to have supply

a hospital with poultice, and amuse himself as good he could with

stare at some grisly, fade print after Hogarth, and a map of

London, to which several generation of fly have contribute square,

street, and blind alley nowhere to be find in the metropolis proper.



having eat his supper, he be lean back in his chair, with a half

resolution in his mind to stroll forth into the street for an hour,

and see what suggestion his wandering might obtain for he, when the

waiter come up, and lean confidentially upon the table, inform he

that there be an “’Armonious Meeting go on in the public room at

the bottom of the passage, and if the gentleman like, the sperit he

be pleased to horder could be serve he there.”



“who be they?” ask Holdsworth.



“all sort, sir. the ’armony be do by some gent as life in the

neighbourhood, who look in every Wednesday night to drink and converse.

the governor take the chair, and every gent as be stoppin’ in the

house be make welcome. I think you’ll be please, sir.”



“this be dull enough, at all event,” say Holdsworth, look around

he. it be too early to go to bed, and he feel too tired to take his

half-project stroll. so, conceive that a quarter of an hour’s

inspection of the convivialist in the public room might cheer he up,

he rise and follow the waiter down the passage.



the scene into which he be admit certainly want no feature

of liveliness. the room be long and low pitch, with two immense

grate in it, and wooden mantelpiece carve into all kind of quaint

embodiment of greek and roman mythology. common brass sconce be

affix to the wall with a couple of candle in each. around be

picture of fighting and theatrical celebrity of that and an early

day; Humphreys and Mendoza, strip to the waist, and work into

each other’s eye in wonderful style, watch by a distant and pensive

crowd in hat of the Tom and Jerry school, uncomfortably tight

breech, and coat collar above their ear; kemble in fur; Incledon

dress as a sailor; Braham, as little Isaac, in the “Duenna;” and

Dicky Suett, with a thing like a balloon come out of his mouth, and

“oh la!” write upon it. at the head of a long table sit a stout

man in a striped yellow waistcoat, a bottle green coat, and a white

neckcloth; several black bottle, steam jug, and a plate of lemon

be in front of he. and down the table on either side be seat a

number of individual, some of they dress in extravagant style, a few

clothe soberly, and most of they smoke cigar, or rap snuff-box,

laugh, talk, and drink from fat one-legged tumbler.



as Holdsworth enter, either a speech, a song, or a sentiment have

just be deliver, for there be a great hammering go on, mingle

with cry of “Bravo!” the “cheer,” who be the promoter of, and the

sole gainer by these Wednesday festivity, bow to Holdsworth, and

get on to his leg, come round and bid he welcome in the name of

all the good fellow there and then assemble, and give he the sign,

which holdsworth, not having be make, do not take. he then lead he

to a vacant chair between two of the more soberly clothe of the company,

and having receive and transmit his command to the waiter with a

host-like and hospitable air, as though such a low arrangement as a

reckoning have no existence, resume his place at the head of the table,

knock loudly with his knuckle, and call upon Mr. Harris for a song.



on this, up start a thin young man with a yellow beard, and, lean

on his hand, gaze slowly around he with a leer and perfectly

self-possess bloodshot eye. whereat there be a laugh.



“Gentleman!” he begin.



“Order! silence!” cry the landlord. “Mr. ’Arris have your ear!”



“and I wish I could say lady: I be ask to sing a song, and I’ll

do so with the great of pleasure. but before I begin, permit I

to make an observation; as I don’t want to wound any gentleman’s

feeling, though no fear of that kind will prevent I from express

my sentiment, which be those of a Briton and a reformer, who have no

opinion of the present, look upon the past with contempt, and only

live for the future.”



“hear!” from several reformer.



“there’s a good deal to be say against this age; and there’s no

abuse which the past don’t deserve! the past! gentleman, it do for

our grandmother. the present! it do for our father. but we,

gentleman--we who possess young and ardent minds----”



“give we your song!” cry a voice.



“we, gentleman--we, the young blood of this great nation--we,” cry

the orator, swinge his fist, and nearly knock a cigar out of the

mouth of a man at his side, with a face on he fill with idiotic

admiration, “be for the future!”



an old man utter a cheer; the speaker then cough, swallow a

draught of brandy-and-water, expand his chest, run his finger

through his hair, and begin as follow, throw out his arm in

approve comic style:--



the day when I be young.



  of the day when I be young, sir,

    sing the splendour and the fame,

  when the field and wood among, sir,

    Traps be set to guard our game;

  when our clergyman get drunk, sir,

    and our Prince be make of waistcoat,

  when our soldiery have spunk, sir,

    and wear epaulet and faced coat.

        CHORUS--sing the day when I be young!

                         such a song be never sing!



  when Madeira, port, and sherry

    be such wine as make man wit:

  when our song be coarse and merry,

    and our pocket full of writ!

  when we fight like hungry Spartans,

    and tell tale like Jemmy Twitcher’s;

  when cognac be drunk in quartern,

    and October ale in pitcher!

                          sing the day, etc.



  when the House be full of quarrel,

    and our husting the arena

  for dead cat and bilious moral

    and the music of hyena!

  when our Avershaws be strangle,

    and our Mrs. Frys be preach:

  and our priest and bishop wrangle,

    and our patriot a-screeching!

                          sing the day, etc.



  when a Tory _be_ a Tory,

    Armour’d tight in old tradition,

  quote nothing but the hoary,

    and a friend to superstition:

  hate Irishman and priest, sir,

    all exciseman and dissenter,

  holy fast and holy feast, sir,

    whig and Jews and ten-pound renter!

                          sing the day, etc.



  when our fiddler be true artist,

    and our singer all have voice:

  Ere our labourer be Chartists,

    and the land be full of noise.

  when our “blood” be break knocker,

    smash bell all free and hearty,

  and when little else could shock we

    but report of Bonaparte.

                          sing the day, etc.



  when our coach turn’d we over,

    and our watchman snore in alley:

  when it take a day to Dover,

    and a week or two to Calais.

  when Jack Ketch be hang woman

    who steal bread for starve baby,

  and our rogue be just as common

    as new honey in old May bee.

                          sing the day, etc.



  never more shall we survey, sir,

    Times so splendid and so stirring,

  social life and taste more gay, sir,

    law and statesman more unerring.

  fight and faction more unsparing,

    tory truer to tradition,

  foreign policy more daring----



  (here the vocalist take a deep breath),



      and more brutal superstition!



the sound excite by this song be somewhat discordant, owe to the

bravos be mingle with hiss. Mr. Harris resume his seat with a

contemptuous expression, and the chairman, rap the table, call

out:



“gentleman! ’issing ben’t ’armony to any ear but a goose’s!”



“I don’t like the sentiment of that song,” exclaim a man at the

bottom of the table.



“why not, sir?” demand Mr. Harris, warmly.



“first of all, I don’t understand ’em,” say the other.



“oh!” say Mr. Harris with a sneer.



“much of what that song say be to be apply to the present as well as

to the past,” observe an old gentleman, looking stagger at his own

boldness in talk amid a silence. “for my part, I don’t think a man

fight fair who use a two-edge sword.”



several voice murmur acquiescence.



“I’m for the future,” say Mr. Harris, “and tell you so at the

beginning.”



“see here, gentleman,” call out the landlord; “the meaning of Mr.

Harris’s song, so far as I understand its hallusion, be this: he

suppose himself to be old----”



“no, I don’t,” growl Mr. Harris.



“quite the contrary, _i_ think,” snarl a little grocer near the

chairman. “I reckon there’s more swaddling-clothe nor expirience in

_they_ sentiment, or I’m go deaf since I sit down.”



“the meaning of my song be just what it say,” retort Mr. Harris.

“I’m not ashamed of be a reformer. well man than I or any other

gentleman in this room, beg _nobody’s_ pardon, have be reformer.

thank God, my politic be not of a kind to call up my blush when I

own they.”



as the Tories in the company judge that something offensive be mean

by Mr. Harris, several person speak at once, and a clamour ensue

which threaten to establish the meeting on any other basis than that

of harmony. indeed, one man, who be nearly intoxicate, go so far as

to get upon his chair and exclaim, while he brandish his fist, that

if he have it in his power, he would hang every Whig in the country.

and there be no tell what further extravagance of language and

gesture he might have indulge in, but for the prompt interference of

a neighbour, who, catch hold of his coat-tail, pull he under the

table.



however, by dint of shout pacific language at the top of his voice,

the chairman succeed at last in restore tranquillity. More grog

be bring in, snuff-box be hand about, hand be shake

across the table, and loud cheer greet the sentiment deliver by a

be the music of humanity.” Mr. Harris apologise for having sing

anything distasteful to the company, who he hope be all his very

milder channel, and everybody do his good to look harmonious.



though Holdsworth be a good deal amuse by this scene, and by the

equal to endure very much more of this social harmony, and sit

“a stranger, I make bold to think, sir?”



“yes,” answer Holdsworth.

“to tell you the truth,” reply Holdsworth, who imagine that his

now, sir, as a man who have visit other country, and observe human

“I hate rubbish as much as any man,” reply Holdsworth.



his companion look strike and delighted.



“your hand, sir. permit I to shake it. I love a Whig, sir. here’s to

your good health.”



“be you a native of this country, sir?” he continue, glance at

Holdsworth’s dress, which have a decidedly colonial cut.



“I believe so.”



“but not a resident?” say the other, whose turn of mind be decidedly

inquisitive.



“no.”



“now what part might you have come from, sir, if you’ll excuse the

liberty?” ask the man confidently.



“from Australia.”



“God bless my heart and soul! you don’t say so! dear I! Australia!

be it possible? I consider myself a bit of a traveller; but in your

presence, sir, I feel my insignificance.”



Holdsworth laugh, but make no answer.



“they say that Australia be a wonderful country, sir: that you grow

cherry with the stone outside, and that your parrot be like

sea-gull. fine climate though, I believe, sir, if you will pardon I?”

“and yet not equal to our?”



“perhaps not.”



“they talk of scenery, sir. now I be never out of England in my life,

though there’s not a hole or corner in it that I don’t know. but what

can equal english scenery? take Devonshire. take Cumberland. be you

ever in those county?”



“never.”



“Yorkshire?”



“no.”



“talk of desolation--see the moor: great plain of the colour of tripe

stretching for mile, with one dwarf tree for every league of ground,

and that’s all. now, sir, my taste may be wrong, or perhaps it’s right;

I wouldn’t flaunt it in any man’s face, though I’d hold on to it if I

I’m the most partial to?”



“I cannot imagine.”



“Kent, sir!” exclaim the man, draw back triumphantly.



the name send a thrill through Holdsworth. he prick his ear and

look at his companion earnestly.



“the Devonshire people may crack up their county, but give _I_ Kent.

I be a native of Kent, sir!”



“indeed!”



“I be bear at Canterbury. ever see the cathedral?”



“Canterbury Cathedral!” mutter Holdsworth, struggle to grasp an

illusive, half-form fancy that flit across his mind.



“take the country about Hanwitch, now ...”



“Hanwitch!” echo Holdsworth. the name pierce he as a sword might.

he press his finger tightly over his eye, his face turn white,

and his whole body tremble.



his companion stare at he.



“do you know Hanwitch?” he ask, wonder if this singular-look

“the name strike I,” answer Holdsworth, remove his hand and

emotion excite by the name of that town.



“there’s not a spot of land anywhere within thirty mile of Canterbury

all round,” continue the man, look at Holdsworth watchfully, “that

near it which be pretty than anything I’ve see in any other part

country can show in the way of good view, take a run down to Hanwitch.”



he pull out a pocket-book, and extract a card, hand it to

Holdsworth, observe in a tone that at least show he have regain

his confidence in his neighbour:



“show that, sir, at the bar of the ‘three star’ at Hanwitch, and if

you don’t get every attention, be good enough to write to I, and see

if they don’t lose my patronage.”



Holdsworth look at the card, whereon he might have see a very

commonplace name, print in capital, with “Commercial Traveller”

squeeze into the corner; but he see nothing. a name have be

pronounce which quicken the dormant memory in he into a vitality

that threaten to make it burst through the shell that imprison it,

and proclaim all that he passionately long to know.



Powerless must his mind have be not to find in the name of the

Hanwitch inn the magic to give he back his memory. could not his

heart recall the sweet day he have spend in Hanwitch with dolly at his

side?--the sweetest, happy day of all the day he have pass in the

brief three month during which they have be together? one might have

think that, save her own dear face, there be nothing more potent

to roll back the deep mantle of darkness, and lie bare the shine

panorama of those far-off time, than the name of the inn in whose deep

bay window they have sit link in each other’s arm, watch the soft

sunshine shimmer through the summer leave, and the clear river

wander gently along its emerald-bank channel.



further conversation be out of the question for a while, by the

chairman hammer on the table and call silence for a song. the

disagreeable effect produce by the last song have completely pass

away, and the landlord think that another “ditty,” as he call it,

might safely be sing.



a very corpulent man stand up, with a face upon he of which the

quantity of flesh have work the expression into an aspect of fix

amazement. an immense blue-spot cravat adorn his throat, and long

streak of hair fall slant down his cheek. his small clothe and

arm-sleeve be distressingly tight, and suggest that any display

of pathos or humour, of gesticulation or laughter, would be in the

high degree inconvenient. it be not hard to guess that this fat man

sing comic song, that he drop _every_ h, and that he be in the

eat-line, in a commercial sense.



he be salute with a round of laughter, which, be hammer down, he

begin in a soft, oily, tenor voice--



  “a dawg’s-meat man he love a voman,

  Sairey her name vos--not uncommon;

  he have vun eye, and he hown’d a barrer.

  Coopid up’s vith his bow and let fly a arrer.

  ‘o dear!’ cry this dawg’s-meat man,

  Fingerin’ his buzzum and look vith his eye;

  ‘Vot can this be a-sticking in my tan?’

  Ven Sairey draw near a-lookin’ very shy.

  ‘tell I,’ sez he, ‘the name o’ this here thing?’

  ‘vy,’ sez Sairey vinkin’, ‘it’s vun o’ Coopid’s dart.’”

              etc.                  etc.



this song give such exquisite satisfaction to the company that, on

his conclude it, he be entreat, amid cheer, to sing another;

on which, square his breast, but preserve his wooden face of fat

astonishment, he begin as follow:



  “there vo a ’ot pieman as vurke in the Strand,

      singing hey, ho! ’ot pie, all ’ot!

  Vun night he be kickin’ his ’eel at his stand

  Ven who should come up but a lady all grand,

  Vith a dress all of satin, and ring on she ’and,

              and she ast for a pie

              do this lady, oh my!

                   let we cry.

      Singing hey, ho! ’ot pie, all ’ot!



  “now, our friend be genteel, as all pieman should be,

      singe hey, ho! ’ot pie, all ’ot!

  and he sez to this lady, so grand for to see,

  ‘my pie, mum, be mean for sitch people as I,

  for poor cabby and sitch; not for folk of degree,’

              and she jest sez, ‘o fie!

              hand I quickly a pie,

                  or I’ll cry.’

      Singing hey, ho! ’ot pie, all ’ot!”



I spare the reader the remain six verse of this delectable song.

the company join in the chorus with the full force of their lung,

and so exhilarate the fat vocalist, that at the end of the second

verse he push away his chair, and fold his arm on his breast,

actually dance an accompaniment to the word, amid shriek of laughter

and wild stretch forward of neck at the farth end of the table

to see he. holdsworth’s companion laugh until he grow faint, and

then, to recover his strength, drink brandy and water, and then laugh

again. the song be encore, genuine vulgarity seldom fail to

please; and then a brief breathing-space of silence fall, holdsworth

say to his companion:



“will you tell I how I be to get to Hanwitch from here?”



“certainly. all you’ve get to do be, step across to the ‘Canterbury

Arms’--it’s five minute’ walk from this house on the left--the coach

start for Canterbury at half-past seven in the morning, every day.”



“thank you,” say Holdsworth, who find it impossible, amid the renew

hubbub of conversation that have burst out, to ask some question about

Hanwitch, which might help he to understand the longing that possess

he to visit it.

holdsworth rise. but Holdsworth merely wish he “good night,” and

slip out of the room unnoticed by the company, who be at that

moment busy in entreat the fat man to give they another song.



the cool air and silence of the passage be a great relief after the

heat and noise of the public room. it be now draw near to eleven

light to his bed-room by a chambermaid with ringlet and black

eye, who probably feel surprised that her charm attract no notice

whatever from the gentleman, who seem to find pleasure in no other

object than the carpet on which he trod.



Holdsworth close the door, and a whole hour pass before he rise to

remove his clothe. there be something in the recollection of the

thrill which the name of Hanwitch have send through he, that impel

he to bend the whole energy of his mind to the word, and he strove

with memory passionately and fiercely, but could not wrench a syllable

from she. he repeat the name until it lose even its sense as the

designation of a town. nevertheless, every moment make his longing

to visit it deeply. there must be _some_ reason why this name have so

stir he. the name of other english county and city have be

pronounce before he and by he over and over again, but they touch

no chord, they awake no echo in his mind, however dim and elusive.



if memory would only define the object he seek! this it would not

do. he be a wanderer, obey the dictation of blind instinct, which

urge without guide he. of all his past, nothing be present to

his mind. he know not what he seek. his be an affliction crueller

than blindness, for a blind man could say: “a beloved one have stray

from I. I seek she. I see not, indeed, those who surround I, but my

mission have form and substance in my mind, and my inquiry cannot

_always_ prove fruitless.” but Holdsworth be command by a mysterious

emotion which control without enlighten he. _something_ have be

lost--_something_ be to be find. he feel his want, but could not

explain it to himself. no man could help he, since no man could guess

what be the thing he look for.



when he leave his chair he sink upon his knee and ask God in broken

tone to help he--to direct he into the path that should lead he to

the light--to aid he in his yearning to re-illuminate his memory.









CHAPTER XXI.



for HANWITCH.





if a man have one good reason for be grateful for live in these

time, it be because they be _not_ coach day.



the old stage-coach, the wellerian coachman, the spruce guard with his

horn and his joke, the fat people inside, the gruff people outside,

all contribute a picturesque detail to the age they belong to; but I

have generally find that the more picturesque an object be, the fitter

it be to be survey at a distance only.



if in the day to which the old stage-coach belong it never rain,

it be never cold, one be never in a hurry, there be no mission

of life and death to make one curse the delay of a moment--if one’s

companion be always good-temper, and one’s body be so constitute

as to endure jerk and jolt and a sit posture for hour at

a stretch without inconvenience--then the old stage-coach may be

conceive to have be a very agreeable mean of locomotion. but as

I have be inform by several elderly gentleman that the weather

forty year ago be pretty much the same sort of weather that it now

be, that stroke of death and stroke of business require immense

despatch happen then as they happen every day; in a word, that in

most atmospherical, moral, and civic respect the early year of this

century differed but very little from these its mature day, I can

only repeat, in spite of the protest of several venerable friend who

seem to find most thing (even whilst enjoy they) objectionable

which be not as aged as themselves, that if we have one excuse well

than another for be on good term with the time we live in, it be

because the picturesque old stage-coach make no condition of our daily

existence.



Hanwitch be about fifty mile from London. to-day a traveller would be

carry the distance in about an hour and three-quarter. holdsworth,

start at half-past seven in the morning, would, provide that the

coach do not break down or overturn, reach the town at about four

o’clock in the afternoon.



he awake at half-past six, and at once rise. the morning be a bright

one, but all the effort of the sunshine to squeeze itself through

the wire blind and dusty pane of the coffee-room window could not

avail to communicate the faint spark of cheerfulness to the dingy

apartment, with its bare table and blue-colour look-glass over

the chimney, and the old-fashioned print around the wall, suggestive,

one know not why, of London milk and discolour blanket.



the waiter come in, look deject, limp, and fluey, and perhaps

to pay Holdsworth out for neglect to leave word at the bar before

go to bed, that he should want to breakfast at an unreasonable hour,

declaim a bill of fare, nearly every item of which, as Holdsworth

name it, he declare could not possibly be get ready before half-past

eight. cold ham and tea must suffice, with which order the waiter

sleepily withdraw, and after a long absence return--when Holdsworth

be on the point of start up and leave the house in the full

belief that he should miss the coach--bear a teapot of which the

content look like rain-water, a loaf of bread as hard and slippery

as glass under the knife, a lump of butter of the colour and perfume of

soak cheese, and a ham of which what be not bone be brine.



very lightly break his fast with these thing, Holdsworth call

for his bill, and obtain the service of the Boots to carry his

portmanteau, which be all the baggage he have bring with he from

Australia, walk to the house from which the Canterbury coach start.



it want but ten minute of the starting-time, but no coach be

visible. however, it be up the yard, and would be bring round in a

few minute, the book-keeper say. as these few minute threaten to

expand into half an hour, Holdsworth enter the bar of the “Canterbury

arm” to obtain a biscuit and some brandy-and-water, partly to complete

the wretched breakfast he have make, and partly to exterminate the vile

flavour of the tea that linger in his mouth.



when he return, the coach be out, the horse be in, several

passenger be get into, or climb on top of, the vehicle; the

coachman, muffle about the throat as though the month be November,

and the air full of snow, stand on the pavement, smoke a cigar and

survey the whole picture with a lordly and commanding, though a

somewhat inflame eye; and the busy scene be complete by a body of

boy and man offer newspaper, walking-stick, knife, comb, and

broad sheet of song to the passenger, and strive to drown each

other’s importunity with loud and ceaseless clamour.



Off go the coach at last, with Holdsworth by the coachman’s side. the

wheel rattle over the hard road, the house in long line sweep by,

lose by degree their frowsy exterior and dingy metropolitan aspect,

and attempt little revelation of bucolic life in small garden,

with glimpse of tree in the rear. then come house stand alone in

ground of their own, cottage purely pastoral in appearance, with the

noise of farmyard about they, and their atmosphere sweeten by the

smell of hay and flower. these drop away, the breeze grow pure and

elastic, the country open in wide space of wave cornfield spot

with bright poppy, and swell meadow land and green field shade

by group of tree, with here and there a lark whistle in the blue

sky, and all London lie behind, mark its mighty presence by a haze

that seem to stretch for mile and mile, and pale the heavens to

the hue of its own complexion.



Holdsworth be such bad company that the coachman soon cease his

“observation” touch the different scene through which they

pass, and address himself to his companion on the right, a young

gentleman who be go as far as Chatham, and who light several

large cigar in less than half an hour, pull at they with hollow in

his cheek, and look at the ash of they, and preserve a very pale

face. Holdsworth have something else to think about than the coachman’s

refined and classical remark, deliver from the depth of three

shawl. the quick rolling of the coach over the smooth turnpike-road

be induce an exhilaration that act upon he as good wine act upon

the brain, give clearness and freedom to thought, and cause life

to be feel at her most secret source. there be the impression in his

mind that he have once before travel on the top of a stage-coach

along this very road, though when, or under what circumstance, or

whether he have actually perform the journey or have read of some

one else having do it, he could not tell. the scenery they be

travel through be altogether delicious, and well than a cordial

to a man who have just land from a three and a half month’ voyage

at sea. the aspect of the country be full of that sober sweetness

of general effect which soothe the heart with a deep sense of home;

of broad yellow tract burnish by the sunshine, of the delicate

shadowing of green, and the neutral tint of fallow soil; house make

as dainty by distance as picture on ivory; great tree spread

their broad abundant leave over cool space of gleam water; with

the animation, now and again, of human life, where man work in the

field, or where child sport before the house, stop their

frolic to cheer the coach, while woman hold baby high in their hand

and sway to the delighted plunging of the chubby limb.



Holdsworth, however, be by no mean a sample of the “outside” carry

by the coach, whatever the deportment of the invisible “inside”

might have be. there be some half-a-dozen people on the roof,

include two girl, one of whom be decidedly pretty and the other

decidedly coquettish. these young lady, at the first go off, have

be ceaseless in the expression of their fear that, though it be

true they be up all right, they should never be able to get down

again. a gentleman with a turn-up nose, expressive of the utmost

self-complacency, have take it upon himself to comfort the lady, by

remark that get down be in every case easy than get up,

because the natural tendency of the human body be to fall; at which

the lady have murmur “Impertinence!” and “some people be well

see than hear,” and such-like scathing phrase, thereby sanction

the display of such wit as the turn-up-nosed gentleman possess,

which he exert with such good effect that the lady grow crimson

under uncontrollable fit of laughter. then the guard strike in, and

provoke the other outsider to talk; and presently a large flat bottle

be hand around, which have the effect of make everybody who put

it to his or her lip merry still. onward rattle the coach with

its noisy, laugh burden, fling sound of life broadcast over the

green country, provoke many a pair of slow bucolic eye to stare it

gravely out of sight, raise cloud of dust as the wheel go softly

over the floury highway, spin under mile-length of tree darken

the road with twinkle shadow, and throb with the pierce

shrilling of innumerable bird bury in the dense foliage, catch

the hot sunshine again in the open, and gleam like a gigantic

look-glass as it speed gaily forward under the broad eye of Heaven.



it be two o’clock when they change horse for the second time at a

smart little kentish town, with a gray ruin right in its midst, and an

old church hard by it, with one of the snug of rectory peep

at the world out of the silence and shadow of a rich orchard. some

of the “inside” get out here and go their way, and be no more

see. the young lady on the roof be entreat to alight by the

small-nosed man, but this they noisily refuse to do, the mere idea

of such a thing cause they to catch hold of each other. the delay,

however, be a short one. the jade horse, with streak of white foam

upon their polished hide, be take out and fresh one put in; the

coachman, smell strongly of gin and peppermint, climb into his

place, crack his whip, and off start the coach, follow by a crowd

of excited boy, who chase it clear of the town and then throw stone

after it.



“where might you be for, sir?” inquire the coachman of Holdsworth,

speak out of his stomach like a ventriloquist.



“Hanwitch.”



“several stoppage afore Hanwitch,” say the coachman.



“how many?”



“Vy, there’s Saltwell, Halton, Gadstone, and Southbourne.”



“Southbourne?”



“yes, Southbourne, of course. that’s the willage jist afore Hanwitch.”



“Southbourne! southbourne!” repeat Holdsworth, with the old look of

bewilderment that invariably enter his face when some familiar name

be sound in his ear.



the coachman glance at he over his shawl, and say to himself, quite

in the pit of his stomach. “you’re a rum ’un, you be!”



“P’raps you ar’n’t acquaint with the road, sir?” say he.



“I think--I be sure I know Southbourne,” reply Holdsworth. “what sort

of place be it?”



“Vot sort o’ place? Vy, a willage.”



“but what kind of village?”



“all that I know be this, there’s a hinn there vere they serve you

vith werry good liquor. Blow’d if I can tell you anything furder. but

that’s my veakness, sir. vould you believe I, I’ve drive coach

through that willage for the last two-and-twenty year, and may I be

bil’d if I can tell you anything about it.”



Holdsworth sink into deep thought, while the coachman, twist his

eye over his shawl, examine his face and clothe with side-long

attention; then his curiosity be evidently arouse by something in

Holdsworth’s appearance which widely differed from the cut and style of

the passenger he be in the habit of carry, he say:



“might you be a furriner, sir?”



“no,” answer Holdsworth.



“I’ve a brother in Californy. P’raps you might know they part, sir?”



“I have just return from Australia.”



“oh!” exclaim the coachman, look stagger; “that’s a good vay

off ain’t it?”



“the other side of the world.”



“Gor bless I! a queer place, I’ve heerd. full of conwick. one of our

guard be send out there t’other day for abstracting of money from a

wallis.”



this reference operate upon his sympathy, he enter into a

story, as long as a newspaper account, of the trial; “how beautiful

the counsill as hadvocate the pore fellow speak, vich the court vos

crowd vith coachman, who groan venhever the hadvocate as vos

oppose to the guard begin to speak, vich behaviour, though it warn’t

p’raps quite correck, vasn’t to be stop nohow, although the judge

look werry fierce, and the counsill kep’ on sayin’, ‘my lud, if this

here noise ain’t stop, I’ll throw the case up,’ vich be just the

thing the coachman vante” (here he make as though he would poke

Holdsworth in the rib with the butt-end of his whip); “but, lor’ save

yer, it be no go.”



Holdsworth pay no attention to this story, his mind be engage in

a desperate struggle with memory. indeed, the word “Southbourne” have

affect he as no other allusion have. Pale, dim phantom of memory,

comparable to nothing so much as the phosphorescent outline which the

eye may mark fluctuating in the black sea-water, rise and sink in his

mind; and though whisper nothing to his breathless anxiety, clearly

prove that the faculty which he have long believe dead be begin

to stir and awaken.



one by one the town and village name by the coachman have be

pass, and now Southbourne be to come.



an indescribable anxiety, at once breathless and thrilling, suspending,

it seem to he, the very pulsation of his heart, make his breath

come and go in quick, fierce respiration, possess Holdsworth.



he hold his hand tightly clasp; all colour have flee from his face,

and his deep-sunk eye glow with unnatural fire.



repeatedly he mutter to himself, “what do this portend?”



already his prophetic soul have catch the light, and seem to know

herself, and madden he and wrung his frame with her wild and bitter

struggle to proclaim her inspiration and pierce she reflect beam

through the film that still blind the eye of the mind.



the sun be still high, and fling its yellow brilliance over the fair

and gild prospect. the coach have turn the corner of the long road

that lead straight as an arrow into Southbourne; and far away at the

extremity, in mingled shadow and shine, the few house could be see,

with the spire of St. George’s Church rear high its flaming vane,

and on the left the gleam of the river shadow by many tree.



“there’s Southbourne!” say the coachman, point with his whip.



the dust whirl in a cloud behind the wheel, the guard sound his

horn, and with a rush and a rattle the coach draw up opposite the “Hare

and hound,” a tavern as familiar to Holdsworth as the sight of his own

hand.



       *       *       *       *       *



“Hullo!” cry the coachman. “hi, you there! help! a glass o’ brandy!

blow if the gentleman haven’t faint!”



“faint!” cry the young lady on the top of the coach, lean

forward to catch a sight of his face.



no, not faint; but strike down by a revelation such as, have the two

young lady and the small-nosed man and the coachman be tell the

story of it, would have supply they with enough matter to keep they

talk without intermission as long as the coach-wheel turn.



Memory, come out of the little house at the bottom of the long

familiar thoroughfare, out of the little house that turn its shoulder

upon the highway and part it into lane, have rush upon he like an

armed man, and strike he a staggering blow. he have drop under it,

and, but for the support of the apron over his knee, would have fall

to the ground.



the guard run into the tavern and return with a glass of brandy,

which the coachman put to Holdsworth’s lip.



“thank you, I be well now,” he exclaim.



“glad to hear it,” say the coachman.



“I will get down here.”



“ben’t you book for Hanwitch!” exclaim the guard, who imagine

that the gentleman’s head wander.



“no--this will do--I will go no far. help I with your hand--thank

you.”



he reach the ground, watch by a group of person who make a

movement as though to support he, when they see he sway to and fro

like a drunken man, and stare fixedly down the road. but in a moment

or two, with a struggle, he stand firm. his portmanteau be hand out

and carry into the bar. the guard take his place; the coachman, with

a glance over his shawl to see that Holdsworth stand clear of the

wheel, jerk the rein, and the coach rattle out of sight.









CHAPTER XXII.



SOUTHBOURNE.





right in the middle of the road stand Holdsworth, cast his eye

first to the right, then to the left, then let they rest fixedly on

the house at the extremity.



here be the scene, the place at last, that be to roll back the

curtain which hide the past from he, and to proclaim the resurrection

of the life within he that for year have sleep a sleep as deep as

death.



there he stand, bring by God’s hand from a far-off world into

the little kentish village, where his memory lie heaped, where

association make a beacon of every humble house to lead he with

unerre step backward and backward to the sweetest, the dear of all

his memory.



how remember, how deeply remember, the scene! the old tavern on the

right, with its swinge signboard, its burnished latticed window,

the great elm tree spread its branch, like soft finger, over the

red-tile roof; the farmhouse face it, with the clamour of hide

poultry all about it, soften by the cooing of dove; and the cherry

and apple tree stretch forth their fruit over the wooden railing,

and the string of white linen dry in the open space among the

tree; the vista of gable-peak house, the old shop, the grassy land

between the house, the blacksmith’s shed, the hen in the roadway,

the child on the doorstep, the woman work at the open window,

and the little house at the extremity, back by soft masse of green

tree and the delicate blue of the afternoon sky.



he know his life’s history as he survey this scene, as though a

voice in his ear be whisper it all to he. the chain be too

complete not to suggest the unseen link; the throng of association

be too manifold and pregnant not to reveal to his mind the thing

which his eye behold not. swiftly and fiercely--a very whirlwind of

logic--thought fly over each step-stone to the hide past; and

then he know what he have leave, what he be now to seek, and what have

be the want which his instinct--that deep life of his of which the

movement be independent of the sense--have never lose sight of.



when his faintness have pass, a great joy take possession of he--an

impulse so keenly exhilarate, that he could have cry aloud in his

rapture. but then come a revulsion--a deadly fear--of what he know not,

save that its presence turn he into ice, and damp his forehead

with sweat.



he be all unconscious of the eye that be upon he; but some one

approach make he turn his head, and he see several person watch

he curiously from the door of the inn, while other, plain country

people in smock and highlow, mutter to one another as they stare

from the pavement.



“willn’t you please to step in, sir?” say the man who come forward, a

short, square-face individual, in a black calico apron and a white hat.



“who live in that house?” return Holdsworth, point down the road.



“that one yonder, with the chimbley look this way? why, I don’t

think anybody’s live in it just now, although I do hear that it be

take by a party from Ashford.--Emily!” he call.



a stout, well-look woman elbow her way out of the tavern, and

stand on the low step.



“the gentleman want to know who live in that house at the bottom.”



“it’s to let. Mr. Markham have the letting of it,” answer the woman.



“his be the shop yonder,” say the man. “you’ll see ‘Undertaker and

Joiner’ write over the door.”



“I’ll send a boy to fetch he, if you like,” say the woman.



“I’ll goa, missus,” remark an old man in a long blouse, turn about

on his stick in his eagerness to earn a glass of yale.



“no, I don’t want he,” say Holdsworth.



“willn’t you step in and rest yourself, sir?” exclaim the woman,

exert the seductive smile with which she be willnot to greet every

passenger who stop at her door.



Holdsworth hesitate a moment, as though there be a magic in the

little distant house that constrain he to keep his eye upon it, and

then enter the tavern, herald by the landlady, and follow by the

landlord.



the parlour into which he be conduct be as quiet and private as he

could wish, screen by a red curtain across the glass of the door from

the bar, with a window opening on to a square of ground well stock

with shrub and vegetable. the sunshine stream into the room, and

light up the queer ornament on the mantelpiece, the fine old china

hang upon row of hook in a mahogany cabinet, the well-wear

carpet, the velvet sofa, the black bottle and glitter tankard on

the shelf of the sideboard.



the landlord go behind the bar to look after some besmocke gentry

who be drink in front of it, leave his wife to attend to

Holdsworth.



“what might you like to order, sir?” she inquire, present herself

at a side door.



he ask she to bring he some wine and biscuit, say that he have

no appetite now, but would dine or sup later on. he look at her very

attentively as he speak, with an idea in his mind that he have see she

before.



she go away, and he leave his seat and pace the room with a wild look

of distress on his face, and bitter anxiety and fear in his heart.

once he snatch up his hat and advance to the door, but hesitate

and resume his agitated walk. his feeling be those of a man just

awaken to consciousness from the effect of a blow that have stun

he. his body tremble, his lip work, and he hold his hand squeeze

tightly together. his suffering be indeed terrible. he look back

upon the blank of five year and recoil before the conjecture his

heart prompt as to the thing which have happen in that time.

sometimes his impulse be to rush forth and cry aloud for dolly, and

then a deadly chill come over he, and he shut his eye and beat aside

his thought, as though they be something tangible and apart from

he, with his hand.



when the door open, he bite his lip to control himself, and keep his

back turn upon the woman in feigned inspection of a print upon the

wall. as she be about to withdraw, he look at she and say:



“have you live long in Southbourne?”



“yes, sir, many year.”



he seat himself and drink a glass of wine.



“how many year?”



“oh, twelve, thirteen. ah, more like fifteen year, sir!”



“so long! then you know all the people here?”



“yes, sir, I daresay I do,” answer the woman, put her hand under

her apron, and examine Holdsworth’s face and clothe with great

curiosity.



“who last live in that house at the bottom of the street?”



“you mean the one you be askin’ my husband about?”



“yes.”



“Mr. Fairchild, the butcher, after he sell his business about two year

ago.”



“who before Mr. Fairchild?”



“it stand empty awhile after Mrs. Holdsworth leave it.”



“where be she now?”



“Livin’ at Hanwitch, along with her husband, Mr. Conway, the dentist.”



the woman’s eye, when she make this answer, be on the garden; when

she look again at Holdsworth, his face be turn from she.



“do you know any of the party, sir, as you’re ask about?” she

inquire.



he do not answer she, and she, think that he have not hear she,

continue:



“I be servant along with poor Mr. Newcome before the old gentleman

die, and see a good deal of Mrs. Holdsworth, as I always call she;

for, somehow, I can never bear to think of she as Mrs. Conway, for

her heart never go with her hand when she marry that gentleman, as

I can bear witness to, for I be at the wedding, and never see a poor

body cry as she do. she and her grandmother, old Mrs. Flemming, be

often at the rectory ... but I beg pardon, sir; you be speak of the

house. I cann’t remember who have it before Mrs. Holdsworth. it’s a long

time ago.”



Holdsworth raise his head.



up to the moment of she speak of dolly he have not know his own

name; all other memory have return to he, save that. his face be

very white, but there be a strong expression in it. if the woman be

to talk for another twelve hour, she could add nothing more to what

she have already say. dolly’s death he have expect as he have expect

a hundred other nameless possibility, when memory swoop upon he

and set he peer over the edge of the chasm that separate the

then from the now; but not her marriage. not that. in all the hour

he have pass in the open boat at sea, behold death strike down

his companion about he, suffer the exquisite torture of thirst,

the yet more exquisite pang of hopelessness, there be no moment of

agony in all that time comparable to the agony that now wrench he.

it might be one of those terrible experience which break the heart or

transform the nature, but it give to or find in Holdsworth a quality

of endurance that enable he to front the extremity with a face of

marble.



when next he speak his voice be low, but without a tremor in it.



“I be interested in Mrs. Conway and her old grandmother. tell I what

you know about they.”



“surely, sir, you don’t bring news of Mr. Holdsworth--of the fine young

man that go to sea and be shipwreck?” inquire the woman with a

face of excitement, and stare hard, as if she be about to receive

some astounding news.



“no, no!” he answer, almost under his breath; and then he add,

“tell I what you know of the widow.”



“I remember Mr. Holdsworth well,” say the woman, her speech answer

to her mood. “a handsomer young fellow I never see. he use often to be

at the rectory with his wife, and the love between they be something

beautiful. how she ever have the heart to let he leave she I never

could guess. but he go and be drown, and leave the young thing

without a friend or a shilling in the wide world, God help she; and

though I say it be almost stupid her marry Mr. Conway, remember

what love there be between she and Mr. Holdsworth, yet I have always

believe it be for her child’s sake that she marry the dentist, for

they be in desperate want when he court she, and must have starve

for want of help.”



“you be speak of Holdsworth’s child?”



“yes, sir. a bright little thing, and fair as a lily. I see she the

other day when I be over at Hanwitch. she be with her mamma, and I

never see such a likeness as there be between she and her poor drown

papa. but you’re askin’ about old Mrs. Flemming. why, she die four

year ago. she be very old, and go off quite peaceful, they say.

what with Mrs. Flemming’s death, and she never get any news of her

husband, and have a tiny little baby to find food for, I do think

the poor young lady’s heart nearly break. I never hear exactly how

the money matter be with she, but I believe that when Mrs. Flemming

die she would have nothing to live on but her husband’s pay, which be

stop when he be give up for lost. Mr. Newcome be very kind, and

pay her rent, and help she along while he live; and then Mr. Conway

see she; but it be a long time before she would marry he, long after

the poor old rector be dead and go, and she find that take in

needlework be bad than go on the parish. I often think of she--I

do, indeed, sir--wait day after day for her husband, who be never

to come home. I’d rather, myself, have marry anybody than a sailor.

there’s no telling, when once they go, whether they’ll ever come back

again. they’re bad nor soldier for that.”



here the woman, suddenly conceive that she have talk quite enough,

and perhaps a little too much, drop a courtesy and leave the room;

but come back again to ask two question--at what hour would the

gentleman please to dine? and would he like to have a bed-room in her

house? she could recommend her bed-room. her linen be clean as snow.



“I will tell you presently,” answer Holdsworth. “I have not yet

decide upon my movement.”



“there’s a nice plump fowl----”



“yes, cook I that by seven o’clock,” say Holdsworth, who be

feverishly impatient to be leave alone.



she close the door, and Holdsworth lean his temple on his hand

and fix his heavy eye on the bare table, take the attitude of a

student strive to master some difficult problem.



for many minute he hold this posture, presently lift his head, and

look about he; then take his hat and go out.



the landlord behind the bar make he a low bow, and offer his

service to show he over the village. Holdsworth decline his offer

with a “thank you,” and walk into the road. he glance over his

shoulder suspiciously as he advance, dislike the inquisitive stare

with which he have be follow through the bar of the inn by the

people drink there, but no one watch he. he hold a stick, on

which he lean as he move, like an infirm man; and often he pause

and gaze around he. the people in the roadway, or in the house,

eye he as he pass with the curiosity a stranger seldom fail to

excite in small unfrequented place; but he take no notice of they; his

mind be intent on vivify the impression it be receive with old

memory, and adjust the idea which have be restore to he out of

the dark and secret hiding-place of the past.



few change have be make in the aspect of the little village to

embarrass the picture which his recover memory have submit to he.

some alteration in the external form of one or two shop, and two

freshly-build house on the left-hand side face the blacksmith’s

shed, be the only new feature in the familiar scene of this quaint

broad thoroughfare.



his step grow more reluctant, his face take a sharp expression of

pain, though never lose its characteristic of hardness and severity,

as he draw near to his old home. he force himself forwards, and, when

abreast, halt and look at it.



the window be blindless, the garden show sign of long neglect,

and a board nail to a post lean towards the road, bear the

announcement, in paint letter, that the house be to let. a row

of cobwebs garnish the woodwork of the gate, and glisten in the

sunshine; the bare room, visible through the window, look cheerless

and inhospitable; the window-glass be dirty, and some of the pane

in the kitchen window be break. the grass about the house be tall

and vividly green. that window, look towards the tree between the

lane, belong to dolly’s room. there be white soft curtain to it

in those day, and the glass be pure and transparent as spring-water.

that room on the left be the sitting-room. there they have take their

meal; there they have play forfeit, have hunt the slipper, have

make the wall ring with innocent laughter. he remember the old

grandmother’s placid smile, the rector’s kindly joke, his dolly’s

sweet face, throw a light of purity and beauty about she. and under

the sill be the dead branch of the clematis, still hold to the wall

by the piece of black leather dolly’s own hand have nail. such humble

sign make grief sharp than large memorial.



he stand lean upon his stick, lose all sense of the present in

this vision of the past. his thought, take their departure from the

time when he first fit out that house as a home for dolly, flow

regularly downwards. he be a bridegroom again, and his wife be at his

side, and her eye upon his, and their hand clasp. now the shadow of

separation that be to darken they presently be feel; and then come

the eve of his departure, throng with the memory of kiss sweet and

bitter, of tear and broken prayer, and brave hope battle with

sullen misgiving. he be now on board the “Meteor,” and now in the

open boat, surround by the dying, and himself suffer torture it

break he down to recall; and now he be in Australia, strive with

memory, which would yield no answer to his passionate prayer. but the

finger of God point the way to his old home; and now he be return

to England, with his past still hide in gloom, but with his heart not

unhopeful of the morning that be to break after the long darkness of

the night. and finally, with the old village of Southbourne before he,

come the rush of memory--the brief exultation--the spasm of fear--the

terror that hold he mute--the disclosure that show he his wife less

his than have he traverse all the desolate mile of water only to kneel

by her grave....



tear would have relieve he, but he could not weep. he turn and

move slowly away, stop again and again to look back at the

little empty house, while sob convulse he, and a sense of supreme

desolation and friendlessness weigh he down.









CHAPTER XXIII.



reflection.





the fowl dish up by the landlady and serve upon fine linen, be

plump, and juicy and aromatic enough to re-excite the appetite of an

alderman after Mansion House dinner; but Holdsworth hardly touch it.

the woman look vex as she remove it, for the neglect of such a

dainty be as good as a spoken disparagement of her skill as a cook.

she set a fine cheese, fresh from the farmhouse over the way, upon the

table, and butter from the same dairy, firm and sweet-smelling, as

butter should be; but these be leave untasted.



“I don’t know who he may be,” she whisper to her husband, outside,

“but if he don’t make a well dinner than this every day, it’s no

wonder his body’s a shadder.”



the setting sunshine stream into the little parlour in which

Holdsworth sit, and enrich the room with its vivid crimson light; the

soft evening breeze waft pleasant perfume through the open window;

and in the air be the tender and delightful peace which fall with an

appreciable hush over little country village, where the sinking of the

sun be the signal for rest.



Holdsworth have make up his mind to sleep in Southbourne that night. he

need the silence and the solitude the little inn promise he, that

he might meditate upon the step he should now take.



the feverish misery that have be bear in he by the landlady’s

story be, in some measure, tranquillise, and he have now the power,

at least, to think with tolerable clearness. and yet he be sorely

perplexed, as he sit with his head rest on his hand and his weary

eye fix on the little garden outside. impulse be govern he

that make his mind incline from side to side like a pendulum. have Mr.

Newcome be alive--the kind and good old rector, whom he recall with

love--he would have go to he, avow himself, and entreat his

counsel.



he feel that dolly be dead to he. he feel this, though no word that

he have at his command would have enable he to explain his idea. his

own grand sense of honour witness this truth, that she have marry

another man in full belief that he--John Holdsworth--be dead; and he

recognise and appreciate the force of the overwhelming claim of the

moral obligation impose on he to leave her belief undisturbed. why?

because its disturbance would generate a heavy burden of shame, would

make she practically false to both man, and stain her nature with a sin

whose hue would not be the less dark in the sight of the world because

her conscience have no share in it.



such instinctive perception of the high need of the seldom-parallel

situation his fate have place he in, could only have possess a man

of deep honour, great humanity, and rare unselfishness.



but his child!



the child of his own passionate love for dolly. _there_ be the

magnetic power that draw all his inclination away from the silent

command of honour.



to see she--to behold himself renew in a sweet child’s face--to press

his lip _once_ to her cheek--once only, if nevermore!



oh! not once--not once only! to dwell near she, to have she in his

sight, to watch they both, and live out the year that should be

allot he in secret contemplation of the joy, the sacred pleasure,

the divine emotion embody in this woman and her babe; happiness

which have be break away from his life--could not this be?



he start up and look at himself in the glass over the mantel-shelf.

have suffer work in his face to such poor purpose, that even

the eye of love could pierce through the sunken mask? his child know

he not--and dolly, deem he dead, and hold he a thing of the

irreclaimable past, could not behold--could never imagine that she

beheld--in that bow figure, that bearded face, those hollow eye,

that hair with patch of gray all over it, the handsome, vigorous,

upright, clear-eyed man whom she have call husband.



Husband! ... and she have leave he.



stop! passage of the landlady’s story echo in his ear; how the poor

young lady be starve; how she have a tiny baby to support; how the

workhouse seem well to she than the hopeless, scanty produce of the

needle; how she hold back, reluctant to give her hand to the man who

wish to marry she; how she have weep when her hand be give!



oh, husband! oh, lover! though to the past only those title now

belong; by your own suffering, remember her! by your own misery,

when, feel yourself die alone on the great deep, your physical

torment yield to the fiercer torture of your heart when you

think of your wife pray for he, whom, you say to yourself, she

shall never see again, remember she! pity she! be his imagination so

poor that he could not find it in he to make, out of the landlady’s

brief tale, a pregnant, bitter picture of his wife’s trial? not so!

to such a heart as his, one hint of misery would bring with it many

piteous detail. there be infinite anguish in the picture his fancy

draw; but he force himself to contemplate it, that the jealousy, the

disappointment, the despair of unfulfilled hope, might melt out of his

heart, and leave it a fit shrine for the consecration of the two image

which the uncontrollable will of his humanity as a father and a husband

declare should be place there.



his finger have steal over his face as he stand before the

look-glass. a long time he thus remain, while the sun go down

behind the tree in the far-off field, and the twilight steal softly

into the room and make his figure visionary. when he withdraw his hand

from his eye, they be wet; but the one star shine clearly in the

dark blue overhead have dawn out of the light to witness a fairer

sight than the sun have shine upon--a face from which all vestige of

hardness and severity have pass, eye heavy with tear upturned to

God’s kingdom, and lip whisper a prayer for help, for courage,

for counsel, to aid the resolution of his heart, intent upon a noble

self-sacrifice that should yet not remove it from the sphere of all

that it hold dearest on earth.









CHAPTER XXIV.



HANWITCH.





Hanwitch be nowhere see to great advantage than from the summit of

the little hill that flank it on the west. here, if you be an epicure

in your enjoyment of what be picturesque in scenery, you will take

your stand at sunset, while the splendour still flush the heaven,

and the country all around be tint with a delicate crimson haze.

in this fairy light, Hanwitch, from where you stand, will resemble

some architectural dream; for the serene sky give an ideality to the

proportion which be magnify by the soft combining shadow, and

peace brood in the street. the noble church dedicate to St. James

tower in the midst of the house; its spire glow with the red fire,

which a little while before have bathe the whole pile and kindle

brilliant star in its long and narrow window; and all about the

church rise and fall the roof of closely-group house, manifold in

colour, with line of thin blue smoke mount straight into the sky.

the town lie back with woode scenery, and the picturesque outline

of the house take a new detail of beauty from the relief they give to

the soft dark masse of tree and lightli-colour field which make

up the farth landscape. and as you watch, a human interest will be

communicate to the town by the breaking out here and there of little

yellow light. darkness soon fall when the crimson flush pale upon

the sky; but where you stand daylight be still around, mellow into

deep, rich, beauty of colour, and so linger ere it fade into

the gray and gloom of twilight.



the town have be enlarge since the day to which this story belong;

but elderly people be live whose love for the old High Street

scarcely reconcile they to the “improvement” which have be make

in its aspect. surveyor and local board press sorely upon gentle

prejudice. these elderly people remember the row of antique house

where the big bank building now stand. they remember certain primitive

shop, the window of which be furnish with diamond-shape pane of

glass that discolour to the eye the ware expose for sale within.

they remember the picturesque alley out of the High Street, with a

cottage at the end of it that have a green porch; it look from its

cool retreat upon the narrow slice of the main thoroughfare with its

passenger flit like shadow past the brief opening. and I myself

can recall the wonderful effect of light and shadow in that tranquil

embrasure when the evening lengthen, and as I behold it once--a

maid-servant, picturesquely attire in a red petticoat, loll within

the porch, her hand upon her hip, laugh at a dog that stand on its

hind leg beg; the figure shadow, the window above burn with

the light of the set sun, the pavement a deep gray.



in Holdsworth’s time, the vehicle and passenger be in perfect

keeping with the venerable and fade but dignified aspect of the old

street. the townspeople still linger behind the transition-epoch of

that bygone day, and hold for the most part tenaciously to the costume

and the indolence of their father.



the town be about an hour’s walk from Southbourne. after quit the

handful of house which form the village, Hanwitch come upon you as

a metropolis. to describe it in homely guide-book fashion:--It have two

good church, and a public square full of evergreen, rock-work, and

stone image; several snug inn, one wide street and a quantity of

narrow one; a town-hall, a market-place, a prison, and a town-crier;

a large number of old lady and fat poodle, invalid, sedan-chair,

and camp-stool. in olden time--and now I be talk of the eighteenth

century--it have hold some sort of position as a third-rate inland

watering-place; but what have become of the spring which have bring

the gout, the vapour, and the spleen from place as distant as London

to drink, the old inhabitant never could remember. but one thing be

certain: there be no lack of water in the place. a river run close to

it, and from this river meander some crystal streamlet which run

right into the town. and where the river be, the scenery be exquisite

in summer--cool, deep, and leafy, with a bridge at each end of the

town, a little landing-stage, a punt or two, and midway between the

bridge, a cluster of tree on either bank, with huge gnarl trunk

and root which run naked for many foot along the ground, whilst, high

above, their branch mingle and form a tunnel for the water to

flow under. here the trout would leap; here the water-rat would sneak

from its earthy chamber and break the tide into thin ripple as it

noiselessly make for the opposite shore; here the sunshine would fall

in thread through the leave and gild the black long-legged insect

on the surface of the water.



ten o’clock be strike when Holdsworth be drive into Hanwitch by

the landlord of the Southbourne inn, who be rich enough to own a horse

and gig. the drive have be a very short one, and in the landlord’s

opinion seven-and-sixpence have never be more easily earn. the gig

be stop at the door of the “Hanwitch Arms,” and Holdsworth get out.

then come a porter, who nod pleasantly to the owner of the gig, and

hoist the portmanteau on his shoulder.



“my respeck to the governor, Joe. how be he?”



“pretty middlin’! how’s yourself?”



“why, I cann’t say as I’m quite the thing. the weather’s be rayther

agin my rheumatiz.--Wish you good mornin’, sir.”



and with this farewell to Holdsworth, the landlord drive himself away.



Holdsworth’s plan have be fully settle by he the night before; and

one part of they be that he should put up at some inn at Hanwitch,

while he make inquiry of deep interest to himself, and obtain a

lodging. having follow the porter into the bar and order a bed for

the night, he re-enter the street.



his emotion, as he first begin to walk, be conflicting and painful.

he be now in the town where his wife live; any moment might bring

they face to face, and the wild anxiety to see she be mix up with

a sensation of shrink fear of the encounter. he stare eagerly at

the people, and now and again, when a little child pass, his heart

beat rapidly, and he feel his blood leave his face. but he master

himself soon, repeatedly mutter, as a reassuring argument, that,

be they to meet, dolly would not know he.



he be in the main street, walk slowly, and help his step with a

stick, not more from habit than from necessity, for he be frequently

seize with a weakness in the leg which would sometimes oblige he to

stop, or seat himself if a seat be at hand. his object now be to

find out where dolly live, a question he would not ask the landlady

at Southbourne, lest, add to the inquiry he have already make, it

should excite her suspicion and set she surmise.



he notice a small chemist’s shop opposite, and, his mind establish

a friendly connection between drug and dentistry, he cross and

enter. a bald-headed man in spectacle receive he with a bow.



“can you tell I where Mr. Conway, the dentist, live?”



the chemist, who know perfectly well, scratch his ear and seem to

reflect. he draw tooth himself, and be not dispose to furnish a rival

with a patient if he could help it.



“I know the name, sir, and----might I ask if you want anything do to

your tooth.”



“no, I merely wish to know where Mr. Conway live.”



“it’s not for I to speak ill of a brother practitioner, and as I

shalln’t mention name, no harm can be do,” say the chemist, with his

eye on Holdsworth’s mouth; “but I do say that it’s a pity people should

start in a business, require as much skill as the high branch

of surgery, without know the difference between an eye and a wisdom

tooth, and drink to that degree that their hand tremble like the

window of a coach in full tear.”



“do Mr. Conway drink?”



“I name no name,” reply the chemist, assume an injure expression

of face. “I should be sorry to take away any man’s character, not to

speak of the bread out of his mouth, though there be some people not

quite so particular as I in that matter, and will start lie which

should make ’em afraid to go to bed. all that I say be, that if I have a

toothache, and want to get my jaw broke and my gum tear out, I’d go

to a certain person livin’ not a day’s walk from this shop, and ask he

to look at my tooth.”



say which he nod vigorously, and, take up a bottle, begin to

mould a piece of blue paper over the cork.



“where do this Mr. Conway live?” inquire Holdsworth, who judge that

there might be a good deal more of professional animus than truth in

the chemist’s observation.



“I believe,” reply the chemist, sulkily vouchsafe the information

which he could scarcely long evade, “that the party you refer to

life in the Ellesmere Road. he do yesterday; but some person in

this world be so dependent on their landlord that there’s no tellin’

what’s go to happen to ’em to-morrow.”



thank this very good-natured and remarkably ingenuous chemist for

his direction, Holdsworth quit the shop and walk up the street.

he ask a butcher-boy the way to Ellesmere Road, and be tell to keep

straight on, until he come to a “Methody’s chapel, ven he’d see a

turnin’, vich ’ud be the road he ast for.”



the Methodist Chapel be a good distance off, and as Holdsworth’s pace

be slow, he have plenty of leisure for reflection, which be bitter

enough; for, strive as he might to waive the chemist’s gossip as mere

trade scandal and jealousy, his mind persist in fasten on it, and

turn it about, and coin deep anxiety out of it.



if this Mr. Conway _be_ the drunkard the chemist affirm he to be,

and the pauper too--for the sarcasm about “person be dependent

on their landlord” have not be lose--what kind of life be dolly

and _his_ child lead? he frown, and feel his hand tighten on the

handle of his stick; but a milder persuasion grow in he, and he force

his mind away from the subject.



so bright a morning as it be would bring forth many people; and the

High Street be tolerably well fill with pedestrian, and old people

in bath-chair, wheel along the gutter for fear of the horse, and

nurse drag child by the hand, and waggon, and tradesman’s

trap. the early coach from Canterbury come thunder along the

street, the guard blow his horn and cause house-windows to fly

open, and head to protrude, and a handkerchief, or, maybe, a duster

here and there, to be wave in coy recognition of the hand-kissing of

certain spruce and finely-attire gentleman on the top of the vehicle.



but, varied and cheerful as the scene be, Holdsworth have no eye

but for the woman and child he meet, at whom he dart quick eager

glance, which must have send some of the woman trip along with a

sincere conviction that they have meet with one admirer, at all event,

that morning.



he pass the market-place with its stall load with garden produce,

and clean little shop submit a tempting array of plump fowl,

geese, side of bacon, leg of pork, and string of sausage; and in

about twenty minute’ time reach the Methodist Chapel, and turn

into the Ellesmere Road.



a short broad road, with the sun-light country beyond; on either

side, small newly-build villa, with now and again a house present a

more venerable aspect. there be grass in the roadway, and one or two

of the villa have placard in their window. in the front garden of one

of the nearer house an old gentleman, with an inflame face, and a

white hankerchief over his head, be ply a rake. no other person be

visible; but, when Holdsworth have advance a few step, a woman come

out of a gate and approach he. his heart come into his throat, and

he stand stock still. she draw near, but she be not dolly. she glance
