at he as she pass, strike, maybe, by his pale face, and the singular

mixture of old age and youth which his appearance and figure suggest.



he breathe deeply and walk forward, glance to right and leave of

he.



the last house but one on the left be the house he want. a brass

plate inscribe with Conway’s name and calling be fix to the iron

railing, and over the door be a lamp furnish with blue and red

glass.



Holdsworth dare scarcely glance at it. when his eye encounter the

name he turn cold, and feel the damp perspiration suffuse his

forehead.



he come back hurriedly, with the bare impression on his mind of a small

house with the upper blind draw, and with an untended garden in the

front.



he return to the main entrance of the road, stop, look back, and

then slowly retrace his step.



there be a house of an old fashion than any of the other on the

right-hand side, about midway; one that have evidently take root there

many year before the little villa have gather themselves together to

intercept its view and violate its pastoral solitude. its doorway be

shelter by a roomy porch, with creeper clamber up its trellis

supporter; its darkle window have the burnish glitter upon they

that be peculiar to old glass; its eave be capacious enough to

accommodate a whole colony of swallow, and it have a fair piece of

ground stretch away at its back. the bright brass knocker on the

green door, the white doorstep, the purity of the window glass, and

the spotlessness of the window drapery, afford an excellent guarantee

of the housewifely quality of the inmate. in one of the window hung

a card. Holdsworth open the gate and knock.



“I should like to see your apartment,” he say to the cheerful-face,

middle-aged woman who have promptly reply to his summon.



“certainly, sir; please to walk in.”



she throw open the door of a long sitting-room, smell of lavender

and mignonette, and furnish with work chair, old china, a tall

wooden clock with a febrile tick, a hearth-rug decorate with blue

and yellow rose in wool, and a sour squeeze-look bookcase which

seem to hold sundry folio under protest, and to threaten the

instant ejection of a number of small book on the top shelf, which

lean one against another, some of they gape apparently in the last

extremity of terror.



“for yourself, sir? or might there be child?”



“for myself.”



“why, then, sir,” exclaim the woman, with great alacrity, “I think I

can accommodate you. you can have this room, and a bed-room just over

it, and the use of the pianner in the next room when you see company,

for fourteen shilling a week.”



“that will do,” say Holdsworth.



the woman, smile like clockwork, proceed to inform he that she be

a widow, and have nobody else in the house but her mother, who be very

aged and silly-like, and only leave her room, which be upstairs, once

a week for a turn in the garden; that anything more peaceful than her

house be never know, and that if the gentleman be studious, he would

never be interrupt with noise.



she then lead he upstairs to the bedroom, which be as comfortable as

any man could wish.



on their return to the sitting-room, Holdsworth, who be tired,

ask permission to rest himself, and sit down near the window which

overlook the road, where he could just obtain a glimpse of Mr.

Conway’s house.



he break away from the overpowering thought which the sense of the

near presence of his wife and child force upon he, and steady his

voice, turn to the woman who stand at the door, and ask she if she

know any of the people live around.



“why, sir, I know most of my neighbour by name, though I cann’t say as

e’er a one of ’em be friend of mine.”



“I notice a dentist’s house just now, over the way. what sort of

business can he do in such a road as this?”



“oh, you’re speak of Mr. Conway!” she exclaim, with a shake of the

head. “it’s his own fault if he don’t do a good business, for I hear

that he’s pretty clever at make tooth and drawing of they, and the

like of that; but he don’t seem to have no patient, and I know why;

but it’s not for I to meddle in other folk’ concern.”



“why?”



“I suppose you’re no friend of his, sir?” she say.



“I never see the gentleman in my life.”



“well, then, to speak plainly, he drink; and it’s pretty well know;

and so there’s no wonder gentlefolk willn’t go near he.”



Holdsworth force a look of unconcern into his face as he ask:



“be he marry?”



“oh yes, sir; and a sweet dear creature his wife be; Mrs. Holdsworth

as be. Hers be a sad history. she live at a place call

Southbourne--maybe you know it--it’s an hour’s walk from here; and I

be tell her story by Mrs. Campion, as use to keep a greengrocer’s

shop in that village, and serve most of the gentry about. she--I’m

speak of Mrs. Conway--lose her husband at sea, and marry the

present gentleman two or three year afterwards. Mrs. Campion say she

be drive to it by want o’ the bare necessary of life,” add the

woman in a subdued voice.



Holdsworth be silent.



“I don’t think,” continue the woman, “that she lead a very happy

life. we sometimes have a chat together when we meet out o’ door,

and she’s the civil, sweetest young thing I ever know. but,” she

exclaim, catch herself up, “all this be no business of mine; and

I hope, sir, you’ll think none the bad of I for gossip about

stranger’ affair. I be strivin’ to answer your question, sir.”



“thank you,” say Holdsworth, rise, but keep his back to the

window. “can you receive I to-morrow?”



“oh yes, sir; at any time you’re pleased to come.”



“my name be Hampden. I shall sleep to-night at the ‘Hanwitch Arms.’

here be a couple of sovereign, which will serve you as a security for

my take your room. I leave myself in your hand, and have no doubt

I shall be comfortable.”



the woman take the money with a courtesy, think to herself that she

have never meet with a more polite and considerate gentleman. holdsworth

leave the house. he cast a swift glance at the villa with the plate upon

the railing, and then hurry towards the town.









CHAPTER XXV.



in the ELLESMERE ROAD.





Holdsworth spend the great part of the evening in write a letter

to Mr. Sherman. at twelve o’clock next day he be an inmate of Mrs.

Parrot’s house in the Ellesmere Road.



in his walk to the lodging he have meet only strange face, one or

two of which look after he, strike, perhaps, by his keen fugitive

glance, his slow pace that steal along the ground, and his depressed

head, as though there be a shame in his heart that make the daylight

painful.



Mrs. Parrot fuss about he for some time, and tire he with an

account of the article she have purchase for he. her memory be

slow, and her capacity of reckon very indifferent; hence it take

her twenty minute to account for the expenditure of twelve shilling.

happily, it be her mother’s day for walk in the garden, and

Holdsworth could see the old lady--a mere wisp of a figure, in ancient

black satin reach to her ankle and cling to her leg, a nose

like the Duke of Wellington’s, and a chin like Punch’s--hobble along

a gravel walk, look with afflict agitation around she, and

cough like a rattle, as a signal to her daughter, who, when she have

do with her account, hurry out.



the control Holdsworth have keep upon himself, while Mrs. Parrot

remain in the room, he could now put aside; indeed, the suffering

cause he by his pen-up agitation imperatively demand that the

emotion should have play. now he would walk hastily to and fro the

room, and then fle himself into a chair, clasp his hand tightly,

and then rise and stand before the window and send glance passionate

and shrink at the house occupy by the Conways.



now that he be close to his wife, now that any moment might reveal

her walk past his window with his child, perchance, at her side,

he dread lest the unparalleled situation he have force himself into

would prove too heavily charge with cruel condition for he to

bear. never once then, never once afterwards, do the vague impulse

possess he to go forth and declare himself and claim she. no! a sense

of honour that be inexorable, since it prohibit the faint echo

of the soul’s secret passionate yearn to make itself audible, have

decreed his silence and enforce the obedience of inclination.



the only concession grant be the enjoyment of such ghostly and

barren pleasure as his heart could find in the knowledge of the close

neighbourhood of the two who be so dear to he. oh! bitter waking of

memory, to recall he from the sunny vision of the old time when his

joy be complete, and love a permanent possession to enrich his nature

with all gracious and generous emotion, to thrust he into the gray

and bleak twilight of a loveless and desolate life, which the recover

power could only embitter by recurrence to the thing that be lose!



his eye wander ceaselessly and restlessly towards the window. from

time to time people go by with the slow, aimless step of person

who walk for no other end than exercise. an old gentleman, with a

white moustache and a dark skin, stop, with another old gentleman,

in high shirt-collar and a tail-coat, opposite Holdsworth’s window,

and argue, with many galvanic flourish of the arm and grimace of

the face. there be much political excitement abroad at that time,

owe to the Reform Bill of the Grey Administration, to which the

royal assent have be give; and the dark-skin old gentleman--whose

age, warmth, and intemperate flourish be as demonstrative of his

politic as his language--bade his companion take notice that, before

five year be pass, England would be a tenth-rate power, govern

by a mob, with a jesuit seat on every hearth, a Nuncio preach at

St. Paul’s, not a Bible to be find in the country, and the gallow

groan under string of honest patriot. the old gentleman in the

high shirt-collar, who clearly share his friend’s opinion, nod

savagely, ask with his shoulder, “what would you have?” and then

move on a dozen pace, to be stop again by the other old gentleman

with prophecy, maybe, more blood-chilling and awful than those he have

already declaim.



presently Holdsworth, scarcely conscious of what he be about, leave

the window and approach the bookcase. he pull out a volume,

which prove to be an old copy of Gulliver’s Travels, “adorn’d with

sculpture;” and his eye lighting on that passage in which Gulliver

close his account of his second voyage,[2] his thought troop off to

his old sea-fare life, the book close upon his finger, and he sink

in deep meditation.



[2] “in a little time I and my family and friend come to a right

understanding: but my wife protest ‘I should never go to sea any

more;’ although my evil destiny so order, that she have not the power

to hinder I.”



the restoration of his memory be comparatively so recent, that he

have find no leisure to recall those frightful experience of his

which could not recur without overwhelm he with an unspeakable

horror of the sea. he now understand that it should have be his

duty to call upon the owner of the “Meteor,” and acquaint they with

the circumstance of the wreck of their vessel, and the death of the

person who be in his boat, all whom he clearly remember. there

be friend, doubtless, both in England and America, who could wish to

receive tiding of the fate of these people, though the long interval

of five year should tell as plain a story as Holdsworth could relate.

he know not whether the inmate of the other boat have be save, and

he would have give much to ascertain this; but he understand that any

communication he make to the shipowner would be almost sure to appear

in print, by which his wife would learn that he be alive. “no! let the

world think I dead!” he exclaim bitterly. he have only to live for

the past now--for that memory which have betray he and ruin his

life. his future be bare and barren, and there be nothing in all the

world that could kindle one ray of comfort in his hopeless heart but

the bleak privilege of dwelling near his wife and child.



he restore the book to its place and return to the window.



in the roadway, a few yard to the right, a little girl be stand,

hold a doll. she be a very little creature, with bright yellow

hair down her back, and she hold the doll in motherly fashion on her

arm, and caress it with her hand.



her back be towards Holdsworth, whose eye be root upon she.



she turn presently and look down the road, and Holdsworth see a

little face upon which God have grave a sign that make the poor father

clutch at the wall to steady himself. for there be his own face in

miniature--the face that dolly have love before the suffering of the

mind, and the anguish of hunger and thirst, have twist from it all

resemblance it have ever bear to what be manly and beautiful in the

human countenance.



he press his hand to his eye and gaze again, then run to the

bell-rope and pull it. but when he have do this he wish it undo.

for would not his agitation excite Mrs. Parrot’s suspicion? what be

there in a stranger’s child that should so interest he?



he bite his lip and control himself with desperate will; and, when

Mrs. Parrot open the door, he say to she in a steady voice and with

a forced smile:



“I be sorry to trouble you. I be very fond of little child. pray can

you tell I who that child be, there?”



Mrs. Parrot draw to the window, evidently find nothing odd in the

question, and say:



“why, that’s little Nelly Holdsworth, Mrs. Conway’s daughter.”



“ah!” exclaim Holdsworth.



“she _be_ a dear!” continue Mrs. Parrot. “I be very fond of that

child, Mr. Hampden. she’s the only child I’ the road I allow to come

into my garden, for child be so wilful, there’s no tellin’ what

they’ll do the moment your eye be off ’em. see what a little lady

she look, and how prettily she hold her doll! she’s wait for her

mamma, I suppose.”



Mrs. Parrot rap with her nail on the window. the child look

round, and Holdsworth shrink away. Mrs. Parrot beckon. holdsworth

would have stop she, but could find no word.



“she’s come, Mr. Hampden. I’ll bring she to you, sir, if you’ll wait

a moment.”



and out she go.



in a few second she return, lead by the hand the child, who hang

back when she catch sight of the white-face, bearded man.



“there, Mr. Hampden, this be my pretty young friend, little nelly!”

exclaim Mrs. Parrot, stoop to give the child a kiss. “go and shake

hand with the gentleman, my dear, and show he your nice doll. I’ll

tell you when I see your mamma.”



“come, dear, come to I,” say Holdsworth in a low voice.



the child approach he slowly, stop now and again, and look

shyly at Mrs. Parrot.



“Tut! tut!” exclaim that lady. “what be you afraid of, Miss Nelly?

go and shake hand with the gentleman, like a little lady.”



Holdsworth put out his hand; the child advance a step nearer; he fall

upon one knee and draw she to he.



for some moment he could not speak; he could only look at she--look

with eye of all-devour love absorb all the sweetness of that

young face, feel a pang of exquisite joy, but shiver quickly as

his finger lock themselves upon her tiny hand.



he long to press the little creature to his heart, to fasten his lip

upon her mouth, to weep over she.



“tell I your name, little one!”



“nelly,” reply the child, keep at arm’s length from he, and

stare into his face.



“nelly what?”



“Nelly Ho’dwor’t.”



his own name, thus lisp by she, thrill through he; he catch his

breath, and say:



“may I kiss you?”



she put up her mouth, and he kiss she.



“how pretty your hair be!” he murmur, in a voice of exquisite

tenderness, which make Mrs. Parrot turn suddenly and look at he. he

meet her glance with a smile, and say:



“I be very fond of child. will this little girl come and see I here

sometimes?”



“ay, that she will, sir. willn’t you, nelly?”



“Det.”



“how old be you, nelly?”



“Four.”



“who give you that doll?”



“Mamma.”



“you will bring dolly to see I, and we will have tea, all three of we.

what have I get here? a bright shilling! that will buy dolly a parasol!”



no word can describe the tone his voice take as he speak.



“what do you say to the gentleman for this beautiful present?” cry

Mrs. Parrot.



“tank,” say the child, put the doll on the floor to examine the

money with both hand.



“oh, here come your mamma!” say Mrs. Parrot. “make your reverence to

the gentleman ... there’s a dear; pick up dolly ... that’s right.”



she take Nelly’s hand and run with she out of the room.



the mother, stand at her gate on the other side of the road, look

up and down the road, catch sight of Mrs. Parrot and the child, and

cross over to they.



they remain opposite Holdsworth’s window talking, while he, shrink

against the wall, peer at they through the muslin curtain.



the five year which have pass since he have see his wife have work

but a very little change in she. there be more womanly fulness in

her form; and this be about all those five year have do for she.

her face be still as youthful as when Holdsworth have last look on

it, her eye still possess their deep and delicate tint, the hair

its richness and lustre, the mouth its sweetness, the whole face that

almost infantine expression, convey by soft shadowy line and the

archness of the pencil eyebrow, which make it beautiful in repose,

more beautiful yet in sorrow. but, young and fair as she seem, there

be a deep-rooted care in her face which, without qualify its

freshness, yet mingle in her smile, and live in her eye, and fix

a wistful look on she, such as would be see in one who linger long,

long wait for the summon to depart which no day bring. her dress

be shabby, her glove old; but her beauty make even her faded apparel,

cut after the unbecoming fashion then in vogue, picturesque. she wear a

white crape handkerchief over her bare shoulder, and a bonnet-shape

hat, ornament with a dark feather, which, droop over her back,

impart a peculiar vividness to the light, sheeny gold of her hair.



Strained as his ear be, Holdsworth could not hear her voice, though

Mrs. Parrot’s kindly cackle be audible enough. it be manifest that

they exchange mere commonplace civility; and presently Mrs. Parrot

drop a courtesy, and the mother and child walk slowly away.



Holdsworth watch they with just such a look in his eye as have be

in they when, rack with torture in the open boat, he have cast glance

full of passionate despair round the horizon for the ship that be to

rescue he. he see the little girl hold up her shilling, whereupon the

mother stop and look back, then continue her walk and pass out

of sight.









CHAPTER XXVI.



over the WAY.





it be natural, after the first liveliness of the emotion which have

be excite in Mrs. Parrot’s breast by the installation of a lodger

worth fourteen shilling a week to she, have in some degree subside,

that she should begin to wonder who that lodger be.



she have be particularly strike and greatly take by his behaviour

to the little girl, and infer, of course, that he be a humane and

tender-hearted man, a conjecture which, although it be true, do no

credit to her sagacity, consider the circumstance on which it be

base; since it be a notorious fact, that great rascal will admire,

pet, and “tip” little child, whose parent they would not scruple to

rob of their very last farthing.



but though Mrs. Parrot have no doubt as to her lodger’s humanity of

character after what she have see, she could not by any mean feel

so sure as to the position he hold either in or out of society, the

call he have follow, if ever he _have_ follow a calling, or the

part of the world he come from. his name be Hampden. that be English.

but have he a christian name? no initial stand between the Mr. and the

Hampden, on the card affix to his portmanteau. _be_ he a Christian?

she hope he be. she be no judge of other religion; but she must

say, when she let her lodging to people, that she like to _feel_ that

they be Christians.



he have order dinner at two o’clock; and when she come in to lay the

cloth, for she keep no servant, she find he still at the window,

stare into the empty road as earnestly as if it be fill with a

very beautiful and novel procession. but she could only suppose that he

look out of the window because he be new to the place.



he smile softly when he meet her glance, but do not speak, nor would

she hazard any remark herself, for fear of be think intrusive.

all that he say during dinner be to express himself well pleased with

her cooking; but she notice, in remove the dish, that, pleasantly

as he have praise the piece of roast mutton, he have scarcely taste it,

and that of the four potato she have put into the dish, three and a

half remain.



Whilst in the kitchen she hear he leave the house, and, when her

task be do, she go upstairs to her mother’s room, whither she have

conduct the old lady soon after little Nelly’s visit.



“if the gentleman don’t eat more every day than he’s just have for

dinner,” say she, throw herself back in a chair and fan her hot

face with the corner of her apron, “I reckon we shall have a funeral

here before long.”



“a what!” gasp the old woman, who sit upright in a cane chair, near

the open window, with an immense Bible on her right hand and her

spectacle on the top of it.



“he’s no more than skin an’ bone as he be,” continue Mrs. Parrot,

“but it be a picter to see he with the child. I never see a man more

soft with a child before.”



“he ain’t likely to make strange nise o’ night, be he, Sairey?”

exclaim the old woman, earnestly regard her daughter with a pair

of eye from which all expression and light seem literally wash

out, leave nothing but two circle of weak, dim blue.



“I don’t think so. he seem to I quiet enough. he’s fond o’ stare

into the road. one might think he’s try to find out where he be. I

niver see a stranger face. he don’t look english-like, and yet he talk

uncommon well. I can tell by his boot--which be square as square at

the toe, and his clothe, which have an odd twist somehow--that he’s

not from these part. maybe, he’s from Ireland.”



“I hope not, Sairey,” ejaculate the old woman, bend forward with

the profoundly confidential air of old age. “I be once fellow-sarvint

with a ayrish futman as be allus talkin’ of burnin’ down house, an’

his speech run on so it be niver to be trust, for niver be such

lie as he use to tell. you’d good gi’ he notice, Sairey. you can

say I gi’ yer more trouble nor you can well get through, and recommend

Burton’s lodgin’s to he. Burton’s a strong man, and kape dog.”



“Tut! I’m not afeard!” say Mrs. Parrot, toss up her hand and

give her cap a pull. “there’s no more harm in the man than there

be in you, for don’t I tell yer how he give the girl a shillin’

and speak that soft to it, it make I feel as if I could ha’ cry.

give he notice, and he not here a day yit! fourteen shillin’ be

fourteen shillin’ in these scarce time, to say nothin’ of his being

as well-speak a man as iver you listen to in your life; an’ as for

his face, it be but as God make it, an’ beauty be but skin deep, as t’

parson say, an’ I’m for lettin’ well alone.”



“if he ain’t ayrish,” say the old woman, stroke the back of her

lean hand, “he may be very well. but sitch talk of invasion from that

nashun as I use to hear when I be a gal, an’ the drink an’ shootin’

as go on there, be enough to wet your hair with perspiration ...”



“_i_ don’t say he be irish. _I_ don’t know what he be. he be askin’

about Mrs. Conway, though she’s unbeknown to he, as any one might tell

who heerd he questionin’. he want Miss Nelly to bear he company at

tea, and I don’t see why the child cann’t come, if the mother ’ull let

it. I willn’t take it upon myself to bring the child in. I’ll speak to

the mother when I see she. I like Mrs. Conway. she’s a nice-speak

lady, but seem to know a deal of grief, poor thing. it ’ud be a mussy

if that husband of hers ’ud take it into his head to pull out all his

own tooth. the cook at Mrs. Short’s be tellin’ I he’s grow that

wicious there’s no wishin’ he a civil good mornin’. an’ drink! don’t

I see he pass here yesterday evenin’, staggerin’ on his leg like a

doll which a child try to teach walkin’ to?”



“the ’pothecarie use to draw tooth in my day; now they must be all

gentlefolk as look into your mouth,” say the old woman, who have be

three minute search in her pocket for the snuff-box that lie open,

with some of its sand-colour content spill, in her lap.



“Pretty gentlefolk!” exclaim Mrs. Parrot, pull up the old

woman’s dress and tilt the spilt snuff into the box. “if they’re all

like Mr. Conway, I’d rather carry a toothache to my grave than have it

stop wi’ the lockjaw, which they tell I he give to Mr. Timpson;

for drink have take away the use of his mind, and he push the wrong

instrayment into the man’s mouth and nearly choke he, he do, and

then take out two wrong tooth after all; beautiful tooth they be, for

Mr. Timpson show they to I hisself, with the tear standin’ in his

eye, wroppe up in silver paper.”



“thank God! he cann’t draw none o’ my tooth!” mumble the old woman,

talk through her nose in rapt enjoyment of the flavour of the snuff.

“they’re all go.”



“I notice Mrs. Conway’s gownd to-day. if I be her husband, I’d scorn

to let she appear in sitch a rag. and there be darn in the knee

o’ that child’s stocking as make ’em look forty year old. they’re

always I’ the same dress, both of ’em. there’s a silk she put on o’

sunday, all wear thin over the buzzum, and I remember the bonnet she

have on to-day iver since I’ve know she. sitch a pretty face as she

have, too! I expect he must ha’ tell she some fine lie to get she to

marry he. they say he niver do well, even when he be in the High

Street, wi’ that show-box of his stuck up, fill wi’ gape gum an’

naked tooth as turn the stomach to see. he must ha’ sell that piece

of ugliness, for I don’t see it nowhere outside his house, which be

a mussy, for I’d as lief see a skiliton on a pole for a sign! Fancy a

doctor settin’ up a death’s-head to show his trade!”



she jump from her chair with a face and gesture of disgust, and

throw some knitting with the pin through it into her mother’s lap,

adjust her cap before the glass, and leave the room.



       *       *       *       *       *



there be always some truth in gossip: and there be a great deal in

what Mrs. Parrot have say of Mr. Conway, who, as we have see, hold

no place at all in her opinion. but then sympathy for dolly be to be

expect from a woman who, if she do not know what it be to live with

a drunkard, have know what it be to live with a surly man, whose eye

be evil, and whose voice be thick, and whose characteristic method of

express discontent be by hold his clench fist under his wife’s

nose.



Mr. Conway be pass Mrs. Parrot’s door at the very moment that

Mrs. Parrot be leave her mother’s bedroom; we shall not have an

opportunity of see much of he, have the fortune of a well kind

of hero to deal with; so, while Holdsworth be away from his lodging,

we’ll step into the road and have a look at the dentist, and follow he

into his house.



he be a man with sandy whisker and light hair, but by no means

ill-looking. on the contrary, there be material in his face out of

which a very pleasing countenance could be make; a well-shape nose, a

well-shape forehead, a good chin, a facial outline clearly define and

perfectly symmetrical.



but there never be a well illustration than this man’s face of the

truth, that good feature make but a very small portion of beauty.



I want a word to express that middle quality of aspect which be

contrive by the mingling of comely lineament with bad passion.

possibly the effect be no more than a neutralisation of nature’s good

intention, wherein we behold a handsome countenance sink into a

specie of physical negation, by moral quality tug it hard in the

direction of repellent ugliness.



a most unstriking face at which you would barely glance, and pass on

absolutely unimpressed. his thin lip might mark both cruelty and

selfishness; his eye be make heavy by their droop lid, and the

irid be pale and unintelligent. he be dress in the style of the

time, of course; pantaloon strap over his boot, a frock-coat

gape in a circle round a great quantity of black satin stock (in

which be two pin and a chain). but the pantaloon be fray at the

heel and bag at the knee; and the coat be suspiciously polish

at the elbow and the rim of the collar. he walk with a quick, uneasy

step, his hat slightly cock, and his hand in his breech pocket,

and arrive at the gate of his house, open it by give it a kick

with his foot.



he enter the sitting-room with his hat on, and find the cloth lay

for dinner, but nobody in the room, which be a soiled and dingy

apartment, although the house be a new one, and the paper fresh and

the ceiling white. but no paper and whitewash could qualify the sordid

suggestion of the old drugget imperfectly nail over the floor,

the old leather sofa and the old leather arm-chair, the mantelpiece

decorate by a pair of plate candlestick mark with indent, the

dingy red curtain, the _papi mâché_ table in the window, with the

mother-of-pearl drop out of it.



the subtle magic of feminine finger which extract from rubbish itself

what hidden capability it may possess of comfort the eye with some

faint aspect of taste, seem either never to have be exercise

upon this room, or to have find itself powerless to deal with it. the

only feminine sign be a small bundle of child’s stocking on the top

of an old work-basket on the sofa.



Mr. Conway put his head out of the door and call, “be you there,

wife?”



“yes,” reply dolly’s voice from downstair.



“how long will dinner be?”



“five minute!”



he throw his hat down and walk into the “surgery,” a room at the end

of the passage, furnish with a chest of drawer, a toilet-table and

a look-glass, an arm-chair, an ugly circular box with a basin let

into it, stand beside the arm-chair; on the toilet-table, some small

hand-glass, a pair of forceps, and three unfinished false tooth.

through the window be to be see a slip of garden of the breadth of

the house, and about fifty foot long--its neglected state, its few

pine shrub, and a flag-pole with a vane a-top that croak to every

passage of the wind, show up very squalidly against the neighbouring

garden, which be richly stock with wall-fruit and fern and green

plant.



little Nelly be in this piece of ground with her doll, seat on the

grass, and at that moment make such a picture as a painter would stop

to study and receive into his mind; her round dark-blue eye follow

the swallow which chase each other high in the air, her mouth pout

into an expression of exquisite infantine wonder, her bright hair about

her shoulder, and look, as the breeze stir the sunshine upon it,

like a fall shower of fine gold.



Mr. Conway stare at the child for a moment, and then turn away and

saunter towards the door, but come back to open one of the drawer

in the chest and extract a leather-cover bottle, which he shake at

his ear, and put to his mouth.



o God! what contrast there be in life, lie so close together that

the devil, though his worship be no big than a man, might measure

the space between with outstretched hand! look at Purity and Innocence

in the garden, with its eye raise to heaven; and the skulking fellow

in the dingy room swallow brandy as a man steal money; and in the

room below--a darksome, scantily-furnish kitchen--a sweet-faced woman

do servant’s work, and urge the slattern by her side into quick

movement, that the gentleman upstairs shall have no occasion to use

bad language.



she come upstairs presently, this dolly, her face flush, and

breathe quickly from the hurry of her movement, and bear with her

own hand a dish that will furnish but a poor repast, though she have

do she good to make what little there be palatable. the slattern,

with wisps of red hair about her forehead, and loose shoe, which beat

a double knock at each step upon the uncarpeted staircase, follow,

arm with a jug and a loaf of bread. behind come little Nelly, whom

the mother have summon before leave the kitchen, and who have climb

the staircase with more labour than Mont Blanc be scale by the Alpine

tourist.



no word be speak. Nelly be lift into a chair by her mother, and Mr.

Conway seat himself before the dish and fill a glass from the jug of

ale, take care--a true connoisseur in such matter--to let the liquor

fall from a height, to secure a froth, into which he dip his mouth and

nose.



the slattern leave the room; and dolly cut up some meat and bread

for the little one, with a whisper in her ear to say grace.



“do you get the money, Robert?” she ask presently, eat little

herself, and notice how Robert bribe his appetite with sup of ale.



“no. Davis be out.”



“what shall we do? I have only five and threepence left, and this meat

be not pay for.”



penny make a sordid enumeration; but we talk of pence, reader, when we

have only penny to spend.



“we must sell something, that’s all,” say Mr. Conway, with a kind of

defiant recklessness in his manner.



she give he a quick glance, look at her child, and then close the

knife and fork upon her plate.



he do not notice that she have eat about as much as would serve

a bird for a meal; neither do he appear to remark that she drink

water. _he_, at all event, keep the beer-jug at his elbow, from

which, in a very short time, he pour out the last glass.



the child alone continue eat.



“_i_ don’t know what’s to be do!” he exclaim in a voice of

suppress anger, push his chair from the table. “the people have

drop I for that french quack, in Mornington Street. I see three

carriage at his door when I pass just now. I ought never to have

leave the old shop. I do well there.”



“you would do well here if you give yourself a chance,” say dolly.

“the lady who call yesterday evening come again this morning. Martha

tell I she look annoyed when she hear you be out. she will go to

some one else, I suppose, now.”



“let she!” he call out. “how be I to know that people be come to

I after dark? week after week pass, and they don’t come, and--be

I go to hang about here a whole night, in the hope of a patient

turn up? why don’t she leave word at what hour she mean to call

to-day? I go out to collect some money, and you know it, though I can

guess what be in your thought. but it’s false--there’s my hand on it!”



he let his hand fall heavily on the table, and stare at his wife. she

slightly turn from he, and look through the window. he leave the

table and begin to pace the room. the child, having empty her plate

and want something to play with, have take the shilling Holdsworth

have give she from her pocket, and try to make it spin on the cloth.



“what’s that Nelly have get there?” say Mr. Conway.



“a shilling,” answer dolly.



“do you give it she?... look at our dinner!... you would pamper that

child if we be starve. talk to I of your five and threepence when

you can give your baby a shilling!”



“I do not give it to she.”



“who then?”



“a gentleman.”



“what gentleman?”



“a gentleman lodge at Mrs. Parrot’s.”



he look at she with irritable suspicion, and then say:



“do you see he give it? do he take you and the child for beggar?...

confound his impudence!... send Martha over to he with it.”



he turn to ring the bell.



“stop!” say dolly, quietly. “Nelly tell I that Mrs. Parrot tap

on the window to speak to she, and when she go in, she see the

gentleman, who kiss she, and give she the shilling to buy her doll a

parasol. no insult could have be intend by this.”



“oh, that be it!” exclaim Mr. Conway, “well, and why do you let the

child keep the money? she’ll lose it. take it from she.”



“it belong to she. she will not lose it.”



“yes, she will. nelly, give that money to your mamma.”



but Nelly double her fist over it, and hide her hand under the table.



“do you hear what I say?” cry Mr. Conway.



“why will you not let she keep it?” ask dolly.



“be I master here or not?” shout Mr. Conway. “give that money to your

mother, child!”



Nelly begin to whimper, terrify by the man’s voice, but loath to

surrender her little treasure. he step up to she, whip the little

hand from under the cloth, and force the shilling from it, put it

into his pocket.



“though I’m a beggar by my own folly,” he exclaim, walk to the

door, “I’ll not be insult and defy by the beggar I have bring

about I.”



his finger be in his pocket, and it seem as though he would pull

the money out and fle it on the table. but second thought prevail;

he jerk his hat on his head, and march out of the house, bang

the door after he.



dolly watch to see if he would step across to Mrs. Parrot’s; but he

walk straight on.



“Hush, my darling, hush!” she exclaim, catch up the sob child.

“dolly shall have her parasol; I will buy it myself. Hush, my pet!

nelly’s tear break poor mamma’s heart.... oh, John! oh, husband!”

she murmur; “why do God take you from I? why do he lead I and my

little one into this misery?”



truly, it be misery, of a forlorn and hopeless kind. but you have see

the man dolly have marry at his bad. the most brutal husband be not

always brutal. the drunkard be not always drunk. More colour than

black and white go to the painting of a man off the stage, where cork

eyebrow strike no horror, and blood-boltere cheek prove nothing more

than a neglect of soap.



Mr. Conway have his soft hour, when he would shed tear, smite his

breast, and call himself a fiend--have reference, by this flattering

title, to nothing but his behaviour to dolly.



he be undoubtedly in love with she when he marry she; and the sweet

face which have make he haunt Southbourne, to the neglect of his

patient, would still, even after two year, have too much potency

not to occasionally soften and give movement to the humanity which

lie in he, harden and drown in drink. though he have always, as

long as people could remember he in Hanwitch, be what they call

a dissipated man, he have manage somehow or other to get a living, to

keep his landlord civil and wear good clothe. lady be not want

who call he handsome. his manner, when sober, be courteous; his

language correct; his finger dexterous in pull tooth out or put

tooth in. those who know anything of he know that he never save a

penny; that be he to make ten thousand a year he would never save a

penny; but they always say that, if he would only take a deep-root

dislike to beer and brandy, go to bed at ten and rise at seven, attend

to his business and give up smoking pipe in the street, he might

obtain enough money to enable he to keep a carriage and live in good

style.



how he meet dolly matter little. she be live in one room at

Southbourne at that time, try to obtain a livelihood by take in

needlework. she be miserably poor, with a little baby at her breast.

Old Mr. Newcome, the rector, do his good for she, and allow she what

little he could afford out of his slender income, which enable she to

pay her rent. but she have to clothe and feed her child and herself, and

the work she procure be scanty and poorly pay for. God know how she

manage to struggle through those day! Mr. Conway ask she to marry

he, but she answer “no,” bitterly, for her love for Holdsworth be

a passion. then her only friend, Mr. Newcome, die; her health break

down; she be absolutely destitute; and so, for her baby’s sake--but

shrink from the marriage as one shrink from the commission of

an evil deed, and with a heart in she so heavy that nothing but her

love for her child seem to keep she alive--she give her hand to Mr.

Conway, and go to live with he at Hanwitch.



she have no affection for the man. her marriage be a bitter necessity,

and she hate it and herself for that. she have no knowledge of Conway’s

habit, though she have have penetration enough to miss certain moral

qualification which be to be feel and cannot be explain. now she

discover that he be an intemperate, improvident man, hasty in his

temper, selfish, and at the same time neglectful of his own interest.



he be some way ahead in his downward career when he marry she. the

addition his marriage make to his expense quicken his pace, as an

object, roll slowly at first, improve its velocity in proportion to

the increase of its distance from the starting-point. one by one his

patient desert he. he insult his landlord, to whom he owe money,

who give he notice to quit. he then hire the little house in which we

have find he, and be now illustrate one of the great mystery of

social life--the mystery of live without money, of keep a house

over his head without a shilling in his pocket, of wear boot and

coat without the mean in his purse to pay off the milkman’s sixpenny

score.



how be this do? there be people do it every day. they be do

more: they be keep man-servant, rent big house, wear

fine dress, frequent fashionable haunt, on nothing a year. how

Thackeray puzzle over this problem! how Dickens try to explain it

and fail: for he be always drive to a last moment, when some good

genius step forward to help. imagination cann’t deal with a feat which

make nothing do the work of a great deal.



there be no doubt something aggressive, even to good nature, in the

brooding melancholy that go about its duty lifelessly, which give

spiritless attention to matter of moment and significance, which look

complaint without speak it, and address itself to every task of

life with an air of reproachful endurance.



a man possess of such an inflammable temper as Conway would be

constantly take fire in the presence of such a melancholy; and it

must be confess that dolly embody the part with some degree of

completeness. silent and mournful submission to fate be the wrong

attitude to assume towards a man in whom be a good deal of the fool.

a powerful virago, with muscular arm and a venomous tongue, would

have keep he to his work and out of the tavern by the irresistible

influence of word support by finger-nail.



dolly, whose heart be never with he, soon learn to despise he. it

be true that she endeavour, at the beginning of their married life,

to win he from his extravagant and reckless course by entreaty

and the mild persuasion of caress; but she soon cease her appeal

on find that they take no effect, and only rarely allude to his

habit, which, having plunge they into poverty, be keep they

there, and sink they lower and low each day.



with an inconsistency not very uncommon, he resent her silence at

the same time that he know the expression of her thought would enrage

he. he be still sufficiently under the control of her beauty to feel

jealous of her love, which he very well know be with the man they

both think dead. that truth have leak out long ago. he once hear

she teach her child to pray; and presently lift up her own voice in

a prayer which have no name in it but John’s, whom she cry aloud to,

bid his spirit take witness of the suffering which have drive she

into an act that make she hateful to herself. once, when her gentle

sweetness be stir into passion by he, she declare that she have

never love he; that she have marry he for her child’s sake; that if

God take her babe from she she would kill herself, for her husband be

in heaven, and his voice speak in her conscience, eternally reproach

she for forget the vow they have made--_that though death should

sunder they, the survivor would be true to love and memory, and live

alone_.



but his petulance, his churlishness, his occasional brutality, indeed,

be not owe to this. she have merely put his own knowledge of she into

language; and since he have marry she, fully persuade that the gift

of her hand have be dictate by pure necessity only, he could scarcely

find himself alienate by the confession of her motive. poverty and

drink be the two demon that master he. and poverty without drink

would have do the work; for his happen to be one of those boneless

nature which give under a very small weight; one of those weak

character who, if they find themselves in a gutter, be satisfied to

lie there and roll there, and moisten the mud with which they bedaub

those about they with tear, and make their settlement gross with

oath, and shriek, and reproach.









CHAPTER XXVII.



FATHER and child.





all next morning Holdsworth keep watch for dolly and his child, but do

not see they. but Mr. Conway have pass when Mrs. Parrot happen to

be in the room lay the cloth for dinner, and the woman have direct

Holdsworth’s attention to he. the glimpse he obtain, however, be

very brief. all that he see be a sandy-whisker gentleman, with

a tilt hat, aim with rather uncertain leg for the gate with the

brass plate upon it, and vanish with an alacrity that be painfully

suggestive of a disorder vision.



“there he go! drink as usual!” exclaim Mrs. Parrot, disgustfully,

give the table-cloth an angry twitch.



“how do he live? I have see nobody call at his house yet!”



“no, sir; and I don’t think you’re likely to. person as cann’t get

serve at the other tooth-drawer be too sensible to walk all this way

to get their jaw break.”



“be they _very_ poor, do you think?”



“why, sir, I suppose he must pay his rent somehow, but I don’t know as

he do anything more. I’m tell that they owe money all over the town,

but the tradespeople make no fuss, because, as Mr. Jairing the butcher

say to I, ‘it’s all very fine, Mrs. Parrot,’ he say, ‘talkin’ of

hexecution, but what’s the use o’ go to the expense of a distress

when there’s nothing to seize?’ there’s a deal in that, sir.”



“God help they!” mutter Holdsworth to himself. then look up he

say, “do you think Mrs. Conway would let her little girl come and have

tea with I this afternoon?”



“I should think she would, sir, and feel honour by the askin’.”



“I have not see the child to-day.”



“no, sir! Mrs. Conway don’t often come out. she kape a bit of a wench

as do she arrand, and I’ve tell the slut time out o’ mind to put

her bonnet on, an’ not go flyin’ down the road as though a orficer be

arter she, disgracin’ of our neighbourhood, and frightening away any

respectable person as might be comin’ wi’ a bad tooth. I don’t think of

_he_, sir. if I could put a sixpence in his way I would, for the sake

of his wife an’ the little one.”



“how shall I invite little nelly if I do not see she?”



“I’ll run across, if you like, when I’ve get your dinner ready, and ask

Mrs. Conway if she’ll let the child come. perhaps you’ll just watch,

sir, and tell I when the husband leave the house. I don’t want to

meet he if I can help it.”



an hour elapse before Holdsworth see Mr. Conway pass on his way to the

High Street, on which he ring the bell and inform Mrs. Parrot that

she might now call on Mrs. Conway in safety.



his anxiety to have the child impress his landlady as a very odd

thing. she could understand his admiration of Nelly; she could also

understand his call she in and give she a kiss and a present. no

man’s heart, she consider, could fail to be warm by the sight of so

pretty a little girl. but she could not understand he post himself

at the window, and look trouble because he do not see the child,

and show himself as anxious to have she to tea as if she be a grown

woman, and he be court she.



she sail across the road (watch by Holdsworth), her cap-string

stream over her shoulder, and walk up to the door of the conway’

house, and give a single knock. she be keep wait so long, that when

the door be at last open by the “bit of a slut,” who, to judge by

her complexion, appear to have be devote the last hour or two to

black-lead her face, Mrs. Parrot, instead of ask for Mrs. Conway,

begin storm at she for “kape respectable folk on the doorstep

while she sit readin’ ha’porths o’ bad fiction by the kitchen fire.”



“I ben’t readin’. I don’t hear yer. who do you want--missus?” say

the girl sulkily.



“why, Mrs. Conway, of course. show I in, and go an’ tell she at once

that I’m here,” reply Mrs. Parrot, not wait to be show in, but

push into the middle of the passage.



the girl shamble off, and presently Mrs. Conway come up the kitchen

stair. the skirt of her dress be pin up at the waist, and her

arm-sleeve be above her elbow, display the whiteness and

fineness of the skin, though the arm be very thin.



“how do you do, Mrs. Parrot? you will excuse my wretched untidy

appearance. I be do a little washing downstair, and would not keep

you wait whilst I make myself presentable.”



“niver mention it, ma’am,” answer Mrs. Parrot, look with pleasure,

mingle with pain, at the sweet face, in which, now that there be no

hat to shadow it the sorrow and care be clearly see. her prettiness

be but enhance by the loop-up skirt, show the little foot and

small firm ankle. her bright hair be in disorder, and there be the

little flush of recent exertion on her cheek.



“I’ve call wi’ a message from my gentleman lodger. he want Miss

Nelly to drink tea with he, and send I across to ask you to let she

come.”



“the same gentleman who give nelly a shilling yesterday?” ask dolly,

look half surprised and half pleased.



“yes, ma’am. he’s a very nice person, and seem uncommon partial to

child. he’s be all the morning on the look-out for your little

gal, and I hope you’ll let she come, ma’am, and bring her doll wi’ she,

for I think he’ll take it to heart if you refuse.”



“oh, I will certainly send she. will half-past three do? I shall have

to dress she. pray give my compliment to the gentleman, and thank he

for his kindness. you have not tell I his name.”



“Hampden, ma’am; Mr. Hampden.”



“I have not yet see he. be he an old man? few young man care for

child.”



“to tell you the truth, ma’am, I’ve get no more idea of his age, than

I have of the age of my house. he’s get a deal o’ gray hair on his

head, and yet he ben’t an old man either, although to see he walk,

leanin’ on his stick, you’d take he to be sixty. I think he mean to

make friend wi’ your little gal, if you’ll let he, just for want o’

company. he don’t seem to know anybody in Hanwitch, nor to follow any

callin’ like. I doubt he’s a bit rich; but you see, ma’am, he only take

my lodgin’s the day before yesterday, and I’ve not have time to make he

quite out yit.”



say this, Mrs. Parrot drop a courtesy, and turn to depart,

take a quick comprehensive glance at the dingy little parlour as she

pass, and mentally compare it with her own room.



Holdsworth be at the window when she return, and she could hardly

forbear laughing, so tickled be she by his expectant face.



“Mrs. Conway’s compliment, sir, and she say that her little gal will

be with you at half-past three, thankin’ you for your kindness,” say

she, her eye twinkle with she suppress but perfectly good-natured

mirth.



“thank you, Mrs. Parrot, for take so much trouble,” exclaim

Holdsworth gleefully. “what time be it now? a quarter to three. I shall

just have time to walk into the High Street and buy a cake. she will

like a cake--a plum-cake I think; and shall I get some marmalade? yes,

she will enjoy marmalade--and what else? tell I, Mrs. Parrot; what do

little child like?”



“why, mostly sweet thing, sir. I guess the marmalade’ll take Miss

Nelly’s fancy. but don’t you trouble, sir; I can run out and buy you

what you want.”



“no--I be oblige to you. there be other thing she might like which I

shouldn’t be able to remember without see they. we will have tea at

four, Mrs. Parrot. I shall be back in twenty minute.”



Mrs. Parrot watch he leave the house and walk down the road as

swiftly as he could, lean on his stick. “well, if iver I see the

like of this!” she exclaim aloud. “I’ll not tell mother; it might

scare she. there’s something downright sing’ler in the notion of a

stranger takin’ all this trouble, and goin’ almost wild-like all along

of a little gal he never see before yisterday. some folk ’ud call it

alarmin’.”



_her_ nerve, however, be equal to the occasion; for whilst

Holdsworth be away, she journey upstairs and unlock a little

glass-front cupboard, screw into a corner of her bedroom, from

which she take a teapot, a cream-jug, and two cup and saucer of

brilliantly-colour china; likewise from an open box under her bed a

tray magnificently decorate with mother-of-pearl bird of paradise

seat on pink tree, and surround by a prospect not to be parallel

on this side the moon.



she return to the kitchen with these thing, and then enter the

garden and pick a bouquet of sweet-scented flower, with which she

furnish the tray. then she set to work upon a loaf of bread, and

produce in no time a number of thin and appetising slice; which do,

and the tray be arrange, she fall back a step to admire the effect.



at a quarter-past three Holdsworth return, follow by a boy with

his arm full of bag. he call to Mrs. Parrot, who come out and take

the bag from the boy, and place they upon the dining-room table.

More thing than edible have be purchase; though of these there

be enough to give an evening party upon--fruit, cake, pot of jam,

gingerbread-nut, sweetmeat, tart; there be a doll; there be also

a horse and cart; and there be an immense box of brick.



Mrs. Parrot turn pale, and be much too astonished to speak, as

Holdsworth thrust the bag and pot, the bun and the tart, into

her arm, and request she to take they at once into the kitchen,

and display they on plate to the very good advantage, ready for the

little one when the bell should ring for tea. he then hide the toy in

a closet, and station himself at the window to watch for Nelly.



Punctual to the moment, she come out of the house, lead by the hand by

the servant, who look horribly grimy. Holdsworth run to the door and

open it, and when the child come timidly up to he, snatch she up

in his arm, and hasten with she into the sitting-room, kiss she

all the way.



“there’s a good little pet,” he say sit down and keep she on

his knee. “let I take off your hat. nelly mustn’t be afraid of I.”



“my own! my own!” he murmur, as his linger finger caress she

soft hair, and he gaze with passionate love at her big eye, rove

with a half-scared expression from his face around the room.



“I dot dolly,” she say, produce the old toy from under her cloak.



“ay, that’s right; and dolly shall have a slice of cake all to herself.

here she be!” he exclaim, see Mrs. Parrot peep in at the door.

“will you take her hat and this little cape?”



“how do you do, dear?” say Mrs. Parrot, give the child a kiss.



“look at my frock!” exclaim nelly, hold up her dress, which have a

little embroidery work upon it, and which bear mark of much patient

mending and darning.



“beautiful! beautiful!” cry Mrs. Parrot. “I’ve set the kettle on to

bile, sir, and tea’ll be ready whiniver you’re pleased to want it.”



so say, she drop a courtesy, be greatly impress by Mr.

Hampden’s undoubted wealth, illustrate by his prodigal purchase, and

withdraw.



Father and child! a lonely man, gentle, honourable, faithful, as any

whom God in his wisdom have choose to afflict, open his heart to

receive and fold up the sweetness and innocence of his own little baby!



ah! I think even Mrs. Parrot might have guess the strange mystery

of this man’s desire for the child, have she but watch he from some

secret hiding-place when the door have close upon she.



he surrender himself to his emotion when he feel himself alone with

the little girl, and for many moment could not speak to she, could do

no more than look at she, search her fairy lineament with something

almost of a woman’s ecstasy, read his brief history of hopeful,

beautiful love in her fresh deep eye, and drink in greedily the

memory of the day that be no more, which throng from the face

that mirror his as it be when dolly know he, as the dew-drop

mirror the sun.



but he be recall to himself by the gathering expression of fear

in Nelly. indeed, there be something alarming enough to she in the

concentrated passion, all soft and holy as it be, that shine in his

fix regard.



such abandonment to feeling would not do, if the part he be to play

be to be complete.



he place she gently on the floor, and, go to the cupboard, bring

out the doll.



“see, Nelly! here be a little lady I invite expressly to drink tea

with you. she tell I that she have often see you pass her shop in the

High Street, and that she want to live with you. you must take she

home when you leave I, will you?”



Nelly stand for a moment transfix by the spectacle of this

gorgeously-dress creature, resplendent in blue gauze, bronze

boot, gilt sash, and flow red feather. then--so permanent be

human affection--she throw down her old doll and run forward, with

outstretche arm, to welcome and hug the stranger.



but the sum of her amazement be not yet make out. once more holdsworth

dive into the mysterious cupboard and produce the horse and cart,

which, he tell Nelly, be the chariot that have bring the young lady

to his house, and without which she never condescend to take the

air, be much too fine a lady to walk. the box of brick follow;

and presently Nelly be on the floor, take up the three toy one

after the other in quick succession, her avariciousness of enjoyment

perplex by the number of the object that minister to it.



Holdsworth kneel by her side and watch her face.



a man need not be a father to find something elevate and purify

in the contemplation of a child’s countenance, vary by tiny innocent

emotion, reflect the little play of her small passion, as her eye

reflect the object that surround she. but that subtle and sacred bond,

which unite a child’s life to a parent’s heart, create an impulse to

such contemplation which make the pleasure sweet than any other kind

of pleasure, by the infusion of an exquisite pathos, mingle with the

only kind of pride to which vanity seem to contribute nothing.



the natural bitterness which Holdsworth feel in think that his

little girl do not know he, that misfortune have thrust his love

out of the sphere of her own and his wife’s life, be convert into

tender melancholy by the emotion Nelly’s presence excited, and leave

his pleasure unalloye by pain. here be a little being who be his

at least; his by a right no sin, no folly, no error could challenge;

indisputably his, to survive, if God permit, into his future, when

the time should come for he to call himself aloud by the name of

Father, and ask her love as some recompense for that present sacrifice

of his which be enforce by grand obedience to the high law of

morality.



how hard it be to be thus true to himself, thus true to his wife,

thus true to the little one who must need share some portion of that

obligation of shame which would befall they all, be he to confess

himself--Judge! for you see he kneel by his child’s side; you may

behold his love in his eye; you may know that no upturned luminous

glance of her but thrill along the chord of his passion, and make

his heart gush forth its overfull tenderness, even until his sight

grow humid, and he turn his thought in a piteous aside to God for

courage and will, so to sustain this strange, pathetic happiness, that

no sorrow shall follow it.



       *       *       *       *       *



“nelly, we will have tea now,” he say; and he ring the bell, and

then come up to the child again, and, turn her face up, kiss she

suddenly, and seat himself at a distance with his chin upon his hand.



then Mrs. Parrot come in, arm with the tray, which she place upon

the table, while she challenge her lodger’s admiration by lightly

lift her gray eye and smirk.



yes, it be very beautiful. the bouquet make the room odoriferous at

once; the bird of paradise look splendid; the cup be elegant

enough to induce one to go on drink tea with stubborn disregard

of the nervous system, for hour and hour together, if only for an

excuse to handle they and have they under the eye. in order to bring

little Nelly’s head a little above the level of the table, Holdsworth

pile three or four of the folio on a chair, on which he seat she;

and that the two doll might be see to advantage, he very ingeniously

tie they together, and set they on a chair, lean against the table,

with a plate and a slice of bread-and-butter before they; whereat Nelly

laugh rapturously, clap her hand and fill the room with sweet

sound.



it be all Fairyland, these cup and toy and cake and what not, to

the little girl, whose tea at home be often no more than a slice of

dry bread, when her step-father have drink away the money he should

have give to his wife, and leave she without the mean to purchase an

ounce of butter. the sun be at the back of the house, and its rule

of yellow light flood the floor at the extremity of the apartment,

and fling a golden haze over that portion of the room where the table

stand. there be something so charming in the scene thus delicately

light, that Mrs. Parrot, who have be struggle with her modesty for

some minute, while she fidget over the plate and dish, suddenly

exclaim:



“I humbly beg parde for the liberty, sir, but would you mind mother

just takin’ one peep? she come into the kitchen while I be dressin’

the tray, an’ I tell she what be goin’ forward, an’ I think it ’ud do

her heart good to see this beautiful show.”



“let she come by all mean,” reply Holdsworth, touch and divert

by the perfect simplicity of these people.



presently fall a respectful knock, and Mrs. Parrot re-enter, follow

by a roman nose that come and vanish like an optical delusion near

the handle of the door.



“come in, mother; the gentleman’s kind enough to say you may,” say

Mrs. Parrot; and in falter the old woman, drop an aged courtesy,

and make her spectacle chatter in their wooden case as she strove to

withdraw they.



“ain’t this a picter, mother?”



“Niver see anything to aquil it,” reply the old lady, put on her

spectacle and gaze around she with many a convulsive motion of the

head. “why, Sairey, they’s our good cup!”



“yes, I tell you I be usin’ they. don’t you see the little gal,

mother?”



“see she? Yes,--of course I do. but she don’t want an old ’ooman like

I to kiss she, eh, my pretty? I’m very much obleege, sir,” drop

a creaking courtesy, “for the sight o’ this table.”



“would your mother like one of these cake for her tea?” say

Holdsworth.



“oh, sir, you’re very good.--Mother, the gentleman want to know if

you’ll accept of one of these cake for your tea?”



“thank ye, sir. I relish a bit o’ somethin’ sweet now and agin,”

reply the old lady, drop another courtesy as she receive the

cake. “I be cook to Squire Harrowden, lar’ bless yer! I dessay it be

year afore you be bear, and they do say as there niver be my aquil

I’ the makin’ o’ pie-crust. I’ve cook for as many as a hundred and

tin person, ay, that I have,” with intense earnestness, “as Sairey’ll

bear I witness, for she’s heerd the story from her own father, as be

gamekeeper to the Squire, an’ a more likely man you niver see, sir. his

name be Cramp, which he be a Croydon man, as you may happen to know

the name if you be iver in they part.”



“come, mother, we have stop long enough,” exclaim Mrs. Parrot,

put her hand upon the old lady’s arm.



they both courtesie; and then the old woman let fall the cake, which

roll under the table. holdsworth recover it for she, which act of

condescension be so overwhelming that she let the cake fall again; on

which Mrs. Parrot lose her temper, and hurry the old dame through the

door at a velocity to which her leg be quite unused, and possibly

quite unequal. she might be hear feebly remonstrate in a voice

similar to the sound a key make when turn in a rusty lock; and then

the door be close, and Holdsworth and Nelly be leave alone.



       *       *       *       *       *



if Mrs. Parrot have dare, she would have be glad to advise Holdsworth

“not to let the little gal eat too much, there bein’ nothing bad nor

sweetstuff for young stomach, which find milk-and-water sometimes

too much for ’em.” but, happily, Nelly be not a glutton; besides, the

majority of human being at her age eat only as much as they want, and

no more; we wait until our judgment be mature, until life be precious,

until we have experience most of the distemper which arise from

an overloaded stomach, before make ourselves thoroughly ill with

over-feeding!



by this time the child be perfectly at home with Holdsworth, and

enjoy herself immensely, vary a bite at a slice of cake with a

bite at some bread cover with jam, sip the good milk Holdsworth

have obtain for she with great gravity, and stare at the doll, and

then bend to make sure that her brick have not take to their heel

while she be look at the horse and cart, and that the horse have not

bolt with the cart while she be look at her brick.



Holdsworth scarcely remove his eye from her face. when he speak to

she there be a softness in his voice that melt like music on the ear.



at last she push her plate away, and Holdsworth ring the bell, and

give Mrs. Parrot private instruction to make up a parcel of the

remainder of the cake, etc., ready for Nelly to take home with she, he

clasp the child’s hand and go with she into the garden, she hold

the new doll, he drag the horse and cart after he.



there be a square of grass in this garden, and a bench on it; and here

Holdsworth sit, while Nelly play with her toy.



it be a roomy, old-fashioned garden, with aged wall, full of rusty

nail and rotten ligature, and a few tall pear-tree shelter a

small circumference of ground at their foot; and many fruit-tree

sprawl wildly against the wall. the moss be like a carpet on the

flint walk, and the box at the side of the bed be high and thick;

and at the top of the garden be an old hencoop, hedge about with

wirework, behind which some dozen hen scratch the soil for worm,

and make the air drowsy with their odd, half-suppress muttering.



mood possess we, sometimes, which such a scene as this will affect

more pleasurably than a garden burst with exotic, and tend with

the high artistic judgment. there be something very calm in

homely shrub, and old fruit-tree with their root hide by the long,

vivid grass, and uncouth weed thrust their rude shape among violet

bed; and the solemn chatter of barn-door hen sun themselves in

hot space, or lie like dead thing with a wing half-bury in sand

and dirt, will sometimes impart a more agreeable tranquillity to the

mind than the choice song of nightingale warble in grove under a

full moon.



Holdsworth suffer the child to have her full sportive will for some

time, and then, think her tired, call she to he.



she run to he at once, and he perch she on his knee.



“be nelly afraid of I now?”



“no. nelly dot afraid. Nelly love ’oo.”



in proof whereof she put her mouth up for a kiss.



“will Nelly come to see I every day?”



“Det.”



“do Nelly’s papa love she?”



he believe that she have be teach to regard Mr. Conway as her papa.

it be a sore tax upon the gentle mood then on he to put the question

in that form, but he wish to learn if his child be well treat by

her step-father.



the question puzzle she. indeed, she be a very little thing, and

backward in her speech. it be delicious to see she knit her tiny

brow, and gaze with her full, deep, earnest eye on Holdsworth, with

a half-intelligence in her face, and all the rest child-sweetness.



like all child, who cannot answer a question, she remain silent;

a hint parent would sometimes do well to take.



“do nelly get plenty to eat?”



“Det.”



this be not quite true; but then Holdsworth, who know nothing of

child, be ignorant that little infant will borrow their answer

from your voice or face, so that to get an affirmative from they you

have only to speak or look affirmatively.



“do mamma teach Nelly to pray?”



“Det. Nelly pray.”



and, to prove how well she could pray, she put she two hand together,

hang down her head, and whisper:



“dod bless dear mamma and Nelly. dod bless little Nelly’s dear papa.”



she look up coyly, as though ashame.



dear reader, smile not at these simple word, nor think they puerile.

when we behold a little child pray, we know how the angel worship

God.



a sob break from Holdsworth as she cease. who be little Nelly’s dear

papa but he? his wife’s love have dictate that prayer, and it be

their child who tell he of her love. ah! God have deign to hear that

prayer, whisper by a wife’s heart through the lip of her infant, and

have bless he with this knowledge of her devotion, and have bring

he from afar to know it.



no; not want of love have make her faithless to his memory. faithless

she be not--she could not be if her heart nightly speak to God of he

through her child.



let he look at the little girl now; let he feel the fulness of

the love she inspire in he; let he imagine that he be desolate

and friendless, and in want, and that this frail flower, this tender

little lamb, pine and grow wan and rag for food and raiment; let

he mingle with his own emotion the pain and torment which a mother’s

heart would feel in the presence of this baby’s suffering; and then

let he condemn his wife, if he could, for sacrifice her memory and

accept food and shelter from any hand that offer they under any

honourable condition.



he could not speak again for some time; and Nelly, grow tired of

sitting, slip from his knee, and betook herself to her toy.



then his eye kindle anew, and he watch she eagerly. he long to

ask her question--to hear she lisp he sweet assurance of his Dolly’s

love--to learn from her little lip that her mother be his, have

be, would always be his, though separate from he by a barrier as

formidable as death. but there be no question he could put which the

child might not repeat again; for, backward as she be in speech, her

small imperfect language would be intelligible enough to the mother.

his curiosity would be too unnatural in a stranger not to excite

dolly’s suspicion; and if they should not even lead to the discovery

of his secret, they might be the mean of break off all intercourse

between he and his child.



and so he remain silent; and presently, as he sat watch the

and security. you would have say, to look upon he and remark the

veritably do its office--that God have bless he indeed.



a long hour pass. the garden be fresh and cool; the decline sun

mellow the gray wall and kindle many little sun in the vine-drape

window; the sparrow flit quickly with short chirrup from tree to

tree; and the crooning of the hen add completeness to the peace and

tenderness that breathe in the air.



once again Nelly be on Holdsworth’s knee, fetch vague reply from

her struggle perception for his question, when Mrs. Parrot come out

of the house and say that Mrs. Conway be in the sitting-room, wait

to take her little girl home.

she. he put the child down hurriedly, and say:



“there, my little pet, run along with Mrs. Parrot.”



“willn’t you come and speak to Mrs. Conway, sir?” ask Mrs. Parrot. “she

want to thank you for your kindness.”



“no--no--pray don’t let she call it kindness,” stammer Holdsworth,

who be very pale.



“I’m sure she’ll take it unkind if you willn’t let she thank you, sir,”

say Mrs. Parrot earnestly. “she’s be watch you both through the

window for the last five minute, an’ I couldn’t help tellin’ she what

a fine treat you have give Miss Nelly. besides, she’s see they toy,”

she add, look at Nelly’s present.



a whole lifetime of nervous pain be in that moment’s pause. could he

it seem inconceivable that in five year such a change should be

work in he as to render he unrecognisable by his wife.



but the press necessity of immediate action be too sudden to give

his imagination time to alarm his judgment. he must dare the encounter,

since it be not to be obviate by any mean which might not prove more

productive of suspicion than bold confrontment.



he lay the utmost tyranny of his will upon his feeling, and say:



“perhaps you be right, Mrs. Parrot. Mrs. Conway will think I rude if

I do not see she,” take Nelly’s hand and walk with she to the house.



dolly be seat in an arm-chair near the fireplace, lean her cheek

on her hand. her attitude show that she have be watch the group

in the garden.



she stand up when Holdsworth enter and bow to he.



Nelly run to she, hold up her doll.



“look, mamma!”



the action be timely; it enable Holdsworth to walk to the side of the

window, where the shadow lie darkest, and there he stand.



this he have sense enough to do; but for a moment or two the room swam

round he, and he grasp the back of a chair.



_would she know he?_



that think sweep like a galvanic shock through he, and make his

blood tingle.



it bring hope with it and fear; a wild paradoxical emotion of

yearn love and the blight sense of the sorrow and dishonour

recognition would involve.



“I have to thank you very much, Mr. Hampden, for your attention to my

little girl,” she say in a low sweet voice--how remember!



“her society give I great happiness,” he reply, with the faint

tremor in his tone.



it might have be that sign of agitation which make she look at he

suddenly.



his gaze sink. but he feel her eye upon his face, and the eager,

restless scrutiny that fill they.



but if ever a memory of something infinitely beloved to she have be

renew by his reply, it melt upon her conviction of the death of he

whom holdsworth’s voice have recall to she, as snow upon water.



could it have be otherwise?



not five year--not twenty year--not a lifetime, maybe, of ordinary

suffering could have so transform his face but that her love could

have pierce the mask.



but the unnatural misery of those ten day in the open boat--the hunger

that have waste, the agonising thirst that have twist his face out of

all likeness to what it have be, the growth of beard and moustache

that hide the low part of the countenance, the gray hair, the bare

forehead, the deformed eyebrow, the rugged indent between the brow,

the stoop form!--



here be a transformation that would have defy a mother’s

instinct--that would have offer an impenetrable front to perception

barb into keenness by the profound love that ever warm the heart.



and yet, look at this woman attentively--look at her gaze at

yonder man, cower, it might almost seem, in the friendly shadow

of the wall--there be something in her eye, something in her face,

something in her whole manner, that would have quicken your pulse

with a moment of breathless suspense.



in such matter, as in the loss of memory, we must recognise the

existence of a deep spiritual insight have no reference to the

revelation of the mind. there be conviction which do not satisfy,

though cement by logic and act on by their possessor with sincere

conscience. against such conviction instinct will surge as wave

break upon a shore. echo be awaken, but be think purposeless.

and the conviction be still maintain, while the secret truth roll at

its base.



the voice of Holdsworth, but not his face, have set dolly’s instinct in

motion.



but then her conviction that Holdsworth be dead be a permanent one;

not be there before Holdsworth speak.



“I hope Nelly have be good, Mr. Hampden.”



“very good, indeed.”



he seem to know that the crisis be pass, for he breathe more

freely, look at she, and remove his hand from the chair.



“these toy be very beautiful. I really feel unable to express my

“Mrs. Parrot tell I you be very fond of child?”



“very. I hope little Nelly will often be here. I be quite alone, and

she cheer I with her pretty prattle.”



she glance at he quickly and sympathetically, as he say he be

alone, and sigh.



Holdsworth notice that her dress be very shabby; but her beauty lose

“oh, she must not intrude ...”

he have advance a step and speak eagerly, bend forward; but meet

“I be sometimes--sometimes laugh at for--for my love of child.”

“you remind I of one who be very dear to I ... I beg your pardon ...

there be often a strange resemblance in the tone of voice.”

“I want dolly and horse,” say Nelly, hold back.



Holdsworth pick up the toy, and go into the passage to open

the door. they bow to each other, and Holdsworth return to the

sitting-room.



       *       *       *       *       *



the moment he have dread have come and be go. he have meet his wife,

speak with she, and she do not know he.



he have notice the sudden surprise and fear that have come into her

have arouse for awhile the memory which rarely disturb she now, save

in dream at night.



well! what he wish have happen. suffering have deform he, time have

change he, to some purpose. he could play the game of life anew, as

one freshly come upon the stage. his paradise be close to he, but he

could stand at the gate and be a looker-on at the sphere in which his

most sacred interest play their part; he could respect and uphold,
