death have cancel; he could have his child for a playmate, and sow in

her heart those seed of love, which, if God should ever suffer she

to know her father, would, in the fulness of time, bless he with an

abundant harvest of happiness.



but, though he would not, for the worth of his life, have have thing

otherwise than they be, yet, as he stand in the room from which the

light have depart with his child’s sweet face, the tear rise to his

eye and sob convulse he.



oh, it be hard to look back upon his suffering, and feel that nothing

but suffering yet remain; hard and bitter to behold those whom he

love, those whose love would spring to meet he be he to make but

one little sign, and to know that he be as dead to they as if the

great desolate sea roll over his body.



but here be a noble self-sacrifice heart that could not long mourn

its own affliction. high virtue be always pregnant with high

consolation, and a good man’s grief for himself be short because he

carry many tender minister to it in his bosom. there be no triumph

now to complete, for his conquest over impulse have be achieve at

Southbourne. he draw to the open window at the back, where the air be

fragrant with the smell of hay from the meadow beyond, and the cool

evening perfume of flower hide amid the shrubbery in the garden,

and watch the sun sink, whilst his thought follow it to the

distant deep, whose breast it overhang, and on whose lonely surface he

have watch it rise and set with a despair the memory of which

fill he with thought too deep for tear.









CHAPTER XXVIII.



DOLLY’S thoughts.





three day pass before Holdsworth see Nelly again. he then, from his

window, beheld her play on the pavement opposite with the horse and

cart he have give she.



he call, and she come run over to he gleefully at once.



Mrs. Parrot be despatch to request Mrs. Conway’s leave that nelly

might stop to tea with Mr. Hampden, and return to say that “the

little gal might, with the great of pleasure.”



again and again Nelly be summon out of the road by Holdsworth,

sometimes of a morning, sometimes of an afternoon, when he could

see she. the little creature soon learn to resist all her mother’s

suggestion that she should play in the back garden; she like the

pavement in the road, especially the pavement opposite Holdsworth’s

look-out for Holdsworth, while she feign to be absorb in her toy.

ah, the artfulness of some little girl! but then there be always

gingerbread and cake for she in the miraculous cupboard in the corner

of Holdsworth’s room; and the temptation to obtain these luxury,

and to evade the slice of bread and cup of thin milk and water which

form her evening meal at home, be sometimes powerful enough to send

the road, actually unobserve by mamma, who, imagine that she still

play in the garden, would be astonish by Mrs. Parrot come across

and say that Miss Nelly be with Mr. Hampden, and please might she

stop to tea.



often, if Holdsworth have the good fortune to see his little girl in

the morning, or early in the afternoon, he would put on his hat, and

he have take nelly for a walk, clasp the child’s hand and stroll with

she into the town.



Nelly enjoy these ramble hugely. their two figure contrast

strangely, and many a woman’s eye would follow they, because the

measure step, the thoughtful brow, the sunken face of the man, and the

golden-haired child at his side, with her bright young face and big

twinkle foot, trip so fleetly and dancingly along, that one would

say she hold his hand to prevent herself fly away, form a picture

which a woman’s heart would love to contemplate for its prettiness.



they would sometimes turn out of the hot street, when Nelly’s listless

glance would show she weary at last of the splendour of the toy-shop

(before which they regularly stop) and wander to the river’s side;

and there, in the shadow of tree, Holdsworth would rest himself, while

nelly clear the space around she of all the daisy and buttercup

she could find.



these be hour of deep and calm enjoyment to Holdsworth, who, until

the chime of the town clock warn he to rise, would lie, with his

head support on his elbow, that his face might be close to Nelly’s

that he might catch every fluctuating expression that make her eye

an endless series of sweet sign, that he might hear every faltering

syllable that fall from her lip.



Soft and cool be the sound the river make, as its gentle tide

gurgle a secret music among the high rush, or ripple round stump

of tree or projection of stone lodge in the bank. wing insect

flash many-colour light upon the eye as they sweep from shadow to

shadow, part by a rivulet of sunshine fall through the opening

in the tree. now and again a trout leap with a pleasant and lazy

splash. from the shore opposite, behind the tree, come the smell of

the warm red clover, mingle with the multitudinous hum of bee. afar,

at a bend of the stream, an angler might stand watch his quill,

with his head and shoulder mirror in the clear water--so exquisite

the counterfeit that one might easily make a parable out of it, and

sermonise slumberously, as befit the drowsy influence of the hot

day, on those illusion of life which mock the heart they mislead in

its search after truth.



once, when Holdsworth be take Nelly home, after a long rest on the

river’s edge, he meet Mr. Conway, who stare very hard, but pass on

without address the child. Nelly draw close to Holdsworth when she

see the man.



Holdsworth know Conway perfectly well by sight now. the dentist have

repeatedly pass the window at which Holdsworth station himself

he would glance with no unfriendly expression towards Mrs. Parrot’s

old-fashioned house.



his walk, when he do not actually reel, as he very often do, might

have be study with some disgust as an illustration of character.

it be a species of gliding movement, such as a man might be suppose

to adopt, whose self-abasement he himself hold irrevocable, and who

have make up his mind no long to walk, but to sneak through life.

the influence of importunate creditor might be mark in the quick,

furtive glancing of the eye, that wander from side to side and

challenge every individual it rest upon. one half-dull perception

it be very natural that Mrs. Parrot’s lodger should be somewhat of

a mystery to he. have no liking for child himself, but, on the

who _be_ he? as Mrs. Parrot make a point of avoid he, he could not

it be something to find out that he have lot of money.

she account for her feeling by refer they to the recollection

which have be abruptly renew--drag, so to say, from the grave in

the thought never occur to she: how _could_ it? she believe he

but even if she have doubt his death; if ever she have cherish the

nor, strange as his suddenly-acquire affection for Nelly might seem to

other, could it come to she as a surprise. the mother’s vanity would

once, when Mrs. Parrot, meet she in the road, say that “it do seem

strange that a man an’ a child, as have niver set eye on each other

do:” dolly answer, “yes; but though I be her mother, yet I must say

that Nelly be a pretty and very win child, and there be nothing

uncommon in stranger take a fancy to child.”



No; that be quite true, Mrs. Parrot answer; and tell a story of a

rich lady admire a little beggar girl in the street; and how the rich

lady take the wench into her carrich, and get the parient’ leaf to

adopt she; and how the beggar girl come into the rich lady’s fortin,

and grow up into a stately an’ ’aughty woman, an’ married a lord, she

do, as be beknown to many.



“it ’ud be a comfortin’ thing to you, ma’am, don’t you think, if Mr.

Hampden be to adopt your little gal. it ’ud be a relief to your

feeling, wouldn’t it?” Mrs. Parrot say.



if some half-form thought, bear a resemblance to Mrs. Parrot’s

view, have flit across dolly’s mind, let we not marvel. never be her

mood sad, never be her secret grief sharper, than when her child’s

future form the subject of her thought. who would give Nelly a home

if she die? who would love the little thing; rear she in the knowledge

of God, of her break-hearted mother, of her poor drown father?



“I could not part with she, Mrs. Parrot; she be the only link that

bind I to bygone happy day. I could not spare she. my life would be

_too_ lonely for I to support it. but I often pray to God that she

may find a friend--such a friend as I be sure Mr. Hampden would make

she--when I be dead.”



this hope--that Mr. Hampden _would_ prove that friend--be the real

source of the comfort that fill her heart each time she see she

little girl trip across to Mrs. Parrot’s house.



she seldom see Holdsworth. sometimes she think he avoid she. twice,

when she be leave or return to her house, she see he in the

porch, and each time he hastily withdraw, when she would have cross

over to speak to he. on rare occasion she meet he come from the

powerfully. she stop and look after he; and go on her way,

brooding, with a little frown of anxious, painful thought.



on his part, the weight of his secret, when they thus meet, face

to face, be insupportable. the wild rush of impulse combat by

inflexible resolution, create a conflict in his breast beyond his

capacity of endurance. he could not have prolong a conversation with

he know now her hour of go and come, and would station himself

behind the curtain, and follow she with exquisite tenderness in his

eye, and sadness, cruell than word can tell, in his heart.



how be this all to end?



here be the thought that now torment he. six week have pass since

he come to Hanwitch. he be live frugally, indeed; and of the money

he have bring with he from Australia a large portion still remain.

but his few hundred pound make a very slender capital; and when they

be spend what then?



he know very well that he could return to Sydney, that Mr. Sherman

would welcome he back, and reinstate he in his old post. but the

mere thought of leave England be misery to he. suppose, under any

plea, he obtain dolly’s leave to take Nelly with he, could he part

from dolly? he might never see she again. then let he think of her

companion; of the sordid, hungry life he _know_ she be lead--know,

though he could devise no expedient for relieve she, that might not

be resent as an affront and lose he nelly’s companionship.



he would rather have she in her grave than leave she as she be.



if urgent distress should ever come upon she, he would be at hand to

succour and support she. and that such urgent distress _must_ come

soon or later--that the day soon or later _must_ arrive when

she and her child would be without a home, he have but to watch the

maunder man who pass his window backwards and forwards day after

day, aimless, sodden, and grow shabby and shabby every week in

his appearance, to know.









CHAPTER XXIX.



a visit.





how be Holdsworth to get a living? for what be he fit? he be a

good clerk; Mr. Sherman have call he so, at least; Hanwitch be a

tolerably large place, and he ought to find no difficulty in obtain

employment. at any rate, he must try.



one morning he put on his hat and walk into the town.



when he reach the High Street, he stop and consider.



there be a bank; he could apply there. then there be a brewery. if

these fail, there remain an insurance office.



these represent polite avocation.



there be shop in abundance, where man, well look than he,

smile over counter, and carry parcel, and stand bare-headed on

the pavement at carriage-door. but Holdsworth be still too much the

sailor at heart to tolerate the notion of shop-serve. he would start

a little school rather than do that. and indeed school-keeping seem

more feasible than anything else. Mrs. Parrot’s lodging would serve

he there; boy would assemble by degree; and he could set and hear

lesson, and teach writing and mathematic, as well as any university

man.



meanwhile, let he try the bank.



it face the market-place in the High Street, have a well-wear door-step

and stout, noisy swinge door. holdsworth enter, and find himself

in a badly-light office, with a counter across it, behind which be

three or four clerk. a man who look a fourth-rate farmer be pay

money in, and whilst he count a great accumulation of greasy silver,

which he have discharge in company with a number of soil, infragrant

cheque out of a leather bag, he pause at every twenty to submit

an observation of a rural nature to the intelligence of an elderly

personage with long whisker, and a somewhat hebraical cast of visage,

behind the counter.



the manager, for so the long-whisker man be, observe Holdsworth to

be a stranger, politely ask he his business.



“can I speak to the manager?”



“certainly, sir; _i_ be the manager. walk this way, please.”



say which, the manager bustle importantly into a back room, and

throw open a side door for Holdsworth to enter.



“pray be seat, sir. nice weather.”



and the manager draw a chair to a desk, clasp his hand on a volume

of interest-table, and fix his eye on Holdsworth.



“I have call to inquire if you be in want of a clerk,” say

Holdsworth.



“I beg your pardon?” exclaim the manager.



Holdsworth repeat his remark, add that he be in want of a

situation, and would be glad to fill any vacancy there might be in the

staff of the bank clerk.



the manager, who have expect something very different from this, get

up instantly; his business-smile vanish, he thrust his hand into his

breech pocket, and exclaim:



“Clerk, sir! who tell you we want a clerk?”



“nobody. I have call here at my own suggestion.”



“God bless my heart! you be quite out of order, sir! really, these

intrusion upon my time .... you should have explain your wish at the

counter. when we want a clerk, we know where to find one, back, sir,

with first-class security and influential recommendation.”



“then I have make a mistake, that’s all,” say Holdsworth, survey

the manager with great disgust; and pay no further heed to the

protest with which the other follow he to the door, he walk into

the High Street.



this summary treatment be enough to last he one day. his indignation

yield to depression, and he return slowly and moodily to his

lodging.



this be the first time in his life he have ever make an application

for employment; and his reception, which be really genteel and

civil compare to the reception experience by man, old and young,

every day, in search of work, at the hand of employer, wound his

sensibility and fill he with a sense of degradation.



he regain his lodging, and endeavour to console himself with

philosophy.



but philosophy, say Rochefoucauld, triumph over future and past ill;

but present ill triumph over philosophy.



his sensibility do not smart the less because he reflect that

hundred of well man than himself have be insult by rejection as

offensive as that with which his inquiry have be encounter.



thought of something tender and innocent will often quell the

stubborn warmth. holdsworth grow mild in a moment when his mind go

to little Nelly.



“I’ll try the brewery to-morrow,” he say to himself; “and if that

fail I, I’ll advertise for a situation; and if nothing come of that,

I’ll start a school.”



thus think he walk to the window, hope to see his child in the

road.



nobody be visible but the old politician with the inflame face, who

be pace slowly along the pavement, his hand lock behind he,

his eye bent downwards, and his brow frown grimly. presently,

holdsworth know, the other old politician, who live at the corner

house, would come out, and there would be much gesticulation, and

violent declamation, and frequent pause, and mopping of the forehead

with red silk pocket-handkerchief. rain have fall in the night, and

cleanse the little garden in front of the villa of the three week’

leave and grass. in the bit of ground before Mrs. Parrot’s house

the flower have wither on their stalk, but the shrub still wear

the bright greenness of summer; the soil be dark and rich with the

grateful moisture, and breathe a fragrance of its own upon the morning

air.



Holdsworth be about to quit the window when he catch sight of Mr.

Conway come out of his gate. he fall a step back, and watch the man

from behind the curtain. Mr. Conway advance a few yard along his own

side of the road, and then cross, with his eye fix on Holdsworth’s

window.



be he come to the house? he move softly and furtively, and when he

be abreast of Mrs. Parrot’s gate, throw a glance behind he, push

the gate open, and knock.



as Holdsworth do not know the man to speak to, he do not for a moment
