the passage, and would like to see he.



the first idea that rush into Holdsworth’s head be, “I be know!”

but conjecture be out of the question, for the man be wait.

“pray show Mr. Conway in,” he say; and in Mr. Conway come.



Holdsworth bow, and so do the other, with a kind of spasmodic

grace--a good bow spoil by nervousness. he have dress himself with

care; he be cleanly shave; his hair be carefully brush; his shirt

collar be white; and his boot shine.



Holdsworth have never before see he so close. the light from the

window fall upon his face and show the cobweb of vein in his eye,

the puffy whiteness of his skin, the blueness of his lip, the tinge of

gleam purple about his nostril, and all the other signal which the

alcoholic fiend stamp upon the countenance of his votary, so that,

let they go where they will, they may be know and loathe by honest

man as his adopt child.



but he be sober now; as sober as a man can be who have drink but a

glass of ale since he leave his bed, but whose flesh be soak with the

abomination of the tavern, and whose brain can never be steady for the

fume that rise incessantly into it.



“Mr. Hampden, I believe?” he exclaim in a creamy voice, stand

near the door, which Mrs. Parrot have shut behind he, and twist his

hat in his hand.



“yes; pray be seat,” reply Holdsworth, look at he steadily,

certain now that the object of this visit be not what he have imagine

it.



Mr. Conway sit down, and put his hat on the floor. his embarrassment,

when his business should come to be know, might show a possibility of

redemption, or at least satisfy we that most of the bad quality he

be accredit with might have be absorb into his nature with the

drink he swallow. no thoroughly bad man could feel the nervousness

that disturb he.



“I have call, Mr. Hampden, to thank you, for your kindness to my

little step-daughter. indeed, sir, both my wife and myself thoroughly

appreciate your goodness. believe we, we do.”



“pray do not trouble to thank I. she be a sweet child, and it make I

happy to have she,” answer Holdsworth, now at his ease, and study

his visitor with curiosity and surprise.



“ah! she be indeed a sweet child. a perfect treasure to her mother,

and quite a little sunbeam in my house--darken, I regret to say, by

misfortune beyond my control to repair.”



“I be sorry to hear that.”



“I never can sufficiently deplore having adopt so ungrateful a

vocation as dentistry. I be bear to well thing, Mr. Hampden. my

father have an influential position under Government; but he die in

poverty, and I be apprentice by an uncle ... pray forgive I. these

matter cannot interest you. privation press heavily upon a man at my

time of life. dentistry seem to fail I; and yet, when I look around,

I find no other calling which I be qualified to espouse.”



he sigh, and pull out a pocket-handkerchief, with which he wipe

his mouth.



Holdsworth be silent.



“poverty I could endure, be I alone in the world,” continue Mr.

Conway; “but it be unendurable to I to witness the good of woman

and the dearest of little child in want. my poor wife do not

complain; but I witness her secret suffering in her waste form and

irrepressible tear, and it go to my heart, sir, to see she, and feel

my miserable incapacity to relieve she.”



“do you mean to say that she be actually in want?” exclaim Holdsworth

in a low voice.



“yes, sir; we all be. as I hope to be save, I haven’t more than two

shilling in the wide world!”



“have you no source of income outside your profession?”



“no. I do well in the High Street; but I have many rival and enemy,

who spread lie report about I, and lose I my good patient. give a

dog a bad name! I leave my establishment in the heart of the town, and

come into this road because rent be cheap here; and God know if I can

tell how I have live since,” he cry passionately, his natural bad

temper break through his affectation of suffering and ill-treatment.

“the pawnbroker have be my only friend! be I to sell the bed from

under I? oh, sir, I think of my wife, of my poor little child--for my

child she be, if love can make she so--and the thought be death to I!”



he flourish his handkerchief and look piteously at Holdsworth.



“how can I serve you?”



“ah, sir!” exclaim Mr. Conway, sink his voice into a yet more

whining note, the while a gleam enter into his eye; “what right

have I to trespass upon the benevolence of a stranger? of a gentleman

who have already place I under a thousand obligation by his kindness

to my little daughter? I feel myself a wretch, sir, when I reflect

upon the unfortunate position I have place my poor wife in. I be

flourish in those day; I could have give--I _do_ give she and

her baby a good home. but what position be so secure that it can stand

against the lie of rivalry and jealousy? the slanderous report of

ruffian who make capital for themselves out of a neighbour’s trifling

error, and--and--oh! damn they!”



“how can I serve you?” say Holdsworth, come quietly back to the

point.



“if I dare name my want to you, sir--if I dare presume upon that

benevolence which you have so signally illustrate in your behaviour to

little nelly, I--I----”



“I be a poor man,” say Holdsworth, as the other pause; “and can

afford but little. but that little be cheerfully at the service of your

wife and child, who must not be allow to want.”



he speak emphatically, to let the man understand the purpose to which

he intend his gift or loan should be apply.



“but for that wife and child, sir,” answer Mr. Conway, apparently

struggle with his emotion, “_could_ I place myself in this position?

be there any personal necessity, however imperative, that would force

I to lose sight of the pride which render starvation preferable to

alm-seeking, to the gentleman bear? no, sir,” he continue, with an

air of injure dignity; “poor as I be, I can still recognise the claim

of my birth upon my action; and I repeat, that be it not for my wife

and her little one, no affliction, however unsupportable, should oblige

I to intrude even upon _your_ benevolence.”



he pause, and see Holdsworth look impatient, exclaim hurriedly:



“if ten pounds----” and stop.



“you wish to borrow ten pound?”



“ah, sir, if I dare----”



“of what service will so small a sum be to you?”



the man look strike; Holdsworth have expect to hear a large sum

name, he think.



“ten pound--to a poor man--to a poor family, sir, ten pound be a

great deal of money.”



“I will lend you ten pound willingly, on condition that you spend it

on your wife and Nelly.”



“certainly, certainly,” reply Mr. Conway meekly. “you may depend upon

be repay, if I have to pawn the shirt off my back to get the money.”



I suppose that this kind of security (generally offer by man who have

not the least idea of repay a loan) must be figurative--a poetical

figure of debt. how far would the shirt off a man’s back help the

redemption of the debt borrow on the strength of it?



Holdsworth give Mr. Conway two five-pound note. the man take they

eagerly, and whilst he bury they in his trouser’ pocket, pour

forth a profusion of thank.



“do Mrs. Conway know of this visit?” ask Holdsworth, stop his

noise.



“no, sir; but believe I, I shall not fail to acquaint she with your

kindness,” he answer, take his hat and rise.



Holdsworth’s impulse be to request he not to speak to she of this

gift--for loan it would be ridiculous to call it. but he check

himself with the consideration that, be Mr. Conway to break his word,

dolly would find food for dangerous questioning in the request.



he say, instead, “you will not forget the purpose for which I have

lend you this money?”



“trust I, sir; trust I,” murmur Mr. Conway, press his hat to his

heart. “if you will give I ink and paper I will make you out an i o u

at once.”



“never mind that. nelly be a grow child, and require nourishing

food: devote the money to she and her mother, and you will make I

grateful.”



he walk into the passage, and Mr. Conway, bow humbly, pass into

the porch, where he stand a moment or two peep at his house; then,

with another bow, hurry into the road, and vanish in the direction

of the town.



       *       *       *       *       *



the poverty of the Conways, then, be unquestionable. Holdsworth have

often speculate upon their position, but have never reach nearer

to the mark than suppose that they live from hand to mouth, and

just make shift to support the day that be pass over they. that

they be actually in want, actually destitute indeed, it have never

enter his mind to imagine. he believe Conway’s story. and it be

very certain that, if the man have no private mean of his own, he must

be hopelessly poor, for he make nothing by his profession. in all the

six week that Holdsworth have be in Hanwitch he have not see as many

people call at Conway’s house; and of these, suppose they to be

patient, half of they have come away after speak with the servant,

doubtless inform that master be out.



but even guess so much, Holdsworth guess only half the truth:

and it be well, perhaps, that he do not know all, for grief must

have master his judgment, and force he into the confession which

he pray, night and morning, for will to restrain. it be after dark

always when dolly, closely veil, would creep down the road, with

some little bundle under her shawl, for the pawnbroker, that she might

obtain a trifle in order to furnish her child with a meal on the

morrow. it be in the privacy of her own home that she labour, as

no menial ever _will_ labour; sit up late night after night, over

the endless task of darn and mend her own and her child’s shabby

apparel; often go supperless to bed, and wake to a day even more

hopeless than the one that have precede it.



the devoted man, who would have give his life to win her happiness,

know nothing of all this. even his little child’s dress tell he no

story, though a woman might have read a full and pathetic narrative

of toil and poverty in the frock, turn and re-turn, mend and

patch, and darn again and again.



Holdsworth seldom see she now: yet, if ever she catch sight of he

at his window, she have always a kindly smile, a grateful nod: and

what with the shadow of her hat over her face, and the distance which

soften the line of care, grief, and weariness into the sweet and

delicate effect of her beauty, he be ignorant of the serious and

wither change that have take place in she, even during the short

time that have elapse since they have last meet and speak in the High

Street.



Nelly come over to he at one o’clock, and he keep she to dinner. the

child be hungry, and as he watch she eating, he think of Dolly.



“have mamma get a good dinner to-day, darling?”



the little thing look puzzle; but upon Holdsworth repeat the

question, answer “Noo.”



he think she be mistaken, since, after what Conway have tell he, the

man’s first action, he believe, now that he have money in his pocket,

would be to attend to his wife’s necessity. but though he repeat

his question in different shape, the child invariably answer, “Noo,

mamma get no din-din.”



“no dinner at all! be you sure, my pet?”



yes, the child be sure, as sure as a child could be.



Holdsworth spring up and rang the bell, and enter the passage

to await Mrs. Parrot. she come out of her kitchen, and Holdsworth

exclaim, master his agitation:



“I want to confer with you, Mrs. Parrot. Nelly tell I that her mamma

have no dinner to-day. be this likely--be this possible, do you think?”



“indeed, sir, since you ask I, I do then, and God forgi’ I for

thinkin’ the bad,” answer Mrs. Parrot.



“but,” cry Holdsworth, “I give Mr. Conway ten pound this morning,

stipulate that he should spend it on his wife and child!”



“he!” exclaim Mrs. Parrot, almost savagely. “the wretch! ten pound!

he’ll spend it all I’ liquor! oh, sir, why don’t you give it to the

poor lady?”



“yes--I ought to have do so,” reply Holdsworth clasp his hand.

“but how could I--what excuse _could_ I have find for send it to

she? oh, Mrs. Parrot! something must be do. I cann’t bear to think of

the poor lady actually dinnerless. what can we contrive? remember--she

be a lady--we must be careful.”



“to think of your lending ten pound to that villin!” cry Mrs.

Parrot, whose mind be stagger by the munificence of the sum and the

artfulness of the man in obtain it. “I niver heerd of such a thing!

and be that his reason for callin’? if I’d ha’ only know his object,

I’d ha’ send he packin’ with his blarney, wouldn’t I?”



“what do you advise?” say Holdsworth, eagerly.



“well, sir, I’m sure I don’t know what to say. she _be_ a lady, and it

wouldn’t do to send her butcher’ meat across, would it? I’ll tell you

what we _could_ do, sir; I could kill one o’ my fowl and leave it with

my compliment, pretend I have kill some yesterday, and wish she,

as a neighbour, to taste my fattening.”



“that will do! but, instead of kill your fowl, take this

half-sovereign and run at once to the poulterer’s, and buy a couple of

pullet. you can then take they across, and she will suppose they be

your own rearing. will you do this?”



“with the great of pleasure, sir; and I’m sure you must have a very

kind heart to take so much interest in poor folk.”



and Mrs. Parrot run off for her bonnet, and be presently hurry down

the road with a market-basket on her arm, and her untied bonnet-string

stream over her shoulder.



Holdsworth wait impatiently for her return, whilst Nelly, who have

finished dinner, toddle about the room, gaze with round earnest

eye into the recess, and the cupboard, and at the shepherd on the

mantelpiece, and the yellow rose on the mat.



in ten minute’ time Mrs. Parrot come back with her face flush with

the heat and exercise, and dart into the house as though she have

sweep half a jeweller’s shop into her basket and be fly for dear

life.



“there, sir, what do you think of these?” she exclaim, drag a

pair of handsomely-flour pullet out of the basket and hold they

at arm’s length, as though they be a pair of ear-ring. “ben’t they

beautie, sir?”



“how can I send they across? will you take they?”



“oh yes. I can jest leave ’em at the door wi’ Mrs. Parrot’s

compliment. she’ll be sure to guess that they’re my rearin’, and save

I from an untruth, though my religion be none so fine, thank God,

that I should be afeard to tell a kind o’ white lie to help any poor

creature as want.”



she then examine the pullet attentively, to make sure that there be

no trade-mark upon they in the shape of ticket, adjust her bonnet,

wipe her face, and walk across the road.



Holdsworth wait in the passage until she return. she be absent a

few minute, and then come back smile, with the lid of the basket

raise to let Holdsworth see that it be empty.



“do you see Mrs. Conway?”



“no, sir, I wouldn’t ask for she,” reply Mrs. Parrot, wipe she

foot on the door-mat. “I jest say to the gal, ‘give this here to your

missis with my compliment, and tell she that they’re ready for cookin’

at once, as they’re be kill long enough.’ I niver see any gal look

like that wench do when she take the pullet. I think she’d ha’

faint. she turn as pale as pale, and then she grin slow-like,

and then laugh wi’ a sound for all the world like the squeak of a dog

that’s smotherin’ under a cushion. here’s your change, sir. pullet,

six shillin’, and one be seven, and two be nine, and two sixpence

make it right. will you please to count it?”



Holdsworth thank she, and return to the sitting-room with a

relieved mind. but scarcely be he seat when Mrs. Parrot knock on

the door, and mysteriously beckon he into the passage.



“I forgot to say, sir, that I ast the gal before come away if she

master be in, and she say ‘no.’ I say, ‘when will he be in?’ she

say, ‘I don’t know, missis; he go out this mornin’, an’ he’s not

be back since.’ Mark what I say, sir!” add Mrs. Parrot, raise an

emphatic forefinger, “he’ll not give a penny o’ that money to his poor

wife, but jest keep away from she till he’s drunk it all out.”



accompany which prophecy with many indignant nod, she walk

defiantly towards the kitchen.



the idea of dolly’s miserable position, never before impress upon he

as it have be that day, make Holdsworth wretche. he seat himself

at the window and stare gloomily and sadly into the road. Nelly come

to he and try to coax he to play with she, but he have no heart

even to meet the little creature’s sweet entreating eye with a smile.

he catch she up, press she to he, and kiss she again and again,

while the hot tear roll down his thin face.



never before be his impulse to tell dolly who he be and snatch she

from the misery, the unmeet sorrow that encompass she, so powerful.

love and pity strove with the dread of dishonour she by the

revelation. could he endure to think that this delicate, gentle girl

be link to a man who neglect she, who might even ill-treat she,

who at that moment might be squander the money that have be give

he on his own gross appetite, without thought of the wife and child

want bread at home? what must be the issue of such a life if it be

permit to endure? soon or later Holdsworth must avow himself to

save she and his child from that uttermost degree of ruin and misery to

which Conway be drag they.



he have hope to devote his life to they. his dream have be that

Conway’s character be not irretrievably bad, that kindly entreaty,

cordial advice, and pecuniary help might bring he to a knowledge of

his folly and set he once more on the high-road to respectability.

such a redemption would have be Holdsworth’s sacrifice; but his own

happiness be as nothing in his eye compare to dolly’s. faithfully

would he have perform his duty to she, nobly would he have vindicate

his own most honourable, most exalted devotion, could he have reclaim

this err man and teach he to give his wife as much happiness as

it be possible for a heart that ceaselessly mourn a dead love, to

know. thus he could have be his dolly’s good angel, and whilst God

permit he, have keep watch over she and her child, dead to her

belief, but active as the holy love could make life in his helpful

secret guardianship.



he perceive the vanity of that hope now, and yet despairingly clung

to it, because, if he surrender it, he feel that he must confess

himself, and from this he shrink as from a deed that would inflict a

deep degradation upon she, while Conway live, than any she could

suffer from her husband’s behaviour.



one must either entirely sympathise with his profound susceptibility

of the obligation his suppose death have force upon he to fulfil, or

ridicule he as a man absurdly fantastical in his view of morality.

there seem no middle standpoint to judge he from.



but unless there be too much austerity in his virtue to make it

admirable, then, to properly appreciate it, we must remember the

extraordinary tenderness of his nature, his exquisite sensibility,

which shrink from the mere thought of tarnish the pure honour of the

woman he love.



that he believe her honour would be tarnish be he to proclaim

himself in the lifetime of her present husband, be enough; and

whether he be right or wrong; whether he be correct in hold the

obligation of the marriage-service holy, binding, and to be disturb

only at the risk of God’s wrath, when incur with a spotless

conscience, when enter upon in innocence and good faith; or whether

he should have regard the marriage-service as a mere civil convention

which make his wife his property, claimable by he on the common ground

of the law of priority, without reference to any action she might have

commit in honest belief that he be dead; one thing we must allow

he--an unparalleled quality of unselfishness, the existence of which,

while it attest the sincerity of his view (since he have his heart’s

deep affection to lose and nothing to gain by retain they),

elevate his conduct to the high point of heroism.



Nelly have never before find he unwilling to romp with she; when he

raise his head she watch his face with a strange, wistful look, and

put her finger to his cheek, say:



“why do ’oo cry?”



he force a smile for answer, caress she, and then place she on the

ground, think she be weary of sit. but she climb upon his

knee again, and repeat her question with great earnestness:



“why do ’oo cry?”



“because I be silly and weak, my little one. I be forget that there

be a good and just God over I, who will hear my prayer and help I,

as he before do, when I be alone on the wide sea.”



he say this aloud, but speak rather to himself than to the child.



“dod love Nelly,” say the little thing, “and Nelly love ’oo. nelly

kiss ’oo.”



that be all the comfort she could give he; but it fall tenderly on

his ear. he kiss she gratefully, rock she gently to and fro in his

arm with his eye on her face. she soon, however, rebel against an

attitude which cripple her limb, and slip on to the floor, and to

amuse she he give she a book with picture in it, which she examine

gravely, talk to herself as little child and aged people do.



in this manner the afternoon pass; but never be Holdsworth more

depressed, more restless, fill with more nameless anxiety and

misgiving.



apart from all moral consideration, his future be terribly uncertain.



suppose the conway leave the town? he must follow they, for he could

not bear the separation; and what would they think of his pursuit?

suppose all his effort to obtain a living fail, what should he do?



at five o’clock Mrs. Parrot come in to put on Nelly’s hat: that be

the regular hour at which the little girl be send home by Holdsworth.



“my apron be dirty,” say the worthy woman, “so I’ll not go across with

you, my dear. but I’ll watch from the porch until I see you safe in.”



so, receive a kiss and a piece of gingerbread from Holdsworth, the

child toddle into the road, and when she be inside the gate, where

her mother would see she, Mrs. Parrot close the door and go back to

she iron in the kitchen.









CHAPTER XXX.



the KNOT be CUT.





a storm break over Hanwitch that night, and leave behind it a strong

wind which sweep up great masse of cloud; and the morning sunshine

stream and darken in quick alternation, and make the air lively

with the movement of shadow.



Holdsworth, deeply disturb by conflict anxiety, have sleep but

little; and at eight o’clock leave his bed and start for a walk before

breakfast, hope that the breeze which thunder about the house would

freshen and inspirit he.



gain the High Street, he turn to the left and walk along a

narrow pathway that take he through the field to Maldon Heights, as

the hill that overlook Hanwitch be call. he climb the grassy

slope and stand awhile on the summit, drink in the hoot wind and

watch the fluctuating scene that run from his foot to the horizon.

the oats and barley in many field be not yet cut; and it be a sight

to see they break into wide space of delicate gold under the sun,

and grow gray again as the cloud-shadow sail over they. when the

sunshine linger awhile, these field seem to reflect the shadow

which have pass, for the wind rush like a dark arm along they,

and press the graceful grain into the likeness of a wave, which

sweep forwards with swiftness, make the field dark where it run.

the farther tree appear to hold steady under the breeze; but there

be near tree which sway their branch in wild gesticulation

of entreaty, and flog the wind as it roar among they, bear

away trophy of green leave and broken twig. the bird breast the

gale with short flight, or turn and yield to the invisible power

with small cry. every object the eye rest on appear in motion,

so lively be the effect of the cloud shadow upon the house and the

weight of the wind upon the surround country.



it be a morning to clear the most hypochondriacal mind of despondency,

and Holdsworth feel its cheerful influence as he stand expose to the

swinge rush of warm air, and watch nature dance to the tune sing

by the wind as it sweep through the sky.



he have make up his mind to call at the brewery that morning, and he

take a look at it as he pass the street in which it stand on his

way home. the gaol-like building, with the steam about its window

resemble rich London fog, which refuse either to stop in or go

away, be scarcely calculate to improve his hope. big beef-face man

in apron roll huge cask out of a courtyard into a cellar fill

with sawdust, damp, and gloom; the throb of the engine could be hear

distinctly, and the wind that blow out of the street come in agitated,

disorder puff, as though the smell of the beer have make it rather

drunk.



Holdsworth shake his head as he pass on. it strike he that there

would be little chance of his get employment in that steaming,

panting, perspire quarter; and that he would be act more wisely

if, instead of challenge rudeness by personal inquiry at place

where nobody want he, he spend a few shilling in advertising for a

situation.



determine to do this, he make what haste he could back to his

lodging, mean there and then to manufacture an advertisement.



he enter his sitting-room, rang the bell to let Mrs. Parrot know he

have return, and sit himself down to consider the term in which he

should make his want know.



“what would you like for breakfast, sir?” say Mrs. Parrot, open the

door.



“oh, anything you please. a new-lay egg if you can find I one.”



“yes, sir. I take four beauty out just now. have you hear the news,

sir?”



“no. what news?”



“well, sir, it’s what I allus think must happen; and day after day

I’ve bin expectin’ it, as mother’ll bear I out. they’ve get the

broker in at the conway.”



“the broker!” exclaim Holdsworth, turn round in his chair quickly.



“yes, sir. their gal tell the milkman just now, as giv’ I the news.

and what’s wuss--leastway some might call it wuss, though I should

consider it a good job myself if I be his wife--Mr. Conway haven’t be

home all night!”



“the villain!” exclaim Holdsworth through his tooth. and then he

jump up and begin to pace the room excitedly.



“stop!” he cry, observe that Mrs. Parrot be about to withdraw.

“be you sure this news be true?”



“oh, I’ve no doubt of it, sir. when the milkman tell I, I be jest

goin’ to run across and see the poor lady, and then I say to myself,

‘what use can I be to she?’”



“I may be of some use, though,” interrupt Holdsworth. “never mind

about my breakfast just yet. when do the man enter the house to take

possession?”



“last night, sir, the gal tell the milkman.”



“great heaven! and have she be alone all night?” he stop short,

seize his hat, and, brush past Mrs. Parrot, go quickly out of the

house.



Mrs. Parrot watch he from the porch, lose in amazement.



he push open the gate, march up to the door, and knock loudly.

his mood be one of deep excitement. the sense of the crush misery

that have fall upon dolly have give a poignancy to feel that set

all self-control at defiance.



the door be open by the servant, and out with she come a smell of

strong tobacco smoke.



“be Mr. Conway in?”



“no, sir, he ain’t,” answer the girl, look behind she and then at

Holdsworth, with a scared face.



“where’s your mistress?”



“in the parlour, sir.”



“I should like to see she.”



“she’s not wisible. she’s in grief, and ain’t to be see.”



“go and tell she that Mr. Hampden have call and would like to say a

word to she.”



“I don’t think----”



“do what I tell you!” exclaim Holdsworth.



the girl slouch backwards and push her head into the parlour-door.



“she ain’t here. she’s go upstairs,” say she, and upstairs she go,

slap the staircase with her shoe as she go.



an individual with a round red face, a white hat, a spot shawl, a

coat nearly to his ankle, a long waistcoat, and a black clay pipe

in his mouth, lounge elegantly out of the room which Mr. Conway have

call his “Surgery” at the end of the passage, and lean collectedly

against the door, nod familiarly to Holdsworth, take his pipe from

his mouth, expectorate, and say “Morning.”



“good morning. be you the man in possession?” reply Holdsworth.



the individual nod and replace his pipe.



“when do you come?”



“last night,” answer the man in a thick voice. “and a werry queer

look-out it be. blow if they’ve get any butter in this house!”



“what be the amount of the debt?”



“Twenty-three pun four and sevenpence,” say the man, remove his pipe

to expectorate again. “be you a creditor?”



“no,” answer Holdsworth, listen for dolly’s footstep.



“then if you vent on your bended knee for gratitood you vouldn’t be

overdoin’ it,” say the man, give Holdsworth a sagacious nod. “there

ain’t above ten pound in the house, and not that. cast yer eye into

that parler. the good of the good be there, and if you can make three

pound out of ’em, I’ll swaller my pipe.”



and then an idea smite he:



“you ain’t come to have a tooth draw, have yer?”



“no.”



“Vot’s your opinion of tooth-drawin’?” inquire the man confidentially,

retire and reappear again, hold up a pair of forceps. “ain’t it

rayther a queer go, don’t you think? I know a barber as draw tooth.

he never use nothing of this kind. Vot do you think he do? bust I if

he don’t set you in a chair, fasten a bit o’ vire to the tooth as be

to come out, and tie t’other end of the vire to the leg of a table.

Ven all’s ready, ‘mind yer eye’ he sing out, up with a razor, rush

at yer makin’ horrible mouth, up jump you, avay you run, and leave

your tooth behind yer!”



he gargle an asthmatical laugh, add: “that’s vot I call a sensible

vay of drawin’ a tooth; no bit of cold iron shove into yer mouth as

if yer tongue vas hair and vanted curling.”



“please, sir, will you step into the parlour and sit down,” say the

girl, thrust her head over the banister and call to Holdsworth.

“Missis’ll be with yer in a minute.”



he enter the wretched little parlour, while the “man in possession”

retreat to the surgery arm-chair, and sit severely contemplate some

unfinished tooth on the table in front of he.



in a few moment Holdsworth hear footstep outside, and dolly come in,

hold Nelly’s hand. she be terribly pale, with a look of terror and

exhaustion on her face painful to see. there be an unnatural sleepless

brilliancy in her eye that heighten her worn, hopeless expression.

she have throw an old shawl over her shoulder, and through the portion

of the fair skin of the neck that be expose the vein show dark.

the hand she give to Holdsworth be like a stone.



he be so overcome by the sight of her misery, that for some moment

he could not speak. the child come up to he and rub her cheek

against his hand.



“this be kind, very kind of you, Mr. Hampden,” she exclaim in a low,

faint voice, sink upon the sofa and shivering as she hug the

shawl about her shoulder.



“you be in great distress, I fear. I only hear the news just now. I

come over to you at once,” he answer tremulously, the fierce beating

of his heart sound an echo through his voice.



“it be what I have be daily expect for many month--for many

bitter, cruel month!” she exclaim. “it have come at last. we be

homeless now. and my husband, who ought to be at my side, have leave I.

he be away all day yesterday and last night. o God! what a night it

have be!” she moan, rock herself to and fro.



“don’t say you be homeless,” he cry; “you have a friend. let I be

your friend. Mrs. Parrot shall give you a home for the present ... if

you will accept it.”



she look at he with stupefied eye as one who doubt her sense,

then say: “we have no claim upon you. oh! how noble-hearted! nelly,

nelly, come to I, come to I!”



the child run to her mother, and, be frighten by the passionate

despair in her voice, hide her face in her lap and burst into tear. but

dolly’s eye remain dry--lose nothing of their wild brilliancy. she

drag her child to she, and sway to and fro with tearless sob that

shake and convulse she.



“I have deserve this,” she presently moan. “I be faithless to the

true love God ever bless a woman with. why be he take from I?

my child be starve, and the sight of her waste body drive I mad

with grief. I never love Mr. Conway--he know it ... he have leave I!

oh! he be a coward to leave I! what be I to do? I be a lonely woman--I

have this child to feed and clothe--I have not a relative to turn

to--and now we be homeless! o God! this be too much, too much!”



she hide her face in her child’s hair.



nothing but the dread that the truth, at that moment, might kill she

to hear, prevent he, as he listen to her heart-break word, from

kneeling to she and call her wife. he watch she with a strange

steadfastness of gaze, and with a face more bloodless than her. the

impulse to avow himself have recoil and drive the blood to his heart;

a faintness overcome he, but he battle with the deadly weakness, and

the well to do so, rise and stride across the room and stand near his

wife and child, look down upon they.



“I will help you to the utmost of my power,” he say, speak slowly,

and with a difficulty that presently pass. “whilst I live, neither

you nor your child will be friendless. trust I, and make I happy by

know I to be your friend.”



she raise her feverishly-light eye, and say in a quick, febrile

whisper: “you cannot take the burden of the three of we upon yourself.”



“no! I would not raise a finger to serve your husband now. he have

money, but he leave you in want all day yesterday, and you have be

alone through the night... but I will befriend you and your child.

whatever I can do shall be do. I be not rich--I would to God I be,

for your sake. be I to pay this debt, I should only delay the loss

of your furniture for a few day; other would come, and I should not

have the money to deal with they.”



“what be I to do?” she wail, cling to her child.



“Mrs. Parrot’s house will be your home for the present. we must wait

until we get news of Mr. Conway.”



“oh, Mr. Hampden, be he not cruel to have leave I in this position!

no one know but God what I have endure during the last year! when I

be battle with poverty alone I be happy and rich. my memory

be fresh and pure, my conscience be clear, but I sacrifice they for

nelly’s sake, and now I be desert and the most miserable woman in the

whole world!”



she break into a long piteous cry, but no tear come into her eye.



“let I take you at once from this wretched home. come!”



he go to the door and hold it open. dolly stare around she like a

sleeper suddenly arouse, and then rise with the child in her arm.

Holdsworth call to the servant and tell she to fetch her mistress’s

hat. the “man in possession” lounge out of the back room and stare

with a dry smile.



“Goin’?” he ask.



Holdsworth do not answer he. the weight of the child be too great

for the half-faint mother, who totter as she stand. holdsworth

take Nelly from she and place she on the ground.



“you ain’t a goin’, missis, be yer?” say the servant, hand dolly

the hat, and whimpering.



“yes,” reply Holdsworth; “and if Mr. Conway should call, tell he

that his wife be at Mrs. Parrot’s.”



“oh, mum, I don’t like to be leave alone with that man!” cry the

servant, look down the passage.



“vy not?” say the man. “if you’re all goin’, who’s to cook my wittle,

I should like to know?”



“_I_’ll not stop!” exclaim the girl. “I wouldn’t trust myself anear

he.”



“you’re free to stop or go, as you please,” say Holdsworth, give she

some money.



“then I ain’t to be pay out arter all?” exclaim the man, strike a

match, and hold it flame in one hand and his pipe in the other.



“not by I,” answer Holdsworth, open the hall-door.



he take Nelly’s hand and give dolly his arm. she draw a long quivering

sob as she pass through the garden; and then, see some inquisitive

face stare over the wire-blind in the opposite house, hang her head

and step out quickly.



Mrs. Parrot, hear they come in, run out of the kitchen, and stand

look from one to the other of they in mute astonishment.



“Mrs. Conway will make a temporary home of your house, Mrs. Parrot,”

say Holdsworth. “you will kindly prepare a bed-room for she and Miss

Nelly, and place your drawing-room at her disposal.”



dolly have sink into a chair. he pour out some wine and hold it to

she, but she wave it away, strive to suppress her sob.



“oh, ma’am, pray don’t take on so,” cry Mrs. Parrot, go up to she.

“Things’ll come right, ma’am. you’ll be heasy an’ comfortable here.”



Holdsworth kneel on a chair beside she, hold the wine. oh, it be

hard that he could not take she to his heart and whisper the word that

would change all her anguish into joy. but if ever the barrier that be

raise between they have be feel, it be feel by he then. her honour

now, more than ever it have be, be become peculiarly his care. the

sense of she be another’s, that his own claim be as naught in the

presence of her belief that she be Conway’s wife, be never before

so sharply feel. her misery have give she in his eye a sanctity that

make his yearn love sacrilegious. Humility conquer emotion, and

he creep away from her side, and stand look at she from a distance,

hold Nelly’s hand.



meanwhile, Mrs. Parrot’s finger be busy with dolly’s hat string and

the shawl over her shoulder, and she murmur incessantly all manner

of kindly sentence, of which their extreme triteness as consolatory

axiom be greatly qualified by her motherly manner.



“there, my dear,” she exclaim, lay the hat upon the table, “drink

a little wine: you’ll be well presently. life’s full o’ trouble,

God know! and there be husband in this world as be enough to make a

woman forget her sect and strike ’em. but a friend, ma’am, be as good

as sunshine to a frost-bite man, and I’m sure you’ve get a good and

kind one in Mr. Hampden.”



“it’s my husband’s desertion,” cry dolly, “that I think of. I don’t

mind the loss of my home. but to think of _his_ desert I and my

little one when he _could_ not know that I have a friend--when I marry

he for Nelly’s sake, to get her bread. yes, Mrs. Parrot, to save she

from starve. and to feel that I defy my conscience only to be

bring so low--_so_ low!”



“God forbid, my dear, that iver _i_ should set husband an’ wife agin’

each other,” reply Mrs. Parrot, glance at Holdsworth, to see how

he might relish her remark; “but I _must_ say that, if Mr. Conway’s

leave yer, it’s a good thing, an’ the last thing on this airth as would

trouble I if I be you. you’ve go through a deal o’ sufferin’ for

he, an’ if he’s desairte you, you cann’t come to bad harm nor be he

to have stand by his home like a man, which he niver be; and there’s

not one o’ your neighbour as don’t know that you’ve have more trouble

than any christian woman I’ this world ought to have. and it may sound

a hard sayin’, but if he’s go,” she exclaim, look defiantly at

Holdsworth, “I hope and pray it’s for good an’ all.”



it often happen in real life, as in book, that a closing remark will

take a weird appropriateness by the sudden confrontment of the fact

of which it be only the shadow. Mrs. Parrot have barely shut her mouth

when the passage echo with the clattering of the knocker on the

house-door. never be such a delirious knocking. Mrs. Parrot turn

pale, persuade that Mr. Conway have come home drunk, and have reel

across to her house to demand his wife and create a horrible “scene.”



dolly raise her head, and it be plain that the same idea have occur

to she, by the indescribable expression of mingled hate, fear, and

loathing that enter her face.



Mrs. Parrot give her moral organisation a twist, run out. scarcely

have she open the door when in burst Martha, the servant from over the

way.



“oh, missis! oh, missis!” she screech, “what do you think? Master’s

drown! o Lord! where’s Mrs. Conway? he’s dead an’ go! here’s the

gent as bring the noos. oh, sir, please tell the missis here!”



she turn, and in her excitement catch hold of the sleeve of a little

stout man who stand behind, and literally drag he forward.



“let go, you fool! what be you a doing of? be you Mrs. Conway?” he

ask of Mrs. Parrot, who stand stare with wide-open eye, grasp

her dress as if she be only wait to take a deep breath before

tear herself in two.



“no, she ain’t! this ain’t Mrs. Conway!” cry the excited Martha.



“you tell I she be here!” exclaim the man.



“so she be; ain’t she, missis?”



“great ’iven! what a clatterin’!” cry Mrs. Parrot, recover she

tongue. “what be it you’ve get to say, sir?”



“why, this,” answer the little man, who be evidently a very

irritable little man--“Mr. Conway’s body be find in the river this

morning at a quarter before seven, and he’s lie now in the Town Hall,

and I’ve come to give the news; and curse I if ever I’ll undertake

such a job again, if I be to be maul about by such a fool as this

when I’m out of breath, and fit to drop with perspiration.”



“Mrs. Parrot! Mrs. Parrot!” call Holdsworth.



the half-distract woman run into the sitting-room, where the first

thing she see be dolly in a dead faint, lie upon the sofa, with

Holdsworth kneeling by her side.



“she overhear your voice!” he exclaim, turn up a face as white

as death. “pray God the shock may not kill she. look to she, Mrs.

Parrot, I _must_ speak to the man outside.”



he jump up and leave the room, and find the little irritable man in

the act of walk away.



“I beg your pardon. one moment!” he cry, run out after he. “pray

excuse my agitation--you have bring shocking news. be it _indeed_

true?”



the little man turn and take in Holdsworth from head to foot, and

answer: “it be true, sir. I’ve see the body myself. it’s in the Town

Hall. he’s be in the water all night, the doctor say.”



“all night?”



“he be find by a man name Williamson. they all know who he be when

they see he. he must have be drunk when he fall into the water, for

the path be wide enough for a horse and cart. Dr. Tanner ask I

to step round with the news as he hear I be come this way. good

morning.”



the little man nod and walk away. have dolly be a rich man’s

wife, a sympathetic deputation, introduce by the churchwarden, might

have make a procession to her house to break the news gently, but how

can you expect sympathy for the wife of a man who die owe everybody

money?



Holdsworth be stun, and stand for some moment stare idly from

the porch. he then return hastily to dolly’s side.



“she’s comin’ to, sir,” say Mrs. Parrot, slap the poor girl’s

hand, and expend what breath she have upon the cold white forehead.

“what awful noos, sir!... Conway dead! I cann’t believe it. and drown,

too! oh, poor wretch!”



“hush!” exclaim Holdsworth.



dolly have open her eye, and be stare blindly at he. he moisten

his handkerchief with water on the sideboard and press it to her

head. Nelly stand at the window gaze at her mother with a look of

wistful fear in her face. at the door be Martha’s countenance, seam

with line of perspiration, her mouth open, and her hair hang like

a string of young carrot over her forehead.



       *       *       *       *       *



“I feel very weak,” mutter dolly, strive to sit upright, but

fall back.



“something terrible have happen. ah! Robert be dead!”



the memory rush upon she like a spasm, and she speak in a cry.



“come, my dear, don’t try to speak yet,” say Mrs. Parrot.



“where be nelly?”



Holdsworth lead the child to the sofa. the mother look at her little

girl, open her arm, and burst into tear.



“thank God for that!” say Holdsworth, turn away. watch her face

as her consciousness have dawn, he have feel that, if tear do not

relieve she, her heart would break.









CHAPTER XXXI.



HUSBAND and WIFE.





the little irritable man have bring true news. the report be all over

the town: everybody be talk of Conway’s death. a woman live in

the road call upon Mrs. Parrot to give she the story, not know

that Mrs. Conway be within. her husband have meet Williamson, the

man who find Conway, and have get the account from he clear of all

exaggeration.



it be just this: Williamson be a carpenter, and be walk to

Thorrold Marsh to execute a repair job at a house there. he be this

side of Hanwitch, just by the bridge face Squire Markwell’s place,

when he see a human hand stick out of the water. he peer and see

a man lie on his back, the water half a foot above his face, show

the drowned figure as plainly as if it be under glass. Williamson

pull off his coat, tuck up his shirt-sleeve, catch hold of the

hand, and up come the body like a cork. the moment he have the body

ashore he know who it be; leave his bag of tool on the bank, and run

as hard as his leg would carry he into the town to give the alarm.

the inspector and two constable, and a couple of man with a stretcher

belong to the Town Hall, start out of the High Street and be

conduct by Williamson to the body. a crowd gather about the tail

of the procession, the body be put on the stretcher, cover up, and

carry to the Town Hall in the sight of a multitude large enough to

diffuse the news through the length and breadth of Hanwitch in ten

minute.



so dead Mr. Conway be, if ever a man be dead in this world; and now,

the woman tell Mrs. Parrot, people be only wait for the coroner’s

inquest, to learn how he come by his death.



but the verdict, however it might run, would be inconclusive, since

there be no witness to show how Conway fall into the water. but

this much be know; that yesterday Conway have call at the “Three

Stars” and order a fine dinner to be get ready for he, with

champagne and the good of wine; and to let the landlord understand

that he mean what he say, he pull out a handful of sovereign and

let they fall into his pocket again, chink! chink! when dinner be

do, he leave the house intoxicate, and what become of he the “three

Stars” don’t know; but the “Pine Apple” do, for he come there in

the afternoon and squeeze himself behind the bar, make love to the

barmaid, drink some tumbler of rum, and get into an abusive argument

with an ostler, whose eye he threaten to blacken if he contradict

he again. on which he be turn out.



that be his day’s history, so far as it be record in human

knowledge. the rest could be guess; and the public be not slow in

explain their theory. of course he be drunk, have roll into the

water, and be too senseless to get out again, though the water where

he lie be not above two and a half foot deep.



nobody care twopence about his death. it give the shop-people

something to talk about until customer drop in, and then it be,

“what’s the next article?” and Conway be forget. when a bubble

explode upon the surface of a stream, nothing mourn. the tide roll

on just the same, with sunshine or darkness in its breast, as the case

may be; the pike lose no jot of their voracity, and gudgeon swim into

their maw; the minnow jump at the fly. shall law, commerce, or

anything else stop because a drunkard be drown? cover he up; let he

hide his face until the pale jury come to take a peep; then pop he out

of sight in a hole, and get back as fast as we may to dinner.



but there be two person on whose destiny this man’s death be to

exercise an influence as wonderful, and gracious, and beneficial, as

though, instead of a dead drunkard, he be a good spirit--an angel

charge with a mission of love, send by God himself to work out and

complete the happiness of the man who have be heavily try, but who,

in his bitter trial, have never be find want.



and I truly think that for such man--man who in their sorrow

reverentially bow their head and say, “God know, I believe in he;

he shall lead I as a little child”--who murmur not, but, praise

their Heavenly Father always, make their action a profound heroism by

obey his voice in all season, not more faithfully in moment of

joy than in moment of anguish--for such man we shall seldom err in

prophesy a time in their life when the heat of the day shall be

shade from they, and their burden and their conflict remove. “o man,

greatly beloved, go thou thy way till the end; for thou shalt rest, and

stand in thy lot at the end of thy day.”



dolly have ask to be leave alone with her child. Deeming Holdsworth a

stranger, she have feel the restraint of his presence upon she, deeply

as she be move by his goodness. her heart ache: misery have master

she. the mere sense of having find a friend in this her time of

piteous need could not suffice she. more be imperative: communion with

God, communion with the husband who, she believe, look down upon she

from Heaven. to no mortal eye could she lie bare the exquisite grief

that lacerate her heart; and though she should find no comfort even

in the Heaven she turn to, yet her full and poignant misery demand

escape in word and tear, and she ask to be leave alone.



Holdsworth enter the room face his own apartment. this be Mrs.

Parrot’s drawing-room. here she have a piano; here she have some

wonderful stuff bird under glass shade; here she herself sit on

sunday with her mother, when her house be unoccupied.



he struggle to calm himself, that he might master and appreciate all

the significance of the position in which he be place by the sudden

death of Conway. but his mood be wild and hurrying; the play of

emotion be quick and painful. he see his wife in her grief; he see she

wrestling, with no eye but her child’s upon she, with the anguish that

fill she; he feel her loneliness: he feel the cruel hopelessness that

weigh in her heart as lead; he feel, above all, the dreadful sense

of degradation which must attend her reflection upon the death of her

husband, Conway; upon the wretched, miserable life she have lead with

he; upon the complete and bitter reversal of the sole end for which

she have marry he.



the barrier that divide they be go. could there be any scruple

now to hold he back from she? if there yet linger one feeling of

delicacy to prompt he to delay his confession for a little, until the

dead be bury, until something of the horror of the sudden death

have yield to time, should it not be remove by the knowledge of her

misery, which he have it in his power to dissipate and turn to gladness?

why should she weep? why should she feel one instant of pain, when he

could change her tear to smile, her grief to joy?



he steal to the door of the room she be in and listen. he hear she

sobbing, and that sound vanquish his last hesitation.



he turn the handle gently and enter. she be on her knee beside

the sofa, her arm twine about Nelly, her face bury in the child’s

lap. she start, look at he, and rise slowly to her foot. he

approach and stand before she.



“will you not trust I as a friend?” he say, in a voice a little above

a whisper.



she try to answer he, but her sob choke her voice. he seat

himself and take Nelly on his knee, and, whilst he smooth the child’s

hair, continue: “there _be_ hope, there _be_ comfort for you and this

little one. check your sob and listen to I. I can give you comfort,

for I have know what it be to lose one that be dearer to I than my

heart’s blood, to lose she and to find she again. she be my wife,

and I leave she to go to sea. the ship I sail in be wreck, and

for many day I lie consume with hunger and thirst in an open boat,

see miserable creature like myself die around I one by one. and

when I be rescue my memory be go; I could not remember my own

name, my home, the wife I have leave, the country I have sail from.

but the voice of God one day direct I to leave Australia and go to

England. I reach London, and there a man speak to I of Hanwitch, a

name familiar and dear to I for my wife’s sake. and when I come to

Southbourne, the beloved old village give I back my memory. I know

whom I have come to seek, and what I have lose. they tell I that my wife

think I dead, and be marry and live with my child here--in this

road--in that house yonder! o dolly! o wife!”



her sweet sad face, as he continue speak, have be slowly upturne

to his, and, when their eye meet, he put the little child upon the

floor and stretch out his arm, crying, “o dolly! o wife!”



but she!



there be a look of petrifaction, strange and more awful even than

death, upon her face; her eye glare, her lip be part: and to

have see she thus stirless, thus white, thus stare, thus breathless,

you would have say that she be dead, even as she sit there.



then the life leap into she, she start from the sofa with a loud

hysterical laugh, and fling herself on her knee before he, cry,

“John! John!”



“dolly!”



“John! John!” she repeat; and she bring his hand to her eye,

and stare at it; and then grasp his knee and raise her face to

his, talk to herself in hurried, inaudible whisper, and fix a

pierce gaze upon he.



“John! John!” she cry out again.



he put his arm around she, and would have press she to his heart,

but she keep herself away with her hand against his breast, preserve

that keen, unwinking, steadfast, wonderful gaze.



“do you not know I, dolly?” he cry. “look at I closely; hear my

voice! hear I tell you of the old dear time! we be to meet in the

summer, do you remember, dolly? and we be never more to part; and

you be to keep a calendar and mark off the day. o God! what weary

day--what endless day to both of we! and do you remember the walk we

take the day before we part, down by the river, where I sit and cry

in your arm because the sight of your sorrow break I down and I have

no more comfort to give you?”



but still she would not let he clasp she. still she keep her hand

press against he, and her eye, now grow wild and unreal with

fear, upon his face.



“o God!” he cry in his agony. “will she not know I? have my secret

come upon she too suddenly? darling! darling! I could not see your

tear, I could not hear your sob, I could not feel the desolation and

misery that be break your heart, and still keep myself hide from

you. oh, bitter have the trial be to watch you--to know you to be

mine--to see my little child--and to be as a stranger to you! call I

John! call I husband! speak to I, dolly! tell I that no change that

pain and suffering have make in I can disguise I from your love!”



she release herself from his arm and spring a yard away from he; and

there, as she stand transfix, watch he with large, steady eye,

she dishevel hair about her forehead, her hand clench, and her

head incline forward, she look like a marble figure of madness, her

habiliment carven to the life.



she have think he dead. for many, many month she have pray to he

as one in Heaven. do she know he now? yes, but as a dead man might be

know--with unspeakable fear and unspeakable love; with the horror of

superstition and the passion of deep affection.



thus they stand for awhile, their eye fix on each other: then a

heavy sigh break from he; he turn to his child.



“nelly, my little one, come to I! I be thy father!”



he extend his arm. the action and word break the spell. with an

indescribable cry dolly flee to he.



“John! John!” she murmur. “my husband--my very own! come back to I

from the dead! come back to I after all this cruel waiting!”



and then she break from he again, and watch he yet again from a

distance, then run and fling her arm around his neck, crying, “John!

John! why do you not come to I before? why do you not come to I

before?”



the hot tear be stream down his cheek now: he hold she tightly,

say, in broken tone:



“we be together--never more to part. I be thy very husband! I have

love thee always! oh, God be praise, the merciful God be praise for

this!”



“nelly, Nelly!” she cry; and she run from he and seize her child,

and hold she up.



“she be ours, John! our little one! we have find papa, nelly! there

he be! there be Nelly’s papa! God have give he back to we--we be

break-hearted just now ... o husband! ... husband!”



she break down; a dangerous excitement have up to this moment sustain

she. she sink into a weeping, sob, faint woman in a moment; but

his arm receive she, his breast pillow she, and there she rest

for many minute, with no sound to break the holy silence that fill

the room but his deep quivering sob.



       *       *       *       *       *



when we peep at they again, a half-hour have pass, and the wife be

seat near the husband with her arm around he; and the child be

on her father’s knee. the fear that throw a film upon the exquisite

emotion of the girl have pass; she be listen to his story,

interrupt he often with quick exclamation of distress, then

fondle he and listen again, vibrate with eagerness, with

love, with amazement, which make her pale face kaleidoscopic with

expression. he be tell she of his suffering in the boat, of his

rescue, of his friend in Australia, of his return to England, of his

arrival at Southbourne; and as she hear he tell the story of his

noble unselfishness--how, to save she from the sorrow and the shame

that must have attend his disclosure, if make while Conway live, he

hold his secret, but could not keep his love from go forth to his

child--she know that he have bring back to she the same grand heart

he take with he five year ago; the same magnanimous quality; the

same pure impulse; the same heroical capacity of self-sacrifice.



and then she tell he her story; and now it be for he to soothe she

with the love that have transform his face and make it beautiful with

a deep and subtler beauty than it have ever before wear. for, as she

recur to those piteous time of her distress, her tear gush forth

afresh and her eye grow wild, as though she do not believe in the

happiness that have come to she at last.



I see they sit in that room while the bright morning sunshine

pour upon the window and flood the floor with its radiance; I hear

the bird singe merrily in the garden, and the cosy chucking of the

hen and the sound of the fresh sweet wind as it sweep through the

pear-tree and send the red-edged leave rustle to the ground.



I see the child’s large deep eye wander from father to mother,

from mother to father, with the small face busy with the unformed

consciousness that struggle in it.



I see the mother careworn and pale, but with the light of rapture

on her face that disclose all its secret sweetness, watch, ever

watch, with soft eye shine with happy tear, the dear one whose

arm be around she.



I see Holdsworth with the patch of gray upon his hair, his sink

cheek and bow figure symbolising while his life shall hold the

unspeakable suffering of mind and body he have know since we first

behold he. I see he, with the calmness of perfect joy mellow his

eye, and enrich his face with a colour that owe its lustre to the

spirit, so that it shall be there in darkness and in sunshine, hold

his wife to his heart, often press his lip to his child, often

glance upwards with look of ineffable gratitude; and I think of

those two line which Goldsmith say be worth a million:



“_I have be young, and now be old: yet never see I the righteous man

forsake, nor his seed beg their bread._”



       *       *       *       *       *



a knock fall upon the door; the door be open, and enter Mrs.

Parrot. do she start dramatically? I promise you there be more

genuine astonishment convey by the little jump she give, as she

fall back a step and then stand stare, than in any movement

design to express wonderment you will see perform on the stage.



so the mystery be solve, be it? so her lodger ben’t a gentleman

after all, but an insidious man who, under pretence of like Nelly’s

company, have be pay attention to mamma! and now, with Conway’s

body lie in the Town Hall, dead only a few hour, be actually

caress the widow in Mrs. Parrot’s respectable house!



Holdsworth and dolly exchange glance, and dolly hang her head with a

look of confusion on her face (and well she may, think Mrs. Parrot) as

Holdsworth put nelly down and rise.



“I be really sorry to introod,” say Mrs. Parrot haughtily, “but my

motive for knockin’, sir, be to inquire when you would like your

breakfast sarve?”



“we’ll talk of that in a moment,” answer Holdsworth. “I have something

to say to you. this lady be my wife!”



“I beg your parding,” say Mrs. Parrot, grow very pale.



“my wife, Mrs. Parrot. you have hear of Mr. Holdsworth who go to sea

and be drown? he be not drown. I be Mr. Holdsworth!”



“you!”



she totter, run forward, grasp the table, and shriek, “you!”



“yes, Mrs. Parrot,” exclaim dolly, “this be John--my own darling

husband, who I think be dead.”



“and do you mean to say, sir,” gasp Mrs. Parrot hysterically, “that

you know who you be yourself all the time?”



“all the time that I have lodge with you.”



“An’ you’ve see your lawful wife day arter day without speakin’ of it,

or sayin’ who you be?”



“yes.”



“because,” stammer Mrs. Parrot, still cling to the table, “because

you say that a wife cann’t have two husband, and so you hide yourself

that you might spare her feelin’s?”



“yes, that be why, Mrs. Parrot,” cry dolly.



Mrs. Parrot take a deep breath, and then, to the amazement of the

other, burst into tear.



“oh, sir, I cann’t help it,” she sob. “I niver do hear in all my

life of such beautiful conduct. Niver .. and be this your child?...

why, of course it be! oh dear! who’d ha’ think that any mortal man

could ha’ act so nobly! oh, sir, let I shake your hand.”



she not only shake his hand, but actually fall against he and kiss

he; and then, overwhelm with her effrontery and her feeling, be

rush out of the room, when Holdsworth stop she.



“one moment, dear Mrs. Parrot. you be the only person in Hanwitch--in

the world I may say--who know our secret. will you keep it? we have

many reason for not wish it know.”



“I will, sir, I promise you,” blubber the honest woman, “since

you ask I; but if it ben’t for that I’d go and spread the noos

everywhere, I would, for I niver heerd of such beautiful conduct

before, niver, in sarmon or anywheer else; and it ’ud be the makin’

of many a man to be tell of it. God bless you both, I’m sure. God

bless you, little gal. you’ve find a good father--a rare good father!”



and out she run choke.



       *       *       *       *       *



so the curtain fall, for the end have come. no need to raise it again,

for you who have sit so kindly and patiently through this little drama

must know as well as I what will become of the two chief character and

their little one when they have make their bow and withdraw. Australia

be before they, with generous friend to welcome they to their new

home, and listen with interest and tenderness to their strange story of

bitter separation, and sweet and sacred reunion.



Enough have be write; the quill that have drive these creation

to this point be but a stump; the hand that hold it be tired; the

companionship of the shadow which have keep I company be break. what

fitter time, then, than now to say good-bye?









POSTSCRIPT.





I must claim the reader’s indulgence while I speak for a moment of that

portion of the foregoing narrative which refer to the hero’s loss of

memory.



that loss of memory have be bring about by trial and suffering

such as I have attempt to depict in the early chapter of this

narrative, be too certain to make it necessary that I should adduce

instance (which be very readily procured) as proof. that such loss

have last, not for month only, but for year, will be see by the

follow anecdote, which suggest this story, and which I extract

from the _noon Gazette_ of July, 1772:--



“last Sunday die at Winchelsea a character of whom a correspondent,

a gentleman distinguish both by his part and benevolence, have

obligingly furnish we with the follow account: that his name

be _William Stephens_, and that he be a mariner, who, many year

since, be press from his home to serve on board his Majesty’s ship

of war _the Vapour_; that he be then married but two week; that

whilst cruise off the Portugal coast _the Vapour_ be wreck, and

_Stephens_, with some other, save his life by cling to a portion

of the wreck, in which condition they languish near three day, and

be then rescue by a french merchant-man, who carry they into

_Bordeau_ (_sic_): that on _Stephens_ be question, he be find

to have lose his memory, on which he be send into England, and be

hire as porter by _Mr. Hudson_, of the _York Inn_, in or near to

_folkestone_, in _Kent_, where he remain for two year in entire

ignorance of his past, until, his memory return, he set off for

Winchelsea on foot, and arrive to find his wife marry to one _Eel_,

a cobbler, whose life Stephens threaten if he do not restore he his

_Nancy_. this the cobbler do, and so the matter end. it occasion

much gossip, and to the end of his day Mr. Stephens (who settle down

as a carpenter, having lose all relish for the sea) be regard with

curiosity, and have to the house of the neighbouring gentry, whom his

singular story never fail to divert.”



there be on record many instance of loss of memory, occasion by

various mean. in some case the deprivation have be complete, and

the restoration sudden, and resemble an abrupt revelation. in other

instance it have be accompany by faint, glimmering, haunt

reminiscence, create indescribable anxiety, but grow up suddenly

into a sound and permanent recovery.
